Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> There are two kinds of oaths which are four kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a> requires a reparation sacrifice for inadvertent breach of a commitment made by oath, “what was pronounced, negatively or positively.” The standard example of a positive oath is somebody swearing that he will eat certain foods. The corresponding negative is an oath that he will refrain from eating certain foods. The exact expression used, לְהָרַ֣ע ׀ א֣וֹ לְהֵיטִ֗יב, by its <i>hiph`il</i> form points to the future. A natural complement are backward looking oaths, if a person swears that he ate or did not eat certain foods in the past (Mishnah 3:1). These four cases are equal in sanctions for willful or inadvertent breach.</i>. There are two kinds of awareness of impurity which are four kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2-3" href="/Leviticus.5.2-3"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2–3</a> requires a reparation sacrifice for a person who became impure, forgot it, and then either ate <i>sancta</i> in his impurity or entered the Sanctuary. The two added cases are that he knew about being impure but forgot that the food was holy or that the place was a Sanctuary.</i>. There are two kinds of export on the Sabbath which are four kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to transport anything on the Sabbath from a private domain to the public domain (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1). “Transport” includes lifting up, moving, and setting down. The two cases where one is liable (for a sacrifice if the sin was unintentional, punishment if the transgression was intentional, and is prosecutable, or extirpation by Divine decree if the crime was intentional but is not prosecutable) are “export” by a person standing inside the private domain, lifting something up inside the domain and putting it down on the outside (e. g., through a window) even without moving his feet, or “import”, somebody lifting an object from the outside to the inside and depositing it there. The two cases where one is not liable refer to a person inside who lifts an object, hands it to a person outside (so that the object never is at rest) and the second person puts it down. Since no one person completed a criminal act, no one can be held liable even though the combined action clearly is forbidden.</i>. There are two kinds of appearances of skin disease which are four kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:2" href="/Leviticus.13.2"><i>Lev</i>. 13:2</a> defines impure skin disease as שְׂאֵ֤ת אֽוֹ־סַפַּ֨חַת֙ א֣וֹ בַהֶ֔רֶת “as elevated spot, or <i>sapaḥat</i>, or a white spot.” This is read as “an elevated spot (which makes the surrounding skin look elevated over the whitish spot) and a really white spot and their appendages”, deriving <i>sapaḥat</i> from the root ספח, “to append, adjoin.” This extends the definition of impure skin disease from two relatively well defined cases to two additional weaker symptoms.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There are two kinds of oaths which are four kinds,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a slightly garbled copy of the first paragraph in Tractate <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1, of which there exists a Genizah parallel (L. Ginzberg, <i>Yerushalmi Fragments from the Genizah</i>, New York 1909, p. 62). The text in <i>Šabbat</i> is original since in both versions, “here” refers to <i>Šabbat</i> while “there” refers to <i>Ševuot</i>, and in addition, the statement of R. Ba logically has to precede that of R. Yose as in the <i>Šabbat</i> text. Probably the scribe of the <i>Ševuot</i> text available to the Leiden ms.’s scribe had omitted the statement of R. Ba and added it in the text when he noticed the omission. S. Liebermann, in his Commentary to the Yerushalmi <i>Šabbat</i> (<i>Hayerushalmi Kipshuto</i>, New York 1995, Jerusalem 1935) holds that the source is <i>Ševuot</i>. This is difficult to accept; the text is from <i>Šabbat</i> but the problem is the discrepancy in meaning of the same expression “two which are four” used in very different meanings in our Mishnah.<br>The problem starts with the rather complicated language of Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1: “There are two cases which are four for exporting and two cases which are four for importing.” The Mishnah then goes on to explain that if a rich person, the owner, stands at the window of a house (which is a private domain) and a poor person stands in the street (the public domain), if then the rich person delivers an object to the poor outside, or the poor reaches inside and takes the object, the person acting is liable to prosecution but the other is not liable. (In fact, the passive participant never did do anything; the expression “not liable to prosecution” is inappropriate.) But if the rich person lifted the object, kept it moving all the time, and handed it to the poor who put it down, nobody is liable since nobody completed a forbidden act. The same naturally applies if the poor takes up a package and keeps it moving until the the owner of the house takes it and puts it down. In this case, the qualification as “not liable” is appropriate since both participants violated a Sabbath prohibition.<br>The question now arises whether the formulation “two which are four” always implies that the status of the two additional cases is different from the two original ones since in our Mishnah the same expression is used for oaths and Sabbath violations.</i> Two which are four for liability and two which are four for no liability, or four for liability and four for no liability<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">S. Liebermann (Note 5) proposes to delete “four which are not liable” as induced by the preceding statement about “two and two” even though the text is common to all three versions at our disposal and it is difficult to assume that the redundant text was taught in the Galilean Academy. For the rules of the Sabbath, the case is simple and there is no redundancy. In the formulation of Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 there are four cases of liability, rich or poor taking out or rich or poor bringing in. There are also four cases where there is no liability, depending on who takes up the object first and who takes over, and what the direction of the move is. The question now is raised whether a similar case can be made for the first clause in Mishnah <i>Ševuot</i> 1:1.</i>? Let us hear from the following: “There are two kinds of oaths which are four kinds,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Abba later in this paragraph should be inserted here as noted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.2">Note 5</a>. While “here” in <i>Šabbat</i> the Mishnah itself explains that there are two cases of liability and two of no liability, the situation in <i>Ševuot</i> is different; all four cases trigger the obligation of a sacrifice for inadvertent infraction and punishment for intentional infraction in the presence of witnesses.</i> Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says so, “there are two kinds of oaths which are four kinds,” not because of liability<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Yose disagrees with R. Abba. Since everybody agrees that there are four cases which trigger a liability for oaths, the fact that the statement about Sabbath is formulated in the same Mishnah and in parallel form implies the same meaning in both clauses. Since Note 7 shows that Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 enumerates four cases of liability, there is no obstacle to reading the Sabbath clause in parallel to the oath clause. It is shown later in the paragraph that there are explicit verses only to forbid export; the parallel prohibitions of import are rabbinic interpretations.</i>? And similarly, “there are two kinds of export on the Sabbath which are four kinds,” because there is liability<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Yose disagrees with R. Abba. Since everybody agrees that there are four cases which trigger a liability for oaths, the fact that the statement about Sabbath is formulated in the same Mishnah and in parallel form implies the same meaning in both clauses. Since Note 7 shows that Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 enumerates four cases of liability, there is no obstacle to reading the Sabbath clause in parallel to the oath clause. It is shown later in the paragraph that there are explicit verses only to forbid export; the parallel prohibitions of import are rabbinic interpretations.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Abba later in this paragraph should be inserted here as noted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.2">Note 5</a>. While “here” in <i>Šabbat</i> the Mishnah itself explains that there are two cases of liability and two of no liability, the situation in <i>Ševuot</i> is different; all four cases trigger the obligation of a sacrifice for inadvertent infraction and punishment for intentional infraction in the presence of witnesses.</i> Rebbi Abba said, there all are about liability, but here we come to state both liability and no liability. This implies four of liability and four of no liability. But did we not state, the doors of the Temple hall were two which are four<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 4:1" href="/Mishnah_Middot.4.1">Mishnah <i>Middot</i> 4:1</a> which explains that the entrance gate to the Temple hall was built in the manner of a city gate, a thick wall closed by an outer double door opening to the outside and an inner two-winged door opening to the inside. The expression “two doors which are four” is simply the description of the structure of the building.</i>? Can you say, liability and no liability<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is inappropriate here.</i>? Should we state twelve cases of no liability<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 counts four actions for which one is liable (complete actions, export and import for the rich person, export and import for the poor.) Then it counts four cases for which one is not liable, but since for any incomplete action one is not liable one could consider the possibility that the poor man reaches into the house, lifts the object which the rich then takes up and deposits on the outside. A similar convoluted action is possible for import; two actions for two actors each result in four non-liabilities.</i>? We come to state cases of no liability which correspond to cases of no liability<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Only those cases are counted where a direct action, resulting in liability if executed by one person, imply no liability if done by two. The convoluted cases of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.2">Note 11</a> are not noted since they do not correspond to a case that could involve only one actor.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>: bar Abba. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 2b-3a" href="/Shabbat.2b-3a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 2b/3a</a>, the argument is by Babylonian Amoraim.</i> said, what is this “no liability” which we stated here? Permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “no liability” is used in Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 in two completely different senses. As noted earlier (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.2">Note 5</a>) if the complete action is performed by one person, the other one is passive and does not infringe on any law; at all times everything he does is permitted. But if the action is completed by two persons, both sinned. While they are not liable for a sacrifice or punishment, they require repentance and Heaven’s forgiveness.</i>! Rebbi Yose said, the poor man and the rich man are one but the Sages counted them as two. Exporting or importing are two but the Sages counted them as one<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since both the rich man and the poor are described as executing the same actions, there is no intrinsic reason why they should be considered separately. It only is to emphasize the importance of the rules of transporting on the Sabbath. But, as will be shown in the sequel, not to bring out is a direct biblical command while not bringing into a private domain from the public one is an inference; the rules of importing must be transferred from those of exporting.<br>In <i>Šabbat</i>, the Genizah text and the first hand of the Leiden ms. read “Exporting or importing are one but the Sages counted them as two” but as S. Liebermann (Note 5) has noted, the reading here is supported by early Medieval quotes.</i>. Exporting on the Sabbath does not include importing; if one exports from a domain this does not include importing<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are no verses spelling out the prohibition of carrying from the public domain to a private one.</i>. If one who exports from one domain to the other, does this not include the one who imports<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Importing into one domain is exporting from another. There seems to be no reason to make a distinction between domains (even though there is a big difference since in a private domain one may carry without restriction but in the public domain only for a distance of less than 4 cubits.)</i>? In addition, from what Rebbi Yasa said in the name if Rebbi Joḥanan: Somebody who imports half the size of a dried fig and exports half the size of a dried fig is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">While any transport from one domain to another on the Sabbath is sinful, it creates a liability only if the object is of a minimal size (<i>Šabbat</i> Chapters 7–8). For solid food, the minimum is fixed at the volume of a dried fig. The two actions mentioned will combine if there was continuous awareness of the Sabbath prohibitions.</i>. And from where that exporting is called work? Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “Moses ordered, they made a public proclamation in the camp,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 36:6" href="/Exodus.36.6"><i>Ex</i>. 36:6</a>. The verse speaks of donations for the construction of the Tabernacle. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 96b" href="/Shabbat.96b">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i> 96b</a>) finds a tenuous connection with the Sabbath by a <i>gezerah šawah</i>, concurrent use of words.</i>” etc. The people refrained from taking objects out from their houses to give them to the collectors, who also did not take out anything from them to import into the office. Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Aḥa understood it from the following: “do not bring out any load from your houses on the Sabbath day, and perform no work.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 17:22" href="/Jeremiah.17.22"><i>Jer</i>. 17:22</a>. While prophetic books are not sources of law, they are authentic evidence for the understanding of the Torah by the teachers of past generations. It is proved that in the understanding of Jeremiah (whose student Barukh ben Neriah is credited with bringing the study of Torah to Babylonia) moving objects from a private to the public domain is a violation of biblical law. This supports the interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 36:6" href="/Exodus.36.6"><i>Ex</i>. 36:6</a>.</i>”
Rebbi Mana said it without attribution<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">He did not ascribe the following remark to R. Joḥanan or any other earlier Amora.</i>; Rebbi Abin in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: We did state two principles which do not compare. “There are two kinds of oaths which are four kinds”; one has to bring four sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3:1, explicit in <i>Sifra Ḥovah</i> (<i>Wayyiqra</i> 2) <i>Parašah</i> 9(8). For the four cases enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.1">Note 1</a>, R. Aqiba requires four separate sacrifices. R. Ismael infers from the forward-looking formulation of the verse that only future-directed oaths can trigger liability for a sacrifice. According to him, for separate oaths in the same period of oblivion at most two sacrifices may be due.</i>. “There are two kinds of appearances of skin disease which are four kinds”; one has to bring two sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The reference to skin disease also is in a difficult Genizah text (G, L. Ginzberg, <i>Yerushalmi Fragments from the Genizah</i>, New York 1909, p. 264 ff.) but it cannot be correct. The purification ceremony of the healed sufferer from skin disease is independent of the particular diagnosis by which he had been declared impure and the number of sacrifices due solely depends on his financial ability. The sequel shows that one has to read “there are two kinds of awareness of impurity which are four kinds,” the second clause in the Mishnah.<br>The difference between RR. Aqiba and Ismael is explained in <i>Sifra Ḥovah</i> (<i>Wayyiqra</i> 2) <i>Pereq</i> 12(7), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 14b" href="/Shevuot.14b">Babli 14b</a>. Both verses <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2</a>,3 describe situations in which a person might become impure; they both end with the remark “it was hidden from him but then he knew and was found guilty,” i. e., he forgot about the impurity, entered the Sanctuary, and then became aware of his transgression. R. Aqiba holds that since impurity is mentioned twice but the Sanctuary only by inference, no sacrifice is due for causing impurity of the Sanctuary, only for entering it in impurity. R. Ismael holds that the repetition of the clause implies the obligation of a sacrifice both for impurity of the person and of the Sanctuary. R. Aqiba will hold that separate sacrifices might be due for impurity originating outside the person (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2">v. 2</a>) and that created in humans (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:3" href="/Leviticus.5.3">v. 3</a>). R. Ismael will hold that separate sacrifices are due for a human entering the Sanctuary in impurity and for the impurity thereby caused to the Sanctuary. The problem is that the Mishnah is anonymous, representing R. Meїr’s Mishnah, which is R. Aqiba’s tradition.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Abin<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abin mentioned at the start of the paragraph is R. Abin the son, head of the Academy of Tiberias at the time of R. Mana in Sepphoris. The reading “R. Eleazar in the name of R. Abin” (In G: “in the name of R. Abun”, at a second occurrence “R. Eliezer ben R. Abun”) is impossible since R. Abin (Abun) the father lived a generation and a half after R. Eleazar. As already recognized by R. David Fraenckel (<i>Qorban Ha`edah ad loc</i>.) one must read “R. Eleazar bar Abinna”, a third generation Galilean Amora.</i> explained it otherwise: “There are two kinds of oaths which are four kinds” was said by Rebbi Aqiba; “there are two kinds of appearances of skin disease which are four kinds” by Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna of the Mishnah is not inconsistent in his use of parallel expressions but the two parallel sentences represent two different tannaןtic positions.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose: why do I need to follow Rebbi Ismael? Does it not come even following Rebbi Aqiba? Knowing and forgetting about impurity of the Sanctuary is the same as knowing and forgetting about impurity of <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah 2:1 explains that even R. Aqiba can hold that “there are two kinds of awareness of impurity which are four kinds” only if he distinguishes between awareness of impurity and awareness of the Sanctuary, there seems to be a possibility following him to require a sacrifice for infringing on the purity of the Sanctuary when there was awareness of impurity but oblivion of the Sanctuary.</i>. He told him, does not Rebbi Ismael have forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary, and we want to follow Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Only R. Ismael requires a sacrifice both for forgetting impurity of <i>sancta</i> and forgetting the Sanctuary (Mishnah 2:6). R. Ḥaggai’s inference is incorrect; R. Aqiba will not require a sacrifice in his case.</i>? Sometimes there is forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary but he is liable for only one sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Even following R. Ismael.</i>. How is this? If one became impure, realized it, then forgot about impurity, entered the Sanctuary, and left; then he became aware. Here is forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary but he is liable for only one sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">There is no guilt attached to being impure. Guilt by impurity is incurred only if either the impure person enters the Sanctuary or eats from <i>sancta</i>. In the case in question there was only one forgetting; there is only one sacrifice required.<br>The sentence is missing in G.</i>. Sometimes there are many forgettings of impurity and many forgettings of the Sanctuary but he is liable for only one [sacrifice]. How [is this]<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Text of G היךְ עבידה.</i>? If one became impure and realized it, then forgot and while being oblivious of impurity entered the Sanctuary and left; then he became aware<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">There is only one oblivion and only one sacrifice.<br>The sentence is missing in G.</i>. He said, there is no sacrifice due for this impurity. Again he became impure and realized it, then forgot and while being oblivious of impurity entered the Sanctuary and left even several times; in the end he became aware. Here are many forgettings of impurity and forgettings of the Sanctuary but he is liable for only one [sacrifice]<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">At the first occasion, he was aware of the Sanctuary but he thought that for his kind of secondary impurity the Sanctuary was not forbidden. Then he entered several times while forgetting about the Sanctuary. Finally he realized his error concerning both impurity and Sanctuary. R. Ismael will agree that only one sacrifice is possible.</i>.
New paragraph. “There are two kinds of appearances of skin disease which are four kinds.” <sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta Nega`im 1:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 6a" href="/Shevuot.6a">Babli <i>Ševuot</i> 6a</a>.</i>“Rebbi Yose said, Joshua the son of Rebbi Aqiba asked Rebbi Aqiba. He said to him, why did they say, ‘There are two kinds of looks of skin disease which are four kinds’<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the three similar statements in the Mishnah, this one does not seem to have legal implications.</i>? He answered him, if not so, what should they have said? He said to him, they could have said ‘starting with eggshell and stronger it is impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">While white spots on one’s skin in general are harmless (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:38-39" href="/Leviticus.13.38-39"><i>Lev</i>. 13:38–39</a>), if they contain discolored hair they potentially are sources of impurity. This is characterized in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:2" href="/Leviticus.13.2"><i>Lev</i>. 13:2</a> as “<i>s´et</i>, adjoint, or shiny spot” which is read as “<i>s´et</i>, shiny spot, or one of their adjoints”. It is indicated that the Cohen has to determine the nature of the impurity but no details are given, possibly to reserve diagnosis to priests. The details therefore are left to tradition. In Mishnah <i>Nega`im</i> 1:1, “shiny spot” is defined by R. Meїr as color of fresh snow, <i>s´et</i> as color of eggshell (or the color of the membrane enclosing a hard boiled egg.) These colors are characterized as appearing as depressions on normal skin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:3" href="/Leviticus.13.3"><i>Lev</i>. 13:3</a>). There are secondary forms for which the spots do not appear as if depressed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:4" href="/Leviticus.13.4"><i>Lev</i>. 13:4</a>), these are described as the color of whitewash used in the Temple and that of white wool. The Sages disagreeing with R. Meїr declare eggshell as a secondary color. Since in the Tosephta eggshell is treated as secondary color, R. Meїr cannot represent the teachings of R. Aqiba in this case.<br>In G there is an added sentence which cannot be reconstructed.</i>’ He said to him, to tell you that anybody not expert for them and their names may not see skin lesions.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since the colors are not described in the Torah, the uninitiated lacks the means of determining purity and impurity.</i>”
From where that they can be joined one to the other<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A discoloration cannot imply impurity unless it contain an inscribed square of the size of half a Cilician bean; this is defined as (36 hairwidths)<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>. The spot does not have to be of uniform color.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, the Sages counted them as two and counted them as four. Just as two can be joined one to the other<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are mentioned together in one verse.</i> so also four can be joined one to the other. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Abin<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abin mentioned at the start of the paragraph is R. Abin the son, head of the Academy of Tiberias at the time of R. Mana in Sepphoris. The reading “R. Eleazar in the name of R. Abin” (In G: “in the name of R. Abun”, at a second occurrence “R. Eliezer ben R. Abun”) is impossible since R. Abin (Abun) the father lived a generation and a half after R. Eleazar. As already recognized by R. David Fraenckel (<i>Qorban Ha`edah ad loc</i>.) one must read “R. Eleazar bar Abinna”, a third generation Galilean Amora.</i>: If it can be joined to what is not of its kind, so much more of its own kind<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse implies that spots classified as <i>s´et</i> and “shiny spot” are to be combined then certainly a shiny spot and one of lesser intensity are one and the same.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, it is not written “they will be” but “it will be”. This teaches that they cannot be joined one to the other<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts everything we know from parallel sources, in particular the otherwise exact parallel in <i>Sifra Tazria`</i>, <i>Parašat Nega`im</i>, <i>Pereq</i> 1(4) which reads מְלַמֵּד שֶׁהֵן מִצְטָֽרְפִין זֶה עִם זֶה “this teaches that <i>they can</i> be joined one to the other.” Already D. Fraenckel in the 18th Century recognized that under the influence of Greek the <i>h</i> sound was lost and there was no difference in sound between שֶׁהֵן and שֶׁאֵין.</i>. Ḥizqiah stated: It is not written “skin diseases” but “skin disease”. This teaches that they cannot<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Again, read “they can”. Since the verse mentions three different diseases, the singular implies that for matters of purity all three are one.</i> be joined one to the other.” <sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">A second version of the discussion between R. Aqiba and his son, not recorded elsewhere.</i>“He said to him, they could have said ‘starting with eggshell and stronger it is impure’ but should not have said, ‘there are two kinds of appearances of skin disease which are four kinds.’ He answered him, it teaches that they are not one superior to the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">One cannot say that the color of fresh snow, which is blinding in bright sunlight, is the same as eggshell, but that for the rules of impurity both are equal and the relation of the color of snow to whitewash is equal to the relation between eggwhite and white (unbleached) wool.</i>” Could they not be one superior to the other? If you say so, you would have said the darkened one is impure, the very darkened is impure. But the Torah said, <i>behold</i>, <i>the diseased spot darkened</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:6" href="/Leviticus.13.6"><i>Lev</i>. 13:6</a>. Since even for a darkened spot there are conditions which have to be satisfied before the sufferer from skin disease is declared pure, it follows that the change of color alone is not sufficient.</i>. The darkened one is impure but the very much darkened is pure. <sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 6b" href="/Shevuot.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i> It follows what Rebbi Ḥanina said, it is comparable to two kings and their two lieutenants<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">ὕπαρχος, lieutenant, <i>proconsul</i>, <i>legatus</i>, the second in command. The decreasing order of brightness is snow, eggshell, whitewash, white wool.</i>. One king is greater then the other king, one lieutenant is greater than the other lieutenant. But the first one’s lieutenant is not greater than the other king. Samuel said, it is comparable to two kings and two of their ambassadors<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He thinks that the secondary colors are much darker than the primary ones.</i>. One king is greater then the other king, one ambassador is greater than the other ambassador. But the first one’s ambassador is not greater than the other king. Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rav (Aḥa) [Ada]<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The reading in parenthesis is that of the ms., the one in brackets that of G. While Rav Ada bar Aḥawa (in the Babli Rav Ada bar Ahavah) is well attested to in both Talmudim, a Rav Aḥa bar Aḥawa is not otherwise known.</i> bar Aḥawa: A king, and his army commander, and the <i>Arghabeṭa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Probably the high Sassanid official mentioned in Greek sources as ἀργαπέτης, a Persion word “commander of a fort.”. The word is discussed at length by Geiger in <i>Additamenta ad librum Aruch Completum</i>, pp. 27b–28b.</i> and the Head of the Captivity. Rebbi Eleasar ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yose: The Mishnah implies that one is no greater than the other. If <i>s´et</i> whose very darkened spot is pure has a second color, the shiny spot, whose very darkened spot is impure, certainly will have a second color. He answered him, look at what you are saying. It has a second degree; should it not also have a third<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is what combines with what for impurity. It is clear from the biblical text that the spots in the original color combine, also that <i>baheret</i> and <i>s´et</i> combine. If one would establish a hierarchy of brightness as the parables indicate and <i>s´et</i> was less than <i>baheret</i>, a combination of <i>baheret</i> with its secondary color would be a combination of degrees 1 and 3, which we had excluded by a previous argument. Therefore <i>s´et</i> and <i>baheret</i> must be coordinate, not subordinate.</i>? What causes you to say that the very white spot, whose very darkened spot is impure, is the <i>s´et</i>? The kind of <i>s´et</i> is like eggshell. <sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Tazria`</i>, <i>Parašat Nega`im</i>, <i>Pereq</i> 1(4). A parallel text from another source is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 6b" href="/Shevuot.6b">Babli, 6b</a>.</i>“שְׂאֵ֤ת, this is <i>s´et</i>. בַהֶ֔רֶת, this is the shiny spot. סַפַּ֨חַת֙ is secondary to the shiny spot. [<i>The diseased spot’s</i>] <i>look is deepened</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:3" href="/Leviticus.13.3"><i>Lev</i>. 13:3</a>.</i>, secondary to <i>s´et</i>. What is the etymology of <i>s´et</i>? Elevated. As the shadow looks elevated compared to the sunny spot. What is the etymology of <i>deepened</i>? It is deep, as the sunny spot looks depressed compared to the shadow. What is the etymology of סַפַּ֨חַת֙? Adjunct. As it is said, <i>adjoin me please to one of the priesthoods</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:36" href="/I_Samuel.2.36"><i>1S</i>. 2:36</a>.</i>, etc.” Rebbi Eleazar said, these are the words of Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Aqiba. But the words of the Sages are that <i>s´et</i> and the shiny spot are one. <i>Sappaḥat</i> is secondary to either one<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since the word is placed between the two expressions.</i>. The Mishnah says so: “<i>Mispaḥat</i> is turned into <i>s´et</i> or strong <i>mispaḥat</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega`im</i> 7:2. מִסְפַּחַת is biblical equivalent of סַפַּחַת (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:6" href="/Leviticus.13.6"><i>Lev</i>. 13:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:7" href="/Leviticus.13.7">7</a>) used both for impure and pure spots, thereby validating the distinction between deeper and much deeper colors.</i>”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In any case<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to violations of the laws of purity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.1">Note 2</a>). A sacrifice to atone for such a violation, either by entering the Sanctuary in a state of impurity or eating <i>sacra</i> in such a state, is possible only if the violation occurred while the perpetrator was oblivious of his state (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2-3" href="/Leviticus.5.2-3"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2–3</a>). This implies that at some earlier time he was aware of his state. If he never remembers, clearly he has no occasion to bring a sacrifice.</i> where there is knowledge at the start and knowledge at the end but forgetting in between there is an increasing or decreasing [sacrifice]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Depending of the perpetrator’s wealth as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1-13" href="/Leviticus.5.1-13"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1–13</a>.</i>. If there was knowledge at the start but no knowledge at the end, the he-goat whose blood in brought inside<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:15-16" href="/Leviticus.16.15-16"><i>Lev</i>. 16:15–16</a>.</i> and the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no Temple, the Day of Atonement protects the perpetrator from judgment by the Heavenly Court.</i> suspend until it becomes a certainty for him and he brings an increasing or decreasing one<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The Day of Atonement suspends but does not eliminate the obligation; there is no statute of limitations.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “In any case where there is knowledge at the start,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 4a" href="/Shevuot.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i> From where that we require knowledge at the start and at the end but forgetting in between? The verse says, <i>it was forgotten</i>, <i>it was forgotten</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:3" href="/Leviticus.5.3">3</a>.</i> two times; this implies that he had knowledge at the start and at the end but forgetting in between. So far for Rebbi Aqiba; following Rebbi Ismael? For Rebbi Ismael [argues] like Rebbi. As Rebbi said, <i>it was forgotten by him</i>, this implies that he knows. “<i>But he knew</i>,” there is knowledge two times. Hence Rebbi Ismael [argues] like Rebbi, and Rebbi like Rebbi Ismael. This comes even according to Rebbi Aqiba; it is the same for knowledge and forgetting about the impurity of the Sanctuary as for knowledge and forgetting about the impurity of <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonian sources [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 14b" href="/Shevuot.14b">Babli 14b</a>, <i>Sifra Ḥova</i> (<i>Wayyiqra</i> <i>2</i>) <i>Pereq</i> 12(7)] this is consistently attributed to R. Ismael. The difference between the two is that R. Aqiba considers every stylistic variation a change in meaning whereas R. Ismael holds that “the Torah is written in the manner of common speech.”</i>. But some want to understand it from the following: <i>He knew and felt guilty</i>. Was it not already said, <i>he became impure and felt guilty</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:3" href="/Leviticus.5.3">3</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The first quote is from the verse about human impurity, the other about impurity from extra-human sources. While it was argued before that one can only forget what one knew, the knowledge explicitly required in v. 3 must be explicit, it cannot have been unconsciously absorbed.</i>? But if it does not refer to knowledge at the beginning, let it refer to knowledge at the end.
Rebbi Bun asked, if he did not know at the end, how could be bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since any obligatory offering cannot be brought voluntarily, the fact that a sacrifice is commanded implies that the person can prove it is obligatory, i. e., he knows that a sin has been committed. Therefore the mention of ‘knowledge” in the verse cannot refer to his knowledge at the moment he offers the sacrifice.</i>? But if it does not refer to knowledge at the end, let it refer to knowledge at the beginning<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">And the mention of forgetting must be interpreted as read by R. Aqiba here and R. Ismael in the Babli sources.</i>. They objected: Is it not written, <i>or his transgression came to his knowledge</i>, <i>he has to bring</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:23" href="/Leviticus.4.23"><i>Lev</i>. 4:23</a>, about the purification offering of the prince. Purification offerings are for inadvertent sins; nowhere is prior knowledge and intermediate forgetting indicated. R. Bun’s argument would force the transfer of the rules for variable sacrifices to purification ones against all tradition.</i>? Then if it does not refer to knowledge at the beginning, let it refer to knowledge at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">A “suspended” reparation sacrifice is brought if the person suspects but is not sure that he has sinned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17-18" href="/Leviticus.5.17-18"><i>Lev</i>. 5:17–18</a>). If he then gains certainty that he has committed an inadvertent sin, a purification sacrifice is due if and only if he gains this knowledge before the next Day of Atonement. R. Bun’s argument is justified.</i>! Explain it as referring to those who are obligated for certain purification and reparation offerings for which the Day of Atonement has passed who have to bring after the day of Atonement, while those obligated for suspended reparation sacrifices are no longer liable. The verse says, <i>or his transgression came to his knowledge</i>, <i>he has to bring</i> even after the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:6:2-7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.6.2-7.3"><i>Yoma</i> 8:6</a> (45b l. 47), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 25b" href="/Keritot.25b">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 25b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 6b" href="/Shevuot.6b">Babli 6b</a>; <i>Sifra</i> <i>Ḥovah (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 11(9).</i> And from where that it speaks only about the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i>? He warned and punished about impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:15-16" href="/Leviticus.22.15-16"><i>Lev</i>. 22:15–16</a>, both warning and punishment are written for priests who would violate the purity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i>. For the laity the corresponding verses are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:19-20" href="/Leviticus.7.19-20"><i>Lev</i>. 7:19–20</a>. The sacrifice for violations in purity is mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2-3" href="/Leviticus.5.2-3"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2–3</a>; one has to establish that no sacrifice is possible for violations of <i>sancta</i> which do not belong to the Sanctuary such as heave.</i> and required a sacrifice about impurity. Since punishment and warning spelled out later on refer to impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut</i>. 26:14</a>. The person who comes to eat his Second Tithe at the place of the Sanctuary has to make a declaration that he followed all the rules; in particular that he did not eat of it while in “deep mourning”, occupied in burying a close relative. Second Tithe has to be eaten in purity but no sanction for violation of its purity is spelled out anywhere in the Pentateuch.</i>, also when He made liable for a sacrifice it is about impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i>. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, since it says, <i>I did not eat from it in my deep mourning</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut</i>.14.</i>, I could think that an Israel who ate tithe in deep mourning should bring a sacrifice. The verse says, <i>from thes</i> e<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>. Prefix מ always is read as partitive, “some but not all.” Since it is not spelled out which infractions of the laws of impurity (or of testimony, or oaths) are included, and which are excluded, the detailed rules are left to rabbinic interpretation. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>.</i>. For some of these he is liable, for some of these he is not liable. I will exclude tithe which is not a deadly sin but will not exclude heave which is a deadly sin as it is said, <i>they would die from it for they desecrated it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:9" href="/Leviticus.22.9"><i>Lev</i>. 22:9</a>. First Tithe (of which heave of the tithe was separated) is totally profane in the hand of the Levite. Second Tithe has to be eaten in purity at the place of the Sanctuary but there is no penalty for violation of its purity. But heave has to be eaten by the Cohen in purity and violation of its purity is a deadly sin.</i>. The verse says <i>from thes</i> e; for some of these he is liable, for some of these he is not liable. Or since there<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The verse mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.2.4">Note 76</a>.</i> [one speaks about] heave, also here heave. But did you not learn it from foreign worship<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice atoning for inadvertent idolatry is declared paradigmatic for all sins in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22" href="/Numbers.15.22"><i>Num</i>. 15:22</a>.</i>? Since foreign worship teaches about all transgressions in the Torah, to say that as foreign worship is special that one is liable for extirpation if done intentionally and for a sacrifice if done unintentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice is spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22-29" href="/Numbers.15.22-29"><i>Num</i>. 15:22–29</a>; extirpation in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:30-31" href="/Numbers.15.30-31">vv. 30–31</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 69a" href="/Shabbat.69a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 69a</a>.</i>. This excludes heave which only is a deadly sin<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">But no extirpation is mentioned for violating purity of heaves.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Ḥinena.</i> said before Rebbi Mana: Did you learn this from foreign worship? Then one should learn from foreign worship that for everything one needs one knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither prior awareness nor forgetting are mentioned as prerequisite for a sacrifice for unintentional idolatry (nor for any other sacrifice not depending on the sinner’s wealth) one would have to explain away the mention of prior awareness for infractions of the laws of purity.</i>! He told him, foreign worship requires a fixed value [sacrifice] but the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i> an increasing or decreasing one. One cannot infer about a fixed value [sacrifice] from an increasing and decreasing one, nor for an increasing or decreasing from a fixed value one<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore the previous argument is invalid; one has to find another argument to exclude any sacrifice for violations of the sanctity of heave.</i>. How did you understand to say that the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2-3" href="/Leviticus.5.2-3"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2–3</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 7a" href="/Shevuot.7a">Babli 7a</a>.</i> speaks about impurity of Sanctuary <i>sancta</i>? It is said here <i>an impure animal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2</a>.</i> and it is said further on <i>an impure animal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:21" href="/Leviticus.7.21"><i>Lev</i>. 7:21</a>.</i>. Since <i>an impure animal</i> mentioned there is about impurity of Sanctuary <i>sancta</i>, so <i>an impure animal</i> mentioned here is about impurity of Sanctuary <i>sancta</i>. Not only Sanctuary <i>sancta</i>; from where the impurity of the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 43b" href="/Zevachim.43b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 43b</a>. The question is whether a violation of the purity of the Sanctuary can be expiated by a sacrifice or whether any such violation requires the full ceremony of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16" href="/Leviticus.16"><i>Lev</i>. 16</a> describing the Day of Atonement.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael"><i>Sifra Ṣav Pereq</i> 14(3–6</a>), partially quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 43b" href="/Zevachim.43b"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 43b</a>.</i>“The verse says: <i>his impurity is on him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:20" href="/Leviticus.7.20"><i>Lev</i>. 7:20</a>.</i>. How did you understand to explain it? About an impure person who ate pure [meat], or a pure person who ate impure [meat]<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This is prohibited in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:19" href="/Leviticus.7.19"><i>Lev</i>. 7:19</a>.</i>? The verse says: <i>his impurity is on him</i>. Impurity of the body, not impurity of the meat. Rebbi says, <i>he ate</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:21" href="/Leviticus.7.21"><i>Lev</i>. 7:21</a>.</i>, <i>his impurity is on him</i>. Impurity of the body, not impurity of the meat. Rebbi Ḥiyya says, <i>sancta</i> are mentioned in the plural<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">A well-being offering is always mentioned in the plural, שְׁלָמִים. It is argued that therefore a singular cannot refer to the sacrifice. The argument is unconvincing since the sacrifice is not called שְׁלָמִים in the plural but זֶבַח שְׁלָמִים in the singular. It also is unnecessary since in 7:20 עָלָיו “on him” refers to the subject וְהַנֶּפֶשׁ “but the person”.</i> but impurity is mentioned in the singular. How can I uphold <i>his impurity is on him</i>? Impurity of the body, not impurity of the meat. Rebbi Meїr says, the verse only speaks of one from whom impurity separates<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A person always can remove his impurity, for simple impurity by immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>, for severe impurities by one of the prescribed rituals. Impure sacral meat must be burned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:19" href="/Leviticus.7.19"><i>Lev</i>. 7:19</a>; it also loses its impurity by rotting but as long as it is meat it remains impure.</i>. This excludes meat from which impurity does not separate.”
If it is so, what does the Day of Atonement help him<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second part of the Mishnah. If at some time the impurity was known, the eventual obligation of a sacrifice is not eliminated by the day of Atonement. Then what is the effect of this day?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, the Day of Atonement acts for him as a suspended reparation sacrifice. In he died before the day of Atonement, <i>the sin is in her</i><sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:31" href="/Numbers.15.31"><i>Num</i>. 15:31</a>. The feminine pronoun refers to נֶפֶשׁ “the breathing person”.</i>. After the day of Atonement it already was atoned for<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Even though an eventual obligation remains for the living person, the guilt has been atoned for.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 7b" href="/Shevuot.7b">Babli 7b</a>, <i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i>4(1–3).</i>“<i>He shall atone for the Sanctuary from the impurities of the Children of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:16" href="/Leviticus.16.16"><i>Lev</i>. 16:16</a>.</i>, etc. In this aspect I have three impurities. The impurity of foreign worship as it is said, <i>to defile My Sanctuary</i><sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:3" href="/Leviticus.20.3"><i>Lev</i>. 20:3</a>.</i>. Sexual offenses as it is said, <i>not to act in the rules of abominations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:30" href="/Leviticus.18.30"><i>Lev</i>. 18.30</a>.</i>. Spilling of blood as it is said, <i>do not defile the Land</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:34" href="/Numbers.35.34"><i>Num</i>. 35:34</a>, a misquote from memory.</i>. I could think that this he-goat atones for all these impurities, the verse says, <i>from the impurities</i>, not all impurities<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Reading the prefix מ as partitive, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.2.4">Note 75</a>.</i>. We find that the verse treated the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i> separately; also here we treat only the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i> separately<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, the Day of Atonement is exclusively for repairing any damage to the Sanctuary.</i>, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Simeon says, from its place it is decided, as it is said, <i>he shall atone for the Sanctuary from the impurities of the Children of Israel</i>, any impurity in the Sanctuary. I could think that this he-goat atones for these impurities, the verse says, <i>and their crimes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:16" href="/Leviticus.16.16"><i>Lev</i>. 16:16</a>.</i><i>.</i> These are the rebellions<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Intentional sins, intended as “breaking the yoke of Heaven”. There is no homily on חַטָּאוֹת “unintentional sins” also mentioned in the verse.</i>, for so it says, <i>the king of Moab rebelled against me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 3:7" href="/II_Kings.3.7"><i>2K</i>. 3:7</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If there is no knowledge at the start but there is knowledge at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">If there was no knowledge at the start and therefore no forgetting, there can be no variable sacrifice. But since the purity of the Sanctuary or of its <i>sancta</i> was impaired, a sacrifice is needed which, however, cannot be that of a particular person. It must be the people’s sacrifice.</i>, the he-goat brought outside<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The holiday purification offering of the Day of Atonement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:11" href="/Numbers.29.11"><i>Num</i>. 29:11</a>) which is treated following the rules of all holidays and is not mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16" href="/Leviticus.16"><i>Lev</i>. 16</a>.</i> or the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no Temple.</i> atone as it is said, <i>in addition to the atoning purification offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:11" href="/Numbers.29.11"><i>Num</i>. 29:11</a>. The atoning purification offering is the one mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:15" href="/Leviticus.16.15"><i>Lev</i>. 16:15</a>.</i>; what the one atones for the other atones for. Since the one inside atones only on matters which were known<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Where there is awareness of violation of the rules of purity. For unknown violations see Mishnah 4.</i>, also the outside one should atone only on matters which were known.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If there is no knowledge at the start,” etc. What can you see to say? Does the one inside suspend and the one outside atone, or the one outside suspend and the one inside atone, or both of them suspend, or both of them atone<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are two purification sacrifices brought on the Day of Atonement, one (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:15" href="/Leviticus.16.15"><i>Lev</i>. 16:15</a> ff.) whose blood is sprinkled on the gobelin separating the Temple Hall from the Holiest of Holies and the incense altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:10" href="/Exodus.30.10"><i>Ex</i>. 30:10</a>), and one (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:11" href="/Numbers.29.11"><i>Num</i>. 29:11</a>) whose blood is sprinkled on the large altar in the Temple courtyard. The question is whether each of these has a separate function or whether the day requires a double sacrifice for all its functions.</i>? What about it? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, I saw something. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish was asking before Rebbi Joḥanan and asked him, what are the differences in atoning? I do not know what he answered him. Rebbi Ze`ira said to him, maybe it is the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.2.5.2">Halakhah 2, Note 90</a>. These are known differences in the power of atonement but have nothing to do with the Day of Atonement.</i> How did you understand to explain it? About an impure person who ate pure [meat], or a pure person who ate impure [meat]? The verse says: <i>his impurity is on him</i>. Impurity of the body, not impurity of the meat. Or both of them atone? What about it? <sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Le</i> v. 16:21. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 36b" href="/Yoma.36b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 36b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 12b" href="/Shevuot.12b"><i>Ševuot</i>12b</a>, <i>Keritu</i> t 25b.</i><i>He should over it confess sins</i>, these are intentional sins, <i>their crimes</i>, these are rebellions<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Intentional sins, intended as “breaking the yoke of Heaven”. There is no homily on חַטָּאוֹת “unintentional sins” also mentioned in the verse.</i>, <i>their mistakes</i>, these are unintentional sins. Then He said, <i>he will atone</i>. Rebbi Immi said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, <i>the he-goat will carry all their sins</i><sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:22" href="/Leviticus.16.22"><i>Lev</i>. 16:22</a>.</i>, he grabbed intentional sins and left out unintentional sins<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In fact only rebellions are mentioned to be carried to the desert even though three kinds of transgressions were put on the scapegoat’s head.</i>, to indicate that just as intentional sins do not carry the obligation of a sacrifice, so those unintentional sins which do not carry the obligation of a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">It is not that intentional sins not carry an obligation of a sacrifice but the sinner is prohibited from offering one (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:30-31" href="/Numbers.15.30-31"><i>Num</i>. 15:30–31</a>.) Unintentional sins only require a sacrifice if the corresponding intentional sin is punishable by extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 1:2" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.1.2">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 1:2</a>), others require repentance and atonement by the Day of Atonement. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 25b" href="/Keritot.25b">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 25b</a>.</i>. And why did they come here? Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Yasa, for suspension<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The commentators differ in what this means. The Day of Atonement suspends punishment to give the sinner time for repentance (<i>Qorban Ha`edah</i>) or the statement refers to the Mishnah that the Day of Atonement eliminates the obligation of a suspended sacrifice (<i>Pene Mosheh</i>). The sequel shows that neither of these alternatives applies but that the first alternative considered in the introductory paragraph applies; one purification sacrifice suspends punishment for certain categories of sins and the second atones.</i>. Would it be understood to suspend for those who eat abominations and crawling things<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Eating non-kosher animals is a sin (<i>Lev</i>. 11, <i>Deut</i> 14) but not one leading to extirpation. Therefore it is not subject to atonement by sacrifice.</i>? Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira: <i>Their mistakes</i>, <i>their mistakes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The first חַטֹּאתָם is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21"><i>Lev</i>. 16:21</a> and refers to the scapegoat and its limited power of atonement, the second one to the final statement <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:34" href="/Leviticus.16.34"><i>Lev</i>. 16:34</a> which declares that <i>all</i> mistakes are atoned for on that day.</i>. Since <i>their mistakes</i> mentioned there are those which carry the obligation of a sacrifice, also <i>their mistakes</i> mentioned here are those which carry the obligation of a sacrifice. This excludes intentional sins which do not carry the obligation of a sacrifice. What is left out by the he-goat brought inside<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are two purification sacrifices brought on the Day of Atonement, one (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:15" href="/Leviticus.16.15"><i>Lev</i>. 16:15</a> ff.) whose blood is sprinkled on the gobelin separating the Temple Hall from the Holiest of Holies and the incense altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:10" href="/Exodus.30.10"><i>Ex</i>. 30:10</a>), and one (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:11" href="/Numbers.29.11"><i>Num</i>. 29:11</a>) whose blood is sprinkled on the large altar in the Temple courtyard. The question is whether each of these has a separate function or whether the day requires a double sacrifice for all its functions.</i> the Day of Atonement suspends. What did it leave out? If there is no knowledge at the start but there is knowledge at the end.<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">This justifies the Mishnah; both actions of the Day of Atonement are needed. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 10a" href="/Shevuot.10a">Babli 10a</a>.</i>

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> But about where there is no knowledge either at the start or at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Undetected infractions of the laws of purity. Mishnaiot 4–6 are reproduced in <i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 5(2–50).</i>, the he-goats of the holidays and the he-goats of the Days of the New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:15" href="/Numbers.28.15"><i>Num</i>. 28:15</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:22" href="/Numbers.28.22">22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:30" href="/Numbers.28.30">30</a>; 29:4,16,19,22,25,28,31,34,38. These are public sacrifices; they atone for damage to public institutions.</i> atone, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Simeon says, the he-goats of the holidays atone but not the he-goats of the Days of the New Moon. What do the he-goats of the Days of the New Moon atone for? For the pure person who ate impure<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Impure sacrificial meat or cereal.</i>. Rebbi Meїr says, the atoning of all he-goats is the same, about the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i>.<br>Rebbi Simeon used to say, the he-goats of the holidays atone for the pure person who ate impure; those of the holidays atone for where there is no knowledge either at the start or at the end, and those of the Day of Atonement where there is no knowledge at the start but there is knowledge at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3.</i>.<br>They asked him<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He objects to the entire line of reasoning. The purification sacrifices of the holidays are given “to atone for you” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:22" href="/Numbers.28.22"><i>Num</i>. 28:22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:30" href="/Numbers.28.30">30</a>; 29:5,11), but no provision is made to ascertain whether atonement is actually needed. This implies that they must be brought even if not needed for atonement. The same applies to the other public offerings for which the purpose is not explicitly stated.</i>, could they be brought one for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">For example, a he-goat was dedicated as scapegoat for the Day of Atonement but escaped, another he-goat was used, and afterwards the original he-goat was recaptured. Since it had been dedicated, it could not revert to profane status. May it be used as purification offering on the next holiday?</i>? He told them, they may be brought. They asked him, since their atoning is not the same<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">As R. Simeon stated in Mishnah 5.</i>, how can they be brought one for the other? He told them, all of them serve to atone for the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The dedication prepares it to atone for impurities, to fulfill a biblical commandment. The particular instances of atonement are not on the mind of the person making the dedication; therefore, the he-goat may be used on all occasions where Scripture uses similar wording. It is noted in the next Halakhah that a dedication for sacrifice, whatever it will be, is sufficient.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “But about where there is no knowledge,” etc. <b>Halakhah 5:</b> “Rebbi Simeon used to say,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: The reason of Rebbi Jehudah is <i>and one goat’s he-goat sin offering for the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:15" href="/Numbers.28.15"><i>Num</i>. 28:15</a>, the sacrifice of the Day of the New Moon. (The verse is quoted not quite correctly.) The root חטא in <i>pā`al</i> means “to sin” but in <i>pi`ēl</i> “to cleanse, to restitute, to purify.” The word חַטָּאת “purification” can also mean “sin” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:9" href="/Exodus.34.9"><i>Ex</i>. 34:9</a>). Here it is interpreted in both senses. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 9a" href="/Shevuot.9a">Babli 9a</a>.</i>. This he-goat atones for a sin known only to the Eternal<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 145, the example given is that of a an unknown grave which makes everybody stepping over it impure; the impure person never could know of his impurity.</i>. I have not only the he-goat of the Day of the New Moon; from where the he-goats of the holidays? Rebbi Ze`ira said, <i>and a he-goat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">In all occurrences (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.4.1">Note 123</a>) the sentence starts with וּ which also could have been left out. This is read as referring to the first case. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 9b" href="/Shevuot.9b">Babli 9b</a>.</i>, the copula adds to the prior subject. Rebbi Ze`ira and<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Probably “and” should be replaced by a comma.</i> Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>He gave it to you to lift the sins of the congregation</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:17" href="/Leviticus.10.17"><i>Lev</i>. 10:17</a>, referring to the inauguration of the Tabernacle which was on the first of Nisan. On that day, three purification sacrifices were offered. 1° A calf, special to this day. 2° A he-goat for the Day of the New Moon. 3° A he-goat by the chief of the tribe of Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 7:16" href="/Numbers.7.16"><i>Num</i>. 7:16</a>). The verse does not spell out to which of the three it refers.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 9b" href="/Shevuot.9b">Babli 9b</a>, the entire argument is quoted as explanation of R. Simeon’s statement; also quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 101b" href="/Zevachim.101b"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 101b</a>.</i>. Where do we hold? If about Naḥshon’s he-goat, it atoned for his tribe. If about the he-goat of the Day of (Atonement)<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Inauguration.</i>, there is nothing similar in later generations<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to the calf (Note 129, 1°) which only in this case served as public purification offering; in all other cases the sacrifice is a he-goat. Since the verse is in the singular, it follows that only one purification offering was burnt; the other two were eaten [<i>Sifra Šemini Pereq</i> 2(2)]. It is characterized as “given to lift the sin of the congregation”; this is asserted only of the New Moon’s Day he-goat. It follows that the calf of the Inauguration was particular for the Sanctuary and the priests, Naḥshon’s for his tribe.</i>. But we must deal with the he-goat of the Day of the New Moon. What about it? It is said here “lifting sin” and it is said there “lifting sin”, <i>Aaron shall lift the sin of the sancta</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:38" href="/Exodus.28.38"><i>Ex</i>. 28:38</a>.</i>. Since there it is the sinfulness of the offerings not the sins of the offerers, also here it is the sinfulness of the offerings not the sins of the offerers<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">It is explicitly stated in the verse that the High Priest’s diadem is only effective to cure unknown disabilities of sacrifices, not of humans. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 25a" href="/Menachot.25a">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 25a</a>, this is the final answer by the fifth Cent. Rav Ashi after a lengthy discussion which also quotes R. Zera (Ze`ira) with a completely different suggestion which is rejected.</i>. What did you see to say, “for the pure person who ate impure”, maybe we should say for the impure person who ate pure? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, Rebbi Jehudah splits the argument of Rebbi Meїr; Rebbi Simeon splits the argument of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah accepts the argument of R. Meїr but excludes the he-goats of the Day of Atonement from the group. R. Simeon accepts the argument of R. Jehudah but excludes the he-goat of the Day of the New Moon.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">One may conjecture that originally the text read ר״י meaning “R. Jehudah” which was misread by a copyist as “R. Joḥanan”. (In Babli texts, ר״י has both meanings with about the same frequency.)</i> agrees that the he-goat brought inside does not atone; rather it suspends. This parallels Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira, <i>he shall make it a purification offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:9" href="/Leviticus.16.9"><i>Lev</i>. 16:9</a>. One would have expected the sentence to read וְהִקְרִ֤יב אַֽהֲרֹן֙ אֶת־הַשָּׂעִ֔יר אֲשֶׁ֨ר עָלָ֥ה עָלָי֛ו הַגּוֹרָ֖ל לַֽיי לַחָטָּאת. Then חַטָּאת would have referred to the he-goat and meant “purification offering.” But the clause וְעָשָׂה֭וּ חַטָּֽאת “he turns it into חַטָּאת” defines the word as “unintentional sin.” The he-goat whose blood is brought into the Sanctuary turns intentional into unintentional sins.</i><i>.</i> He fixed it for suspension, that it could not be changed<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">It cannot be used for any other purpose. If the companion scapegoat would die before it is slaughtered, it could not be used for any other purpose; it must be sent grazing until it develops a bodily defect or becomes too old to be used as a sacrifice, then be sold and its value used to buy other sacrifices. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Acharei Mot, Chapter 2 5" href="/Sifra,_Acharei_Mot,_Chapter_2.5"><i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 2(5</a>).</i>.
A he-goat which was not brought on the holiday should be brought on the Day of the New Moon. If it was not brought on the Day of the New Moon it should be brought in the future since from the start public sacrifices were dedicated only to be brought onto the outside altar<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>. It is forbidden to bring profane animals into the Sanctuary precinct. Therefore all animals brought into the Sanctuary have to be dedicated beforehand. One is careful to make only the most general dedication in order not to lose the use of the animal if something goes wrong.</i>.
But did not the Day of Atonement already atone<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Day of Atonement leaves a clean slate, why does the following holiday of Tabernacles need another 8 purification sacrifices?</i>? Rebbi Mana said, since it is written, <i>the pilgrimage of Unleavened Bread</i>, <i>the pilgrimage of</i><i>Weeks</i>, <i>and the pilgrimage of Tabernacles</i><sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:16" href="/Deuteronomy.16.16"><i>Deut</i>. 16:16</a>. Since the other two holidays are far removed from the Day of Atonement, the sacrifices are needed.</i>, it is as if they all atoned one atonement. Rebbi Bun said, since they all atone for the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i>, it is as if they all atoned one atonement.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yasa: The one who stands before the Ark on New Year’s holiday does not have to mention the New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The reader stands before the Ark. Before the invention of printing, he was the only one having a prayer text before him and was supposed to recite all prayers aloud. Except on holidays, the congregation were supposed to recite the <i>Šema`</i> and the <i>`Amidah</i> by heart.<br>In the main prayer, the `<i>Amidah</i>, in all four times (evening, morning, <i>musaf</i>, afternoon) one does not mention that New Year’s Day also is New Moon Day. (In the Ashkenazic rite, in which the verses describing the sacrifices of the day are recited, the New Moon is mentioned in the quote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:6" href="/Numbers.29.6"><i>Num</i>. 29:6</a>.)</i>. Rebbi Aḥa bar Pappus said, it was stated thus: <sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">A related text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 3:12" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).3.12">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i>3:12</a>.</i>“The one who stands before the Ark on New Year’s holiday in the morning, the House of Shammai say, he prays eight [benedictions]<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The three beginning and the three final benedictions required daily, one additional benediction for the Sabbath and one for the holiday. The House of Hillel require that the middle benediction refer both to holiday and Sabbath.</i>, but the House of Hillel say seven. For <i>musaf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Every New Year’s Day the <i>musaf</i> prayer contains three middle benedictions, one to praise God’s Kingdom, the second to His sitting in judgment over the world, the third remembering the <i>shofar</i> blowing at Mount Sinai and the expectation of the <i>shofar</i> blowing announcing the coming of the Messiah. In this version, everybody agrees that the holiday is mentioned in the declaration of God’s Kingdom; the only difference between the Houses of Shammai and Hillel is that the former require a separate benediction for the Sabbath whereas the latter hold that the Sabbath is mentioned together with the holiday in the fourth benediction. None of the parties mentions the New Moon.</i>, the House of Shammai say ten, but the House of Hillel say nine.” Should he not say eleven<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">An extra one for the day of the New Moon.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, where do they disagree? In a matter which needs a separate benediction. But here even on a weekday he simply includes it<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">In the morning, afternoon and evening prayers on a Day of the New Moon there is no additional benediction; the New Moon is mentioned in an insert in the first of the last benedictions, “Service”, which is a prayer for the restoration of the Temple service with a supplication that our prayers be accepted in lieu of sacrifices.</i>. So he should mention it in “Service”<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">The formula used on the other 11 months of the year. The current text originates from the middle benediction of <i>musaf</i> of New Year’s Day.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked, since the two he-goats of New Year’s day come because of the New Moon, why do you say that he does not have to mention the New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">While only the elevation offering of the Day of the New Moon is mentioned in the list of sacrifices for New Year’s Day (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:6" href="/Numbers.29.6"><i>Num</i>. 29:6</a>), the traditional interpretation includes also the day’s purification offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:15" href="/Numbers.28.15"><i>Num</i>. 28:15</a>). Then one should include a mention of the New Moon at least in “Service”.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, does Rebbi Abba bar Mamal<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read “R. Yose” since R. Ba bar Mamal is not mentioned in the Halakhah.</i> not ask correctly? As it was stated: “The two lambs of Pentecost<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Read: “the two he-goats of Pentecost,” one prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:30" href="/Numbers.28.30"><i>Num</i>. 28:30</a> for the holiday, the other in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:19" href="/Leviticus.23.19"><i>Lev</i>. 23:19</a> to accompany the two leavened breads which introduce flour from the new harvest to the Sanctuary. This is a statement of R. Simeon in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.1.1">Tosephta 1:2</a>.</i> and the two he-goats of New Year’s Day.” If the first atoned, what does the second atone for? For impurity that happened between them.<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.6.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:7, Note 166</a>.</i> Is that not Rebbi Simeon’s? And Rebbi Simeon splits atoning<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The answer is not acceptable since the <i>baraita</i> is attributed to R. Simeon who in Mishnah 5 explained that different categories of purification sacrifices atone for different categories of impurity.
