His opinion about the he-goat accompanying the two leavened loaves has not been recorded.</i>. But if both of them were for the New Moon? Rebbi Abba Mari said, you cannot do that, since it is written, <i>in addition to the elevation offering of the month</i><sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:6" href="/Numbers.29.6"><i>Num</i>. 29:6</a>. Since the verse makes a clear distinction between the sacrifices for the Day of Remembrance (New Year) and the New Moon, certainly for R. Simeon they must have different purposes.</i>. In addition, from what we have stated, “twelve for the twelve months of the year.<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.1.1">Tosephta 1:2</a>, a statement of R. Simeon about 32 public purification sacrifices to be brought every year. There is exactly one for each month.</i>” A patrician stood before the Ark and did not mention the New Moon; they praised him<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">When only the outline of the topics of benedictions were given but no prayer text were prescribed.</i>. Rav Hoshaia asked: think of it, if they slaughtered both of them simultaneously? What impurity happened between them? Rebbi Bun said, if all of Israel are proper, would they not bring what the Torah prescribed for them<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He objects to the entire line of reasoning. The purification sacrifices of the holidays are given “to atone for you” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:22" href="/Numbers.28.22"><i>Num</i>. 28:22</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:30" href="/Numbers.28.30">30</a>; 29:5,11), but no provision is made to ascertain whether atonement is actually needed. This implies that they must be brought even if not needed for atonement. The same applies to the other public offerings for which the purpose is not explicitly stated.</i>?
“They asked him, could they be brought one for the other,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the words of the Sages [imply] that one may change; the words of Rebbi Simeon [imply] that one may not change<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">It may be assumed that the Sages follow R. Meїr, for whom all public purification sacrifices have the same purpose. Then it is obvious that the particular day for which an animal is brought should not have any relevance for the substance of the sacrifice. But for R. Simeon (and also R. Jehudah) there should be a difference; even if in an emergency R. Simeon permits using a he-goat on the next available occasion, he might forbid intentional change.</i>. The words of the Sages [imply] that one may change, and you say “could they be brought one for the other”<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Since for the Sages the answer obviously is positive, why does it have to be asked at all?</i>? They objected to him according to his argument<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">They asked R. Simeon; for themselves the answer was clear.</i>. According to your argument, since you say that one may not change, could they be brought one for the other? Rebbi Yose said, since public sacrifices are designated only by use<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Animals dedicated for public sacrifice are dedicated “for any public sacrifice where they might be needed.” The exact kind is determined only at the time of slaughter.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">This follows R. Yose (the Tanna) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 4:6" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.4.6">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 4:6</a>: “Even if somebody did not intend [any of the specific uses] it is qualified; it is a stipulation by the court that the thought is determined by the officiating priest.” Since the officiating priest can disqualify a sacrifice by thought, e. g., the intention to eat the sacrificial meat outside the allotted time or place, he also qualifies the sacrifice by his thought if the animal had been dedicated as sacrifice. For a private sacrifice this implies that even if the owner had a disqualifying thought but the officiating priest served having the correct thought, the sacrifice is qualified. The Babli agrees as explained in Maimonides’s Mishnah Commentary <i>ad</i> <i>loc</i>.</i>: “For the purpose of the sacrifice for which it is brought it was sanctified from the beginning.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah said in his<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon (ben Ioḥai).</i> name, the rams of the New Moon Days atone for the pure person who ate of impure [sacrifice]. Those of the holidays add to them in that they atone for the pure person who ate impure [sacrifice] and for [infractions] of which there was knowledge neither at the start nor at the end. Those of the Day of Atonement add to them in that they atone for the pure person who ate impure [sacrifice] and for [infractions] of which there was knowledge neither at the start nor at the end and those for which there was no knowledge at the beginning but there was at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the Tanna of Mishnah 5. R. Simeon does not hold that the different kinds of public purification sacrifices are for different kinds of offenses but that there are different kinds of effectiveness, the power of the sacrifice of a more holy day is strictly greater than that of the day of lesser holiness.</i>.<br>They said to him, did not the teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon (ben Ioḥai).</i> use to say that they may be sacrificed one for the other? He said, yes. They said to him, if it is so then those of the Day of Atonement could be brought on the Days of the New Moon. But how could those of the Days of the New Moon be brought on the Day of Atonement to atone an atonement which is not theirs<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rams of the Day of Atonement were destined for two additional powers not in those of the New Moon.</i>? He told them, all of them serve to atone for the impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">125,</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The dedication prepares it to atone for impurities, to fulfill a biblical commandment. The particular instances of atonement are not on the mind of the person making the dedication; therefore, the ram may be used on all occasions where Scripture uses similar wording. It is noted in the next Halakhah that a dedication for sacrifice, whatever it will be, is sufficient.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “They said to him, did not the teacher use to say,” etc. Rebbi Yose the Southerner said before Rebbi Jonah: Would it not have been necessary to state, if it is so then those of the Days of the New Moon could be brought on the Day of Atonement since one increases holiness but one does not diminish; but those of the Day of Atonement cannot be brought on the Days of the New Moon since one does not diminish holiness<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">A general principle (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 3:3:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.3.3.15"><i>Bikkurim</i> 3:3, Note 57</a>; <i>Yoma</i> 3:8 41a l. 10, <i>Megillah</i> 1:12 72a l. 47, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.30"><i>Horaiot</i> 3:3 Note 151</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 12b" href="/Yoma.12b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 12b</a>). Since this principle cannot be overridden, it is an argument not for practice but against R. Simeon’s opinion that the sacrifices can be substituted one for the other and for R. Meїr’s that they cannot.<br>The argument presupposes that the cumulation of cases for which the sacrifices atone indicates a higher state of holiness.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Bun<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The name tradition is impossible. The second generation R. Eleazar cannot transmit in the name of the third generation R. Bun I or the fourth generation R. Bun II. Probably one should read: R. Bun in the name of R. Elazar or even R. Yose ben R. Bun in the name of R. Eleazar. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.5">Note 24</a>.</i> explained it by another explanation<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">To uphold the text of the Mishnah. Since the argument is directed against one made in the Academy of R. Jonah, of the last generation of Galilean Amoraim, it should be attributed to the absolutely last Amora R. Yose ben R. Bun.</i>: if it is so then those of the Day of Atonement could be brought on the Days of the New Moon, for included in their atoning is the atoning of the Days of the New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">He asserts that all purification sacrifices have the same status of holiness but their effectiveness depends on the intent of their dedication. One sacrifice atones for all instances for which it was dedicated but none for which it was not dedicated. He must assume that the dedication was for a purification sacrifice, not for “a sacrifice whichever it will be” since the only public sacrifices of rams are purification sacrifices including the scapegoat.</i>, but those of the days of the New Moon cannot be brought on the Day of Atonement, for they atone only their atonement. For if anybody ate five olive-sized pieces of fat and dedicated four sacrifices, being of the impression that he had dedicated five, did he atone<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that he is obligated to bring five different sacrifices for five different inadvertent sins punishable by extirpation of which eating fat is the paradigm (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:2-5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.2-5.7"><i>Horaiot</i> 3:3</a>). If he offered only four, one sin by necessity remains without atonement.</i>? Or if he ate four olive sized pieces of fat, dedicated five sacrifices, being of the impression that he had dedicated four, not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Automatically all sins are atoned for (even though one would expect the case never to happen since the owner of the sacrifice is required to confess his sin while leaning with his hands on the head of the sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:29" href="/Leviticus.4.29"><i>Lev</i>. 4:29</a>), and probably would detect his error.)</i>? And so <sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.1.1">Tosephta 1:2</a>.</i>“Rebbi Simeon used to say, thirty-two rams are brought for the public every year. Thirty one outside, they are eaten. One inside which is not eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Some of the blood of the purification offering of the Day of Atonement is brought inside the Sanctuary; the rest has to be burned outside the Sanctuary (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:23" href="/Leviticus.6.23"><i>Lev</i>. 6:23</a>). All other purification sacrifices must be eaten by the priests, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:22" href="/Leviticus.6.22"><i>Lev</i>. 6:22</a>).</i>. And the scapegoat. Twelve for the twelve months of the year. Eight on Tabernacles, seven on Passover, two on Pentecost, one for the day and one for the bread. One on New Year’s Day and one on the Day of Atonement.” <sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 100" href="/Psalms.100"><i>Midrash Tehillim</i> 100</a>. It is standard Galilean doctrine that the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 90-100" href="/Psalms.90-100">11 Psalms 90–100</a> were composed by Moses (even <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 99" href="/Psalms.99"><i>Ps</i>. 99</a>!), not only <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 90" href="/Psalms.90"><i>Ps</i>. 90</a> as indicated by its header. In the Babylonian tradition (transmitted by prayer texts) Moses was the author of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 90-91" href="/Psalms.90-91"><i>Pss</i>. 90–91</a> and the Sabbath of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 92" href="/Psalms.92"><i>Ps</i>. 92</a>.</i> When Moses heard this he said, it follows that anybody for whom the doubt of a transgression arises should bring all these sacrifices! Rebbi Tanḥuma in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: When the Holy One, praise to Him, said to Moses, <i>he shall confess</i><sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21"><i>Lev</i>. 16:21</a>. This resolved Moses’s problem and informed him that his prior concern, that the slightest doubt might impose an unbearable financial burden on the sinner, was unfounded.</i> <i>on it</i> etc., he started and said, <i>A Song of confession</i><sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 100:1" href="/Psalms.100.1"><i>Ps</i>. 100:1</a>. Usually, one translates “a song of thanksgiving” since this is appropriate for the תוֹדָה sacrifice [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 9:3" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.9.3"><i>Lev</i>. <i>r</i>. 9(3</a>)].</i>, inspired by <i>he shall confess on it</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Intentional impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Those that cannot be taken care of otherwise; for example, if a person intentionally ate impure <i>sancta</i> but was not duly warned beforehand. Then he cannot bring a sacrifice which is reserved for unintentional sins. He cannot be punished in court since he was not warned and therefore criminal intent cannot be established. The contamination of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i> is removed by the Day of Atonement; whether the person’s guilt is removed without due repentance is a topic for the Halakhah.</i> of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i> is atoned by the he-goat whose blood is brought inside and the Day of Atonement. The remainder of the transgressions mentioned in the Torah, minor or serious ones, intentional and unintentional, known and unknown, positive commandments and prohibitions, extirpations and capital crimes, the scapegoat atones<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">This is reformulated in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Intentional impurity of the Sanctuary.” etc. That means it atones for intentional infractions and suspends for the unintentional<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The purpose of the sacrifice is to safeguard the integrity of the Sanctuary. Therefore it has to repair all infractions which cannot be repaired otherwise, i. e., intentional infractions that cannot be prosecuted (for lack of eye witnesses or prior warnings). Since severe unintentional infractions (those if intentional would be punished by Divine extirpation or judicial execution) require a sacrifice, the public offering does not absolve the sinner from his obligation; it only suspends the damaging influence on the Sanctuary. The statement is incomplete since infractions for which the penalty is not spelled out in the Pentateuch (“simple infractions”) cannot be atoned for by a sacrifice and, if committed against the Sanctity of the Sanctuary or is <i>sancta</i>, must be atoned for by the public offering.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is repeated in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 12b" href="/Shevuot.12b">Babli, 12b</a>. From here on to the end of the Halakhah there is a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:6:2-7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.6.2-7.3"><i>Yoma</i>8:6</a> (<b>י</b>).</i> Are not minor sins [positive commandments and]<sup class="footnote-marker">183a</sup><i class="footnote">Text of <i>Yoma</i>.</i> prohibitions; are not serious ones extirpations and capital crimes<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah mentions “minor or serious transgressions”; this covers all biblical commandments. But “positive commandments and prohibitions, extirpations, and capital crimes” also describe all biblical commandments. Therefore the last clause of the Mishnah must be read as explanation of the preceding one.</i>? Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yoma</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah”. The reading here is supported by the Babli.</i> Jehudah said, so is the Mishnah: “Minor or serious ones. Those minor ones, whether he committed them intentionally or committed them unintentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer either to infractions for which the penalty is not spelled out (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.6.2">Note 182</a>) or to cases where guilt cannot be ascertained. The standard example is that of a person who ate one of two pieces of meat, one of which was kosher, the other one severely forbidden either as forbidden fat or as sacrificial meat which became impure (Rashi, Note 183). If it is not possible to ascertain which of the two he ate, then a suspended reparation sacrifice is due; but the obligation to bring a suspended reparation sacrifice is cancelled by the Day of Atonement, as determined in the next paragraph (and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.2">Halakhah 1:2, Note 69</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.10"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:1 Note 18</a>).</i>. Those intentional ones, whether he obtained knowledge of them or did not obtain knowledge of them<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the situation described in the previous Note. If he intended to eat one of the two, knowing that one was severely forbidden, he either committed no sin or he committed a serious crime for which no personal sacrifice can atone.</i>. The following are minor sins: positive commandments and prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">No biblical penalty is attached to the failure to fulfill a positive commandment. “Prohibitions” are those to which no biblical penalty is attached. The basis for the statement are the homiletics quoted at the end of this Halakhah.</i>. Serious ones, extirpations and capital crimes. Just as the he-goat whose blood is brought inside atones for intentional infractions and suspends for the unintentional<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Yoma</i> text adds: “for which no sacrifice is due.” This is understood, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.6.2">Note 182</a>.</i>, the same holds for the scapegoat<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Which carries away “all their iniquities”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:22" href="/Leviticus.16.22"><i>Lev</i>. 16:22</a>). This last statement is missing in the Babli <i>Ševuot;</i>, it is discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 25b" href="/Keritot.25b"><i>Keritut</i> 25b</a>. The scribe in <i>Yoma</i> originally wrote the same text as here, then crossed out כן “the same holds” and wrote “atones”.</i>.”
It is understandable if it did not come to his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Then no private sacrifice is due and the public sacrifice must atone for the damage done to the sanctuary.</i>. If it did come to his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Did we not imply that the public offering does not relieve the individual of his obligation to bring a sacrifice?</i>? Was it not stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.4"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:1, Note 20</a>. The parallel in <i>Yoma</i> seems to quote instead from parts of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 6:4" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.6.4">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 6:4</a>. Purification sacrifices must be brought even after the Day of Atonement but obligations of suspended reparation sacrifices are eliminated.</i> “From where that those obligated for purification sacrifices and certain reparation sacrifices for whom the Day of Atonement had passed, are obligated to bring them after the Day of Atonement, but those obligated for suspended reparation offerings are no longer liable?” Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, whether it was known to him on it, or not known to him on it, did not the Day of Atonement already atone<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">He asks whether the Day of Atonement eliminates the possibility of a suspended reparation sacrifice for the possibility of a sin committed prior to the Day. The positive answer was deduced from biblical verses in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.2-10"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:1</a>.</i>? Rebbi Simeon in the name of Levi [Sokhia]<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">This is the name (“from Sokho”) by which this Amora of the first generation is quoted in <i>Yoma</i> and other places by R. Simeon (ben Laqish). The name given here does not appear anywhere else. In the Yoma text, the sentence appears after the next; this seems to be more appropriate.</i>, the Mishnah speaks of one who rebels against the day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">While it is inferred from the Mishnah that the answer to R. Abun bar Ḥiyya’s question is positive, it is pointed out that the answer still might be negative if the person in question rejects the notion of the Day of Atonement and does not want to be its beneficiary. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 7a" href="/Keritot.7a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 7a</a> (partially <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 13a" href="/Shevuot.13a"><i>Ševuot</i> 13a</a>), has another example: a person who violates the Sanctuary late on the Day of Atonement and then dies. This example shows even according to Rebbi (later in the Halakhah) who holds that the Day of Atonement atones even without repentance, that the answer to R. Abun bar Ḥiyya might be negative.</i>. Why did he<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna of the Mishnah.</i> not say, if it was not known to him on the Day of Atonement? His words imply that even if it was not known to him on the Day of Atonement, the Day of Atonement atones<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">While it is inferred from the Mishnah that the answer to R. Abun bar Ḥiyya’s question is positive, it is pointed out that the answer still might be negative if the person in question rejects the notion of the Day of Atonement and does not want to be its beneficiary. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 7a" href="/Keritot.7a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 7a</a> (partially <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 13a" href="/Shevuot.13a"><i>Ševuot</i> 13a</a>), has another example: a person who violates the Sanctuary late on the Day of Atonement and then dies. This example shows even according to Rebbi (later in the Halakhah) who holds that the Day of Atonement atones even without repentance, that the answer to R. Abun bar Ḥiyya might be negative.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">After the first two sentences, which are identical here and in Yoma except for the spelling of R. Ze`ira’s name, the text in <i>Yoma</i> is quite different (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.6.5">Note 202</a>).</i> A positive commandment, even if he did not repent. A prohibition? Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira, only if he repented<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">While the Mishnah in Rav Jehudah’s interpretation treats positive commandments and simple prohibitions in parallel, there is a difference between the two kinds of sins. The non-performance of a positive commandment is atoned for even without repentance while the atoning for breaching simple prohibitions requires repentance.</i>. If one said, “the elevation offering does not atone,<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical text does not indicate for which kind of sin an elevation offering does atone but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:4" href="/Leviticus.1.4"><i>Lev</i>. 1:4</a> indicates that it atones. The next paragraph will investigate for which sins it is atoning.</i>” does the elevation offering not atone? It atones even against his will. If one said, “the Day of Atonement does not atone,” does the Day of Atonement not atone? It atones even against his will. “I cannot accept that it atone for me,” it does not atone against his will<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">In the prior formulation, it was simply a false statement. But if somebody said, I am opting out, the atoning power of sacrifices shall not be valid for me, what he offers would be profane. If there is no offering, there cannot be atonement.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina ben Rebbi Hillel said, the opposite is reasonable<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Since he brings the offering on his own initiative, if it is not brought for atoning it does not atone. But the Day of Atonement is given by God; it is not up to man to say what it can or cannot do. This is clearer from the <i>Yoma</i> text which therefore must be taken as original:<br>הָאוֹמֵר. אֵין הָעוֹלָה מְכַפֶּרֶת. אֵין הָעוֹלָה מְכַפֶּרֶת עָלי. מְכַפֶּרֶת הִיא. אֵי אֶיפְשִׁי שֶׁתְּכַפֵּר לִי. אֵינָהּ מְכַפֶּרֶת לוֹ עַל כָּרְחוֹ. אֵין יוֹם הַכִּיפּוּרִים מְכַפֵּר מְכַפֶּרֶת הִיא. אֵי אֶיפְשִׁי שֶׁיְּכַפֵּר לִי. מְכַפֵּר הוּא לוֹ עַל כּוֹרְחוֹ. אָמַר רִבִּי חֲנַנְיָה בְּרֵיהּ דְּרִבִּי הִלֵּל. לָא כוֹלָא מִן הָדֵין בַּר נַשָּׂא מֵימוֹר לְמַלְכָּא. לֵית אַתְּ מֶלֶךְ.<br>If one said, “the elevation offering does not atone,” “the elevation offering does not atone for me,” it atones. “I cannot stand that it atone for me,” it does not atone against his will. “The day of Atonement does not atone,” it atones. “I cannot stand that it atone for me,” it atones for him against his will. Rebbi Hanania ben Rebbi Hillel said, it is not up to that man to say to the King, you are no King.<br>Here the introductory statement of R. Ḥanina (Ḥanania) ben R. Hillel is missing correctly. The two texts are separate formulations.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 7a" href="/Keritot.7a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 7a</a>, there is a related discussion.</i>. It is not up to a person to tell the King, “you do not reign.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">In slightly different formulation in <i>Yoma</i>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 7:3" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.7.3"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 7(3</a>) the paragraph is reproduced and the doctrine is attributed to R. Simeon ben Ioḥai.</i> The elevation offering atones for thoughts. What is the reason? <i>What rises in your spirits will not be</i><sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 20:32" href="/Ezekiel.20.32"><i>Ez</i>. 20:32</a>.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, the elevation offering atones for your spirits<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">This is an untranslatable pun. The verbal noun הָעוֹלָה “that which rises” (<i>scil</i>. “your thoughts”, f.) is identified with the homonym הָעוֹלָה “the elevation offering”.</i>. And so in <i>Job</i> he says, <i>maybe my children sinned and cursed God in their hearts</i><sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 13a" href="/Shevuot.13a">Babli 13a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 85b" href="/Yoma.85b"><i>Yoma</i> 85b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 7a" href="/Keritot.7a"><i>Keritut</i> 7a</a>. The parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 45b" href="/Yoma.45b"><i>Yoma</i> (45b</a> l. 63 ff.) at the end reports the opposite of the tradition here.</i>. This implies that the elevation offering atones for thoughts.
<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 13a" href="/Shevuot.13a">Babli 13a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 85b" href="/Yoma.85b"><i>Yoma</i> 85b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 7a" href="/Keritot.7a"><i>Keritut</i> 7a</a>. The parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 45b" href="/Yoma.45b"><i>Yoma</i> (45b</a> l. 63 ff.) at the end reports the opposite of the tradition here.</i> Rebbi says, the Day of Atonement atones for all sins against the Torah except for him who tears away the yoke, or who breaks the Covenant, or who finds aspects in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">This was explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:53-57" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.53-57"><i>Peah</i> 1:1 Notes 199–213</a>; cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.1.2"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1, Note 8</a>. One who tears away the yoke is he who recognizes the authority of the Torah but decides to break its laws; one who breaks the covenant is he who tries to reconstruct a prepuce and does not circumcise his sons; one who finds aspects in the Torah is he who denies the divine origin of the Torah.</i>, where it atones if he repented but does not atone otherwise. Rebbi Zebida said: Rebbi Yasa asked: Does Rebbi think that the Day of Atonement atones without repentance? There came Rebbi Ashian, Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Day of Atonement atones without repentance, and death cleanses without repentance<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yoma</i>: Rebbi agrees that the Day of Atonement does not atone without repentance, but death atones without repentance. The Babli supports the statement here about the Day of Atonement; it has no statement of Rebbi about the power of Death.</i>. We have stated thus: The day of death equals repentance. Who stated this? Rebbi! Then what we stated<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.6.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 8:7</a>.</i>, “death and the Day of Atonement atone with repentance,” does not follow Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah requires repentance also on the Day of Atonement. Rebbi formulated an anonymous Mishnah, i. e., authoritative doctrine, following the majority against his own opinion.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is not only in <i>Yoma</i> but also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:1:8-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.1.8-9"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1, Notes 23–37</a>, 14–22 (and in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 86b" href="/Yoma.86b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 86b</a>). For part of the text there exists a Geniza fragment (G) edited by L. Ginzberg in his <i>Yerushalmi</i> <i>Fragments</i> (1909) p. 267. The main difference between the Leiden ms. text here and in <i>Yoma</i> and the two other sources is that in the Leiden text the quotes of most verses are shortened so that the punch lines mostly are missing. Therefore the <i>Sanhedrin</i> and G texts should be considered original.</i> Rebbi Matthew ben Ḥarash asked Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah in the Academy, did you hear the four types of Atonement which Rebbi Ismael explained? He answered him, there are three in addition to repentance. One verse says, <i>return</i>, <i>naughty children</i><sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 3:22" href="/Jeremiah.3.22"><i>Jer</i>. 3:22</a>.</i>, etc. But another verse says, <i>for on that day</i>, <i>He shall pardon you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:20" href="/Leviticus.16.20"><i>Lev</i>. 16:20</a>.</i>, etc. And another verse says, <i>I shall visit their crime with the rod</i><sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 89:33" href="/Psalms.89.33"><i>Ps</i>. 89:33</a>.</i>, etc<i>.</i> And another verse says, <i>the iniquity of this people shall not be atoned for until you die</i><sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 22:14" href="/Isaiah.22.14"><i>Is</i>. 22:14</a>.</i><i>.</i> How is this? If somebody violates a positive commandment and repents, before he moves from there it will be forgiven to him. About this one it says, <i>return</i>, <i>naughty children</i>, etc. If one transgresses a prohibition and immediately repents, repentance suspends judgment, and the Day of Atonement pardons. About this one it says, <i>for on</i><i>that day</i>, <i>He shall pardon you</i>. If one intentionally transgressed [sins punishable by] extirpations or death penalties, repentance and the Day of Atonement atone half, and sufferings atone half. About this one it says, <i>I shall visit their crime with the rod</i>, <i>and their iniquities with plagues</i>. But by whom the Name of Heaven was desecrated, there is no power in repentance to suspend judgment, nor in the Day of Atonement to pardon, nor in sufferings to scour; but repentance and the Day of Atonement suspend, and death scours with sufferings<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 3:22" href="/Jeremiah.3.22"><i>Jer</i>. 3:22</a>.</i>. About this one it says, <i>the iniquity of this people shall not be atoned for until you die</i>. From this we learn that death scours.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are the words of Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, Rebbi Ismael, and Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say that the scapegoat pardons. How does it pardon? Rebbi Ze`ira said, by and by. Rebbi Ḥanania<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">This name is used throughout in G and <i>Yoma</i>.</i> said, at the end. What is between them? If somebody died in-between. In the opinion of Rebbi Ze`ira, he already was pardoned. In the opinion of Rebbi Ḥanina, he was not pardoned. Rebbi Ze`ira said, a <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ḥanina: There is strength in the he-goat which is not in the Day of Atonement, and in the Day of Atonement which is not in the he-goat: The Day of Atonement pardons without a he-goat, but the he-goat does not atone without the Day of Atonement. The he-goat pardons immediately but the Day of Atonement only at nightfall. Rebbi Huna said, the question was raised before Rebbi Jeremiah and he said, explain it if they intended to bring another he-goat but they did not bring it. Rebbi Yose said, but does the Holy One, praise to Him, not see into the future? Then He should pardon immediately.
Rebbi Jeremiah said that Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked: <i>Justice protects the one on the straight path</i><sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:6" href="/Proverbs.13.6"><i>Prov</i>. 13:6</a>.</i>. <i>Evil deeds will pursue the sinner</i><sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:4" href="/Proverbs.13.4"><i>Prov</i>. 13:4</a>.</i>. <i>He will watch over the feet of His pious ones</i><sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:9" href="/I_Samuel.2.9"><i>1S</i>. 2:9</a>.</i>. <i>While He makes scoffers targets of scoffing</i><sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:34" href="/Proverbs.3.34"><i>Prov</i>. 3:34</a>.</i>. Fences are made fences and doors doors. Is it so that fences make fences and doors doors? But so it is: Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac: If a person is careful the first, second, and third times not to commit a sin, then the Holy One, praise to Him, will watch over him, as it is said, <i>all this God will do twice, three times with a man</i><sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 33:29" href="/Job.33.29"><i>Job</i> 33:29</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, but only if the person does not revert [to sin]. What is the reason? It does not say <i>the triple thread</i> “will never snap” but rather <i>will not quickly snap</i><sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 4:12" href="/Ecclesiastes.4.12"><i>Eccl</i>. 4:12</a>.</i>. If you work on it, it will split.
Rebbi Huna said in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: There is no forgetting before the Holy One, but for Israel He becomes forgetful. What is the reason? As it is said, <i>Who is a Power like You</i>, <i>forgives sin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:18" href="/Micah.7.18"><i>Micha</i> 7:18</a>.</i>. “He forgets” is written. And so David said, <i>You forgot Your people’s sin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 85:3" href="/Psalms.85.3"><i>Ps</i>. 85:3</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Together Israel, Cohanim, and the Anointed Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">This fraction of a sentence is the end of the preceding Mishnah. The scapegoat atones equally for Israel, Cohanim and the High Priest. The expression “Anointed Priest” is biblical (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15"><i>Lev</i>. 6:15</a>); as explained in Tractate <i>Horaiot</i> it excludes the High Priests of the Second Temple who were invested, not anointed.</i>. What is the difference between Israel and a Cohen or the Anointed Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference not only on the Day of Atonement, but all year round: the purification offering of the Anointed Priest (not the invested one) is a bull, but everybody else’s a lamb. For impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i>, everybody has to bring a sacrifice depending on his wealth except the Anointed Priest who is exempt (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.7.1">Mishnah <i>Horaiot</i> 2:7</a>).</i>? Only that the blood of the bull atones for Cohanim in matters of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, just as the blood of the he-goat which is brought inside atones for Israel, so the blood of the bull atones for the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The actual atoning sacrifices on the Day of Atonement are separate for the High Priest (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:6" href="/Leviticus.16.6"><i>Lev</i>. 16:6</a>), the common priests (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:13" href="/Leviticus.16.13">v. 13</a>), and the people. But for the action of the scapegoat they are all equal.</i>. Just as the confession of the scapegoat<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21"><i>Lev</i>. 16:21</a>.</i> atones for Israel, so the confession of the bull atones for Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the anonymous Mishnah and restricts the action of the scapegoat to the benefit of the people.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Together Israel, Cohanim,” etc. But we did not state<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">The Anointed is mentioned in the anonymous statement, the first sentence of the Mishnah. Why is he not mentioned in R. Simeon’s statement?</i> “the Anointed.” Why did we not state “the Anointed”? If you say that we are discussing things which do not imply the obligation of a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">For sins which do not require a purification sacrifice, the status of the High Priest is no different from that of a common priest. The mention of “priests” includes the High Priest.</i>, it is correct not to state “Anointed”. If you say about things which imply the obligation of a sacrifice, why do we not state “Anointed”<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">Since the purification sacrifice of the Anointed Priest is different from that of everybody else (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.7.1">Note 226</a>), it is necessary to mention him if one asserts that the scapegoat really serves everybody.</i>? It was found stated “also the Anointed Priest.”<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">In a <i>baraita</i>.</i> This implies that we are discussing things which do not imply the obligation of a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">In R. Simeon’s statement, the Anointed is not mentioned by design; therefore, the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.7.2">Note 231</a> applies.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, even if you say, about things which imply the obligation of a sacrifice, if it was done intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">It is not that intentional sins not carry an obligation of a sacrifice but the sinner is prohibited from offering one (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:30-31" href="/Numbers.15.30-31"><i>Num</i>. 15:30–31</a>.) Unintentional sins only require a sacrifice if the corresponding intentional sin is punishable by extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 1:2" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.1.2">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 1:2</a>), others require repentance and atonement by the Day of Atonement. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 25b" href="/Keritot.25b">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 25b</a>.</i>. As you say, intentionally it needs atoning and the atoning of the Day of Atonement, here also it needs atoning and the atoning of the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">As explained by R. Eleazar ben Azariah, intentional sins will be forgiven only by prior repentance combined with the Day of Atonement.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 6:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.6.2.2-3"><i>Yoma</i> 6:2</a>. However, there the sequence of topics is different. <i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 2(6–8).</i><i>Shall be stationed alive</i>; this teaches that it the future it is going to die<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:10" href="/Leviticus.16.10"><i>Lev</i>. 16:10</a>. Since the verse later only requires that the scapegoat be sent to a cliff (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:22" href="/Leviticus.16.22">v. 22</a>) in the desert (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21">v. 21</a>), but nothing is said what happens to the goat there, it is inferred that since it is emphasized that it must be alive as long as it is standing <i>before the Eternal</i> that later it will not continue to live, but will be pushed over the cliff to its death (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Acharei Mot, Chapter 2 7" href="/Sifra,_Acharei_Mot,_Chapter_2.7"><i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 2(7</a>,8), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 6:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.6.2.2-3"><i>Yoma</i> 6:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 40b" href="/Yoma.40b">Babli <i>Yoma</i>40b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 65a" href="/Yoma.65a">65a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 71a" href="/Yoma.71a">71a</a>.</i>. How long must it live? Up to <i>he will finish from atoning to Sanctuary</i><sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:20" href="/Leviticus.16.20"><i>Lev</i>. 16:20</a>. If the scapegoat dies before the blood of the other he-goat was sprinkled on the gobelin and the incense altar in the Sanctuary, the ceremony has to be repeated. Later, no new he-goat has to be provided.</i>, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Simeon says, up to the moment of confession<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">Until after the High Priest leaves the sanctuary to put his hands on the scapegoat to confess the people’s sins (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21">v. 21</a>). If the he-goat dies at the moment the High Priest starts to put his hands on its head, no new he-goat is needed.</i>. In Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, the confession is indispensable<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither the blood of the bull nor that of the he-goat may be brought inside the Sanctuary without prior confession (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:11" href="/Leviticus.16.11">v. 11</a>) the scapegoat may not be sent to the desert without confession; without a scapegoat the atoning is not complete.</i>. In Rebbi Simeon’s opinion, the confession is not indispensable<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">Since for him the High Priest only has to stand next to the scapegoat, the final confession itself is needed only if the scapegoat is alive at that time.<br>Commentators note that the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 40b" href="/Yoma.40b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 40b</a> seems to switch the positions of RR. Jehudah and Simeon in this matter, but since <i>Sifra</i> parallels the Yerushalmi this seems to be unlikely. Maimonides does not mention the matter in his Code.</i>. What is the difference between them? <sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">This is a <i>non sequitur</i>. But in the <i>Yoma</i> text, which has to be considered as original, the scapegoat is treated first, i. e., that a scapegoat sent away without confession for R. Jehudah requires an entire new ceremony with formal confession, but not for R. Simeon. Then it is asserted that by analogy if the bull whose blood is to be brought into the Sanctuary was slaughtered without confession, for R. Jehudah the slaughter is invalid and the entire ceremony has to be repeated, but not for R. Simeon.</i> If he slaughtered without confession. In Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, he must bring another bull. In Rebbi Simeon’s opinion, he does not have to bring another bull. The same holds for the scapegoat. If he sent it without a confession. In Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, he must bring another goat. In Rebbi Simeon’s opinion, he does not have to bring another goat. If he confessed, slaughtered, then the blood was spilled. Do you say, does he have to bring another bull and confess a second time or did he do his duty with the first confession<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">If the bull was slaughtered correctly but for some reason its blood could not be brought into the Sanctuary, does R. Jehudah require a full repetition of the ceremony including confession?</i>? The same holds for the scapegoat; must he cast lots a second time or did he do his duty with the first confession<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">If the scapegoat dies before it can be sent away, one needs a replacement. May one bring a single he-goat as replacement or does one have to bring two he-goats, cast new lots, use one as scapegoat and let the other one graze until it develops a bodily defect when it can be sold and one buys other sacrifices with the money? With the <i>Yoma</i> text one has to read:<br>צָרִיךְ לְהַגְרִיל פַּעַם שְׁנִייָה אוֹ כְבָר יָצָא <i>בְּהַגְרִילוֹ</i><br>שֶׁלָּרִאשׁוֹן.<br>“Must he cast lots a second time or did he do his duty with the first <i>casting of lots</i>?” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 39b-40a" href="/Yoma.39b-40a">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 39b/40a</a>.)</i>?

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The kinds of awareness of impurity are two<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Either centered on <i>sancta</i> or the Sanctuary, as detailed in the Mishnah. All violations of the Sanctuary and <i>sancta</i> in a state of oblivion require a sacrifice depending on the wealth of the perpetrator (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:2-13" href="/Leviticus.2.2-13"><i>Lev</i>. 2:2–13</a>).</i> which are four. If one became impure, realized it, then the impurity was forgotten but he was aware of <i>sancta</i>; or <i>sancta</i> were forgotten by him but he remembered impurity; or he forgot both, ate <i>sanctum</i> and realized it after he had eaten: he is obligated for a variable sacrifice.<br>If one became impure, realized it, the impurity was forgotten but he was aware of the Sanctuary; or the Sanctuary was forgotten by him but he remembered impurity; or he forgot both, entered the Sanctuary and realized it after he had left: he is obligated for a variable sacrifice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The kinds of awareness of impurity,” etc. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: It is obvious about the last awareness, until he knows that he is liable to bring a sacrifice for it<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:3" href="/Leviticus.5.3"><i>Lev</i>. 5:3</a> requires a sacrifice if after forgetting about impurity <i>he knew and felt guilty</i>. Since as a general rule no obligatory sacrifice can be offered voluntarily, the verse implies that a variable value sacrifice for violations involving impurity is possible if the offerer can prove that he is liable for the sacrifice.</i>. Is the same true for the first awareness<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There can be no forgetting if there was no prior knowledge. Must this prior knowledge be one of certainty or can it be one of possibility?</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 19a" href="/Shevuot.19a">Babli 19a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 6:5" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.6.5">Tosephta <i>Ṭahorot</i> 6:7</a> (the full text later, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.2.1.10">Note 41</a>).</i>: Two paths, one impure and one pure. He walked on one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It was known that one of the paths passed over a spot where a corpse was buried. The spot is no longer recognizable and people do no longer remember which of the paths it was. Anybody walking on one of the paths is possible impure by forming a “tent” over a corpse.</i>, entered the Sanctuary, left, sprinkled repeatedly, immersed himself<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He observed the ritual of removing the impurity of the dead (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>) by being sprinkled with water containing ashes of the Red Heifer on the 3rd and 7th days and then immersing himself in a <i>miqweh</i>.</i>, walked on the second one, and entered the Sanctuary; he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since he walked both paths, he certainly polluted the Sanctuary by entering while impure. Even though he cannot determine which time he entered while impure, he can be sure that he did it exactly once; this is enough to trigger the obligation of a sacrifice.</i> and he knows that he is liable to bring a sacrifice for it. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this is Rebbi Ismael’s<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2:6.</i> who said that he is liable for forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, we thought that one could say that Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said this if he was certain that he was impure, he had forgotten the impurity and entered the Sanctuary. But not if it was in doubt whether he was impure or pure, he was oblivious of the Sanctuary and entered the Sanctuary. Since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this is Rebbi Ismael’s who said that he is liable for forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary: this implies even if it was in doubt whether he was impure or pure<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that for R. Aqiba, who admits a variable sacrifice only for cases where impurity was known, forgotten, and remembered, no sacrifice was possible in this case since the impurity was never known. It only is known that the two possibilities both were realized and there is no third alternative.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is everybody’s opinion since a doubt of awareness is awareness<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since we accept the principle of the excluded middle (i.e., a statement is either true or false) a proof that the person could not have been pure both times is proof that he was impure (at least) once. For awareness of impurity no awareness of the exact time of impurity is needed. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 19b" href="/Shevuot.19b">Babli (19b</a>) points out that this argument is needed only for variable reparation sacrifices since for purification sacrifices no prior knowledge is required.</i>. Since at a time when he does not know whether he was impure or pure you say it is certain knowledge, if he knows for certain that he is impure but does not know whether he is liable for a sacrifice then certainly this should be awareness<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">It is irrelevant to him if he does not know when the obligation of a sacrifice started since the competent Temple authority can determine that his obligatory sacrifice is legitimate.</i>.
In Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion, if one became impure but it was for him in doubt, would this be for him like forgetting, which makes him liable<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">He became impure without noticing it. Then a thought arose in his mind that he might have become impure. He forgot about it, committed an offense against the laws of purity, and finally discovered that he actually had been impure to start with. Does this qualify for the sequence awareness - forgetting - awareness required for a variable sacrifice?</i>? Each of them keeps to his opinion, as they disagreed:
<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.10"><i>Horaiot</i> 3:2, Notes 40–45</a>, and part of a longer disquisition (Notes 36–45). The quote here starts in the middle and, therefore, is not very intelligible. But in <i>Horaiot</i> the text is complete and completely intelligible.</i> One ate five times the volume of an olive; he separately realized a doubt about each one. Afterwards it became known to him as a certainty. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the knowledge about his doubt determines his kind of sacrifices. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of sacrifices. Rebbi Yose bar Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees that for the Anointed Priest the knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of purification sacrifice. What is the reason? <i>Like purification offering</i>, <i>like reparation offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:7" href="/Leviticus.7.7"><i>Lev</i>. 7:7</a>.</i>. The knowledge about his doubt determines the transgression for one who brings a suspended reparation offering. The knowledge about his doubt does not determine the transgression for one who does not bring a suspended reparation offering.
The argument of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems to be inverted. There he says, the knowledge about his doubt determines his kind of transgression. But here he says, the knowledge about his doubt does not determine his kind of transgression. There, his reparation offering determines it. Here what do you have?
The argument of Rebbi Joḥanan seems to be inverted. There, he said, if part of the sin was atoned, all of the sin was atoned. And here he says so? Rebbi Joḥanan said this only for the last realization which does not require any sacrifice.
Rebbi Joḥanan said: In any case of doubt in the public domain, the doubt has to be resolved as pure, but one does not allow to act<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 4:11" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.4.11">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i>4:11</a> (and made explicit there in Chapters 5–6) that any doubt which arises in the public domain whether something there is impure can be disregarded. Rebbi Joḥanan’s new statement is that even though this is accepted practice, one does not teach this and if a person comes to ask about it, one does not say that the thing is pure.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Parah</i>11:2. While in general the rules of purity for the water in which ashes of the Red Cow are to be dissolved to purify from the impurity of the dead are very strict, if for heave something is declared pure, such as a doubt of impurity arising in the public domain, it also must be declared pure for the sprinkling water. But if judgment is suspended, i. e., if the heave may not be eaten by a Cohen for perhaps it is impure (when its consumption would be a deadly sin), but it cannot be burned for perhaps it is pure and then the destruction of <i>sancta</i> would be sinful, the water is considered impure and must be poured out on the ground to avoid further pollution.<br>The expression חַטָּאת biblically refers to the Red Cow (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:2" href="/Numbers.19.2"><i>Num.</i>19:2</a>), not to the water which is called מֵי נִדָּה “throwing water”.(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:20-21" href="/Numbers.19.20-21"><i>Num</i>. 19:20–21</a>).</i>: “Any case of doubt which was declared pure for heave is pure for sprinkling water, and in any case where it remains unresolved for heave the sprinkling water is poured out.” There is nothing unresolved for the sprinkling water; either it is genuinely pure or genuinely impure. Rebbi Ze`ira said, the Mishnah deals with a situation not caused by a sacrifice; what Rebbi Joḥanan said refers to a situation caused by a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah in <i>Parah</i> instructs the carrier of purifying water to disregard any doubt arising in the public domain, the instruction of R. Joḥanan that one does not allow anybody to act on this rule cannot be maintained. The answer is that R. Joḥanan stated his rule only for <i>sancta</i> of the Sanctuary; for all <i>sancta</i> not connected with the Sanctuary it is a publicly stated rule that a doubt in the public domain has to be disregarded.</i>. Everybody agrees that if the person transgresses and acted, it is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Only if somebody acted as if it was pure and after the fact comes to ask a rabbinic authority, one has to declare the matter as pure even in the Sanctuary. “Everybody” includes R. Aqiba who in certain cases declares such a doubt as causing impurity (<i>Ṭahorot</i>5:1).</i>. The arguments of Rebbi Joḥanan are inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Later in this paragraph, when he declares that a <i>nazir</i>, who is forbidden to become impure by the impurity of the dead, must treat a certain case of doubt of impurity as a case of certain impurity.</i>, he says, if he transgresses and acted, it is impure. But here he said, if he transgresses and acted, it is pure. There in a private domain, here in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">And everybody agrees that in a private domain any doubt of impurity has to be treated as certainty of impurity.</i>. But you could even say, there and here in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Ze`ira.</i>. The arguments of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish are inverted, the arguments of Rebbi Joḥanan are inverted, since they disagreed: A <i>nazir</i> became impure by a grave in the abyss<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A corpse, visibly the victim of an accident not seen by witnesses and found buried in straw or pebbles or other loose material. One may assume that the corpse was buried by the accident, rather than humans. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.2.4"><i>Nazir</i>9:2 Notes 59ff</a>.</i>, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the <i>nazir</i> does not shave, but Rebbi Joḥanan said, the <i>nazir</i> has to shave. That is since Rebbi Joḥanan said, a doubt of awareness is awareness<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since we accept the principle of the excluded middle (i.e., a statement is either true or false) a proof that the person could not have been pure both times is proof that he was impure (at least) once. For awareness of impurity no awareness of the exact time of impurity is needed. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 19b" href="/Shevuot.19b">Babli (19b</a>) points out that this argument is needed only for variable reparation sacrifices since for purification sacrifices no prior knowledge is required.</i>, even if the first was because of a crawling animal and the second by a corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">While everybody agrees that the <i>nazir</i> is impure if it should become clear that he passed by after the accident and therefore violated the terms of his vow, in R. Simeon ben Laqish’s view the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:9" href="/Numbers.6.9"><i>Num</i>. 6:9</a> clearly requires that the <i>nazir</i> be aware of his impurity at the moment it happens to be required to shave and start the ceremony described there. This makes the rules of <i>nazir</i> parallel to the rules of the variable sacrifice which also presuppose prior knowledge (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:2-5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.2-5.7"><i>Horaiot</i>3:3</a>). But for R. Joḥanan, any awareness of any impurity, even a minor one which can be remedied by simple immersion in water, is counted as full awareness of impurity.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">These two sentences have no place here; they are copied from <i>Horaiot</i>3:3, Notes 76,77.</i> The first before he converted and the second after he converted. The first before he grew two pubic hairs, the second after he grew them.
Somebody became impure through a corpse and knew it; by a crawling animal<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">One of the eight animals, reptile or rodent, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29" href="/Leviticus.11.29"><i>Lev</i>. 11:29</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:30" href="/Leviticus.11.30">30</a>, whose carcass makes a person impure by contact. The impurity is removed by immersion in a sufficient amount of water and the following sundown. Therefore this is considered a minor impurity in contrast with the impurity induced by a corpse (or leprosy and some bodily discharges) which need elaborate ceremonies of purification.</i> and did not know it. The minor impurity is not mentioned with the severe impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A violation of the purity of the Sanctuary or its <i>sancta</i> can be remedied by a (variable value) sacrifice only if there had been prior awareness of the impurity but oblivion at the moment of the violation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.2.1.1">Note 1</a>). If the person enters the Sanctuary oblivious of his impurity but having had prior knowledge of his severe impurity, his having no knowledge of the minor impurity does not invalidate his knowledge of severe impurity; he is liable for a variable value sacrifice.</i>. Somebody became impure through a corpse and did not know it; by a crawling animal and did not know it. Then it became known to him about the crawling animal, and after that it became known to him about the corpse. Since it first became known to him about the crawling animal, is there minor impurity together with the severe one, or since the impurity of the corpse preceded, should the impurity of the crawling animal and the knowledge about it be pushed aside so that the minor impurity not be mentioned with the severe impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">One must assume that he entered the Sanctuary having had knowledge but momentarily being oblivious of his minor impurity but before becoming aware of his severe impurity. Before he brought his sacrifice he became aware of the prior severe impurity. If he had not become aware, clearly he would be liable for a variable sacrifice. For the violation of the purity of the Sanctuary by his severe impurity he cannot bring a sacrifice since he had no prior awareness. Does he still have to bring the sacrifice for the minor impurity or does his inability to bring one for the severe impurity disable him from bringing the one for simple impurity? No answer is given.</i>? Somebody became impure through a corpse and knew it; by a crawling animal and did not know it, and entered the Sanctuary. When he was oblivious of the corpse he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">All conditions for a variable value sacrifice are satisfied; the minor impurity which was unknown to him cannot interfere with the sacrifice for the severe violation.</i>; when he was oblivious of the crawling animal he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since he never had prior awareness of this impurity by the time he entered the Sanctuary, the basic conditions for a sacrifice are missing.</i>. If he went to immerse himself for the severe impurity and the minor impurity was additional, it is counted also for the minor impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since the purification from the impurity of the dead at the end also requires immersion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:19" href="/Numbers.19.19"><i>Num</i>. 19:19</a>), the latter automatically eliminates the minor impurity even if the minor impurity was not on his mind while he was immersing himself.</i>. In which respect? If he immersed himself and then entered the Sanctuary he is not liable.
If he went to immerse himself for a minor impurity and a severe impurity was additional, it does not count for him for the severe impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">While it is possible to cleanse oneself from multiple impurities with one immersion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 8a" href="/Keritot.8a">Babli <i>Keritut</i>8a</a>, bottom), the immersion is effective only for those for which it is done. The only exception is a minor impurity if the immersion is for a severe one without thinking of the minor one.</i>. In which respect? If he immersed himself and then entered the Sanctuary he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For a variable value sacrifice because of the desecration of the Sanctuary by the severe impurity.</i>. Therefore not for a severe impurity and a severe impurity was additional<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For impurities of equal severity awareness of the impurity is indispensable.</i>. Therefore not for a minor impurity and a minor impurity was additional<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For impurities of equal severity awareness of the impurity is indispensable.</i>. If he went to immerse himself for severe impurity and tree limbs and wall extensions were additional<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Impurity of the dead is spread not only by contact but also by “tent”. A person being under the same roof with a corpse in general becomes severely impure. A roof does not have to be man made. A tree under which a corpse or parts of a corpse are lying forms a tent in this sense. The same holds for outcropping of rocks or stone walls or bushes that grow in crevasses of stone walls. It is assumed here that the person underwent the full ceremony of cleansing from the impurity of the dead while not being aware that he also had passed under such a tree or outcropping. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.1">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i>7:3</a>.</i> are these like severe impurity since Rebbi Joḥanan said that for heave all of them are [impure] from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.3"><i>Nazir</i>7:3, Note 158</a>.</i>, or are they like minor impurity since the <i>nazir</i> does not shave because of them<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If a person walked under such a tree limb or wall extensions where a piece of a corpse of at least the size of an olive might be buried, he becomes possibly impure by the severe impurity of the dead but as long as this is only a possibility, not a certainty at the moment of his passing by, a <i>nazir</i> cannot shave his hair off because of this impurity.</i>?
An androgynus<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He has both male and female genitals, a penis with testicles and a vagina. It is not known whether he is male or female.</i> who saw whitish [effluent]<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Gonorrheal discharges from his penis. This causes severe impurity in a male. Similar discharges from a female’s urethra are pure.</i> and entered the Sanctuary is not liable. If he saw red [effluent]<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Menstrual blood causes impurity to a woman; prolonged discharge causes severe impurity. No flow of blood causes impurity in a male.</i> and entered the Sanctuary, he is not liable. If he saw simultaneously whitish and red and entered the Sanctuary, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is impure both as a male and a female, he is impure and forbidden access to the Sanctuary.</i>. There is a Tanna who states: he is not liable. Rebbi Joḥanan said, this is Rebbi Simeon, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 19a" href="/Shevuot.19a">Babli 19a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 6:5" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.6.5">Tosephta <i>Ṭahorot</i> 6:7</a> (the full text later, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.2.1.10">Note 41</a>).</i>: “Two paths, one pure and one impure. He walked on one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It was known that one of the paths passed over a spot where a corpse was buried. The spot is no longer recognizable and people do no longer remember which of the paths it was. Anybody walking on one of the paths is possible impure by forming a “tent” over a corpse.</i>, entered the Sanctuary on the first one, sprinkled repeatedly, immersed himself<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He observed the ritual of removing the impurity of the dead (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>) by being sprinkled with water containing ashes of the Red Heifer on the 3rd and 7th days and then immersing himself in a <i>miqweh</i>.</i>, on the second one and entered; he is liable. Rebbi Simeon declares him not liable in this case<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the knowledge of a doubt is not the certain knowledge which triggers the liability for a sacrifice for impurity in the Sanctuary. Since between the two occasions he removed the possible impurity from the first path, it could be that the second time he was perfectly pure entering the Sanctuary.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah in the name of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct text of the Tosephta as seen from R. David Pardo’s commentary (Jerusalem 1977).<br>While R. Simeon following the first quote would agree with the anonymous Tanna if there was no purification in between the two entries, R. Simeon ben Jehudah holds that no logical argument (either, or, and no third possibility) satisfies the requirement of direct awareness.</i> declares him not liable in all cases.” One understands the androgynus, for whitish [effluent] you declare him to be a woman, for red [effluent] you declare him to be a man. But here there is no known impurity. Rebbi Samuel bar Sosartai said, explain it if he forgot the first<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since another requirement for the sacrifice was the element of forgetting, he must have forgotten about the possible first impurity both times he entered the Sanctuary. There is no certain knowledge preceding any of the two occasions according to both versions of R. Simeon’s position.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked, where do we hold? If he forgot it forever, it is one path<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody can bring a personal sacrifice for a sin unknown to him; as long as he realizes only one incident there is only a possibility, not certain knowledge, of a violation of the Sanctuary’s sanctity. But offenses to be atoned for by a variable sacrifice do not qualify for suspended reparation offerings; no sacrifice is possible.</i>. If he forgot repeatedly, there are two paths. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Simeon parallels Rebbi Eliezer in the interpretation of Ḥizqiah<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 7, R. Eliezer and R. Aqiba both hold that not being unaware of the Sanctuary but being unaware of impurity triggers the obligation of a variable sacrifice. In Halakhah 2:6, Ḥizqiah holds that they disagree in that R. Eliezer requires prior awareness of the kind of impurity but R. Aqiba only of the fact of impurity.</i>. Just as Rebbi Eliezer said there, until he knows whether he did become impure by a crawling animal or a corpse, so Rebbi Simeon says here, until he knows whether he did become impure by red [effluent] or by whitish. One understands there since he said, a doubt of awareness is awareness<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since we accept the principle of the excluded middle (i.e., a statement is either true or false) a proof that the person could not have been pure both times is proof that he was impure (at least) once. For awareness of impurity no awareness of the exact time of impurity is needed. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 19b" href="/Shevuot.19b">Babli (19b</a>) points out that this argument is needed only for variable reparation sacrifices since for purification sacrifices no prior knowledge is required.</i>. But here, since he saw whitish and red together, it is certain knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">There is nothing unclear here about the facts of impurity, only about the person’s body. Both impurities are body- produced; they fall in the same category.</i>.
Rav Ḥisda asked: What is the situation if he entered the Sanctuary thinking it was a synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He knows that he is impure and knows that as such he may not enter the sanctuary but he does not recognize the Sanctuary. The question can be asked only according to R. Ismael (Mishnah 6).</i>? Rebbi Yose said, the following is what we are saying: One knew that there exists impurity in the world but he did not know whether one is liable for a sacrifice because of it, that certainly is awareness<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since there was awareness of impurity, his ignorance of the law is irrelevant.</i>. But the following is where we hold, if it was clear to him that he was impure but he was oblivious of the Sanctuary, and he entered the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Ismael he is liable for a sacrifice but not for RR. Eliezer and Aqiba.</i>. And so if it was clear to him that this was the Sanctuary, he was oblivious of the impurity and entered the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">He is liable for a sacrifice according to all opinions.</i>.
Rebbi Yose asked: If one ate half the volume of an olive being aware of the Sanctuary but oblivious of impurity and about half the volume of an olive being aware of impurity but oblivious of the Sanctuary; do the oblivions combine<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This question is not answered here. It is asked again in <i>Šabbat</i>7:1 (9b l. 46) where it is not answered either. But there the question is compared to one whether violations of the Sabbath combine if both are small so as not to trigger the obligation of a purification sacrifice, the one made in oblivion of the Sabbath Day but awareness of the categories of forbidden work, the other in awareness of the Sabbath but oblivion of the categories of forbidden work. The latter question is not answered in the Yerushalmi either but it is discussed at length in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 70b-71b" href="/Shabbat.70b-71b">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i>70b–71b</a>), the Galilean participant in the discussion being R. Ze`ira.</i>?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Whether one entered the courtyard or the addition to the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical definition of “Sanctuary” as applied to the Temple Mount in Jerusalem applies only to the area sanctified by Solomon and his successors during the existence of the First Temple, and its reconstruction by Nehemiah. It excludes the additions to the Temple area and the Temple Mound made by Herod. Similarly, the city of Jerusalem which represents the Camp of the Israelites in the desert is only the First Temple city, with the area between the First Temple wall and the Damascus gate being of questionable sanctity.</i>, because one adds to the city and the temple courtyards only by King and Prophet, and <i>Urim and Tummim</i>, and the Synhedrion of Seventy-One, and two thanksgiving offerings, and song, with the Court walking and the two thanksgiving offerings after them. The inner one is eaten but the outer one is burned. Any [addition] which was not made with all these, one who was entering there is not liable for it<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">There can be no liability to bring a variable sacrifice for entering the Herodian additions to the Temple Mount in a state of impurity.</i>.
