<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Whether one entered the courtyard,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah is a very truncated and inferior copy of parts of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.3.4"><i>Sanhedrin</i>1:5 (Notes 288–294</a>). The second paragraph is from the end of that Halakhah, Note 326. Its inclusion indicates that all of that Halakhah should be studied here.</i> Rav said, at the start: <sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 24:19" href="/II_Samuel.24.19"><i>2S.</i>24:19</a>.</i><i>David ascended following Gad’s word</i>, that is King and Prophet. <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 3:1" href="/II_Chronicles.3.1"><i>2Chr</i>. 3:1</a>.</i><i>Solomon started to build the Temple of the Eternal in Jerusalem</i>, these are Urim and Tummim. <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 3:1" href="/II_Chronicles.3.1"><i>2Chr</i>. 3:1</a>.</i><i>And his father David</i>, this is the Synhedrion, <sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:7" href="/Deuteronomy.32.7"><i>Deut</i>. 32:7</a>.</i><i>ask your father and he will tell you.</i> Song, <i>after them went Hoshaia and the officers</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 12:32" href="/Nehemiah.12.32"><i>Neh</i>. 12:32</a>.</i>. Thanksgiving sacrifices, <i>I put up two thanksgiving sacrifices</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 12:31" href="/Nehemiah.12.31"><i>Neh</i>. 12:31</a>.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rebbi Yudan said, what is written, walking? No, being in procession.
Because it was the vulnerable spot of Jerusalem; it could be conquered from there.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one became impure in the Temple courtyard, then was oblivious about impurity but remembered the Sanctuary, [or] was oblivious about the Sanctuary but remembered impurity, [or] was oblivious of both. If he prostrated himself, or tarried that he could have prostrated himself, or left on a lengthy path, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody entered the Sanctuary area legitimately because he was pure and then became impure in the sacred precinct (e. g., a dead lizard was lying there and he accidentally touched it), no sin attaches to him if he leaves immediately on the shortest possible path. If he tarries or leaves on a longer path, he commits a sin for which he will be liable if the original awareness was followed by any forgetting.</i>. But on the shortest path he is not liable; this is the positive commandment in the Sanctuary about which they are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">“They” are the members of the Supreme Court who do not have to offer a sacrifice for a wrong ruling in this matter (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.4.1">Mishnah <i>Horaiot</i>2:4</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one became impure in the Temple courtyard,” etc. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: One verse says, <i>he defiled the dwelling place of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:13" href="/Numbers.19.13"><i>Num</i>. 19:13</a>.</i>, another verse says, <i>he defiled the Sanctuary of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:20" href="/Numbers.19.20"><i>Num</i>. 19:20</a></i>. Why is this? To state a difference between one who became inside and one who became outside<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The two verses appear in the same Chapter. There must be a reason for the use of two synonyms. It is a hint that the rules are different for one who enters while impure and one who becomes impure inside. (There is another opinion, <i>Sifry Num.</i>126, that מִשְׁכָּן describes the movable Tabernacle and מִקְדָּשׁ the permanent Temple. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 16b" href="/Shevuot.16b">Babli 16b</a> brings both opinions in the name of R. Eleazar.)</i>. If he became impure (inside)<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the places of “inside” and “outside” have to be switched. A person already impure becomes guilty and eventually liable for a sacrifice at the moment his head and most of his body are inside the sacred precinct; one becomingimpure inside if he tarries long enough to prostrate himself.</i> not unless he bring his head and most of his body inside; if he became impure (outside)<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the places of “inside” and “outside” have to be switched. A person already impure becomes guilty and eventually liable for a sacrifice at the moment his head and most of his body are inside the sacred precinct; one becomingimpure inside if he tarries long enough to prostrate himself.</i> not unless he stay for prostrating. If he became impure inside and entered further inside<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Do we say that if he became impure inside and moved his head and body further inside instead of towards the outside that he is guilty on two counts?</i>? Let us hear from the following: “If he prostrated himself, or tarried that he could have prostrated himself.” If he tarried, is that not as if he brought his head and most of his body inside, but you are saying, “he tarried that he could have prostrated himself.” And here, not unless he tarried that he could have prostrated himself<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah makes it clear that only the time elapsed inside the sacred precinct determines his liability, not the direction of his motion.</i>. How is that? About which of them does he become liable? About the first or the last<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The moment he became impure or the moment he stayed longer than necessary.</i>? The colleagues say, about the first. Rebbi Yose told them, one says to him, leave, and you say about the first? But we must hold about the last<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This essentially is a repetition of the previous argument: Not the fact that he became impure determines his guilt but the time he tarried inside. The argument is recast as introduction to the next paragraph.</i>.
There, we have stated: “They removed the cup and the censer for him”<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.5.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma.</i>5:1</a>. The reference really is to the end of that Mishnah: “The High Priest takes the censer in his right hand and the cup in the left hand.” In general there is a rule that all priestly service in the Temple has to be performed with the right hand only. The left hand has to be used here to make it possible for the High Priest to enter the Holiest of Holies only once. On that it is noted (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 5:1:4-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.5.1.4-9"><i>Yoma</i>5:2</a>, 42b l. 52) that if he went twice, using only his right hand, the service is valid. Nevertheless, it is sinful to enter the Temple building more often than necessary. About this the paragraph here is then copied in <i>Yoma</i>.</i>. About which of them does he become liable? About the first or the last? The colleagues say, about the last. Rebbi Yose told them, one says to him, enter, and you say about the last? But we must hold about the first<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first entry is necessary in any case, the High Priest cannot be faulted for the first entry even if he only carries the censer.</i>.
The measure of prostrating is ten cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If the time frame described in the Mishnah by “prostrating” is measured by the difference in length between a lengthy and a short path, since it cannot be made dependent on the speed of one’s body motions.</i>. How is this? If the lengthy path was twenty but the short one ten. He went five. If he went in this direction, five, if in the other, fifteen<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The only thing that counts is the difference in the lengths of the paths, not the total lengths.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, he never becomes liable unless the lengthy path exceed the short one by (ten)<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">From the following it is clear that one has to read “twenty”.</i>. How is that? If the lengthy path was thirty but the short one ten. He went five. If he went in this direction, five, if in the other, twenty-five. What is the duration of prostrating<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If one measures not by length but by time, still admitting differences between one person and the next.</i>? Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: up to a greeting from one person to another<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The time frame needed to speak the two words שָׁלוֹם עָלֶיךָ.</i>. Abba bar Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This is misspelling for Abba bar bar Ḥana; cf. his statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.1.15"><i>Berakhot</i>2:1, Note 51</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.1.2"><i>Nazir</i>4:1 Note 12</a>.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: up to a greeting from a student to his teacher, “peace be with you, my teacher.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The time frame allowing three words to be spoken, which in general is the definition of “immediately”. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 16b" href="/Shevuot.16b">Babli 16b</a> the time given is either the time needed to recite the entire verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 7:3" href="/II_Chronicles.7.3"><i>2Chr</i>. 7:3</a> or at least half of it.</i>”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is the positive commandment about the menstruating woman<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">For which the High Court has to bring a purification sacrifice if they rule wrongly, <i>Horaiot</i> Mishnah 2:4.</i>? If he was having sex with a pure one and she said to him, I became impure, if he separates immediately he is liable,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">For extirpation if he acts intentionally, for a sacrifice if unintentionally.</i> for his separation is as pleasurable to him as his entry<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">He has to stay immobile until his erection subsides; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.5.3"><i>Horaiot</i>2:5 Note 69</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What is the positive commandment about the menstruating woman,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph does not belong here; it is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.5.2"><i>Horaiot</i>2:5 (Notes 60–63</a>) and refers to the statement of the Mishnah there that the High Court never incurs the liability for a purification sacrifice for a wrong decision about the purity of the Sanctuary. It is explained that a purification sacrifice is due only for wrong rulings in matters that require fixed value purification sacrifices, not matters of the Sanctuary which trigger obligations of variable value sacrifices.</i> Cahana said, is it impossible that there was knowledge at the beginning and knowledge at the end and oblivion in between? Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon objected before Rebbi Mana: Could that be for the teachers? He told him, we have a problem with those entering and you bring us teachers? What about it? Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, <i>commandments, commandments</i>. Since <i>commandments</i> mentioned there are about fixed-value [offerings], here also about fixed-value [offerings].

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer says, <i>the crawling animal and he became oblivious</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev.</i> 5:2</a>.</i>, he is liable for forgetting the crawling animal but is not liable for forgetting the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical text never hints at the possibility that anybody could be unaware of being in the holy precinct; therefore no penalties are spelled out for this case.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, <i>and he became oblivious while he was impure</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev.</i> 5:2</a>.</i>, he is liable for forgetting impurity but is not liable for forgetting the Sanctuary. Rebbi Ismael says, <i>and he became oblivious, and he became oblivious</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:3" href="/Leviticus.5.3"><i>Lev</i>. 5:3</a>, speaking of impurities generated by the human body.</i><i>,</i> two times to make him liable for forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opinion formulated in Mishnah 1. The Mishnah is quoted <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i>12(7).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Eliezer says,” etc. Ḥizqiah says, there is disagreement between them<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Halakhak 1, Note 45. R. Eliezer requires awareness of the cause of his impurity, R. Aqiba only requires awareness of impurity. This is the only opinion mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 18a" href="/Shevuot.18a">Babli, 18a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, interpreting the verse is between them; there are Tannaim who state, he is liable for forgetting impurity but is not liable for forgetting the Sanctuary, and there are Tannain who state, he is liable for forgetting the crawling animal but is not liable for forgetting the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">RR. Eliezer and Aqiba only differ in the way they deduce the law from the verse, not in the substance of the meaning. The formulation is just a matter of style.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Eliezer seems inverted<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 4:2" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.4.2">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i>4:2</a> where R. Eliezer and R. Joshua disagree in the case that a person knows that he has inadvertently committed a sin which if intentional is either one subject to Divine extirpation or a capital crime, but he does not know which law he broke. Examples are a person who inadvertently ate a piece of meat which either was forbidden fat or disqualified sacrificial meat, or a man who slept with a woman but he does not know whether it was his wife during her menses or his sister. R. Eliezer declares him liable for a fixed value purification sacrifice since in any case he committed a deadly sin; R. Joshua declares him not liable (and therefore prevented from sacrificing) as long as he cannot specify which prohibition he broke. It is implied that in the case of a variable value reparation sacrifice for violation of the purity of the Sanctuary the opinions are switched; R. Eliezer requires knowledge of the kind of impurity (e. g., “a crawling animal”) whereas R. Joshua only requires awareness of impurity. Ḥizqiah would trace R. Aqiba’s opinion to his teacher R. Joshua.</i>. There he says, even if he did not know. But here he says, not unless he knew. <sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The arguments quoted for R. Eliezer belong to R. Joshua and vice-versa.</i> There, <i>by which he sinned</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:23" href="/Leviticus.4.23"><i>Lev</i>. 4:23</a>, the purification sacrifice of the Prince. The verse insist, <i>he became aware of his transgression by which he sinned</i>, he can state the paragraph which he broke. But <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2</a> only requires awareness of impurity.</i><i>,</i> not unless he knew by what he became liable. Here, <i>while he was impure</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev.</i> 5:2</a>.</i>, in all cases. Rebbi Ḥinena said, here “by what” is not written. The argument of Rebbi Joshua seems inverted. <sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Eliezer’s argument. As formulated in the Mishnah, he reads <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:2" href="/Leviticus.5.2"><i>Lev</i>. 5:2</a> as requiring awareness of the nature of his impurity (or, taking vv. 2,3 together, at least certain knowledge of the kind of impurity, whether simple or severe.) But in v. 4:23 and certainly v. 27, the purification sacrifice of a commoner, where it is only required that <i>he sinned inadvertently</i>, can be read as authorization for a sacrifice if only the fact was known that a sin was committed.</i> There he says, <i>while he was impure</i>, not unless he knew by what he became liable. Here, <i>by which he sinned,</i> in any case. How does Rebbi Eliezer uphold <i>by what</i>? This excludes the one who is occupied<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Accepting that R. Eliezer refers to 4:27, the peculiar language of 4:23 still has to be explained. He excludes a person who was intent on doing something permitted when it happened that he broke a prohibition, e. g., that he was intent of sleeping with his wife when she was permitted to him and in the dark of night his sister substituted for her. She sinned but he did not.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> There are two kinds of oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1:1. This Chapter treats “blurted oaths” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>) where a person makes an oath that he shall do or not do certain things, which do not involve others. A breach of such an oath triggers a liability for a variable sacrifice (or if made before witnesses, punishment by flogging.) The liability for a variable sacrifice is subject to the usual conditions for such a sacrifice, prior knowledge, oblivion, and remembrance.</i> which are four kinds: An oath that I shall eat, or that I shall not eat; that I ate, or that I did not eat. An oath that I shall not eat, when he ate the most minute amount, he is liable, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. They said to Rebbi Aqiba, where do we find that one who eats the most minute amount should be liable, that this one be liable<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In all other matters, only food in the volume of an average olive triggers obligations.</i>? He told them, where do we find that a person talks and has to bring a sacrifice; but this one talks and has to bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In general, only actions, not words, trigger obligations; but an oath is simply words (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.10.3">Note 166</a>). In commercial transactions an obligation is enforceable only when it was confirmed by an action, such as a signature or a handshake. But there are other possible exceptions, such as blasphemy and apostasy (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.1.4">Note 21</a>).</i>!
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There are two kinds of oaths which are four kinds,” etc. One understands “that I shall eat, or that I shall not eat.” “That I ate, or that I did not eat”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">We do not talk here about judicial oaths which by necessity are about past events, but “blurted oaths”. How can a statement about past events ever lead to a variable sacrifice? For a future directed oath it is possible that he had the honest intention of keeping what he swore to but later he forgot. But for the past we must assume that he knows what he did.</i>? Where do we stand? If it is known that he ate and he swore that he did not eat, it is a false oath<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Under the right conditions he can be punished for it, but there can be no atoning sacrifice.</i>. But if he was of the opinion that he ate and swore that he did not eat? Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: If he is inadvertent for its sacrifice or intentional for its sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrifice is possible only for an inadvertent sin. In this context, “inadvertent” may mean that the perpetrator was not aware that his act was criminal or sinful, or that he was not aware that the act if inadvertent requires a sacrifice.</i>. But “I was of the opinion that this is not an oath” is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A statement qualifies as an oath only if it is pronounced as such, not as a simple statement.</i>. But it must be that he was intentional in the deed but in error about the sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrifice is possible only for an inadvertent sin. In this context, “inadvertent” may mean that the perpetrator was not aware that his act was criminal or sinful, or that he was not aware that the act if inadvertent requires a sacrifice.</i>. Did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumot</i>7:1 Notes 5,6; <i>Šabbat</i>11:5 (13b), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.7"><i>Ketubot</i>3:1 Note 30</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.4.2"><i>Bava Qamma</i>7:2 Note 29</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 69a" href="/Shabbat.69a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>69a</a>.</i>: Intentional about fat and in error about the sacrifice, one warns him, he is flogged and has to bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices are possible only for inadvertent deadly sins. If a person knew that eating fat was forbidden but did not know that it was a deadly sin, he simultaneously committed an intentional sin and an unintentional deadly sin. If he had been duly warned by two witnesses not to break the law, he can be flogged for the intentional sin and has to bring a sacrifice for the unintentional deadly sin even though there cannot be two penalties for one crime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:4-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.4-14"><i>Terumot</i>7:1 Notes 19–70</a>).</i>. This is impossible, as Rebbi La said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: So did Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Who is R. Aqiba’s opponent. All of Mishnah 1 is R. Aqiba’s teaching. R. Ismael opposes adding backward looking oaths as blurted oaths.</i> answer Rebbi Aqiba: Do we find a situation where one is liable if intentional because of a false oath and in oblivion because of blurting<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">A future directed oath, where it cannot be verified instantly whether it will be kept or violated, is an actionless crime and cannot be prosecuted (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.1.1">Note 3</a>). The preconditions of a sacrifice for a blurted oath negate the possibility of judicial penalties.</i>? Should not Rebbi Aqiba retort, we find a situation where one is liable if intentional because of a false oath and he brings a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If R. Aqiba did accept R. Joḥanan’s argument, it would be possible for a person to be flogged for violating the prohibition of perjury (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:12" href="/Leviticus.19.12"><i>Lev</i>. 19:12</a>) and still be liable for a sacrifice. This would make R. Ismael’s objection irrelevant.</i>? But we deal here with the case that he was sure that he ate, and he swore, and it turned out that he had not eaten. Not about him who said that this is not an oath? There<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If he denies that he intended or pronounced an oath, there is no oath and the entire discussion is irrelevant.</i>, if he says this is not an oath; but here if he knows that this is an oath but he errs because of something else<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">He made the oath in good faith but his mind was distracted by other things. It is an inadvertent sin which qualifies for a sacrifice.</i>.
Where do we hold? If about one who said, an oath that I shall eat according to Torah standards<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The standard minimal amounts of food which in biblical rules either are needed for the validity of an act or may lead to prosecution of a criminal act. This is the volume of an average olive. R. Aqiba must agree that if somebody insists that his food intake be measured by biblical standards, the minimum is an olive-sized piece. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 21b" href="/Shevuot.21b">Babli disagrees (21b</a>) and holds that R. Aqiba agrees with R. Simeon that there is no lower limit for prosecutable offenses (<i>Pesaḥim</i>3:1 29d l. 61).</i>, even Rebbi Aqiba will agree. And if about one who says, an oath that I shall not taste, even the rabbis will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Tasting by definition involves minute amounts (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 22a" href="/Shevuot.22a">Babli 22a</a>).</i>. But we hold, about minute amounts. Are minute amounts eating? Explain it following Rebbi Aqiba who said that a minute amount is eating. What is the difference between them<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Between R. Aqiba and his opponents in the second part of the Mishnah.</i>? “An oath that I shall eat this loaf,” and he ate it except a minute amount. In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba he is liable, in the opinion of the rabbis he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Let ε denote the volume of an olive. If somebody swore that he would (or would not) eat a certain amount <i>m</i>, for R. Aqiba he fulfilled (or violated) his oath if he ate exactly amount <i>m</i>. But for the rabbis he fulfilled (or violated) his oath if he ate more than <i>m</i>-ε but less than <i>m</i>+ε. This is the basis of the subsequent examples. If he had sworn to eat exactly one loaf, for R. Aqiba he violated his oath by eating a minute quantity less than a whole loaf but for the rabbis he swore truly.</i>. “That I shall not eat this loaf,” and he ate it except a minute amount. In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba he is not liable, in the opinion of the rabbis he is liable. Also for blurted oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This expression is difficult to understand and may be a copyist’s error since the preceding two examples are prime examples of blurted oaths. Maybe one should read “vows”, since the example uses the language of vows denying usufruct.</i> it is so: “An oath that my wife may not have any usufruct from me if I shall eat this loaf,” and he ate it except a minute amount. In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba his wife is forbidden [to have usufruct], in the opinion of the rabbis his wife is permitted. “That I shall not eat this loaf,” and he ate it except a minute amount. In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba his wife is permitted [to have usufruct], in the opinion of the rabbis his wife is forbidden. Also with property it is the same. “An oath that my properties be forbidden to me if I shall eat this loaf,” and he ate it except a minute amount. In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba they are forbidden, in the opinion of the rabbis his properties are permitted. “That I shall not eat this loaf,” and he ate it except a minute amount. In the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba his properties are permitted, in the opinion of the rabbis they are forbidden.
Does not the blasphemer have to bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This is a matter of contention. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.1.1">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i>1:1</a>, the anonymous Tanna, supposed to represent the school of R. Aqiba, lists the blasphemer as one who has to bring a purification sacrifice for inadvertent sin but the Sages insist that the blasphemer cannot bring a sacrifice since a sacrifice is restricted to atone for actions.</i>? The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: They answered him according to his<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous Tanna in our Mishnah here, who also is supposed to represent the school of R. Aqiba.</i> argument. Following your argument, since you say that the blasphemer does not act, where do we find that somebody talks and brings a sacrifice that this one talks and brings a sacrifice? Rebbi Abba from Carthage asked, is not Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s argument inverted? There<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:9:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.9.8"><i>Sanhedrin</i>7:13 (Note 282</a>) R. Simeon ben Laqish explains that for R. Aqiba the conjurer of magical spells does not act since he simply speaks, but here he attributes the same opinion to the opponents of R. Aqiba.</i> he said, for Rebbi Aqiba the blasphemer does not act. But here he says, the blasphemer is acting in the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Two Tannaïm<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The inconsistency is not R. Simeon ben Laqish’s but is the Mishnah’s choice of representatives of the reputed teachings of R. Aqiba. Since R. Aqiba’s students were scattered by the Hadrianic persecutions and only a few survived, the divergence of traditions is easily explained.</i> following the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba. One said, the blasphemer does not act, and the other said, the blasphemer does act.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An oath that I shall not eat, when he ate and drank<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the general acceptation of “eating” is “ingesting nourishment”, this is the meaning of the word if used in an oath unless the wording of the oath clearly indicates that a more narrow meaning is intended, such as “ingesting solid food”.</i> he is liable only for one. An oath that I shall not eat or drink, when he ate and drank he is liable for two.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah does not refer to Mishnah 2 but to the last part of Mishnah 1 where it is stated that an oath to refrain from eating includes a prohibition of drinking but separate oaths for solid and fluid food mean just that. The text is a slightly inaccurate copy of a text in <i>Ma`aser ŠœŠeni</i>2:1, Notes 8–23 and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:3:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.3.2-15"><i>Yoma</i>8:3</a> (45a); a parallel discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 22b-23a" href="/Shevuot.22b-23a">Babli, 22b–23a</a>. The essence of the argument is that if one mentions eating, the accompanying drink is included, but speaking of drinking no solid food is intended.</i> Drinking is subsumed under eating but eating is not subsumed under drinking. Rebbi Jonah understood all this from: <i>Therefore, I told the Children of Israel, none of you shall eat blood</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:12" href="/Leviticus.17.12"><i>Lev</i>. 17:12</a>.</i>. Where do we hold? If about congealed blood, was it not stated that congealed blood is neither food nor drink? <sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This text does not belong here; it was copied again from Halakhah 1, text between Notes 17,18.</i>(But we hold, about minute amounts. Are minute amounts eating? Explain it following Rebbi Aqiba who said that a minute amount is eating.) And was it not stated: If one liquefied the blood and swallowed it, or froze fat and ate it, if there is the volume of an olive he is liable. What does Rebbi Jonah do with this? It is not food to become impure in the impurity of food, or fluid to become impure in the impurity of fluids. Rebbi Jonah changed and explained the Mishnah: <i>You shall spend the money for anything you desire</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut</i>. 14:26</a>. The argument is about the part of the verse which is not quoted, that the money of Second Tithe may be spent for all kinds of edibles, cattle and sheep, wine and liquor.</i>. Where do we hold? If about one who gives the taste of wine into a cooked dish, is that not spoiling the taste? But we hold with everything and the Merciful called it “eating.” The rabbis of Caesarea said, explain if about <i>gomraya</i> and <i>orzaraya</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ma`aser Šeni</i>2:1, Note 16. For the second word the preferred reading seems to be that of the other two sources, אורזנייה, which may denote cedar resin (J. Levy) or a derivative of אוֹרֶז “rice”. The readings for the first word, גמרייה, גמזוזיניה, גומננייא show that the scribe did not know what to do with it; it may be a derivative of “gum” (<i>gummi, κόμμι</i>) (E. G.) used in the preparation of liquors.</i>, since anything that is auxiliary to food is like food.
Rebbi Yose understood all this from the following: “An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate and drank he is liable only for one.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1.</i>” The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose, but it is said following this, “an oath that I shall not eat nor drink, when he ate and drank”<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1.</i> should he be liable only for one? Rebbi Yose told them, if somebody said, an oath that I shall not eat this loaf, and he continued, an oath that I shall not eat this other loaf, when he ate both of them would he not be guilty on two counts?
Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel sources correctly: Ḥananiah.</i> in the name of Rebbi Phineas understood all this from the following: “An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate inedible food and drank undrinkable fluids, he is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5.</i>” Therefore if he ate edible food and drank drinkable fluid he is liable.
Rebbi Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel sources correctly: Abba Mari.</i> understood all this from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:1:1-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.1.1-13">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i>8:1</a>.</i>: “If he ate and drank in one forgetting he is liable only once.”
Rebbi Ḥinena understood all this from the following: <i>I did not eat from it in my mourning,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut.</i> 26:14</a></i> but I drank?
So far if he said, that I shall not eat, but he drank. If he said, that I shall not drink, but he ate? Drinking is subsumed under eating but eating is not subsumed under drinking.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate wheat bread, and barley bread, and spelt bread, he is liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">While he broke his oath many times, he is liable only for one sacrifice since there is only one oath to break.</i>.<br>An oath that I shall eat neither wheat bread, nor barley bread, nor spelt bread; when he ate wheat bred, and barley bread, and spelt bread, he is liable for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He made three oaths, each one requiring a separate sacrifice.</i>.<br>An oath that I shall not drink; if he drank many kinds he is liable only for one. An oath that I shall drink neither wine, nor oil, not date syrup, if he drank he is liable for each single one.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An oath that I shall eat neither,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">There is a long dissertation about this Halakhah in the commentary <i>Mishneh Lammelekh</i> to Maimonides, <i>Hilkhot Ma’akhalot Asurot</i>14:12.</i>“An oath that I shall not eat bread” and he wrapped it in reed leaves or grape leaves and ate, he is liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “only once” is only because of the following sentence. For the bread wrapped in inedible leaves one has to state that he broke his oath even if the bread itself did not touch his mouth. (Grape leaves are edible when cooked; they may be marginally edible when raw.)</i>. “An oath that I shall not eat bread nor grape skin nor grape seeds, and he wrapped it in grape skins and grape seeds, he is liable for each one<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Mishnah.</i>, and if he was a <i>nazir</i> he is liable for three<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation is not quite correct. For the oath he is liable for three sacrifices (or, if duly warned, for three floggings) but for breaking the <i>nazir</i>’s prohibition of anything coming from grapes (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:4" href="/Numbers.6.4"><i>Num</i>. 6:4</a>) he is liable for flogging if duly warned, not for a sacrifice. His liability would be three sacrifices and one additional sin.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, in comprehensive form. But in detail, no oaths fall on prohibitions. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even in comprehensive form no oaths fall on prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 22b" href="/Shevuot.22b">Babli 22b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 23b" href="/Shevuot.23b">23b</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.5"><i>Nazir</i>1:2 (Note 55</a>) R. Ze`ira proclaims as undisputed what here and in the Babli is R. Joḥanan’s opinion. Since all future Jewish souls were present at Mount Sinai, he already has sworn to keep the precepts of the Torah. Therefore an oath to break Torah prohibitions is void. R. Joḥanan holds that if an oath is valid since it contains matters not involving Torah prohibitions it is valid in general since an oath is either valid or invalid. R. Simeon ben Laqish (and in the Mishnah, R. Simeon ben Ioḥai) hold that oaths can be partially invalid; the parts infringing on Torah precepts are always void (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate inedible food and drank undrinkable fluids, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In popular usage, swallowing inedibles is not called “eating”. Judicial interpretation of oaths must follow popular usage.</i>. An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate carcass or torn meat, abominations or crawling animals, he is liable; Rebbi Simeon declares him not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Eating forbidden food still is called eating. R. Simeon will agree with this statement; he holds not only that an oath to break biblical commandments is void in itself but breaking the law to fulfill an oath invalidates the oath.</i>. If he said, a <i>qonam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Qonam</i> is a substitute for <i>qorbān</i> “sacrifice” which in the context of an oath or a vow means “it shall be forbidden to me as if it were sacrificial meat”, cf. <i>Introduction to Tractate Nedarim</i>.</i> that my wife may not benefit from me if I had eaten today, if he had eaten carcass or torn meat, abominations or crawling animals, his wife is forbidden benefits<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">He can neither have marital relations with her nor let her eat anything that is wholly or partially his, nor let her live in his house: he must divorce her and pay the marriage settlement in full. R. Simeon will agree.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An oath that I shall not drink,” etc. In this case<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 5.</i>, Rebbi Simeon declares not liable. Rebbi Simeon follows his own opinion, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makkot 3:2" href="/Mishnah_Makkot.3.2">Mishnah <i>Makkot</i>3:2 Notes 27–32</a>. <i>Tevel</i> is produce from which heave and the heave of tithe have not been removed, whose consumption except at harvest time is a deadly sin.</i>: “How much does he have to eat from <i>ṭevel</i> to be liable? Rebbi Simeon says, anything<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In R. Simeon’s opinion, biblical prohibitions are absolute, but infringing on a prohibition in a minute amount, for edibles less than the size of an olive, does not trigger the obligation of a sacrifice. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 24a" href="/Shevuot.24a">Babli 24a</a>.</i>; but the Sages say, the volume of an olive. Rebbi Simeon told them, do you not agree that one who eats an ant is liable? They told him, because it is a creature. He answered them, also a grain of wheat is a creature<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">He does not defend his point of view but shows his opponents that even in their opinion a complete fruit or animal is biblically forbidden even if it is smaller than an olive but still visible with the naked eye. This is accepted as practice, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.4"><i>Berakhot</i>6:1, Notes 14–18</a>.</i>.” In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, why does Rebbi Simeon declare not liable? Rebbi Ze`ira said, Rebbi Simeon follows his own opinion. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Simeon: <i>You shall deprive yourselves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:29" href="/Leviticus.16.29"><i>Lev</i>. 16:29</a>.</i>, of what is permitted to you, not of what is forbidden to you<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Eating non-kosher food violates a simple prohibition; breaking the fast on the Day of Atonement is a severe sin subjecting the unrepentant sinner to extirpation. R. Simeon declares eating non-kosher food on the Day of Atonement as violation of a simple prohibition (which if committed inadvertently does not make the perpetrator liable for a sacrifice). If the stringent prohibition of the Day of Atonement does not include forbidden food then an oath which never can lead to extirpation cannot include forbidden food either.</i>.
Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked: According to Rebbi Joḥanan, who said that comprehensive oaths fall on prohibitions, should we not state “an oath that I shall not eat; when he ate”<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 3:4" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.3.4">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i>3:4</a>, that a person may become liable for 5 sacrifices for eating one olive-size bite: an impure person who on the Day of Atonement ate sacrificial fat which was a leftover from a prior day. Eating fat is always forbidden; eating sacrificial fat is illegitimate use of <i>sacra,</i> classified as larceny (requiring restitution and a reparation sacrifice); eating any <i>sacra</i> while impure is punishable by extirpation. The mention of the Day of Atonement shows that the Mishnah is formulated for the Rabbis, not R. Simeon. The question then is, if the statement of R. Joḥanan be true that an oath containing a general prohibition also refers to things biblically prohibited, the Tanna, who obviously is looking for an example showing the maximum of criminal acts that can be committed by one action, should have added that the person who ate the fat had previously made an oath that he would not eat at all, for a total of 6 sacrifices. That no oath is mentioned in the Mishnah seems to prove that R. Joḥanan’s position is rejected by the Mishnah; it cannot be accepted in practice.</i>? Rebbi Ze`ira said, in the whole lifetime of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal we did not find an answer; after he had died, we found an answer. Where do we stand? If one said, an oath that I shall not eat meat, including Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since oaths have to be interpreted according to common usage, not lawyers’ jargon, an oath “that I shall not eat” will be interpreted as oath not to eat permitted food; the question of R. Abba bar Mamal does not apply. But an oath to become vegetarian is a valid oath and in Temple times would prevent the person from fulfilling the commandment of eating of the Passover sacrifice. Since in contrast to vows oaths cannot be dissolved by a rabbi, this is a case where a general oath supersedes a biblical commandment.</i>; that I shall not eat during these ten days<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Ten days of Penitence, from New Year’s day to and including the Day of Atonement. In this case breaking the fast is a double sin since it also means breaking the oath. The entire argument is for the Rabbis, so it includes eating non-kosher food.</i>, including the Day of Atonement, then the oath falls on prohibitions. Rebbi Yose said, one may infer from here if in front of him were olive-sized pieces of slaughtered [meat] and one of carcass meat and he said, [an oath] that I shall eat these ten olive-sized pieces, since he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This follows R. Simeon. If there can be no punishment for breaking the oath relative to the carcass meat then there cannot be one for eating the other 9 since the oath was for 10, not for 9 pieces.</i> for the carcass meat, he neither is liable for the remainder.
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: Why did he not say, “an oath that I shall eat of the slaughter of X”? Is there not fat? If he slaughters deer. Is there not the hip sinew? About fowl. Is there not blood<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Joḥanan it is difficult to find any oath involving meat that should be valid at all, since he requires an oath which forbids permitted foods; as a consequence it will also apply to prohibitions. But if somebody forbids himself anything which a person X will slaughter, the oath includes the fat of the slaughtered domestic animal forbidden by biblical standards. Since an original oath forbidding biblically forbidden things is void, the entire oath is voided. Similarly for wild animals, whose fat is permitted (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23"><i>Lev</i>. 7:23</a>), the hip sinew still is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 32:33" href="/Genesis.32.33"><i>Gen</i>. 32:33</a>), and the blood of a fowl is forbidden like the blood of four-legged animals (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:27" href="/Leviticus.7.27"><i>Lev</i>. 7:27</a>).</i>?
Rebbi Ḥinena asked: Why did he not say, “an oath if I would eat of X” without detail<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The difficulty encountered in the preceding paragraph can easily be circumvented if the oath is made on a piece of meat which does not contain any forbidden fat and is drained of its blood. Then the only problem is the problem that for R. Simeon the number of required sacrifices is not a measure of the number of prohibitions which were violated; the Tanna might want to formulate his statement to take care of R. Simeon’s opinions also. But since the Mishnah requires a separate sacrifice for eating forbidden fat on the Day of Atonement, it certainly cannot be formulated for R. Simeon, who denies that the prohibitions of that day refer to forbidden food.</i>? Because of Rebbi Simeon. But if following Rebbi Simeon, how could we state the Day of Atonement, since Rebbi Simeon stated, <i>you shall deprive yourselves</i>, of what is (forbidden)<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the words in parentheses have to be switched, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.4.2">Note 52</a>.</i> to you, not of what is (permitted)<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the words in parentheses have to be switched, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.4.2">Note 52</a>.</i> to you. Only one has a fixed rate sacrifice, the other a variable one<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna of <i>Keritut</i> counts only the maximum of fixed-rate sacrifices; he could agree that with an oath a liability for a variable rate sacrifice could be added. Nothing can be inferred about the disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>.
They objected, is there not impurity of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i> which need a variable rate [sacrifice] and did the Tanna not state it<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The previous explanation is incorrect. The Tanna of <i>Keritut</i> mentioned a sacrifice for eating <i>sancta</i> in impurity; this requires a variable value sacrifice.</i>? We come to state only things carrying a liability to extirpation; there is no extirpation for oaths.
They objected, there is no extirpation for larceny and it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The previous explanation is incorrect. The Tanna of <i>Keritut</i> mentioned a sacrifice for illegally appropriating Temple property for private use. There is no extirpation mentioned for this, only restitution with a fine and a reparation sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>). Only fixed-rate purification offerings are restricted to atoning for unintentional sins carrying a penalty of at least extirpation, not reparation sacrifices. This finally yields the correct answer.</i>! We come to state only things which cannot become permitted after being forbidden. Oaths may become permitted after being forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since oaths may be made for a certain time only.</i>. This<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing mentioned here but the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 3:4" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.3.4">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i>3:4</a> where R. Meïr says that if it was on a Sabbath and the person brought the piece of meat between his teeth from a private to a public domain he is liable for an additional sacrifice.</i> implies that there are no categories of work for the day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The rules for the Sabbath specify 39 categories of forbidden work (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>7:2), each of which triggers liability for a separate sacrifice. Since the anonymous Tanna placed the case on the Day of Atonement, and any work forbidden on the Sabbath is forbidden on the Day of Atonement, R. Meїr could simply have added carrying also on the day of Atonement. Since he has to place that Day on a Sabbath, it follows that any and all infringements of the sanctity of the Day of Atonement trigger the same liability of a sacrifice. There never can be more than one for a given Day. The conclusion of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 14a" href="/Keritot.14a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i>14a</a>, is formulated more narrowly: Transporting from domain to domain is not a separate desecration of the Day of Atonement.</i>, for if anybody violated all of them in one forgetting he is liable only for one [sacrifice] for all of them. But if anybody violated each of them separately, would he not be liable for each single one?
In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, if one said, “an oath that I shall not eat unleavened bread”, he is forbidden to eat unleavened bread in the night of Passover. “That I shall not eat unleavened bread in the night of Passover,” he is flogged and eats unleavened bread<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">While leavened bread is forbidden for seven days on Passover, there is a positive commandment to eat unleavened bread only on the first night (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:18" href="/Exodus.12.18"><i>Ex</i>. 12:18</a>; cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, pp. 328–329.) An oath not to fulfill this commandment is void; the oath is void and incurs the penalty of void oaths. But an oath never to eat unleavened bread is valid and prevents its maker from fulfilling his duty without breaking the oath.</i>. “That I shall not sit in the shadow”, he is forbidden to sit in the shadow of a booth<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">On the festival of Tabernacles, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i>23:42</a>. The argument is totally parallel to that of the preceding example.</i>. “That I shall not sit in the shadow of a booth,” he is flogged and sits in the shadow of a booth.
Not only unspecified, but even “X”. Not only comprehensive but even in detail. Not only in the future but also in the past. Not only the rabbis but even Rebbi Aqiba. Not only carcass and torn meat but even dust<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 24a" href="/Shevuot.24a">Babli (24a</a>) explicitly declares ingesting dust as not eating.</i>. 

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Whether matters of himself<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">All the rules of liability for a variable value sacrifice spelled out in the preceding Mishnaiot are valid for all kinds of oaths irrespective of their content or meaning.</i>, or matters of others, or material matters, or immaterial matters. How is this? An oath that I shall give to X, or that I shall not give, that I gave, or that I did not give, that I shall sleep, or that I shall not sleep, that I slept, or that I did not sleep, that I shall throw a pebble into the sea, or that I shall not throw, that I threw, or that I did not throw. Rebbi Ismael said, he is liable only for the future, for it is said <i>to cause evil or cause good</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>. The causative refers to the future.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba said to him, if it is so then this refers only to matters of causing evil or good; from where matters which do not refer to causing evil or good? He answered him, from the additional text of the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The continuation of the quote, <i>anything which a person will blurt out in an oath</i>, which seems to be superfluous since the sentence starts: <i>Or a person who would swear blurting out with his lips</i>. The addition indicates that the verse should not be interpreted narrowly. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.5.3">Note 83</a>.</i>. He answered, just as the additional text of the verse is for this, the additional text of the verse is for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Oaths stating facts about the past.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> He wants to teach himself a pretext<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence in Mishnah 5. Since this refers to a vow, which may be annulled by a rabbi or a court, all the restrictions described for oaths are eliminated. Even if he made his vow dependent on minute details, or refers to something in the past, or to minute quantities, or even to worthless things, the vow is valid since he only wants to find a pretext to divorce his wife.</i>. “Whether matters of himself,” etc. Rebbi Abba in the name of Samuel: “An oath that X gave a mina to Y,” and it turns out that he did not give, since it was not in his power for the future, it is not in his power for the past<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">He never could swear that X will give Y since it is not in his power to force X to give. This is all about liability for a variable sacrifice, not monetary liabilities. Since witnesses do not swear, this is not a case of perjury.</i>. Rebbi Yose objected, about phylacteries there is nothing about the future but there is about the past<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody swore falsely that he put on <i>tefillin</i> he is liable for a sacrifice (Mishnah 9).</i>! He told him, phylacteries came from another source. <i>To cause evil or cause good</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>. The causative refers to the future.</i>; just as doing good is optional so doing bad is optional; this excludes anything involving a prohibition or anything involving a permission<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The expression היתיר “permission” probably is induced by the usual opposites prohibition - permission. What really is intended here is מצוה “commandment” which is the opposite of something optional; <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Parashah</i>9(6), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 27a" href="/Shevuot.27a">Babli 27a</a>.</i>. <i>Anything which one will blurt out</i>, excluding a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Whose words have no legal consequences.</i>. <i>A person in an oath</i>, excluding a person not acting at his own will<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Parashah</i>9(9), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 26a" href="/Shevuot.26a">Babli 26a</a>. This includes even a person acting by his own will on false information or false remembering.</i>. <i>And it will be forgotten by him</i>, he will be oblivious of the oath. Or could I think that the object was forgotten by him? The verse says, <i>in an oath</i> a<i>nd it was forgotten by him</i>; he is liable for forgetting the oath; he is not liable for forgetting the object. Could one not add forgetting the object to forgetting the oath so that he should be liable? What is the difference between this and forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary so that he should be liable twice following Rebbi Ismael, who said forgetting impurity and forgetting the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.3">Chapter 1, Note 23</a>.</i>? There it is written <i>it was forgotten by him, it was forgotten by him</i> twice<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i>12(7), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 14b" href="/Shevuot.14b">Babli 14b</a>; Mishnah 2:6.</i>. Here it is written <i>it was forgotten by him</i> once. I could think that one who swears to the detriment of others should be liable: the verse says, <i>to cause evil or cause good</i>; just as doing good is optional so doing bad is optional<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since harming anybody is forbidden, an oath to harm another person does not trigger liability for a sacrifice if it is broken. The example shows that in this case breaking the oath may be a meritorious act. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 27a" href="/Shevuot.27a">Babli 27a</a>.</i>. I shall exclude one who swears to the detriment of another that he should not be liable. For example, if one swears that he will not provide food for another. He saw him in convulsions and gave to him.
How does Rebbi Ismael explain the verse? <sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>. The causative refers to the future.</i><i>Or a person, if he would swear blurting out with his lips</i>, a general statement. <i>To cause evil or cause good</i>, a detail. A general statement followed by a detail; the general statement contains only what is in the detail. But the detail only contains matters of causing evil or good! But it is so: <i>To cause evil or cause good</i>, a detail. <i>Anything which a person will blurt out,</i> a general statement. A detail followed by a general statement, everything is included; this adds matters directed towards the past. But it is so: <i>Or a person, if he would swear blurting out with his lips</i>, a general statement. <i>To cause evil or cause good</i>, a detail. <i>Anything which a person will blurt out,</i> a general statement. A general statement followed by a detail followed by a general statement, you only argue in the pattern of the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">In the version of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 1" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.1"><i>Sifra</i>(<i>Introduction</i>1</a>), in the list of the thirteen hermeneutical principles of R. Ismael one finds (5) a general statement followed by a detail, (6) a detail followed by a general statement, (7) a general statement followed by a detail followed by a general statement you only argue following the pattern of the detail, (8) a general statement dependent on the detail, (9) a detail dependent on the general statement. Rules 8 and 9 mean that if the general statement can only be understood by the detail or vice versa, rules 5 and 6 do not apply. It then is explained in §7 that if a general statement is followed by a detail, only the detail is intended. §8: If a detail is followed by a general statement, the general statement adds to the detail. Examples are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:2" href="/Leviticus.1.2"><i>Lev</i>. 1:2</a>: <i>From animals, from cattle, or from small cattle</i>. This implies that sacrifices are restricted to cattle, sheep, or goats. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a>: <i>A donkey, an ox, a sheep, or any animal</i>. The rules of caretakers apply to any animal. Then it becomes a problem how to treat a verse which contains general statement, detail, general statement, whether to apply rule 5 (eliminating the final general statement by rule 9), or rule 6 (eliminating the first general statement by rule 8), or rule 7. In the preceding derivation, the arguments have been suppressed that rules 8 and 9 do not apply and, therefore, only rule 7 is relevant. The standard example for an application of rule 7 is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut</i>. 14:26</a>, about permitted uses of Second Tithe money at the place of the Sanctuary: <i>You may spend the money for anything you desire</i>(general), <i>for cattle, or small cattle, or wine, or liquor</i>(detail)<i>, or anything you wish</i>(general). The common denominator of the items in the detail describes animal or vegetable food; Second Tithe money can be used for any food derived from animals (generated from semen) or plants (growing from seeds).<br>Since the first part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a> fits Rule 7, it is clear that the rule applies not only to oaths intended to cause good or evil but to a larger set of oaths which, however, have to conform to the idea underlying “causing good or bad things”. Obviously one of the ideas is that events caused are later in time than the cause. This is R. Ismael’s interpretation of the verse. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 26a" href="/Shevuot.26a">Babli 26a</a>.</i>. Since the detail is explicit, matters of causing evil or good, from where matters not causing evil or good? <sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah.</i>“He answered him, from the additional text of the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">73,</sup><i class="footnote">The continuation of the quote, <i>anything which a person will blurt out in an oath</i>, which seems to be superfluous since the sentence starts: <i>Or a person who would swear blurting out with his lips</i>. The addition indicates that the verse should not be interpreted narrowly. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.5.3">Note 83</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This is not an additional argument. The additional text shows that the rule to be applied is rule 7, not rule 5. R. Aqiba follows a different system. For him the sentence structure is not general, detail, general but expansive, restrictive, expansive, which he reads as including everything except what is completely different from the detail quoted as restriction.</i>. He answered, just as the verse added for this, the verse added for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The text of R. Aqiba’s answer is the text of the Mishnah in the Babli. It is known that the separate Mishnah in the Yerushalmi is not from the Yerushalmi text. The Mishnah text in Maimonides’s autograph is that of the separate Yerushalmi Mishnah.</i>.” You cannot<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah cannot be quoted as proof that R. Ismael conceded to R. Aqiba.</i>, as Rebbi Hila said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: So did Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Who is R. Aqiba’s opponent. All of Mishnah 1 is R. Aqiba’s teaching. R. Ismael opposes adding backward looking oaths as blurted oaths.</i> answer Rebbi Aqiba. Do we find cases where one is liable for intentional action because of a false oath but if in oblivion because of a blurted oath<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">A future directed oath, where it cannot be verified instantly whether it will be kept or violated, is an actionless crime and cannot be prosecuted (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.1.1">Note 3</a>). The preconditions of a sacrifice for a blurted oath negate the possibility of judicial penalties.</i>? Could he not have objected, do we find cases where one is liable for intentional action because of a false oath and he has to bring a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If R. Aqiba did accept R. Joḥanan’s argument, it would be possible for a person to be flogged for violating the prohibition of perjury (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:12" href="/Leviticus.19.12"><i>Lev</i>. 19:12</a>) and still be liable for a sacrifice. This would make R. Ismael’s objection irrelevant.</i>? He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba to R. Ismael.</i>, do you agree that there are cases which are not matters of causing evil or good, even if they are not written<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are not mentioned in the verse. For לי נן read לֵי[ת אִי]נֻּן.</i>? He told him<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael to R. Aqiba.</i>, even though I accept cases which are not matters of causing evil or good, are they only written if they be matters of causing evil or good<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious from rule 7 that the obligation of a variable sacrifice for a blurted oath must hold for a larger set than “causing bad or good things”. The only problem is to define this larger set and the causative employed definitively excludes oaths regarding the past. The Tanna of the Mishnah cannot accept R. Ismael’s hermeneutical rules.</i>? Therefore never for the past<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since the oath is void, he is prevented from sacrificing if it was unintentional. If it was intentional he can be prosecuted for a vain oath, forbidden in the Ten Commandments.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he swore not to observe a commandment but failed not to observe it, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">42,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 22b" href="/Shevuot.22b">Babli 22b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 23b" href="/Shevuot.23b">23b</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.5"><i>Nazir</i>1:2 (Note 55</a>) R. Ze`ira proclaims as undisputed what here and in the Babli is R. Joḥanan’s opinion. Since all future Jewish souls were present at Mount Sinai, he already has sworn to keep the precepts of the Torah. Therefore an oath to break Torah prohibitions is void. R. Joḥanan holds that if an oath is valid since it contains matters not involving Torah prohibitions it is valid in general since an oath is either valid or invalid. R. Simeon ben Laqish (and in the Mishnah, R. Simeon ben Ioḥai) hold that oaths can be partially invalid; the parts infringing on Torah precepts are always void (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>).</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since the oath is void, he is prevented from sacrificing if it was unintentional. If it was intentional he can be prosecuted for a vain oath, forbidden in the Ten Commandments.</i>; to observe a commandment and did not observe it, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The oath to keep the commandments is valid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 119:107" href="/Psalms.119.107"><i>Ps.</i>119:107</a>); why does breaking this valid oath not imply liability for a sacrifice or criminal prosecution?</i>, though it would have been logical that he should be liable, the words of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra. Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra said, since he is liable for a permitted purpose, for which he is not sworn to from Mount Sinai, is it not logical that he should be liable about a commandment to which he is sworn from Mount Sinai<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 22b" href="/Shevuot.22b">Babli 22b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 23b" href="/Shevuot.23b">23b</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.5"><i>Nazir</i>1:2 (Note 55</a>) R. Ze`ira proclaims as undisputed what here and in the Babli is R. Joḥanan’s opinion. Since all future Jewish souls were present at Mount Sinai, he already has sworn to keep the precepts of the Torah. Therefore an oath to break Torah prohibitions is void. R. Joḥanan holds that if an oath is valid since it contains matters not involving Torah prohibitions it is valid in general since an oath is either valid or invalid. R. Simeon ben Laqish (and in the Mishnah, R. Simeon ben Ioḥai) hold that oaths can be partially invalid; the parts infringing on Torah precepts are always void (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>).</i>? They said to him, no. What you say about an oath for permitted purpose where He made “no” equal to “yes”, can you say that about an oath concerning a commandment, where He did not make “no” equal to “yes”? Since one who swears not to observe a commandment but failed not to observe it, is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In matters not involving biblical precepts, an oath is valid whether formulated in the positive or negative. But for biblical precepts King David already did decide that one may swear to keep them (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 119:106" href="/Psalms.119.106"><i>Ps.</i>119:106</a>); only oaths to break commandments are intrinsically void.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he swore not to observe a commandment,” etc. Rebbi Mani said, so is the Mishnah: “He is liable, the words of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Jehudah ben Bathyra gives an argument to hold a person liable if he breaks any oath, R. Mani wants to read simply “he is liable, the words of R. Jehudah ben Bathyra”.</i>” What Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra said refers to prohibitions in general. “An oath that I shall eat carcass meat,” but he did not eat it. He is not liable. “An oath that I shall eat,” and he ate<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody, including R. Jehudah ben Bathyra, agrees that somebody who breaks an oath to break a Torah commandment is never liable for a sacrifice. The only problematic cases are somebody who keeps an oath to break a Torah commandment or who breaks an oath to keep a commandment. In both cases he might be liable to prosecution for breaking the commandment; the only question here is whether he is liable for a sacrifice, which is answered in the negative by the majority and in the positive by R. Jehudah ben Bathyra.</i>. If you would say that he is liable, they should have objected to him: No. You declare about an oath for permitted purpose where He made “no” equal to “yes” and “yes” equal to “no”, can you declare this about an oath concerning a commandment, where He did not make “no” equal to “yes”<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Oaths about profane subjects may be formulated either as declarations of intent to do something or to refrain from it; about biblical precepts the only admissible ones are to keep the commandments. For R. Mani’s interpretation of R. Jehudah ben Bathyra’s argument to hold, the Mishnah should have been formulated differently. As it stands, the formulation of the Mishnah has to be accepted and R. Mani’s correction rejected.</i>?
From where a warning about an oath for a permitted purpose? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, <i>he shall not profane his word</i><sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>, the paragraph about profane vows. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 21a" href="/Shevuot.21a">Babli 21a</a>.</i><i>,</i> he shall not make his words profane<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">He translates biblical into rabbinic Hebrew.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “An oath that I shall not eat this loaf, an oath that I shall not eat it, an oath that I shall not eat it;” he is liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">He made three oaths but the second and third are void since they forbid what already is forbidden.</i>. This is the blurted oath<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">It is the paradigm for all rules about “blurted oaths”.</i> where in case it be intentional one is liable for flogging<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">If he was duly warned not to break his oath but broke it before witnesses he can be prosecuted in criminal court for breaking the commandment of <i>he shall not profane his word</i>.</i>, and if unintentional for a variable sacrifice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An oath that I shall not eat this loaf,” etc. “An oath that I shall eat this loaf today,” the day passed and then he ate it. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both say, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Criminal liability.</i>; not for the same reason. The reason of Rebbi Joḥanan, because he cannot be duly warned<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">No criminal prosecution is possible without evidence that the criminal was duly warned not to commit the crime (cf. <i>Introduction to Tractate Sanhedrin,</i> on Chapter Five). The warning must be delivered shortly before the criminal act, so the accused cannot claim to have forgotten. In this case criminality would be inaction; this is not subject to warning.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 3b" href="/Shevuot.3b">Babli, 3b</a>, brings the same example and connects this with the dispute between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish whether a warning can be delivered even if it is conditional because there is no certainty that the contemplated action will be criminal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 11:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.11.7.6"><i>Yebamot</i>11:7 Note 171</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.6"><i>Nazir</i>8:1 Note 48</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:4:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.4.2-12"><i>Pesaḥim</i>5:4</a> fol. 32c; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 3b" href="/Shevuot.3b">Babli 3b</a>). Since R. Joḥanan holds that a conditional warning is acceptable, the Babli is forced to switch the attributions in this case. As a consequence it follows that for the Yerushalmi here, in R. Joḥanan’s opinion this would not be a conditional warning but one which it is impossible to deliver. (In his Notes to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Shevuot 3b:2:2" href="/Tosafot_on_Shevuot.3b.2.2">Tosaphot <i>s. v.</i> אבל</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 4a" href="/Shevuot.4a">Babli 4a</a>, R. Akiba Eiger essentially notes that the Babli’s argument cannot be read into the Yerushalmi.)</i>. The reason of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, because it is a prohibition without action<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.1.1">Note 3</a>.</i>. What is the difference between them? He burned it or threw it into the sea. If you say, because he cannot be duly warned<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the witnesses are present at the moment when the loaf is thrown into the sea he cannot be warned since what he does is not breaking the oath but making its fulfillment impossible; there is no biblical paragraph prohibiting this action.</i>, he is not liable. If you say, because it is a prohibition without action, there is an action.
