Rebbi Phineas asked, if he was asthenic<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀσθενής. He is unable to eat an entire loaf during the day and he knows it. Therefore it is a false oath, not a blurted one.</i>? Since it is not in his hand for the future, it is not in his hand for the past<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">He never could swear that X will give Y since it is not in his power to force X to give. This is all about liability for a variable sacrifice, not monetary liabilities. Since witnesses do not swear, this is not a case of perjury.</i>.
Rebbi Yose asked, “an oath, an oath, an oath, that I shall not eat,” and he ate. What is the rule? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, let us hear from the following: “'An oath that I shall not eat this loaf, an oath that I shall not eat it, an oath that I shall not eat it;' he is liable once.” Because he mentioned <i>this</i>. Therefore, if he did not mention <i>this</i>, he would be liable for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Since he specified the loaf in the first oath, the others were vain. But if it was not specified, the oaths are concurrent but separate and each one carries its own liability. This is amplified in the next paragraph.</i>.
Abime the brother of Ḥefa<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">He is called עיפא in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 28b" href="/Shevuot.28b">Babli 28b</a>, where their dispute is about other cases and Ḥefa always has the correct answer. They are Babylonian Amoraim of the fourth generation.</i> said, I had studied <i>Nedarim</i> and <i>Ševuot</i>; Ḥefa wanted to examine him. He had before him five loaves and said, an oath that I would not eat this loaf. He returned and said, these two. He returned and said, these three. He returned and said, these four. He returned and said, these five. Then he ate the first. He<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Abime.</i> said to him, he is liable for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since each oath adds to the preceding prohibition, even R. Joḥanan should agree that the addition in each oath is valid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.6.1">Note 42</a>).</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Ḥefa.</i> answered, he is liable only once, for when he mentioned the first he made it like carcass meat; the following make an oath fall on something prohibited and oaths cannot fall on prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan will agree that successive additions of prohibitions are valid only if the prohibitions vary in kind, not if the domain of applicability of one prohibition is enlarged.</i>.
Ḥefa continued to examine him. He had before him five loaves and said, an oath that I would not eat these five loaves. He returned and said, these four. He returned and said, these three. He returned and said, these two. He returned and said, this one, and ate the first one. He<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Abime.</i> said to him, he is liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Since 4,3,2,1 all are contained in 5, all oaths following the first are void.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since each oath adds to the preceding prohibition, even R. Joḥanan should agree that the addition in each oath is valid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.6.1">Note 42</a>).</i> answered, he is liable for each single one; if somebody said “an oath if I would eat five loaves” and he ate four, is he not free from liability<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not eat five, he did not break the oath. In this case, instead of “not liable” one should say “permitted.”</i>?
Rebbi Yose said, the brother of Ḥefa seems to be reasonable in the later case and Ḥefa in the prior.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For an intentional vain oath one is liable to be flogged; if in error one is not liable. What is a vain oath<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">An oath which either is obviously false or one which he is forbidden to keep.</i>? One swears to change what is known to man, about a stone pillar that it is of gold, a man that he is a woman, or a woman that she is a man. One swears about an impossibility, if I did not see a flying camel, if I did not see a snake like the beam of the olive press. If one said to witnesses come and testify for me, an oath that we shall not testify for you<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">This is sinful if they know testimony, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>.</i>. One swears not to keep commandments: not to make a booth<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">On the festival of Tabernacles, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i>23:42</a>. The argument is totally parallel to that of the preceding example.</i>, not to take the <i>lulav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i>3:2</a>.</i>, not to put on phylacteries<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of Halakhah 10, the text also is in <i>Nedarim</i>3:2 (<b>נ</b>), explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:3:1-5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.3.1-5.2">Notes 37–78</a>.</i>; these are vain oaths for which one is liable to be flogged if intentional, and free from prosecution of unintentional.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “For an intentional vain oath one is liable to be flogged,” etc. There<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i>3:2</a>.</i>, we have stated: “If I did not see on this road [crowds] like those who left Egypt.” <sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of Halakhah 10, the text also is in <i>Nedarim</i>3:2 (<b>נ</b>), explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:3:1-5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.3.1-5.2">Notes 37–78</a>.</i> Is it impossible that crowds like those who left Egypt went by? But we are dealing with one look. Was it not the case that when Diocletian<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In Nedarim: Julianus Caesar. This is the correct text since Diocletian never made war “there”, i. e., in Babylonia.</i> went there, 1’200’000 [men] went with him? But we must be dealing with one look.
“If I did not see a snake like the beam of the olive press.” <sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <i>Nedarim</i> either is from another textual tradition or is rather corrupt.</i> Did not the snake of king Sapor swallow camels and carts? When they wanted to kill it, they filled camel bags with straw and put glowing coals inside; it swallowed them and died. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, I saw the hide of a snake made over eight spaces between pillars<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μεσοστύλιον “space between columns.”</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Jacob said, I saw the hide of a snake covering a state coach<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>carruca</i>“travel coach”.</i>. Samuel said, square. If square one could state even a small one<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In Samuel’s opinion a square snake is more wonderful than an oversized one since nothing in nature is square, as stated later in the text.</i>. Rebbi Yudan the father of Rebbi Mattaniah said, so it is. It is the way of this Tanna to use only big words, as we have stated: “A flying camel.” Could he not have stated, “a flying rat”?
<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph in addition is from <i>Ma`serot</i>5:7, Notes 122–129.</i> It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, there is nothing square from the six Days of Creation. Rebbi Berekhiah objected: Did we not state: “The body of <i>baheret</i> is like a square Cilician grit. The place for a grit is nine lentils. The space for a lentil is four hairwidths; in all 36 hairwidths.<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega`im</i>6:1. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.1.1">Chapter 1, Note 4</a>.</i>” Rebbi (Yosna)<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">For the otherwise unknown name יוסנה the other two sources read the well- attested name Bisna.</i> said, that in itself says that there is no square. Why was it stated “squared”? That he should square it<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Determine its surface area, standard Euclidean terminology.</i>. And כגעה<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <i>Ma`serot</i> reads ננעה, in <i>Nedarim</i>כנעה. The latter probably is the correct text, “lice”.</i>? It is full of knots. But there is the bunch of <i>pila</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">A kind of spice, cf. <i>Ma`serot</i> Note 129. Possible Latin <i>pila,</i> among other meanings also “ball, globe” (E. G.).</i>! It is round below. Some wanted to say, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said this only about creatures, There is square in foods, there is no square in creatures.
<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If it can be shown that they committed perjury and were duly warned.</i>“An oath that we do not know testimony for you;” they are flogged because of witnesses’ oath<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 26b" href="/Shevuot.26b">Rashba (<i>Novellae on Tractate Ševuot,</i> on 26b</a>, in the edition by J. D. Ilan, Jerusalem 1993, col. 97, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:8:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.8.8">Note 145</a>) declares this unintelligible since it contradicts Mishnah 4:2.</i>. “An oath that we shall not testify for you;” they are flogged because of blurted oath<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since by the first oath he became obligated to eat the loaf by biblical rules, the second oath has a similar status as an oath to violate a biblical commandment.</i>.
“An oath that I shall eat this loaf;” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since by the first oath he became obligated to eat the loaf by biblical rules, the second oath has a similar status as an oath to violate a biblical commandment.</i><i>Vain</i> and <i>untruth</i> both were said together, which is impossible for the ear to hear and the mouth to say. <i>Its desecrator shall be put to death</i> and <i>on the Sabbath day two sheep</i><sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 31:14" href="/Exodus.31.14"><i>Ex</i>. 31:14</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:9" href="/Numbers.28.9"><i>Num</i>. 28:9</a>.</i> were said together. <i>Do not wear ša`aṭnez</i>, <i>fringes you shall make for yourselves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:11-12" href="/Deuteronomy.22.11-12"><i>Deut</i>. 22:11–12</a>.</i>, both were said together. <i>The nakedness of your brother’s wife</i> and <i>her brother-in-law shall come to her</i><sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:16" href="/Leviticus.18.16"><i>Lev.</i> 18:16</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:5" href="/Deuteronomy.25.5"><i>Deut.</i> 25:5</a>.</i> were said together. <i>You shall not move property</i>; <i>any daughter inheriting real estate</i><sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 36:8-9" href="/Numbers.36.8-9"><i>Num.</i> 36:8–9</a>.</i> both were said together, which is impossible for the mouth to say and the ear to hear. And so it says, <i>God spoke once, two I heard from this</i><sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 62:12" href="/Psalms.62.12"><i>Ps.</i> 62:12</a>.</i><i>.</i> And it says, <i>is not My word like fire, says the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 23:29" href="/Jeremiah.23.29"><i>Jer.</i> 23:29</a>. The reference is to the end of the verse, <i>and like a hammer splintering rock.</i></i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">A corrupt copy of the text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.6"><i>Nedarim</i>3:2, Notes 60–65</a>. R. La (Ulla) is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 29a" href="/Shevuot.29a">Babli, 29a</a>.</i> A vain oath, if one swears to change what is known to men; a false oath if he swears to substitute. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: anything known to two persons is a (vain)<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">One has to exchange “vain” and “false” as in <i>Nedarim</i>. A vain oath is obviously vain, a false oath is materially false but may not generally be seen as such.</i> oath, to three it is (false)<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">One has to exchange “vain” and “false” as in <i>Nedarim</i>. A vain oath is obviously vain, a false oath is materially false but may not generally be seen as such.</i>. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: If it is known to two persons and another one at the end of the world, it is a vain oath. What is the difference between them? If he was untrue before two [persons], threw it into the sea, and they had warned him because of a vain oath; in the opinion of R. Joḥanan he is not flogged, in the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar he is flogged. If they had warned him because of a false oath, in the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar he is flogged, in the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is not flogged<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">With the <i>Nedarim</i> text, one has to switch “flogged” and “not flogged”.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">With <i>Nedarim</i> read: Rav.</i> Jehudah, even an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בֵּיצָה; it may not be a scribal error but a dialectal identification of ظ for ض.</i> and a pearl. What means “even”? But, for example, an egg and a pearl.
<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.7"><i>Nedarim</i>3:2 Notes 66–69</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta <i>Nedarim</i>2:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 24b" href="/Nedarim.24b">Babli <i>Nedarim</i>24b</a>.</i> It was stated: “Just as vows of exaggeration are permitted, so oaths of exaggeration are permitted.” But was it not stated: Vows of exaggeration are forbidden? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Pedat: Here about those who insist, there about those who do not insist. You may even say in both cases if they insist; here it is about one who intends that his oath should fall on his property, “an oath that my property [should be forbidden] to me.” Then his property is forbidden [to him]. But in matters of flogging, he cannot be flogged. Just as speeding-up vows are permitted, so speeding-up oaths are permitted. It is the same case; if he intends that his oath should fall on his property, “an oath that my property [should be forbidden] to me.” Then his property is forbidden [to him]. But in matters of flogging, he cannot be flogged.
Ḥizqiah said: he who swears that two are two is flogged for a vain oath. Rebbi Menahem<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Nedarim</i>: Ḥaggai.</i> in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: He who saw rain falling and said Κύριε πόλυ βρέξον<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">O Lord, make it rain a lot (Musaphia). The invocation of the Lord is considered a violation of the Third Commandment. It is possible to read the first word as a contraction Κῦρι. The statement shows that Κύριος was accepted as a Divine Name.</i> is flogged for a vain oath. Rebbi Onias, Rebbi Jacob ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Naḥman: 24 city councils<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βουλή.</i> were in Judea and they all were destroyed because of true vain oaths, as it is written: <i>For the vain did I hit your sons</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 2:30" href="/Jeremiah.2.30"><i>Jer.</i>2:30</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “An oath that I shall eat this loaf; an oath that I shall not eat it.” The first one is a blurted oath, the second a vain oath<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since by the first oath he became obligated to eat the loaf by biblical rules, the second oath has a similar status as an oath to violate a biblical commandment.</i>. If he ate it he violated a vain oath, if he did not eat it he violated a blurted oath.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A blurted oath,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">There is a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.9"><i>Nedarim</i>3:2, Notes 74–78</a>, which is not an exact copy. While repeated vows are separate obligations, of repeated identical oaths only the first is valid; the others are vain oaths. There is no notion of a vain vow.</i>“ ‘An oath that I shall not eat this loaf, an oath that I shall not eat it’, he is liable only once”<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">He made three oaths but the second and third are void since they forbid what already is forbidden.</i>. Something which would be forbidden for vows and is permitted for oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">It is not really permitted for oaths since the second oath is vain, but the second never has the status of a valid oath.</i>. Not on this was it said but on the following: “Whether matters of himself<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">All the rules of liability for a variable value sacrifice spelled out in the preceding Mishnaiot are valid for all kinds of oaths irrespective of their content or meaning.</i>, or matters of others,” as Rebbi Abba said in the name of Samuel: ‘An oath that X gave a mina to Y.’ If it turns out that he had not given, since it is not in his hand for the future it is not in his hand for the past<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">He never could swear that X will give Y since it is not in his power to force X to give. This is all about liability for a variable sacrifice, not monetary liabilities. Since witnesses do not swear, this is not a case of perjury.</i>. It was found stated on both cases: This is more stringent for the past than for the future: If he says “I shall not eat, I shall not eat,” he is liable only once. “I did not eat, I did not eat,” he is liable for each single time<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case, the first oath creates a prohibition; no oath can be made concerning an existing prohibition. In the second case, a false statement is reinforced by an oath; there is no upper limit on the number of lying oaths a person might make.</i>.
“An oath that I shall eat this loaf; an oath that I shall not eat it.” The first one is a blurted oath, the second a vain oath<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since by the first oath he became obligated to eat the loaf by biblical rules, the second oath has a similar status as an oath to violate a biblical commandment.</i>. How does one deal with him? One tells him to eat; it is better to break a vain oath than a blurted one<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of Rosh (#24) is: It is better to break a vain oath than blurted and vain ones. The two oaths have completely different status: Since the second one is vain at the moment it is made, the making of the vow is sinful and its maker is liable to be flogged. The only active oath is the first and this one has to be kept.</i>.
“An oath that I shall not eat this loaf; an oath that I shall eat it.” The first one is a blurted oath, the second a vain oath. How does one deal with him? One tells him not to eat; it is better to break a vain oath than to break a vain and a blurted one.
“An oath that I shall eat this loaf today; an oath that I shall not eat it today.” Then he ate it. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he kept the first oath and invalidated the second<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joḥanan, the valid first oath prohibits the making of the second which automatically becomes a vain oath subject to the statutory punishment for vain oaths but irrelevant as oath. R. Simeon ben Laqish notes that the first oath contains a promise, not a prohibition. Therefore, there is no rule which prohibits making the second oath. Making the second oath is sinful because it invalidates the first but from the point of view of the law of oaths it is possible. Since he becomes liable for punishment for breaking the first oath, the second is active and should be kept. By eating the loaf, the person commits a second sin.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 28b" href="/Shevuot.28b">Babli, 28b</a>, has a completely different taking on this problem and refers it to the problem whether a criminal warning is possible if it is not clear that a criminal act was intended (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.7.2">Note 104</a>).</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he invalidated the first one and did not keep the second.
“An oath that I shall not eat this loaf today; an oath that I shall eat it today.” Then he ate it. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he invalidated the first oath and kept the second<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Swearing the second oath was a criminal act. Since the second is not a prohibition, it can be fulfilled.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he invalidated the first one. One tells him to keep the second with another loaf<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Since he said אֹכְלֶנָּה “I shall eat it” and not אֹכַל כִּכָּר זוֹ “I shall eat this loaf” it is better to interpret <i>it</i> as not referring to the same loaf as does the first oath. Then while nothing can be done to mitigate the sin of breaking the first oath, the second oath can be changed from a vain oath to a new, valid oath referring to something different, not connected with the first.</i>.
“An oath that I shall eat this loaf today; an oath that I shall eat it today.” Then he ate it. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he kept both of them. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he kept the first one and one tells him to keep the second with another loaf<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">This is a situation similar to the preceding one. For R. Joḥanan, the second oath is vain, subject to punishment as such, but kept by keeping the first. For R. Simeon ben Laqish the second oath is not vain if <i>it</i> is interpreted as referring to another loaf; both oaths can be kept without sin.</i>.
“An oath that I shall not eat this loaf today; an oath that I shall not eat it today.” Then he ate it. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion he is twice liable<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Once for breaking the oath, and once for uttering a vain oath.</i>. Is he only once liable in Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">By referring the second oath to an indefinite other loaf.</i>? Even Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees that he only emphasizes the prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, R. Simeon ben Laqish might agree that the repetition indicates reinforcement and that any reasonable interpretation must refer both oaths to the same loaf, agreeing in this case with R. Joḥanan.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A blurted oath applies to men and women, relatives and unrelated persons, qualified and disqualified persons, before a court or out of court, from his own mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">This list is quite unnecessary here, it would have been sufficient to say that the rules of blurted oaths apply to every competent adult. The details are listed in contrast to the rules for witnesses’ oaths which are restricted to unrelated qualified males made to swear by others. Excluded relatives are enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:6:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.6.2-10"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:7</a>, disqualified persons in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:5:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.5.2-16"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:6</a>.</i>. For intentional violation one is liable to flogging, for unintentional [to bring] a variable sacrifice.<br>A vain oath applies to men and women, to relatives and unrelated persons, to qualified and disqualified ones, in court and out of court, and by his own words<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">This list is quite unnecessary here, it would have been sufficient to say that the rules of blurted oaths apply to every competent adult. The details are listed in contrast to the rules for witnesses’ oaths which are restricted to unrelated qualified males made to swear by others. Excluded relatives are enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:6:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.6.2-10"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:7</a>, disqualified persons in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:5:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.5.2-16"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:6</a>.</i>. For intentional violation one is liable to flogging, for unintentional he is not prosecutable<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The Third Commandment contains no determination of the punishment. Therefore it can be prosecuted as a standard infraction, punishable by flogging only after due warning and swearing in the presence of two eye witnesses.</i>. In both cases<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Blurted and vain oaths.</i>, one who is sworn to by another person can be liable. How is this? One said, “I did not eat today, I did not put on phylacteries today.” “I want you to swear to it,” if he said “Amen” he<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The person who is challenged to swear. By answering Amen he has sworn even though he himself did not utter any oath nor invoke the Divine Name.</i> is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A vain oath,” etc. <i>A person, a person</i>. Since by <i>a person</i> which was said for an oath of testimony He made the one at whom the oath was directed like one swearing, so also by <i>a person</i> which was said for an oath of deposit He made the one at whom the oath was directed like one swearing<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">The text here can be understood by a comparison with Halakhah 4:8. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a> starts: <i>But a person who would sin</i>. V. 21 stars: <i>A person who would sin</i>. The topic of v. 1 is a person whom another person makes swear indirectly (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.10.1">Note 164</a>) that he will testify in court on his behalf but who then reneges on his promise, thereby breaking his oath. V. 21 describes a person <i>who commits sacrilege towards the Eternal by lying to his fellow man about a deposit, or a partnership, or robbery.</i> One commits sacrilege by swearing falsely. The parallel language implies that swearing falsely about money matters does not necessarily mean to utter a formal oath. The sin of swearing falsely also has been committed if the injured party says to the accused in the presence of witnesses, “swear to me that you do not owe me” and the person then answers “Amen”.</i>.
This is the principle<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 21a" href="/Shevuot.21a">Babli 21a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 16a" href="/Makkot.16a"><i>Makkot</i>16a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 3a" href="/Temurah.3a"><i>Temurah</i>3a</a>. In the Babli the formulation of this (undisputed) principle is attributed to R. Yose the Galilean.</i>: One flogs for any prohibition involving an action, but one does not flog if there is no action except for one who substitutes<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Before an animal can be sacrificed, it has to be sanctified by dedication (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9" href="/Leviticus.27.9"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9</a>). Once sanctified, it is forbidden to substitute another animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10">v. 10</a>). If somebody would substitute, both the original and the substitute are dedicated. Therefore the oral declaration of substitution is at the same time the real act of sanctification which makes the animal prohibited for all profane use. This argument is not found in the Babli.</i>, or swears, or curses a fellow man by the Name. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This does not include one who substitutes; substituting implies speech and action<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Before an animal can be sacrificed, it has to be sanctified by dedication (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9" href="/Leviticus.27.9"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9</a>). Once sanctified, it is forbidden to substitute another animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10">v. 10</a>). If somebody would substitute, both the original and the substitute are dedicated. Therefore the oral declaration of substitution is at the same time the real act of sanctification which makes the animal prohibited for all profane use. This argument is not found in the Babli.</i>. From where one who swears falsely? Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: <i>For the Eternal will not cleanse</i>; but the judges will cleanse him<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:7" href="/Exodus.20.7"><i>Ex.</i>20:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:11" href="/Deuteronomy.5.11"><i>Deut</i>. 5:11</a>. As the Babli explains, if the verse simply had said “he will not be cleansed”, then a vain or false oath would be an unpardonable sin. But since it said, <i>the Eternal will not cleanse</i>, it implies that punishment by the earthly court will remove the sin from the Heavenly ledger.</i>. From where one who cursed his fellow man by the Name? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: <i>To fear the Name</i><sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:58" href="/Deuteronomy.28.58"><i>Deut</i>. 28:58</a>. While the language is that of a positive commandment, the context shows that its violation is punishable.</i>, etc. The one who swears falsely in the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish from where? Since he swore falsely, he does not fear. The one who cursed his fellow man by the Name in the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish from where? Since he cursed, he does not fear. What is the difference between them? If one swore falsely and cursed a fellow man by the Name. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion he is liable for two [punishments]<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Since the two offenses violate two different prohibitions. But for R. Simeon ben Laqish they are repeated violation of the same law, punishable only once.</i>. In Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion he is only liable for one.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An oath about testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a> requires a variable value sacrifice by a person who <i>heard an imprecation when he had knowledge</i> and refuses to testify. This is read to mean that a person is approached by a party in a civil suit and asked to testify in their behalf. If then either he swears an oath that he will testify in court (“by his own word”) or the party asks him to swear that he will appear (“by the word of others”) while he answers “Amen” but does not utter an oath by himself, he becomes liable for the sacrifice if he reneges on his commitment.</i> applies to men but not to women<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since women are not admitted as formal witnesses in court, the rule of an oath of testimony cannot apply to them.</i>, to unrelated persons but not to relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since relatives are barred from appearing as witnesses in court, the rule of an oath of testimony cannot apply to them.</i>, to qualified but not to disqualified ones<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Felons are not permitted to appear as witnesses in court; the rule of an oath of testimony cannot apply to them.</i>; it applies only to those admitted to testify<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Even if their disability only is a rabbinic tradition they will not be heard and the rule of an oath of testimony cannot apply to them.</i>, in court and out of court, and by the person’s own words. By the words of others they only become liable if they renege before a court, the words of Rebbi Meїr. But the Sages say, whether by the person’s own words or by the words of others they only become liable if they renege before a court<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since testimony is used only in court, a refusal to testify outside of court is irrelevant and cannot trigger liability.</i>.<br>They are liable both for intentional [violation of the] oath and for erroneous one<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If he swore falsely that he did not know testimony but did not know that this makes him liable for a sacrifice.</i>, and for intentional [refusal of] testimony, but one is not liable unintentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If honestly he was erroneously thinking that he did not know testimony.</i>. What is one liable for if intentional? A variable value sacrifice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An oath about testimony,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This text essentially is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.2-8"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:10, Notes 150–165</a>, with a related text in <i>Yoma</i> 6:1. The many parallels in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 30a" href="/Shevuot.30a">Babli (30a</a>,b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 62b" href="/Yoma.62b"><i>Yoma</i> 62b</a>), Tosephta, and halakhic Midrashim are indicated in Sanhedrin.</i><i>Rams</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:5" href="/Leviticus.16.5"><i>Lev</i>. 16:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:7" href="/Leviticus.16.7">7</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:8" href="/Leviticus.16.8">8</a>. Any indeterminate plural means 2, the minimum of many.</i>, the minimum of rams are two. Why does the verse say <i>two</i>? That they be equal.
<i>Sheep</i>, the minimum of sheep are two. Then why does the verse say <i>two</i>? That both be equal<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 27:38, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:3" href="/Numbers.28.3"><i>Num</i>. 28:3</a>.</i>.
The minimum of <i>birds</i> are two. Then why does the verse say <i>two</i>? that both be equal<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:4" href="/Leviticus.14.4"><i>Lev</i>. 14:4</a>.</i>.
The minimum of <i>trumpets</i> are two. Then why does the verse say <i>two</i>? that both be equal<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:1" href="/Numbers.10.1"><i>Num</i>. 10:1</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥaggai objected to Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct reading, also given in <i>Yoma</i>, not R. Yasa as in <i>Sanhedrin</i>.</i>. Is there not written: <i>The two men shall stand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:17" href="/Deuteronomy.19.17"><i>Deut</i>. 19:17</a>.</i>? Now, is not two the minimum of “men”? Why does the verse say <i>two</i>? That both be equal? But it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:17" href="/Deuteronomy.24.17"><i>Deut</i>. 24:17</a>.</i>: <i>Do not bend the lawsuit of the proselyte</i>, <i>the orphan</i>, … That means that a proselyte can have a lawsuit against one who is not a proselyte, an orphan may have a lawsuit against one who is not an orphan, a widow against a married woman. Then why is there written <i>two</i>? It is free to be combined and to infer from it an <i>equal cut</i>. It is said here <i>two</i> and it is said there <i>two men were left</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 11:26" href="/Numbers.11.26"><i>Num</i>. 11:26.74</a></i>. Since there one speaks of men but not women nor underaged, also here men but not women nor underaged. From this we learn that a woman may not be a judge; consequently a woman may not be a witness.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Huna in the name of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Here it seems better to read “Rav Joseph” with the other two sources.</i>. It is said here <i>two</i> and it is said there<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut</i>. 19:15</a>.</i> <i>by the mouth of two witnesses</i>. Since there it must be by the testimony of two witnesses, also here by the testimony of two witnesses. Then why does the verse say <i>two</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:17" href="/Deuteronomy.19.17"><i>Deut</i>. 19:17</a>.</i>? Lest one of them be standing while the other be sitting; one says everything he has to say, but to the other one says, make your statement short. With one he puts up, to the other he is unfriendly.
Rebbi Jehudah said, I heard that if the judge wants to let both of them sit, he may tell them to sit down. What is forbidden is that not one be standing and the other sitting; one says everything he has to say, but to the other one says, make your statement short. Rebbi Ismael says, one says to him, either you dress as he is dressed or pay him to be dressed as you are.
Rebbi Abba said in the name of Rav Huna: The witnesses have to stand while testifying. What is the reason? <i>The two men shall stand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:17" href="/Deuteronomy.19.17"><i>Deut</i>. 19:17</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: Also the parties have to stand at the moment the verdict is given. What is the reason?: <i>Who have the quarrel before the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:17" href="/Deuteronomy.19.17"><i>Deut</i>. 19:17</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs also have an almost identical copy in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:9-21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.9-21"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:10, Notes 166–200</a>. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 27b" href="/Sanhedrin.27b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 27b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 28a" href="/Sanhedrin.28a">28a</a>).</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:16" href="/Deuteronomy.24.16"><i>Deut</i>. 24:16</a>.</i>: <i>Fathers shall not be killed because of sons</i>. Is it not already written, <i>each one should be killed for his own crime</i>? Why does the verse say, <i>fathers shall not be killed because of sons</i>? Fathers shall not be killed on the testimony of sons, and sons shall not be killed on testimony of fathers. From here that witnesses shall not be relatives of the accused.
From where that witnesses may not be relatives of one another? Think of it, if they be found perjured, would each of them not be killed by the other’s testimony? From where that witnesses may not be relatives of the judges? Think of it, if one of them be found perjured, he could not be killed unless the other also was found perjured. If you say so, would he not be killed by the other’s sentencing?
From where that judges may not be relatives of one another? The Torah said, kill on the testimony of witnesses, kill on the sentence of judges. Since witnesses may not be relatives of one another, neither may judges be relatives of one another.
So far only fathers and sons; from where the other relatives? Rebbi Ze`ira says, <i>and sons</i> includes the remaining relatives.
So far according to Rebbi Aqiba.
From where following Rebbi Ismael? Rebbi Ismael stated: <i>The congregation shall judge between the beater</i>, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:24" href="/Numbers.35.24"><i>Num.</i>35:24</a>; <i>between the slayer and the avenger of the blood</i>.</i>. The congregation be neither relatives of the murderer nor relatives of the murdered. Rebbi Yose said, otherwise you would say that the court is engaged in vendetta. This implies that the judges may not be related to the accused. And from where that the witnesses may not be related to the accused? The Torah said, kill on the testimony of witnesses, kill on the sentence of those who vote<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the judges; the expression is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:2" href="/Exodus.23.2"><i>Ex</i>. 23:2</a>.</i>. Since judges may not be related to the accused, neither may witnesses be related to the accused. From where that witnesses may not be relatives of one another? Think of it, if they be found perjured, would they not be killed by each other’s testimony?
From where that witnesses may not be relatives of the judges? Think of it, if one of them be found perjured, he could not be killed unless the other also was found perjured. If you say so, would he not be killed by his sentence?
“To qualified but not to disqualified ones.” For it is said, <i>if he does not tell</i>, <i>he has to bear his punishment</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21a</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a></i>. If he told, the other would have to pay money. This excludes one where the other would not have to pay money even if he told.
“Before the court.” To exclude a single witness. If they told him that they would accept his word as if there were two witnesses, from where? The verse says, <i>if he was a witness</i>, <i>had seen or known;</i> <i>if he does not tell he shall bear his punishment</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21a</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a></i>. One who is qualified to testify according to biblical standards; this excludes a single witness who is not qualified to testify.
“Outside of court.” <i>If he does not tell</i>, <i>he has to bear his punishment</i>. If he told, one would have to pay money. This excludes outside of court where the other would not have to pay money even if he told.
From where two witnesses? <i>And he is a witness</i>, this makes two. Following Rebbi Ismael, who said, any place where the Torah mentions a witness without further determination it implies two witnesses; unless the verse informs you that a single witness is meant. It was found stated in the name of Rebbi Ismael: “Two witnesses.” Can a single witness be found guilty of a blurted oath? Since it is possible to say that one person could team up with him, then he would be subject to the oath of testimony, you could find him liable for an oath of testimony. How could you find him guilty of a blurted oath?
Should a relative be found guilty of a blurted oath? Does it follow what Rebbi Abba said in the name of Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.9.2">Chapter 3:6, Note 75</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 49b" href="/Shevuot.49b">Babli 49b</a>.</i>: “An oath that X gave to Y,” and it turns out that X had not given; since there is nothing in the future there is nothing in the past. Or the following: “Where is my ox?” He responded, “I do not know what you are referring to.” Rebbi declares him not liable for a keeper’s oath but liable because of a blurted oath. Rebbi Joḥanan said, since it is a religious duty to appease him, he is not liable because of a blurted oath. In the rabbis’ opinion, is there no religious duty to appease him? One appeases with truthful statements, not with lies.
Rebbi Ismael stated: <i>He has to bear his punishment</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the judges; the expression is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:2" href="/Exodus.23.2"><i>Ex</i>. 23:2</a>.</i>, a sacrifice. From where that one needs a court? “Telling, telling”. Since <i>telling</i> mentioned there is before a court, also telling here is before a court.
<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This is a careless copy of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.10"><i>Soṭah</i> 1:1 Notes 56–71</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:4:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.4.7"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:4 Note 87</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.21"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:10 Note 197</a>.</i> Or like the following. “One accepts the witnesses’ testimony only if they saw it together. Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥa says, even if they saw it one after the other.” Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac in the name of Rav Isaac who said in the name of Rav: The Sages agree with Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥa with regard to witnesses of firstlings and witnesses of squatters’ rights. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: the same holds for testimony regarding signs. In that case, it is obvious if one says, I saw two hairs on his back<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">There is a sentence missing: “the other says, I saw two hairs on his side. If”.</i> and one says, I saw one hair on his back and the other says, I saw one hair on his belly, that is nothing; so much more his back and his back<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">It should read: “back and side”.</i>. Two are saying, we saw one hair on his back; and one is saying, I saw one hair on his belly. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun and Rebbi Hoshaia ben Rebbi Shammai, one said, it is invalid, but the other said, it is valid. He who says it is invalid considers him as one who testifies to half a sign. He who says it is valid? I say, maybe it was rubbed off. One says, I saw two hairs on his back; and one says, I saw two on his belly. Rebbi Abba said, everybody agrees that this is valid. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, everybody agrees that this is invalid [testimony]. Rebbi Yose says, this is in disagreement. Rebbi Yudan said, this is in disagreement. Rebbi Yose said to Rebbi Ḥaggai, does not Rebbi Yudan follow my opinion? He answered, I am disagreeing with his teacher, so much more with him. Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Ḥaggai was correct. If a document was signed by four seals, if one person (permitted)<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This clearly is corrupt. In both cases, read: “testified to”.</i> two, and another (questioned)<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This clearly is corrupt. In both cases, read: “testified to”.</i> the other two, and the document was attacked, is that worth anything? Does not every single signature need two witnesses? And here, every single sign needs two witnesses. Rebbi Ḥanina learns it from the years of squatting rights. If one [witness] testified that he ate from the property the first, second, and third years and another testified that he ate it the fourth and fifth years, is that worth anything? Does not every single year need two witnesses? And here, every single sign needs two witnesses.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is an oath about testimony? One said to witnesses, come and testify for me. “An oath that we do not know testimony for you;” or they said to him, we do not know any testimony for you, “I am asking you to take an oath upon this;” if they said “Amen”, they are liable<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a> requires a variable value sacrifice by a person who <i>heard an imprecation when he had knowledge</i> and refuses to testify. This is read to mean that a person is approached by a party in a civil suit and asked to testify in their behalf. If then either he swears an oath that he will testify in court (“by his own word”) or the party asks him to swear that he will appear (“by the word of others”) while he answers “Amen” but does not utter an oath by himself, he becomes liable for the sacrifice if he reneges on his commitment.</i>. If he asked them five times outside of court to take an oath; when they came to court and admitted it they are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since refusal of testimony outside of court is irrelevant (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.1.1">Note 6</a>).</i>. If they deny, they are liable for each single one. If he asked them five times in court to take an oath and the refused, they are liable only once. Rebbi Simeon said, what is the reason? Because they cannot come back and admit<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since the courts operate on the principle that a witness can testify only once, i. e., he cannot change his testimony, after a first refusal in court the witness would not be permitted to change his statement. The additional oaths put on the witnesses are pointless; the court should prohibit them. In the language of the Babli, כֵּיוָן שֶׁהִגִּיד אֵינוֹ חוֹזֵר וּמַגִּיד “after he told, he does not return to tell”.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What is an oath about testimony,” etc. <i>A person</i>, <i>a person</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">A comparison of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a> about oaths concerning testimony and 5:21 about the person accused of larceny who swears falsely in purgation by oath; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.10.2">Chapter 3:12, Note 165</a>. The parallel use of identical terms is an <i>equal cut</i> which allows transfer of rules from one occurrence to the other.</i>. Since there it is from his own words, also here from his own words. Since here it is from others’ words, also there it is from others’ words<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In v. 5:1 the potential witness <i>hears the sound of an imprecation</i>; others formulate the oath to which he is asked to assent. In 5:21 he himself formulates the oath <i>to deny a deposit</i>, <i>or a loan</i>, <i>or robbery</i>. The <i>equal cut</i> allows one to transfer one situation to the other; oaths about testimony may be formulated by the potential witness himself; oaths of a person accused of larceny may be formulated by the aggrieved party.</i>. Rebbi Meїr explains the equal cut which was said here: Since there it is said about his own words, so also here about his own words. Also the rabbis explain the equal cut which was said here. Since others’ words said there are in court, also others’ words said here are in court<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph refers to the disagreement between R. Meїr and the rabbis in Mishnah 1.<br>The formulation here presupposes that one knows what was explained elsewhere about interpretation of equal cuts [<i>Yebamot</i>11:1 Notes 30,34 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 9:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.9.1.2-16"><i>Sanhedrin</i>9:1</a>); Chapter 5:2]. R. Meїr holds that the laws to be transferred are what can be read off the corresponding verses; the rabbis transfer laws only in the context of the verses on both sides.<br>Verse 5:21 reads, <i>to deny a deposit</i> … <i>and he swears to a lie</i>; it is understood that this creates guilt whether or not it was before a court. But testimony is before a court; therefore v. 5:1 only speaks of guilt incurred in a court trial. R. Meїr holds that an oath formulated by another person following the situation of v. 1 transferred to v. 21 cannot create liability for a sacrifice outside of court, but an oath pronounced by himself (v. 21) always creates such a liability. The rabbis hold that while we accept that the equal cut shows that oaths formulated by himself are covered by v. 1, the context of that verse forces the conclusion that one refers to court proceedings only.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Is a relative liable for an oath about testimony formulated by himself? Can we hear it from the following: since they inferred formulation by himself for an oath about testimony only from oaths about deposits, and since there relatives are liable, so they also are here<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This question makes sense only according to R. Meїr who disregards context. Relatives are barred as witnesses and judges; they are not barred as claimants. If somebody embezzled the property of a relative, that relative can go to court. Therefore, oaths about deposits can be sworn to relatives. Would R. Meїr agree that oaths about testimony, even though they cannot be enforced in court, create liabilities for sacrifices?</i>? Rebbi Yose said, can relatives here be inferred from relatives there<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even R. Meїr will agree that an equal cut allows one to transfer only rules that make sense in the new context. Since oaths about testimony are void among relatives, they may create liabilities for “vain” oaths but no liability for variable sacrifices.</i>?
Rebbi Yudan the Cappadocian asked: He made him swear five times by his own formulation before the court: would he not be liable for each instance separately? Rebbi Yose said, is this not the Mishnah: “Rebbi Simeon said, what is the reason? Because they cannot come back and admit<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since the courts operate on the principle that a witness can testify only once, i. e., he cannot change his testimony, after a first refusal in court the witness would not be permitted to change his statement. The additional oaths put on the witnesses are pointless; the court should prohibit them. In the language of the Babli, כֵּיוָן שֶׁהִגִּיד אֵינוֹ חוֹזֵר וּמַגִּיד “after he told, he does not return to tell”.</i>?” But here, since they can come back and admit, they are liable for each single instance<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is his own oath, it is not testimony and not under the rule that nobody can testify twice in the same case.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he made him swear five times in his own words and let him swear five times by the mouth of others, what<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Again this is a question following R. Meїr. It is clear that the demand that they answer to an oath formulated by the person who wants to force testimony must be made in court. Therefore, one has to assume that the first five oaths were made outside of court. Then they are not subject to the rule that there can be only one testimony. The next question is about the status of an oath of denial of knowledge before the other even had asked for their testimony.</i>? Since Rebbi Meїr considered “in his own words” as if in court, would they determine the sacrifice on the first occasion, even without request? It should come like the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.2.5">Tosephta 2:11</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 31b" href="/Shevuot.31b">Babli 31b</a>). The <i>baraita</i> is formulated independently from the Babylonian Tosephta.</i> “Why do you go after us? An oath that we do not know any testimony relevant for you. Should they be liable? The verse says, <i>and he heard the voice of an imprecation</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>.</i>. Only one who hears an imprecation<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta states clearly, “they are not liable unless he requested [the testimony]”.</i>. This excludes those who did not hear a voice.” Would he be liable from his own mouth for real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">All examples in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-22" href="/Leviticus.5.21-22"><i>Lev</i>. 5:21–22</a> (a deposit, a loan, extortion and robbery, a find) refer to monetary claims about movables. Since there can be no sacrifice for an oath about deposits relating to real estate, one might argue that there can be no sacrifice for an oath about testimony involving real estate, asserted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a>.</i>? From his own mouth would he be liable for fines<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">These fines are biblically imposed for misdeeds. Whether there can be an oath about these is in dispute between the majority and R. Simeon, Mishnah 5:6.</i>? From his own mouth would he be liable for a variable value sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">From the equal cut (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.2.2">Note 32</a>) we know that for a false oath regarding testimony formulated by the potential witness he is liable for a sacrifice. Is that the variable value sacrifice for a false oath regarding testimony or a fixed value sacrifice required for a false testimony about deposits?</i>?
Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: in error, a sacrifice; intentional, a sacrifice. But if he said, I was of the opinion that this oath does not exist, he is free<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to Mishnah 2. The only case in which one is not liable is if he was of the opinion that swearing to evade appearing as a witness was not forbidden. Then his action is without criminal intent.</i>. Ḥizqiah stated: At any place where the Torah mentions <i>sin</i> without attribute you include intentional equally with unintentional unless Scripture inform you that it is unintentional<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases of purification sacrifices mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev</i>. 4</a> it is emphasized that these apply only to atone for חֵטְא בִשְׁגָגָה “sins in error.” In most cases of reparation sacrifices described in Chapter 5, there is no mention of error. It follows that the word חֵטְא alone covers both intentional and unintentional sins.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If both of them disavowed simultaneously, both of them are liable; one after the other, the first is liable but the second is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">If there are only two witnesses and one refuses to testify even though he had assented to an oath that he would testify, the second witness becomes a single witness whose testimony cannot compel the defendant in a civil suit to pay money. All the testimony of a single witness in a civil suit can do is force the defendant to swear that he does not owe money. Since the refusal of the second witness to testify does not inflict a monetary loss on the claimant, he is not liable for a sacrifice unless he disavowed simultaneously with the first.</i>. If one reneged but one confessed, the one who disavowed is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The action of the first witness deprived the claimant also of the testimony of the second; this triggers liability.</i>. If there were two groups of witnesses, if the first group disavowed and after this the second, both are liable since testimony could be upheld by either one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Only two witnesses are needed for conviction; the two groups of witnesses are independent of one another and the order of their appearance is irrelevant.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If both of them disavowed simultaneously,” etc. If they dedicated a sacrifice and said, we are going to disavowe in court. This is what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, <i>his sacrifice for the Eternal for his vow of nazir</i>; that his vow of <i>nazir</i> precede his sacrifice, not that his sacrifice precede his vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:21" href="/Numbers.6.21"><i>Num</i>. 6:21</a>. An obligatory sacrifice cannot be brought voluntarily. Therefore it cannot be dedicated before the obligation exists. The dedication cannot be undone (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9" href="/Leviticus.27.9"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9</a>). Therefore, the original sacrifice has to be rededicated as voluntary offering and a new sacrifice be given as obligatory sacrifice. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.9.3"><i>Nazir</i> 2:9 (Note 118</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.2.4">3:2 (Note 32</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nazir (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Nazir_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 2:6</a>, <i>Num</i>. <i>rabba</i> 10(42).</i>. If they were sworn to out of court, dedicated a sacrifice and said, we are going to renege in court, how do you treat this? As if his sin did precede his sacrifice or since the are liable only in court as if their sacrifice did precede their sin<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered. Possibly it is a sequel to R. Jeremiah’s questions in the preceding Halakhah; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.6.5"><i>Terumot</i> 10:11 Note 110</a>.</i>?
So is the Mishnah: “If one disavowed but one confessed, the one who disavowed is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The question is why does the Mishnah speak of “one” and not “first” or “second”? The sentence must refer to the first clause in the Mishnah, that both disavowed together but then one of them changed his mind.</i>” Rebbi Yose said, our Mishnah if the second changed his opinion immediately after the speaking of the first. Because if the first changed his opinion, one would accept him; then the second would become the first<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">“Immediately” is defined either as the time needed to say “peace upon you” or “peace upon you, my master” [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.1.15"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:1 (Notes 50–52</a>), <i>Mo</i>`<i>ed qaṭan</i> 3:7 (83c l. 37), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.1.2"><i>Nazir</i> 4:1 Note 12</a>]. Anything said immediately following testimony is part of the testimony. The rule that a witness cannot change his testimony does not apply to corrections made immediately. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 32a" href="/Shevuot.32a">Babli 32a</a>.</i>.
If they were ten<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Ten witnesses asked as a group to testify.</i>. There are Tannaïm who state, the first one is liable and all the rest are not liable. There are Tannaïm who state, the last one is not liable and all the rest are liable. The one who said, the first one is liable and all the rest are not liable, holds with him who said, the testimony can be verified by the remaining [witnesses]. The one who said, the last one is not liable and all the rest are liable, holds with him who said, testimony that was partially invalidated is totally invalidated<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Both opinions are expressed in Mishnah <i>Makkot</i> 1:12–13 (in most Mishnah editions, Mishnah 1:8). It is obvious that the attributions have to be switched. If in civil suits any testimony supported by 2 witnesses is acceptable then only the last, single, remaining witness is not liable (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.3.1">Note 46</a>). But if the testimony of a group is only acceptable if all witnesses are qualified and testify in parallel then the first disqualification disqualifies the group and only the first witness is liable.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says so: “if there were two groups of witnesses.” He only said “two”. Therefore if there had been one<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation of the Mishnah is “two groups of two witnesses” and not “four witnesses”.</i>, testimony that was partially invalidated it is totally invalidated.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you that you should come and testify for me that I have a claim against X for a deposit, and a loan, and robbery, and a lost object.” “An oath that we do not know testimony for you;” they are liable only once. “An oath that we do not know that you have a claim against X for a deposit, and a common venture, and robbery, and a lost object;” they are liable for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since each single item mentioned would need separate testimony, the detailed oath counts as a separate oath for each detail. The list is taken from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-22" href="/Leviticus.5.21-22"><i>Lev</i>. 5:21–22</a>.</i>.<br>“I am putting an oath on you that you should come and testify for me that I have a claim against X for a deposit of wheat, and barley, and spelt.” “An oath that we do not know testimony for you;” they are liable only once. “An oath that we do not know that you have a claim against X for a deposit of wheat, and barley, and spelt;” they are liable for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">56,</sup><i class="footnote">Since each single item mentioned would need separate testimony, the detailed oath counts as a separate oath for each detail. The list is taken from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-22" href="/Leviticus.5.21-22"><i>Lev</i>. 5:21–22</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since a claim to money’s worth is equal to a claim to money.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you that you should come and testify for me,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <i>Sifra</i> <i>Ḥova</i> <i>(Wayyiqra 2) Parashah</i> 8(8–10).</i> From where that this only refers to monetary claims? Rebbi Eliezer said, it uses here “or” and it uses “or” with a deposit<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>, “or” is used twice, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-22" href="/Leviticus.5.21-22">vv. 21–22</a> four times.</i>. Since the “or” used with a deposit only refers to monetary claims, also the “or” used here only refers to monetary claims. The “or” of the homicide will disprove<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:22-23" href="/Numbers.35.22-23"><i>Num</i>. 35:22–23</a>, in the description of accidental homicide, “or” is used twice.</i> since they do not refer to monetary claims. One argues about “or” accompanied by an oath from “or” accompanied by an oath; the “or” of the homicide cannot disprove since they are not accompanied by an oath. The “or” of the deviant woman will disprove<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:14" href="/Numbers.5.14"><i>Num</i>. 5:14</a>, the presumption of innocence of the deviant woman is introduced by “or”. The imprecation is not the woman’s but the Cohen’s, v. 19.</i> since they are accompanied by an oath and do not refer to monetary claims. One argues about “or” accompanied by an oath not accompanied by a Cohen from similar “or”; the “or” of the deviant woman cannot disprove since they are accompanied by a Cohen. The “or” of blurting lips will disprove<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>, “or” is used twice.</i> since they do not refer to monetary claims. One argues about “or” where He made intent equal to error<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding Chapters, blurted oaths create a liability for a sacrifice only if they were broken in a period of forgetting, i. e., unintention- ally. There is no mention of unintentional sin for liability in cases of oath about testimony or monetary damages.</i> from similar “or”; the “or” of blurting lips cannot disprove since there He did not make intent equal to error. Rebbi Aqiba says, for some of these one is liable, for some one is not liable. For monetary claims one is liable; for non-monetary claims one is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:5" href="/Leviticus.5.5"><i>Lev</i>. 5:5</a>: <i>It shall be if he causes damage by</i> some <i>of these</i>; some will require a sacrifice but not others. The decision what to include is left to the religious authorities guided by the hermeneutical principle of “equal cut”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 17(1).</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, He made liable here and he made liable for a deposit. Since deposits only refer to monetary claims, so here also it only refers to monetary claims<sup class="footnote-marker">41,</sup><i class="footnote">All examples in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-22" href="/Leviticus.5.21-22"><i>Lev</i>. 5:21–22</a> (a deposit, a loan, extortion and robbery, a find) refer to monetary claims about movables. Since there can be no sacrifice for an oath about deposits relating to real estate, one might argue that there can be no sacrifice for an oath about testimony involving real estate, asserted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 17(2).</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you that you should come and testify for me that X owes me full damages or half damages<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">For damage caused by another’s animals to the claimant’s animals, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35-36" href="/Exodus.21.35-36"><i>Ex</i>. 21:35–36</a>.</i>, double restitution or quadruple and quintuple restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment of the thief, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:3" href="/Exodus.22.3"><i>Ex</i>. 22:3</a>; 21:37.</i>, that X raped or seduced my daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical fines for seducing a virgin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:16" href="/Exodus.22.16"><i>Ex</i>. 22:16</a>) or raping her (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:29" href="/Deuteronomy.22.29"><i>Deut</i>. 22:29</a>).</i>, or that my son hit me<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If the son hit his parents without causing a wound, they can sue him for damages; it is a monetary claim. But if he injured them it is a capital crime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:15" href="/Exodus.21.15"><i>Ex</i>. 21:15</a>).</i>, or that my neighbor injured me<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:18" href="/Exodus.21.18"><i>Ex</i>. 21:18</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.8.1.1">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i> 8:1</a>.</i>, or that he set fire to my grain stack on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is a deadly sin punishable by extirpation, the desecration is not a case for the earthly court and has no influence on a possible damage suit. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.5.4"><i>Giṭtin</i> 5:4 Notes 138–139</a>.</i>;” these are liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 17(1).</i> It was stated: Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, why does the verse say, <i>and he is a witness</i>, <i>or saw</i>, <i>or knew</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>.</i>, etc.? I said this only for testimony which may be accepted about knowing without seeing, or seeing without knowing. <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 33b" href="/Shevuot.33b">Babli 33b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.2.3">Tosephta 2:5</a>.</i> How is knowing without seeing in monetary claims? “Give me my 200 <i>zuz</i> which you are holding.” “I do not have anything of yours.” “But did you not confess before X and Y”? “They should testify and I shall pay.” This refers to knowing without seeing. They came and said, whether he confessed to him or whether he robbed him, we do not know. Whether he gave him a loan we do not know<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses came and testified that they saw a transaction but they have no knowledge about the kind of transaction it was. Had the debtor not said that he would pay if the witnesses came, that testimony would be worthless, but now he has to pay. The Babli assumes that the testimony is without a disclaimer.</i>. How is seeing without knowing in monetary claims? “Give me my 200 <i>zuz</i> which you are holding.” “I do not have anything of yours.” “But did I not count them for you in a meeting with X and Y”? “They should testify and I shall pay.” This refers to seeing without knowing. They came and said, whether he counted for him or whether he robbed him, we do not know. Whether he took a loan from him, we do not know. “Give me the fine for my daughter which you owe me<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical fines for seducing a virgin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:16" href="/Exodus.22.16"><i>Ex</i>. 22:16</a>) or raping her (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:29" href="/Deuteronomy.22.29"><i>Deut</i>. 22:29</a>).</i>.” But he says, “I never in my life was found liable for a fine.” Witnesses testify that he was found liable for a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is found in court to be a liar he is barred from swearing to clear himself from the claim. Therefore even a weak testimony will buttress the claim against him.</i>, but we do not know whether the fine was for his daughter or the fine for another woman. “You raped or you seduced my daughter.” But he says, “I never in my life did rape or seduce a woman.” Witnesses testify that he raped a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is found in court to be a liar he is barred from swearing to clear himself from the claim. Therefore even a weak testimony will buttress the claim against him.</i>, but we do not know whether it was his daughter or another woman. “You killed my ox and cut down my orchard.” But he says, “I do not know.” He is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, e. g., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 12b" href="/Ketubot.12b"><i>Ketubot</i> 12b</a>, בָּרִי וְשֶׁמָּא בָּרִי עָדיף “between certain and perhaps, certain is preferred.” If a claim is asserted as certain but the defense is that possibly it is false, there is no defense.</i>. “You told me to kill and cut down,” one follows the majority of orchards. What means, one follows the majority of orchards? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, if his ox was goring, he would have told him; if the orchard was barren, he would have told him<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 91b" href="/Bava_Kamma.91b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 91b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, in matters of monetary claims, since he may tell him, you told me to kill and cut down, even if he told him “I did not kill, I did not cut down,” he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since the burden of proof is on the claimant.</i>.

Halakhah 6
