<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you that you should come and testify for me that I am a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the claimant may bring the suit in order to be recognized as a Cohen because he wants to receive a priest’s prebends, the suit is classified as one for status, not for money.</i>, that I am a Levite<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The same holds for a Levite who may receive tithe.</i>, that I am not the son of a divorcee, that I am not the son of a woman having received <i>ḥalîṣah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The last two cases are suits to be declared a qualified priest, not directly for monetary claims.</i>, that X raped or seduced his daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Capital crimes; the deflowered daughter cannot sue for damages since a possible death sentence precludes damage suits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:4-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.4-14"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Notes 19–70</a>).</i>, or that my son injured me<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If the son hit his parents without causing a wound, they can sue him for damages; it is a monetary claim. But if he injured them it is a capital crime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:15" href="/Exodus.21.15"><i>Ex</i>. 21:15</a>).</i>, or that my neighbor injured me or set fire to my grain stack on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">A capital crime.</i>:” these are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">All these suits are not for monetary claims; even though there may be a duty for witnesses to testify, no sacrifice is due for a refusal to obey a summons under oath in a case which never can result in monetary claims.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you,” etc. <b>Halakhah 8:</b> “I am putting an oath on you,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 35a" href="/Shevuot.35a">Babli 35a</a>, <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 11(4).</i><i>A person who would sin</i>, <i>a person who would sin</i> as an equal cut<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">A comparison of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a> about oaths concerning testimony and 5:21 about the person accused of larceny who swears falsely in purgation by oath; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.10.2">Chapter 3:12, Note 165</a>. The parallel use of identical terms is an <i>equal cut</i> which allows transfer of rules from one occurrence to the other.</i>. Since <i>a person who would sin</i> which was said further on refers to an active monetary claim<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">There is liability for a sacrifice only if the requested testimony was for the claimant in a suit under the law of obligations. This excludes testimony for the defendant or for the claimant in a suit for breach of promise.</i>, also <i>a person who would sin</i> must refer to an active monetary claim.
“I am putting an oath on you,” etc. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said, there is no money when there is flogging<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this paragraph refers to Mishnah 7 (and in the Leiden ms. Halakhah 9 precedes Halakhot 7 and 8) declaring that a claim about damage on the Day of Atonement is a monetary claim which according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.11"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1 Note 51</a> is true only for R. Joḥanan but not for R. Simeon ben Laqish. The latter holds that since a violation of the Day of Atonement in front of witnesses after due warning will expose the perpetrator to flogging, there can be no monetary claim even if there are no witnesses and the case of desecration is not one for the courts.</i>. He explains it following Rebbi Meїr who said, he is flogged and he pays<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The same explanation is given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.3"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1 Note 7</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 33b" href="/Ketubot.33b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 33b</a>.</i>. In any case he did not make him lose money. Is it not that since he wanted to give him but did not give to him; it is as if he made him lose money<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to Mishnah 9. The formulation of the Mishnah is necessary since a breach of promise could be considered inflicting monetary loss. It is necessary to state that this loss does not qualify under the rules of equal cut.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you that you should come and testify for me that Mr. X promised to give me 200 <i>zuz</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian half-sheqel, identified with the Roman denar.</i> but did not give them to me.” These are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">There is liability for a sacrifice only if the requested testimony was for the claimant in a suit under the law of obligations. This excludes testimony for the defendant or for the claimant in a suit for breach of promise.</i> since one is liable only for an (oath about)<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This is a scribal error. With all other Mishnah sources on has to read תְּבִיעַת “claim of.”</i> money similar to a deposit.<br>“I am putting an oath on you, that you shall come and testify for me when you know testimony for me.” These are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If they disobey the imprecation. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a> is quite explicit that it applies only to persons who already know the testimony.</i> since the oath preceded the testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is elliptic. The oath preceded the possibility of testimony.</i>.<br>If he stood in the synagogue and said, “I am putting an oath on you, that you shall come and testify for me if you know testimony for me.” These are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since the imprecation is not directed at any specific persons.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am putting an oath on you,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ševi`it</i> 10:9 Notes 133–134; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.7"><i>Giṭtin</i> 6:1 Note 39</a>, <i>Bava meṣi`a</i> 4:2 Note 49; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49a" href="/Bava_Metzia.49a">Babli <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>49a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 13b" href="/Bekhorot.13b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 13b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Chapter 8 7" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Chapter_8.7"><i>Sifra Qedošim Pereq</i> 8(7</a>).</i> Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody wanted to change his mind after he had promised a gift to another person, he may change his mind. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi asked before Rebbi Abbahu: Did He not say “a true yes”<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:36" href="/Leviticus.19.36"><i>Lev</i>. 19:36</a>. The pun identifies the measure הִין with the Aramaic equivalent הֵן of the Hebrew כֵּן “yes”. It shows that -ֵ was pronounced like -ִי (Itacistic η = ῖ). The verse is read as exhortation to be honest in business dealings.</i>? At the moment when he said it, it was a true yes.
Why<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah 11, that a wholesale imprecation is invalid.</i>? Because he does not know them, or because he does not intend to ask them<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Is it because the person demanding testimony does not know the identity of the person whom he is asking to testify or does he not know whom to ask to testify because he does not know who would be able to testify.</i>? What is the difference between them? If he obligated them in pairs of two. If you would say because he does not know them, he knows them. Therefore the reason only can be that he does not intend to ask them<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In the Sephardic tradition of the Mishnah, independent and in the Babli, including Maimonides’s autograph, this statement is part of the Mishnah. In the Ashkenazic tradition of the Babli, as exemplified by the Munich ms., the Mishnah is identical with the version given in the Yerushalmi. In <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Parashah</i> 8(6) the language is that of the Sephardic Mishnah, based on the expression <i>and he is a witness</i> of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 35a" href="/Shevuot.35a">Babli (35a</a>) infers from this verse that the demand for testimony must be personal; it is not enough that the claimant know the identity of the witness, the request must be delivered individually. If the claimant sees a group of people which includes the two whom he wants as his witnesses, he may not address the group collectively; he must summon them personally.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he said to two persons, I am putting an oath on you, X and Y, that you shall come and testify for me if you know testimony for me, but they knew from another witness<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is hearsay evidence inadmissible in court. If one of a group of witnesses is disqualified, the other one at most could force the defendant to swear; he never could force him to pay money. Therefore the oath is invalid also for the party which would be qualified.</i>, or one of them was a relative or disqualified. These are not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he said to two persons, I am putting an oath on you,” etc. Rebbi Mana understood it from the following, “or one of them was a relative or disqualified.” Therefore, unless one of them was a relative or disqualified, they are liable. And he knows who they are. Therefore the reason can only be that he does not intend them. Rebbi Yose understood it from the preceding, “since the oath preceded the testimony.” Therefore, if the oath did not precede the testimony, they are liable. And he knows who they are. Therefore the reason can only be that he does not intend them<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This again refers to Mishnah 11, it is a continuation of the preceding Halakhah. All the Mishnaiot quoted presuppose that the claimant knows exactly whom he wants as witness; nevertheless his imprecation is ineffective. Therefore it may be assumed that also in the case of Mishnah 11 it would be ineffective even if he knew exactly whom he intended.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he sent through his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">To ask the witnesses to testify. Even though the slave is his personal property and acts as his messenger he is disqualified since he is not the claimant and has no <i>persona</i> in law.</i> or the defendant said to them, “I am putting an oath on you that you shall come and testify for him,” they are not liable unless they hear from the mouth of the claimant<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">To create liability, the oath must be delivered directly by the claimant asking for the testimony. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.7.1">Note 87</a>. <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Parashah</i> 8(4).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he sent through his slave,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, why does the verse say, <i>if he does not tell</i>, <i>he has to carry his iniquity</i>? <i>Not</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">This is a misquote which makes the text unintelligible. The masoretic text of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5" href="/Leviticus.5"><i>Lev</i>. 5</a>:8*1 writes the negation as לוֹא which is read both as לוֹ and as לֹא. In the first version, אִם לוֹ יַגִּיד, the liability may only be triggered <i>if he told him</i>, if the claimant personally challenged the witness and if the witness personally accepted the challenge (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 35a" href="/Shevuot.35a">Babli 35a</a>). In the second version, אִם לֹא יַגִּיד, the liability is triggered if he does not testify.</i>, from the mouth of the claimant. Our Mishnah needs what Rebbi Eleazar said, and what Rebbi Eleazar said needs our Mishnah. If we had stated but Rebbi Eleazar had not stated, we would have said that they are liable if they had heard from the claimant but had sworn to the defendant. Therefore what Rebbi Eleazar said is necessary. If Rebbi Eleazar had stated but we had not stated, we would have instructed that they are liable if they heard it from the defendant but had sworn to the claimant. Therefore one needs our Mishnah and needs what Rebbi Eleazar said, only if they heard from the claimant and swore to the claimant.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I put an oath upon you, I command you, I bind you,” these are liable<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">These are valid versions of a summons to testify.</i>. “By Heaven and Earth,” they are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">“Heaven” is not a sobriquet of God’s Name.</i>. “By א-ד, by י-ה, by the Almighty, by Sabaoth, by the Gracious and Merciful, by the Forbearing and Most Benevolent,” and all substitute names<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">א-ד stands for Adonay, י-ה for YHWH. A substitute name is any translation of any Name of Attribute of God in any vernacular.</i>, they are liable. One who curses by any of these is liable, the words of Rebbi Meїr; but the Sages declare him not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The crime of blasphemy (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:15" href="/Leviticus.24.15"><i>Lev</i>. 24:15</a>) refers only to blaspheming the Name (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:11:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.11.2-4"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:14</a>).</i>. One who curses his father or mother by any of these is liable, the words of Rebbi Meїr; but the Sages declare him not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The same holds for cursing father and mother (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:17" href="/Exodus.21.17"><i>Ex</i>. 21:17</a>).</i>. One who curses himself or another person by any of these violates a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:14" href="/Leviticus.19.14"><i>Lev</i>. 19:14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Section 2 13" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Section_2.13"><i>Sifra Qedošim Parashah</i> 2(13</a>).</i>. “May God punish you, so may God punish you,” this is the imprecation mentioned in the Torah. “May He not punish you, may He bless you, may He do well with you,” Rebbi Meїr declares liable<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">If the claimant says “may He not punish you if you come to testify”, or “may He bless you if you come to testify”, for R. Meїr this implies the opposite, i. e., “may he punish you if you do not come to testify” etc.</i> but Rebbi Jehudah declares not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Jehudah, what has been said explicitly is the only thing that counts.<br>The Mishnah versions in the Babli and in the independent Mishnah mss. all read “but the Sages declare not liable.” It is clear from the Halakhah that this also was the reading in the Yerushalmi underlying the Halakhah since it was necessary to state that the Mishnah represents R. Jehudah’s opinion.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I put an oath upon you,” etc. to the end. “I put an oath upon you” without addition<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously he has to add what he actually demands from them but he does not have to invoke the Name, neither does he have to state that this is an oath; the verb alone is sufficient.</i>, they are liable. “I am binding you, I am roping you in,” they are liable.
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai, as our Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be a scribal error; one should read as in the next sentence כֵּינִי מַתְנִיתִין “so is our Mishnah.”</i>. But some say, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, so is our Mishnah: “Rebbi Meïr declares liable but Rebbi Jehudah declares not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Jehudah, what has been said explicitly is the only thing that counts.<br>The Mishnah versions in the Babli and in the independent Mishnah mss. all read “but the Sages declare not liable.” It is clear from the Halakhah that this also was the reading in the Yerushalmi underlying the Halakhah since it was necessary to state that the Mishnah represents R. Jehudah’s opinion.</i>” Rebbi Meїr follows his own opinion and Rebbi Jehudah follows his own opinion, as we have stated: “If he cursed them by a substitute name, Rebbi Meїr declares him guilty but the Sages free him from prosecution.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.11.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:14</a>. Only here is opinion of the Sages attributed to R. Jehudah; in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 66a" href="/Sanhedrin.66a">Babli (Sanhedrin 66a</a>) it is attributed to R. Menaḥem ben R. Yose.</i>”
“One who curses himself or another person by any of these violates a prohibition.” What about flogging? The colleagues say, he cannot be flogged. Rebbi Yose said to them, why? Because it is a prohibition not involving an action. But is not one who substitutes and one who swears falsely also a prohibition which involves no action<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.10.3">Chapter 3:11, Notes 166–168</a>, and the parallels indicated there. It seems that R. Yose considers any speech as an action, against the consensus that speech can be considered action only if recognized as such by a verse.</i>?
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Meïr is the one who says that out of a negative one understands a positive<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This is the position of the Yerushalmi here and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 7:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.7.1.2">7:1 (Note 11</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 21b:3" href="/Eruvin.21b.3">Eruvin 3 (21b l. 24</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:4 Note 136</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.4.4"><i>Nedarim</i>1:4 Note 158</a>. (The explanation given there that the statement of R. Yasa in the name of R. Joḥanan is a rhetorical question is incorrect; the statement is a straightforward declarative sentence.) The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 36a" href="/Shevuot.36a">Babli (36a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 11a" href="/Nedarim.11a"><i>Nedarim</i> 11a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 13b" href="/Nedarim.13b">13b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 17a" href="/Sotah.17a"><i>Soṭah</i> 17a</a>) is totally opposed; it proposes to switch the attributions in the Mishnah between R. Meїr and the Sages.</i>. “May He not punish you if you come and testify for me.” Therefore, if you do not come and testify for me He shall punish you. There is not only an imprecation accompanied by an oath. From where one not accompanied by an oath be like one which is accompanied by an oath? The verse says, <i>and heard an imprecation, and heard a voice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>.</i>, to make one without imprecation like one with an imprecation. Therefore not an imprecation without an oath. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: There is no difference; an imprecation without oath is the same as an oath without an imprecation<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 36a" href="/Shevuot.36a">Babli 36a</a>, <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Parashah</i> 8(2).</i>.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An oath about a deposit applies to men and women, unrelated persons and relatives, qualified and disqualified persons, before a court or out of court, from his own mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In this respect the oath about money matters is equal to the rules of a blurted oath (Mishnah 3:11–12), not to the oath of testimony. All restrictions about women, relatives, and disqualified persons, refer only to witnesses and judges, not to parties in adversary proceedings.</i>. From the mouth of another he is liable only if he denies in court, the words of Rebbi Meїr. But the Sages say, whether by his own mouth or the mouth of others, from the moment that he denies, he is liable. He is liable for intent about the oath, or error about it with intent about the deposit, but he is not liable for error about the latter<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:25" href="/Leviticus.5.25"><i>Lev</i>. 5:25</a> (in addition to restitution of 125% of the amount embezzled) is due if the false oath was intentional, or if the oath was in error but the embezzling was intentional; it is not due if the oath was factually false because the maker of the oath did not realize that he had the deposit, or forgot about it. The Babli (but not Maimonides) reads the last word as שִׁגְגָתָהּ referring to the oath; he is not liable if he was ignorant of the fact that false oaths are forbidden and that they trigger liability for a sacrifice.</i>. What is he liable for in case of intentional violation? A reparation offering in the value of two Šeqalim<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:25" href="/Leviticus.5.25"><i>Lev</i>. 5:25</a> prescribes a reparation offering “in its value”; the value defined in 5:15 as “<i>Šeqalim</i>”. An indeterminate plural always means 2 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.1.2">Chapter 4, Note 8</a>). The biblical <i>Šeqel</i> is the traditional “King’s weight” of about 12g silver, about equal to the tetradrachma of the early Principate, and double the rabbinic <i>Šeqel</i> of two <i>zuz</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1, Note 122</a>.) <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Parashah</i> 13(14).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An oath about a deposit,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For this and part of the next Halakhah there exists a rudimentary Genizah fragment edited by L. Ginzberg, <i>Yerushalmi Fragments</i>, New York 1909, pp. 285–286 (G).</i> It was stated: Is an oath in his own formulation of an oath of testimony like an imprecation; in his own words is an oath about a deposit like an imprecation<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It was established in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:10:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.10.5">4:14 (Note 119</a>) that for requests of testimony imprecations without oaths and oaths without imprecations trigger liabilities for a sacrifice. As noted before, the natural setting of an oath or imprecation for testimony is one formulated by the claimant; for oaths about deposits it is one formulated by the defendant. No imprecations are mentioned in connection with oaths about deposits. The unresolved questions are whether an imprecation formulated by the potential witness triggers liability and what the status is of an imprecation substituting for an oath is a case of disputed monetary claim?</i>? Rebbi Yose said, since it is written “a person, a person<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1" href="/Leviticus.5.1"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.6.2">Chapter 4:3 Note 31</a>.</i>”, everything is here and everything is there. Rebbi Mana said, an imprecation made in court is the topic of disagreement between Rebbi Meїr and the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Not only oaths; Mishnah 4:1.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The following is also quoted by Alfassi (<i>Shevuot</i> Chapter 5) and is the subject of extensive commentaries by Nachmanides, R. Nissim Gerondi, and Rosh. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 36a" href="/Shevuot.36a">Babli 36a</a>.</i><i>He lied to his fellow man</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21" href="/Leviticus.5.21"><i>Lev</i>. 5:21</a>.</i>, if his confession was eliminated by the oath<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Liability for a sacrifice is created only if the false oath saves the defendant from a sentence which would obligate him to pay the claimant. Cf. S. Liebermann, Review of S. Asaph מספרות הגאונים, Tarbiz 5(1934) pp. 395–400.</i>. It excludes one who lied to one of partners; it excludes one who lied where there are witnesses and a document<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Swearing falsely to only one of the partners has no monetary consequences as long as the defendant did not also lie to the other partners. Swearing falsely about an obligation which can be proven by witnesses or documents is pointless.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that if two people took a loan from one person, even if they did not write “we are responsible and warrantors for one another”, they are responsible and warrantors for one another; but one does not act on this<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The implication that rules governing one debtor and two creditors can be applied to one creditor and two debtors is not found in the Babli but accepted by Rif (§1043) and all subsequent codifiers; the note that one does not act on this, but writes joint liabilities in all contracts, was not accepted by Rif and his successor Josef ibn Migash (cf. I. Tashma and H. Ben Shammai, <i>Kobez al Yad</i> 8(18) Jerusalem 1975 p. 179 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.5.1.2">Note 10</a>).</i>. If two made a joint deposit and one came to retrieve his property, one does not listen to him<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Without explicit authorization by one of the depositors to the trustee, a joint deposit may be returned only to the joint depositors.</i>. Should he not be treated as denying his part and be liable? If one made a deposit with two people. If one denied it, he is liable. If the other denied it, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If both trustees denied the existence of the deposit at different times, both are liable.</i>. If the deposit was worth one <i>peruṭa</i>, does this not result in each of them bringing a sacrifice for half a <i>peruṭa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>peruṭa</i> (a Hasmonean coin of about 2g bronze) is the smallest amount for which one may go to court. (Half- <i>peruṭa</i> coins have been found.) Since a half-<i>peruṭa</i> cannot be the subject of a law suit, it cannot be the cause of liability for any kind of oath. Should not the sacrifice for an oath about a half-<i>peruṭa</i> be forbidden as false dedication?</i>? What here? If one would swear, and swear, and swear, would he not bring a sacrifice for half a <i>peruṭa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since he has to bring three sacrifices for oaths about one <i>peruṭa</i>, each one is for a third of a <i>peruṭa</i>.</i>? There, each oath refers to a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth. Here no oath refers to a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The previous argument is wrong. Multiple infractions all concerning the same <i>peruṭa</i> need separate atoning. A deposit given to two people jointly must be at least two <i>peruṭas</i> worth to trigger liabilities for sacrifices.</i>.
Rebbi La: Both<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read ר׳ יוחנן instead of ור׳ יוחנן and delete the first “both”. G is of no help. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 37b" href="/Shevuot.37b">Babli (37b</a>) only quotes R. Joḥanan.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both are saying, there is no ”witnesses “here<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">While they agree that an oath denying any documentary obligation is futile, they hold that a debt proven only by the oral testimony of witnesses is difficult to enforce and, therefore, the debtor may see a chance to evade monetary obligations by a false oath. In the <i>baraita</i> quoted at the start of the preceding paragraph the mention of “witnesses” should be deleted.</i>. Why? Because they might die. But might not a document be lost? Therefore, the reason can only be that they might forget their testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">There are several reasons why testimony by witnesses alone may be impossible; this might encourage false oaths.</i>. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 8:4.</i>: “Where is my ox? He told him, it is lost. I want you to swear, he said, Amen. But witnesses testify that he ate it. He has to pay the value. If he confessed himself, he has to pay the value, and the fifth, and the reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The second person has accepted to watch over the ox without being paid and without having the right to use the ox for work. If anything happens to the ox except cases of gross negligence, the trustee does not have to pay. The moment he uses the ox for his own purposes, he becomes liable for damages. Since he swears falsely that it was lost, he swears falsely for monetary gain and becomes liable. (Had he claimed that the ox was stolen, he would have had to pay the thief’s fine in addition to paying for the ox.) If he confesses in order to clear his conscience, he becomes eligible for atonement of his sin which is effected by paying an additional fifth (from above, i. e., a quarter from below) and a sacrifice in the value of 2 <i>šeqalim</i>.</i>.” The Mishnah [applies] if he does not know that there are witnesses. He comes to tell you, even if he knows that there are witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation of the Mishnah shows clearly that there were witnesses available when he swore falsely. Nevertheless, he has to bring a sacrifice if he confesses. Why do R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish have to formulate a statement which clearly is implied by a Mishnah? The answer is that the Mishnah may be interpreted that the trustee swore falsely in the belief that there were no witnesses; he swore for monetary gain. The Amoraic statement adds that even had the trustee known of the existence of witnesses, only they were not present at the moment of his oath, it still is an oath for monetary gain since the witnesses might forget their testimony in the meantime. The formulation “he”, not “they”, in the last sentence may support the reading of the Babli which only mentions R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says so: “If there were two groups of witnesses, if the first group reneged and after this the second, both are liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:4.</i>” One understands that the first group becomes liable<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This text is very difficult and all commentators and editors follow the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 32b-33a" href="/Shevuot.32b-33a">Babli (32b/33a</a>) and switch the places of “first” and “second”, for the reasons explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.3.4">Halakhah 4:4, Note 54</a>. But this seems to be excluded by the text of G, which becomes the more credible as its scribe clearly did not understand the text since he misread מצוּיִין “being apt to” as מצֻוִּים “being commanded”.<br>Therefore one is forced to explain that the first group clearly violates their oath of testimony by reneging on it. The question is why the second group is not freed from their obligation by the first since strictly speaking only two witnesses are needed by biblical standards; any additional witnesses appear superfluous. The answer is that additional witnesses never are superfluous since any witness may forget, if not the whole incident so some detail which might invalidate his testimony in cross examination. One knows that the testimony of a group of witnesses is valid only <i>after</i> it was accepted in court.</i>. Why the second? Not because they might forget their testimony? <sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G; cf. the preceding Note.</i>[Here also, because they (are commanded) to forget their testimony.]
<i>He denied it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:22" href="/Leviticus.5.22"><i>Lev</i>. 5:22</a>.</i>, not him<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 22(6) more in detail: “About it but not about its finder.” This Tanna and both Talmudim reject the understanding of the verse as given in the Targumim, that the person swearing had found the lost property and now denies it. This would be covered by “robbery” listed in v. 21. Rather it is that the person searching for his lost property, such as his donkey mares, asks a person whether he knows either of the property or of the person who might have taken it. If he under oath falsely denies any knowledge, according to this Tanna be becomes liable for a sacrifice only if he wrongly denies knowledge about the property, not about the person who might have appropriated it.<br>The big question then becomes, why should the person asked for testimony be liable for anything since by necessity he is a single person and the testimony of a single person cannot force a judgment. As Ravad explains in his Commentary to <i>Sifra</i>, this argument would be unconditionally valid only for oaths about testimony, not for oaths about causing monetary loss. His scenario is, e. g., that the lost animal was standing on another person’s property. The original owner took it but now the person from whom it was taken claims ownership and wants it back. The second person agrees that he found the animal but he disputes the fact that it is the animal which had belonged to the first. In this situation, testimony of one witness about ownership of the animal is sufficient for a judgment in favor of the first person since it is not a judgment to transfer property but to confirm an existing status. On the other hand, testimony that the second person took possession of some lost property but which failed to identify the property is worthless and its denial cannot trigger any liability by the potential witness.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 22(6), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 105b" href="/Bava_Kamma.105b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 105b</a>. In the Babli: “Three kinds of oaths [about lost property.]”</i>“Ben Azzai says, there are three kinds of lost properties. One who knows about it and its finder; about it but not about its finder; neither about it nor about its finder.” Rebbi Onias in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: All of them to relieve from liability<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is attributed to the early Galilean Amora R. Ḥanina.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yose: This is not so<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is the authoritative opinion of Samuel.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked, why should it be to relieve from liability? Rebbi Mana said, the three kinds of lost property cannot be “one who knows about it and its finder; about it but not about its finder; neither about it nor about its finder.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli points out, if somebody swears although he does not not know about the whole affair he swears truthfully and no liability of any kind can arise. In place of “neither about it nor about its finder” one must read “about it and its finder.”</i>” About it and its finder, that is where we hold. But it must be about one who denies [knowledge] about it and its finder; about its finder but not about it; about it but not about its finder<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Only the middle clause is in dispute between Ben Azzai and the first Tanna.</i>. So is the <i>baraita</i>: “About it and its finder.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is an oath about a deposit? He said to him, “give me the deposit which you hold for me.” “An oath that I am holding nothing of yours;<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This is the oath from his own mouth.</i>” or that he said to him, “I am holding nothing of yours;” “I am putting an oath upon you” and he said “Amen”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is the oath from another’s mouth.</i>; this one is liable. If he made him swear five times whether in court or out of court and he denied, he is liable for each single one. Rebbi Simeon said, what is the reason? Because he may return and admit<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The rule that “after he told, he does not return to tell” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.2.4">Halakhah 4:3, Note 30</a>) restricts witnesses but not parties to a trial.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What is an oath about a deposit,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah is a reworking of Halakhah 4:3 with in most cases correctly switching “here” (5:21) and “there” (5:1) and “oath of testimony” and “oath of deposit”. The explanation of <i>equal cut</i> is taken from <i>Yebamot</i> 11:1 (Notes 30,34) as indicated in the Notes to 4:3. The text of G ends at the start of this Halakhah.</i><i>A person</i>, <i>a person</i>. Since here it is from his own words, also there from his own words. Since there it is from others’ words, also here it is from others’ words. Rebbi Meїr explains the equal cut at the place it comes from: Since here it is said about his own words, so also here<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously this should read “there.”</i> about his own words. Also the rabbis explain the equal cut which was said about it. Since others’ words said there are in court, also others’ words said here are in court.
<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a new question, with the arguments about oaths of testimony and deposits switched. In structure and argument it is completely parallel to 4:3, Notes 34,35.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Is a relative not liable for an oath about deposits formulated by others? Can we hear it from the following: since they inferred formulation by others for an oath about deposits only from oaths about testimony, and since there relatives are not liable, so they neither are here? Rebbi Yose said, relatives here can be inferred from non-relatives there.
Rebbi Yudan the Cappadocian asked: He made him swear five times by the formulation of others out of the court: would he not be liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">If this refers to oaths about deposits, this would be the Mishnah and there would be no room for any question. It seems therefore that the question is asked about oaths of testimony and the hypothesis is that there should be liability for each one of the oaths.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, is this not the Mishnah: “Rebbi Simeon said, what is the reason? Because they can come back and admit?” But here, since they cannot come back and admit, they are liable only once.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he made him swear five times, since Rebbi Meїr considered “by his own words” as in court, would this determine the sacrifice on the first occasion, even without request<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This is a bad adaptation of the text in 4:3. Following R. Meїr, since assent to an oath formulated by others is inferred from oaths about testimony, if applied to money matters does this need a claim by the other party? There can be no question about “by his own words” since that is what <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20" href="/Leviticus.5.20"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20</a> ff. is all about. It is difficult to imagine a situation in which others would formulate an oath if they have no claim.</i>? It should come like the following:<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This is a mangled quote from a text similar to the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a>: “He said to him, give me the deposit which you hold for me. Why do you go after me? An oath that I am holding nothing of yours. This one is liable and has to pay for the value of the object, an added fifth, and bring a reparation sacrifice for two tetradrachmas, in distinction from an oath about testimony.”<br>A clear sign of the corruption is the use of the plural in the words of the claimant which makes no sense in a question about deposits.</i> “Why do you go after us? An oath that we do not know any testimony relevant for you. He pays the value plus a fifth and brings a reparation sacrifice for two tetradrachmas, in distinction from an oath about testimony.”
Would he be “not liable” from others’ mouth for real estate? From others’ mouth would he be liable for a reparation sacrifice worth two <i>šeqalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 37b" href="/Shevuot.37b">Babli 37b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a>) it is obvious that the oath applies to claims for real estate which have to be proven by documents.</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If five people were claiming from him and said to him, give us the deposit which you are holding for us. “An oath that I am not holding anything of yours’ ”; he is liable only once. “An oath that I am not holding anything of you, and of you, and of you;” he is liable for each one separately. Rebbi Eliezer says, on condition that he repeat “an oath” at the end. Rebbi Simeon says, not unless he repeat “an oath” for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah and in all parallel sources the name is not “R. Eliezer” but “R. Eleazar”. The latter name is correct as contemporary of R. Simeon; cf. R. Rabbinovicz, <i>Diqduqe Soferim Ševuot</i>, p. 78, Note ז.<br>The majority [following R. Meїr in <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 17(1)] holds that an oath addressed to a group of people is a single oath, but one addressed to separate individuals counts as multiple oaths according to the number of persons mentioned in the oath. R. Eleazer holds that this is true only if the person swearing indicates that he intends the one sentence to be considered a separate oath for each individual addressed, by repeating at the end that “this is an oath.” Without such a final statement the mention of “oath” refers only to the first subject addressed in the oath. R. Simeon holds that a statement formulated as one oath is one oath; there is liability for multiple sacrifices only if it was clearly said that separate oaths were made for each claimant.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If five people were claiming from him,” etc. I could think that also for an oath about a deposit He did not make one assenting to an oath like making an oath? Rebbi Eleazar said, this is not so. But also for an oath about a deposit we are treating one assenting to an oath like one formulating an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The text as it stands is unintelligible. The first “also” contradicts what was stated in Halakhah 4:5 that the rules of oaths about testimony and deposits are derived from one another. An oath about testimony by its nature is imposed by another party; there it was established that the reluctant witness also is liable if he himself formulates the oath that he will appear as a witness and then reneges. Therefore, this “also” has to be deleted.<br>The oath about a deposit by its nature is offered by the person denying that he is holding anything. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 34b" href="/Shevuot.34b">Babli (34b</a>) and <i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i>11(3) state that in this case an oath imposed by another person does not trigger the liability for a sacrifice. The Yerushalmi considers this possibility and rejects it.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said: The words of Rebbi Eleazar, only if he mentioned “oath” at the start and at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Then multiple sacrifices are due for multiple lies.</i>. The words of the Sages, if he mentioned “oath” at the start but not at the end. Or if he mentioned it at the end but not at the start in the opinion of the rabbis he brings a sacrifice for each single one, in Rebbi Eleazar’s opinion he brings one sacrifice for everything<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In both cases, a single mention of “oath” at the end is equivalent in force to a single mention of “oath” at the start.</i>. If he mentions “oath” at the start but did not mention it at the end, does the oath fall on the middle ones<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question only for R. Eleazar. Does R. Eleazar only require one sacrifice because he considers everything together as one statement, or does he restrict the oath to the denial of the claim of the first person mentioned? If the denial of the claim of the first person was correct, but some denials to other persons were false, in the first case the trustee would have to bring a sacrifice but not in the second.</i>? Come and see: If he had mentioned it at the start and the end, did he mention it for the middle ones? But you are saying that it is valid; so here it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since the difference between R. Eleazar and R. Simeon is that the latter requires a separate mention of “oath” for each single denial while R. Eleazar requires only two irrespective of the number of denials in between, it is obvious that the first eventuality mentioned in the preceding Note is the correct interpretation.</i>.
Rebbi Yudan from Cappadocia asked: What about “an oath that neither wheat, nor barley, nor spelt I am holding for you<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The wording is that of Mishnah 5. However, there one has to translate “wheat, or barley, or spelt” and the oath is true only if he holds neither kind. But here the oath is true if he does not hold at least one of them. This follows from de Morgan’s formulas (using v “or”, &amp; “and”, ¬ “not”):<br>¬(AvBvC) = ¬A&amp;¬B&amp;¬C,<br>¬(A&amp;B&amp;C) = ¬Av¬Bv¬C.</i>”? Is it as if he had mentioned “oath” at beginning and end<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question following R. Eleazar, assuming that he disagrees not only in the case of Mishnah 3 but in all similar cases.</i>? Since the oath would be confirmed by any one of them, he is liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to read this as three distinct oaths; there can be no disagreement between the rabbis and R. Eleazar in this case.</i>. “I am not holding for you wheat, or barley, or spelt, an oath,” is this as if he had mentioned “oath” at beginning and end? Since the oath would be confirmed by any one of them, he is liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question for the rabbis. Does placing the mention of “oath” at the end of a sentence intended as oath constitute a second oath?</i>. Is it so if this were a blurted oath? “An oath that I shall not eat; I shall not eat, an oath”? In the rabbis’ opinion he brings a sacrifice for each one<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since this clearly represents two distinct oaths.</i>. In Rebbi Eleazar’s opinion does he bring one for the entirety? Even Rebbi Eleazar will agree that he is only emphasizing the prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:9:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.9.8">Halakhah 3:11, Note 161</a>. Since there is only one subject, R. Eleazar should consider the two clauses as parts of one statement and require only one sacrifice. But he will agree that the second statement is meant to reinforce the first; therefore if he breaks one he breaks both and must bring two sacrifices.</i>. It only would be problematic if it were about the past. “An oath that I did not eat; I did not eat, an oath.” In the rabbis’ opinion he brings two sacrifices (for each one). In Rebbi Eleazar’s opinion he brings one (for each)<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For the rabbis, the two clauses are two separate oaths; for R. Eleazar they are two parts of one oath. Since the oaths have only one object, the references to “each one” or “all of them” are copyists’ errors induced by the use of these expressions in the other cases.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu, Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This name tradition is impossible. Either one has to read R. Jeremiah, R. Abbahu, or, since the question fits R. Jeremiah’s style, instead of R. Abbahu one has to read R. Abba (or the name of another last generation Amora.)</i> asked: If one said, “an oath wheat and wheat I am not holding;” what? Let us hear from the following: “Not from place X, not from place Y,” he is liable. Rebbi Yose said, they considered a multiplicity of places like a multiplicity of kinds. If he had said, not from this place, not from this place; it would have been useful<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">If the same object is mentioned twice in an oath. There is no proof from a <i>baraita</i> that a false oath “I am not holding grain from place X nor from place Y” is considered only one oath. To be applicable to the question the same place name would have to be repeated.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥaggai asked: “10 oaths that I am not holding;” what?
Rebbi Yose asked: “An oath, an oath that I am not holding for you,” what? Rebbi Yose said, let us hear from the following: “ ‘An oath that I shall not eat this loaf; an oath that I shall not eat it’. Then he ate it. He is liable only once.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3:8 (3:7 in most independent Mishnah mss.). This clause is missing in the Mishnah of the Yerushalmi; another indication that the Mishnah of the Leiden ms. does not have the same source as the Yerushalmi Tractates.</i>” Because he mentioned “this”. Therefore, had he not mentioned “this” we would be liable for each one. Is it the same for a blurted oath? “An oath that I shall eat carcass meat, and wheat bread, and barley bread, and spelt bread.” Since he is not liable for carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3:5; this argument follows R. Simeon.</i> he is not liable for the remainder.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “Give me the deposit, the loan, the robbery, and the lost object which you are holding for me.” “An oath that I am not holding anything of yours;” he is liable only once. “An oath that I am holding for you neither a deposit, nor a loan, nor a robbery, nor a lost object;” he is liable for each separate item<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The same arguments made for oaths addressed to different people can be made for oaths covering different subjects; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.5.3.1">Note 44</a>. The dissents of RR. Eleazar and Simeon apply here also.</i>.<br>“Give me the wheat, and the barley, and the spelt, which you are holding for me.” “An oath that I am not holding anything for you,” he is liable only for one. “An oath that I am not holding wheat, and barley, and spelt, for you,” he is liable for each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The same arguments made for oaths addressed to different people can be made for oaths covering different subjects; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.5.3.1">Note 44</a>. The dissents of RR. Eleazar and Simeon apply here also.</i>. Rebbi Meїr says, even if he says, “wheat, and barley, and spelt,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the defendant did not use the language of the claimant, as long as the meaning is the same there is liability for a false oath. The claimant used the vernacular plural to describe his claim; the defendant used the biblical collective (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 9:31-32" href="/Exodus.9.31-32"><i>Ex</i>. 9:31–32</a>) which in form is identical with the singular. In the Halakhah, the collective for R. Meїr is read as singular for the anonymous Tanna.</i>” he is liable for each single one.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Give me the deposit, the loan,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This entire Halakhah is a slightly defective copy of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.3.2-6"><i>Nazir</i> 4:3</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 5:3:3-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.5.3.3-7">Notes 48–60</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon implies that if it turns out that he had no wheat, he is not liable for the rest. Rebbi Abba said, even Rebbi (Yasa)<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Nazir</i>: R. Jehudah. Since there is no Tanna R. Yasa, the reading “R. Jehudah” has to be accepted. In the next Halakhah, R. Jehudah is identified as the anonymous Tanna of Mishnaiot 3–5.</i> will agree with this. If it turns out that he had no wheat, does [the oath] refer to the other kinds? The colleagues said, it does not. Rebbi Ze`ira said, it does. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, a <i>baraita</i> supports what the colleagues said. “A woman made a vow as a <i>nazir</i> and her friend heard it and said, ‘so am I’; the first woman’s husband heard it and dissolved [the vow] for her; the first one is permitted and the second forbidden. <sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Nazir</i>, “R. Simeon said, if …” Since the next paragraph refers to R. Simeon, this must also be the text here.</i> If she said, ‘my intention was only to be like her,’ both are permitted.” Because she said, “to be like her, in her state”. Therefore, if she did not say “to be like her, in her state”, the first is permitted and the second forbidden. Since there, where the essence is not an oath, you say it applies, here, where there was an original oath, not so much more? What Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon implies if it turns out that he had no wheat, he is not liable for the rest, if he connects: If he says, [my obligation for] barley shall be like that for wheat; [my obligation for] spelt shall be like that for wheat.
The following is obvious: If her husband did not dissolve [the vow] for the first woman and she (remained)<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Read with <i>Nazir</i>: “she violated her vow.”</i> in her vow, she is whipped. Can the second he whipped? Rebbi Yose said, since one is whipped, the other also is whipped. Rebbi Yudan said, is this a stipulation of the court? Should not the second be treated as one who said, “a shall be a <i>nezirah</i>[after] thirty days”<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that the first became impure; then her <i>nezirut</i> is suspended while she undergoes the purification rite. Should the second woman’s vow be suspended while the <i>nezirut</i> of the first is suspended?</i>? Rebbi Simeon follows his own opinion, for he said, it depends on the collection<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence does not belong here; it is from the end of Halakhah 6. The correct text is in <i>Nazir</i>: R. Simeon declares him not liable since his offering was not in the custom of offerers (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 12:3" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.12.3">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 12:3</a>). A vow is either valid or not, it cannot be intermittent.</i>.
“Give me the wheat, and the barley,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the words of Rebbi Jehudah imply collectively one sacrifice, in detail three sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This determines the anonymous author of the Mishnah who makes the liability for sacrifices dependent on the way the vow is formulated as R. Jehudah. This is confirmed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 38a" href="/Shevuot.38a">Babli, 38a</a>.</i>. How is this? A detail wheat, a detail barley, a detail spelt. Collectively one sacrifice, in detail three sacrifices. Detail a third wheat, detail a third barley, detail a third spelt, are these taken together? Come and see; although he holds many details, even so they can be formulated collectively or in detail. In which respect? To make him liable for sacrifices. If he held four kinds, two kinds in one detail, two kinds on two details. even so they can be formulated collectively or in detail<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Irrespective of the complication of the transaction, there always is a formulation of the oath which avoids mentioning any detail.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.2"><i>Giṭtin</i> 9:7, Notes 85–101</a>. The following paragraph describes efforts by two men to divorce their wives with one document to save on costs for scribe and parchment.</i> As Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: “And I am greeting X,” one can assume that he signed concerning everything; “I am greeting X,” he signed only for the greeting. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, “I am greeting X,” one can assume that he signed concerning everything. What means “collectively”? Rebbi Joḥanan said, “X divorces Y and Z U.” What means “collectively”? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, “We, X and Z, divorce our wives at place A.” Rebbi Ze`ira said, Rebbi Joḥanan agrees that if he mentions divorce for each one separately that he needs the typical text<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">While the <i>Giṭtin</i> text everywhere has טוֹפֶס “boilerplate document” which possibly is Accadic (from the Sumerian) <i>ṭuppum</i> “document”, the spelling here indicates Greek τύπος “figure, form” used as “prescribed form” (of the document.)</i> and witnesses for each one separately. The strength of Rebbi Joḥanan is from the following: “ ‘That I shall not benefit from this one; a <i>qorban</i> for this one and <i>a qorban</i> for that one.’ Each single one needs a separate opening.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 9:7.</i>” Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “If he wrote a separate text for each of them and the witnesses signed at the end, [only] the one with which the witnesses are read is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭtin</i> 9:7</a>.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.2"><i>Giṭtin</i> 9:7, Notes 93–101</a>. The omissions in the <i>Giṭtin</i> text show that the text here is original. (This has been recognized in the text there.)</i> Samuel said. the detailed statement for Rebbi Meїr is the general statement for Rebbi Jehudah; the detailed statement for Rebbi Jehudah is the general statement for Rebbi Meїr. Rebbi Samuel said in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira, the words of the rabbis show that the detailed statement for Rebbi Meïr is not the general statement for Rebbi Jehudah and the detailed statement for Rebbi Jehudah is not the general statement for Rebbi Meïr, as Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: “And I am greeting X,” one can assume that he signed concerning everything; “I am greeting X,” he only signed for the greeting. If you would say that the detailed statement for Rebbi Meїr is the general statement for Rebbi Jehudah, even if he said “I am greeting X,” can one assume that he signed everything? Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah implies that the detailed statement for Rebbi Jehudah is not the general statement for Rebbi Meїr, as we have stated: “Rebbi Meїr says, even if he says, ‘wheat, and barley, and spelt,’ he is liable for each single one;” and nobody says “even” unless he agrees to an earlier statement. Rebbi Ḥinena said, in Rebbi Meïr’s opinion, whether he said, “wheat, and barley, and spelt,” or “wheat, barley, spelt,” it is a general statement and details. In Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, if he said, “wheat, and barley, and spelt,” it is a general statement and details. If he said “wheat, barley, spelt,” it is a general statement without details<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Since he reads the words as singulars, not collectives, the entire statement is one unit: “I am holding for you not even a single grain each of wheat, barley, spelt”.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “You raped or seduced my daughter,” and he says “I did not rape, I did not seduce.” “I am asking you to swear,” and he says “Amen,” he is liable. Rebbi Simeon frees him from liability since he would not pay the fine by his own admission<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The fines are the statutory fines of the seducer of a virgin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:16" href="/Exodus.22.16"><i>Ex</i>. 22:16</a>) and the rapist of a virgin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:29" href="/Deuteronomy.22.29"><i>Deut</i>. 22:29</a>). Since these fines are imposed in criminal law, they cannot be imposed based on a confession since all confessions are inadmissible in criminal court. It was established earlier (4:4, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 5:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.5.2.4">Note 40</a>) that an oath can create liability for a reparation sacrifice only if the action of the defendant led to a claimant’s monetary loss.</i>. They told him, even though he does not pay the fine by his own admission, he pays for shame and diminution by his own admission<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Payments for inflicted shame, diminution of marriage prospects, and for rape victims’ pain, are civil claims for which “the admission by the defendant is worth 100 witnesses.” If the daughter is a minor, all money is due to her father.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “You raped or seduced my daughter,” etc. Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The words of Rebbi Simeon [imply that] the main claim is for the fine, which is not so for the words of the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 38b" href="/Shevuot.38b">Babli 38b</a>. In the interpretation of the Babli, R. Simeon holds that a person will go to court for the claim of a sum fixed by law rather than one depending on the sentiment of the judges; the Sages hold that a person always will prefer a civil claim which can be settled by the agreement of the defendant to a criminal trial depending on witnesses.</i>. Rebbi Illa said, the words of Rebbi Simeon if he claims the fine, shame and diminution; the words of the Sages if he claims shame, diminution, and fine<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This argument is similar to the previous one. For R. Simeon one will prefer a criminal trial upon the successful completion of which the civil claims can easily be prosecuted; for the Sages one prefers the civil procedure, upon successful conclusion of which the claim in criminal court has much better chances.</i>. The rabbis say, the words of Rebbi Simeon if he advances three claims; the words of the Sages if he advances one claim<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence most likely is corrupt; Rebbi Simeon deals with the situation where there only is a criminal case (one claim); the Sages consider the civil claims as paramount (three claims).</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked before Rebbi Yasa, did you hear explicitly from Rebbi Joḥanan, or from his word<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This is always a problem in oral tradition; it is not obvious who formulated the statement. In most cases of a lengthy chain of transmission, the actual formulation is not that of the first author. Since R. Ze`ira immigrated to Galilee after R. Joḥanan’s death. he has no way of knowing. R. Yasa explains that he formulated the sentence because R. Joḥanan had endorsed a position equal to that of the rabbis quoted earlier.</i>? Rebbi Yasa said, for he is of the rabbis’ opinion. Rebbi Mana said, even if he is of Rebbi Illa’s opinion, the words of Rebbi Simeon if he advances one claim; the words of the Sages if he advances three claims<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">There really is no material difference between R. Illai and the rabbis since a claim for the fine must be brought in criminal law; the claims for shame and diminution would have to be adjudicated later in civil court. The first claim according to R. Simeon is a single criminal claim.</i>. “Not only matters for which he has to pay the capital, from where double payment, quadruple and quintuple payment, the rapist, and the seducer, and the calumniator? The verse says, <i>everything about which one would swear falsely.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Ḥova (Wayyiqra 2) Pereq</i> 22(9). This source quotes <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21" href="/Leviticus.5.21"><i>Lev</i>. 5:21</a> instead of 5:24 quoted here.<br>The cases enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-22" href="/Leviticus.5.21-22"><i>Lev</i>. 5:21–22</a> are all about money or money’s worth which the defendant withholds from the claimant, a condition for liability for a sacrifice after a false oath. Double restitution is due from the thief, quadruple or quintuple from the cattle and sheep rustler who sells or eats the animal. The calumniator is the man who accuses his wife of adultery between preliminary and definitive marriages; if proven guilty in criminal court he pays a fine of 100 tetradrachmas (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:19" href="/Deuteronomy.22.19"><i>Deut</i>. 22:19</a>).</i>” Is the calumniator’s not a fine? Rebbi Jeremiah says, this is Rebbi Simeon’s who said, all depends on the collection<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since he denied only the fine, not the capital amount.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “You stole my ox,” but he says “I did not steal;” “I am asking you to swear” and he says “Amen”, he is liable. “I stole but I did not slaughter or sell it;” “I am asking you to swear” and he says “Amen”, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since he denied only the fine, not the capital amount.</i>. “Your ox killed my ox,” but he says “he did not kill;” “I am asking you to swear” and he says “Amen,” he is liable.<br>“Your ox killed my slave,” but he says “he did not kill;” “I am asking you to swear” and he says “Amen”, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since the amount payable is the biblically fixed amount of 30 šeqalim, about a Roman pound of silver (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:32" href="/Exodus.21.32"><i>Ex</i>. 21:32</a>), it has the status of a fine.</i>. He said to him, “you hurt me and injured me,” but he says “I did not hurt you and I did not injure you;” “I am asking you to swear” and he says “Amen”, he is liable. His slave said to him, “you knocked out my tooth and blinded my eye,” but he says “I did not knock out your tooth or blind your eye;” “I am asking you to swear” and he says “Amen”, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation of the master to manumit the slave whom he has injured is biblical and has the character of a fine.</i>. This is the principle, anybody who has to pay on his own admission is liable; but anybody who does not have to pay on his own admission is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The person who denies any fine adjudicated under criminal law.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ “You stole my ox,” etc. to the end of the Chapter. <sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.10.2"><i>Ketubot</i>3:10, Notes 143–156</a>.</i> Rebbi Isaac asked, should one have to pay the value of the slave by his admission? But what is his problem? Are the entire thirty a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since the amount payable is the biblically fixed amount of 30 šeqalim, about a Roman pound of silver (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:32" href="/Exodus.21.32"><i>Ex</i>. 21:32</a>), it has the status of a fine.</i> or is only the excess over his value a fine? If you say, all the thirty are a fine, he does not pay. If you say, the excess over his value is a fine, he pays. There, we have stated: “ ‘Your ox killed my slave’, but he says ‘he did not kill;’ ‘I am asking you to swear’ and he says ‘Amen’, he is not liable.” Rebbi Ḥaggai said before Rebbi Yose: Explain it if he killed a slave covered with boils. He said to him, but it says afterwards, “ ‘Your ox killed my son’, but he says ‘he did not kill;’ ‘I am asking you to swear’ and he says ‘Amen’, he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> is not found in any other source.</i>” You could explain it if he was covered with boils, then he would not be liable! You cannot do this, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah `<i>Arakhin</i>3:3, about a person killed by an ox known as goring. The entire sentence is not found in <i>Ketubot</i>; this seems to point to <i>Ševuot</i> as the primary source.</i>: “If he killed a human, he has to pay his worth.” Rebbi Ezra said before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ketubot</i>: R. Ḥaggai before R. Yose; probably dittography.</i> Explain it following him who said, <i>he shall pay redemption of his person</i>; of the injured<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:30" href="/Exodus.21.30"><i>Ex</i>. 21:30</a>. It is not clear whether “his” refers to the slain person or the owner of the dangerous ox (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.6.3"><i>Bava qamma</i>4:8 Note 108</a>). In <i>Ketubot</i> one reads the verse as referring to the owner of the ox; then it is obvious that the money is a fine even though its amount is not biblically fixed. On the face of it, this reading might be preferred; cf. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:30" href="/Exodus.21.30"><i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai ad Ex</i>. 21:30</a>. Rashi in `<i>Arakhin</i> points to <i>Ex</i>. 21:30 as the source of the Mishnah there; the addition of the sentence (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 5:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.5.6.2">Note 91</a>) supports the reading here as correct and that in <i>Ketubot</i> as corrected <i>lectio facilior</i>. Even according to the reading here, the identification of the redemption money as weregilt defines it as a fine.</i>. He said to him, if following him who said, <i>he shall pay redemption of his person</i>; of the injured, all is a fine
Does one tell him, be in the clear with Heaven? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 74b" href="/Bava_Kamma.74b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>74b</a>.</i>: “It happened that Rabban Gamliel knocked out a tooth of his slave Tabi. He came to Rebbi Joshua and said to him, I found a reason to manumit my slave Tabi. He told him, you have nothing in your hand; fines only are imposed through witnesses and in court.” This implies that one does not tell him, be in the clear with Heaven. Rebbi Gamliel ben Rebbi Illai<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ketubot</i>: ben Avina, at other places ben Ininia. R. Mana here is R. Mana I; in the preceding paragraph he was R. Mana II.</i> asked before Rebbi Mana, does Rabban Gamliel hold with him who says, one is permitted to manumit? He told him, in itself it says that it is forbidden to manumit, for otherwise he should have manumitted him immediately.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A judicial oath<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of a claim not proven by witnesses or documents where the defendant disputes part of the claim, the judges will impose an oath on the defendant that he owes not more than he admitted. The basis is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>, where the expression <i>where he says, this is it</i> is read as partial admission on the part of the defendant. The oath cannot be required by biblical standards if the defendant rejects the claim in its entirety; it cannot be administered by rabbinical standards if the amount in dispute or the amount admitted are below a certain threshhold.</i> is about a claim of two silver coins<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">“Silver” denotes the smallest silver coin struck in Hasmonean times, the <i>obolos</i>, <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>6</sub> of a <i>denar</i>. The silver half- <i>oboloi</i> minted in Yehud in Persian times had long disappeared when the Mishnaic system of currency was formulated.</i> and the acknowledgment of one <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A copper coin of Hasmonean times, of varying weight, but in the Mishnah assumed to be <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>32</sub> of an <i>obolos</i>. Half- <i>peruṭot</i> have been found.</i>. If the acknowledgment is not of the kind of the claim he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in Mishnaiot 3 ff.</i>. How is this? “You are holding two silver coins for me;” “I am holding only one <i>peruṭah</i>.” He is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the claim to be adjudicated is 2 <i>oboloi</i> minus 1 <i>peruṭah</i>, i. e., 63 <i>peruṭot</i>, the statutory minimum of 64 <i>peruṭot</i> is not reached; the oath cannot be imposed by rabbinic rules.</i>. “You are holding two silver coins and a <i>peruṭah</i> for me;” “I am holding only one <i>peruṭah</i>.” He is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the claim in dispute is a full two <i>oboloi</i>.</i>. “You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me,” “I am not holding anything for you,” he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since the claim is rejected in its entirety, it must be proven by documents or witnesses; the oath cannot be imposed by biblical standards.</i>. “You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me,” “I am holding only 50 <i>denar</i> for you,” he is liable. “You are holding a <i>mina</i> for my father,” “I am holding only 50 <i>denar</i> for you,” he is not liable since he is like one returning a lost object<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since a son cannot swear in place of his father, the son would have to accept a declaration by the defendant that he had owed to the father but returned everything to the latter during his lifetime; the defendant has to be believed if he admits part of the claim.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A judicial oath,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This text is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:2-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.2-33"><i>Qiddušin</i>1:1</a>, explained there in detail in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:4:3-6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.4.3-6.1">Notes 77–98</a>. Parallels are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 11b" href="/Kiddushin.11b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>11b</a>.<br>The question is, why do the House of Hillel require a larger minimal amount for litigation before a court than the House of Shammai but a much smaller sum than the House of Shammai for legal marriage by symbolic acquisition. The answer is that different biblical verses are the basis. Since a Hebrew slave girl is a minor sold by her father for a maximum of 6 years, or until she reaches the age of 12, or until she is married by the person who buys her or one of his sons. In order to get the maximum for his money, the man buying her might use her as a servant up to the last day of her servitude and then marry her on that day. Since if the original price is divided by the sum of all days of her servitude and only pennies pay for the service of one day, only pennies are left on the last day but nevertheless the marriage is legal.
