Therefore only pennies are needed for a legal marriage ceremony.</i> The claim, the House of Shammai say, an <i>obolos</i>, but the House of Hillel say, two <i>oboloi</i>. The argument of the House of Shammai seems inverted. There, they say, “silver” is a <i>denar</i>, but here, they say that “silver” is an <i>obolos</i>. The argument of the House of Hillel seems inverted. There, they say that “money” is a <i>peruṭah</i> but here, they say that “money” is two <i>oboloi</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: The House of Shammai learn from the initial sale of a Hebrew girl. Since her initial sale was by [at least] a <i>denar</i>, so her preliminary marriage is by [at least] a <i>denar</i>. The House of Hillel learn from the end of her diminution. Since the end of her diminution is a <i>peruṭah,</i> so her preliminary marriage is by a <i>peruṭah.</i> What is the reason of the House of Shammai? As it is said, <i>she leaves gratis, without silver</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:10" href="/Exodus.21.10"><i>Ex</i>. 21:10</a>.</i>. Would we not know that it is without money? Why does the verse say, <i>without silver</i>? From there, that she is sold for more than silver. And what is more than silver? A <i>denar</i>. But maybe “silver” is a <i>peruṭah</i>, more than silver two <i>peruṭot</i>. The smallest silver coin is an <i>obolos</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">“Silver” denotes the smallest silver coin struck in Hasmonean times, the <i>obolos</i>, <sup>1</sup>/<sub>6</sub> of a <i>denar</i>. The silver half- <i>oboloi</i> minted in Yehud in Persian times had long disappeared when the Mishnaic system of currency was formulated.</i>. So why is it not an <i>obolos</i>? Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Judah bar Pazi: For if she wants to diminish, she diminishes every year by an <i>obolos</i> and leaves. Could she not diminish by a <i>peruṭah</i>? Rebbi Abun said, think of it. If she wanted to compute the diminution at the start of the sixth year, there would be a <i>peruṭah</i> left. But the start of the diminution must be an <i>obolos</i>, the end of the diminution a <i>peruṭah</i>. If there is only one <i>peruṭah</i> left, can she not pay the diminished amount and leave? Just as the last diminished amount is a <i>peruṭah</i>, so her preliminary marriage should be a <i>peruṭah</i>! What is the reason of the House of Hillel? Since her last diminished amount is a <i>peruṭah</i>, you know that her preliminary marriage is by a <i>peruṭah</i>. Think of it, if there is only one <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth left, can she not pay the diminished amount and leave? Just as the last diminished amount is a <i>peruṭah</i>, so her preliminary marriage is by a <i>peruṭah</i>.
The argument of the House of Hillel seems inverted. <i>If a person give to his</i> <i>neighbor,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex.</i>22:6</a>.</i> etc. If to teach that the court will not act on less than a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth, is it not already written, <i>to incur liability for it</i>? To exclude anything not worth a <i>peruṭah</i>. From here, that it should be more than silver. And what is more than silver? Two <i>oboloi</i>. But maybe “silver” is a <i>peruṭah</i>, more than silver two <i>peruṭot</i>. The smallest silver coin is an <i>obolos</i>. So why is it not an <i>obolos</i>? <i>Or vessels</i>; since vessels are two, also “money” is two. How do the House of Shammai interpret <i>or vessels</i>? Following what Rebbi Nathan stated, <i>or vessels</i>, including clay vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.20"><i>Qiddušin</i>1:1 Note 96</a> for the arguments which show that this reading is impossible.</i>. Samuel said, if he claimed from him two needles and he admitted to one, he is liable. Rebbi Ḥinena said, only if they are worth two <i>peruṭot</i>, that the claim should be about a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth and the confession about a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Shevuot 39b:10:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Shevuot.39b.10.1"><i>Tosaphot</i>39b, <i>s.v</i>. מה</a>.</i>. This follows the House of Shammai who do not learn money’s worth from “vessels”. But following the House of Hillel who learn money’s worth from “vessels”, since vessels are two, also “money” is two. Similarly, since “money” means two <i>oboloi,</i> also “vessels” means two <i>oboloi</i>’s worth.
Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: In any case where two [witnesses] make him liable to pay money, a single witness sets him up for an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 40a" href="/Shevuot.40a">Babli, 40a</a>, this is a tannaitic statement commented upon by Samuel.</i>. But do not two [witnesses] make him liable for real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since anything can be decided upon the testimony of two witnesses, possession of real estate can be transferred without documentary proof by the testimony of witnesses. Similarly, real estate can be attached in foreclosure for unpaid fines upon the testimony of two witnesses.</i>? It is a difference since one does not swear about real estate. But do not two [witnesses] make him liable for a fine? There is a difference since one does not swear about a fine. But do not two [witnesses] make him liable for a <i>peruṭah</i>? Is it so? Have we not stated: “A judicial oath is about a claim of two silver coins and the acknowledgment of one <i>peruṭah</i>.” Our Mishnah, when he swears by his own formulation. What (Rebbi)<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A slip of the scribe’s pen; the first generation Samuel only had a medical, not a rabbinic degree.</i> Samuel said, when he swears by the formulation of others. Rav Ḥisda and his group disagree. “A judicial oath”, any judicial oath. There is no difference whether he swears by his own formulation or he swears by the formulation of others, he cannot be liable except for a claim<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whether the sentence is incomplete and one should add “of at least two <i>oboloi</i>”, or that only the situation of monetary claim and denial can be adjudicated by judicial oath, to support the opponents of R. Joḥanan in the next paragraph. The interpretation of the statement in the Babli has no relation to the discussion here.</i>.
“And the acknowledgment of one <i>peruṭah.</i>” Rebbi Joḥanan said, if somebody claims that another had stolen<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is about <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>, the basis of the Mishnah. A deposited something with B. B claims that the item was lost and as unpaid trustee he is not liable for damages. A accuses B of having appropriated the item for himself, i. e., to have stolen it. R. Joḥanan holds that this claim is no different from all other claims adjudicated under the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6-8" href="/Exodus.22.6-8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6–8</a> and, therefore, an oath can be imposed on B only if the latter acknowledges liability for part of the claim.</i>, the latter is not liable unless he partially admit. But all his colleagues<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 106b" href="/Bava_Kamma.106b">Babli (<i>Bava qamma</i>106b</a>, bottom) he is R. Ḥiyya bar Joseph, a student of Rav and member of R. Joḥanan’s court.</i> differ from him. How do the colleagues uphold <i>for this is it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a> reads: <i>About anything criminal, about an ox, about a donkey, about a sheep, about a garment, about anything lost, if he says, for this is it, the suit of them shall come before the Elohim, he whom the Elohim find guilty shall pay double to his neighbor. Elohim</i> means “the powerful;” it can be applied both to God and to judges. From this double meaning it is inferred that judges impose an oath before God on the accused if the latter has acknowledged <i>for this is it,</i> i. e., a partial admission. On the other hand, double restitution is the fine for the thief. Therefore R. Joḥanan is justified in his conclusion that since v.6 declares the entire paragraph to be about deposits, the entire sentence deals with the case of A accusing B of theft of the deposit.</i>? If he claims money from him. If he claims money from him, is that <i>double he shall pay to his neighbor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The colleagues agree that a fine can be imposed only for theft, but they hold that the clause <i>for this is it</i> does not apply to deposits but to repayment of loans and debts (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:24" href="/Leviticus.5.24"><i>Lev</i>. 5:24</a>). They have to take the position that this very long sentence deals with different subjects in different parts and that an oath is due on demand of the claimant for any accusation that a deposit was stolen.</i>? But this is a mixture of paragraphs.
Rebbi Ze`ira said, he is not liable unless the denial be of two silver coins not counting the acknowledgment of one <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Mishnah, after partial admission of any claim, the amount in dispute must be at least two <i>oboloi</i>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 39b" href="/Shevuot.39b">Babli, 39b</a>, this is the position of Rav, R. Ḥiyya bar Joseph’s teacher, disputed by Samuel who only requires the total claim to be for at least two <i>oboloi</i>.</i>. This follows Rebbi Joḥanan’s colleague.
“ ‘You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me’, ‘I am not holding anything for you’, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since the claim is rejected in its entirety, it must be proven by documents or witnesses; the oath cannot be imposed by biblical standards.</i>.” Even “you are holding a lot for me,” “I am not holding anything for you”, he is not liable? But the following: “You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me.” “I am holding only a <i>peruṭah</i> for you.” He is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 1, the sentence about the person who claims he owes only 50 denar seems redundant. It only is inserted to indicate that for the liability to swear, a <i>mina</i> is no different from two <i>oboloi</i>. If the defendant admits to owing a <i>peruṭah</i> and the claim is at least 2 <i>oboloi</i>, there is liability. The lower bound is not a function of the size of the claim.</i>. Rav and Rebbi Joḥanan both are saying: Only if he made the loan in the presence of witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that undocumented loans never can trigger liability for an oath. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 41a" href="/Shevuot.41a">Babli 41a</a> (in the name of Rav only.)</i>. But if he made the loan not in the presence of witnesses, he could tell him, you gave me a loan but I returned half of it. Rebbi Yudan said, in money matters one does not argue “because.” Because he could have told him, you did not give me a loan, he could tell him, you gave me a loan but I returned half of it<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, such an argument is called מִגּוֹ “because”. In the absence of witnesses or documents, if the defendant presents an argument which is less favorable to him than another argument which he could have advanced, the court is forced to give him the benefit of doubt and free him from the oath. It will be seen in Halakhah 7:1 that such arguments are acceptable in general, supporting Rav and R. Joḥanan against R. Yudan.</i>? The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “ ‘You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me;’ he said ‘yes’<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In front of witnesses.</i>. The next day he said, ‘return it to me,’ ‘I returned it to you;’ he is not liable. ‘I am not holding anything for you,’ he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since he had agreed in the presence of witnesses that he owes the money, he cannot change his confession later; he does not swear but has to pay. This last sentence is not in the Mishnah of the Yerushalmi but appears in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss., including Maimonides’s autograph. The Halakhah shows that it should also be read in the Mishnah.</i>.”
“`You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me,’ ‘I am not holding anything for you.’ ” Issy<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 41a" href="/Shevuot.41a">Babli 41a</a>. In the Babli, he always appears as Rav Assi even though his ordination preceded that of Rav for whom the title “Rav” was invented.</i> said, one who gives a loan to another in the presence of witnesses should claim repayment only in the presence of witnesses. Rebbi Avin said, the word of Issy: If one gives a loan to another in the presence of witnesses, [the debtor] should not repay except in the presence of witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This is not an obligation of the lender’s as implied by Issy’s formulation, but advice to the borrower to protect himself from double claims by the lender.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with him: “ ‘You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me;’ he said ‘yes’<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In front of witnesses.</i>. The next day he said, ‘return it to me,’ ‘I returned it to you;’ he is not liable. ‘I am not holding anything for you,’ he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">28,</sup><i class="footnote">Since he had agreed in the presence of witnesses that he owes the money, he cannot change his confession later; he does not swear but has to pay. This last sentence is not in the Mishnah of the Yerushalmi but appears in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss., including Maimonides’s autograph. The Halakhah shows that it should also be read in the Mishnah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since the second part of the Mishnah explicitly mentions payment in front of witnesses, the first part must assume that payment was not made before witnesses, even though the admission of the debt before witnesses is equivalent to a loan given before witnesses. The Mishnah contradicts Issy’s formulation; it is compatible with R. Avin’s. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 41a" href="/Shevuot.41a">Babli, 41a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 18a" href="/Ketubot.18a"><i>Ketubot</i>18a</a>, explicitly notes that repayment in the presence of witnesses is not a legal requirement.</i>.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me;” he said “yes”<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2.</i>. The next day he said, “return it to me,” “I returned it to you;” he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Since he already admitted his entire obligation in the presence of witnesses, there is no occasion for an oath. It is up to the claimant to prove the defendant’s guilt by witnesses or documents.</i>. “You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me;” he said “yes.” “Return it to me only in the presence of witnesses.” The next day he said, “return it to me,” “I returned it to you;” he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">To pay the entire sum, see the preceding Note.</i> since he is required to return it in the presence of witnesses.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me;” etc. Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to be identical with R. Avin quoted in the preceding paragraph (which also refers to Mishnah 2) and the father of R. Yose ben R. Bun.</i> said, it is usual for people to give loans to others not in the presence of witnesses but to ask for repayment in the presence of witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is not legally necessary, it is recommended that requests for repayment be made in front of witnesses to obtain admission by the debtor of the existence of the debt as presupposed in the Mishnah.</i>.
“You are holding a <i>mina</i> for me;” he said “yes.” “Return it to me only in the presence of X and Y.” The next day he said, “return it to me,” “I returned it to you;” he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">To pay the entire sum, see the preceding Note.</i> since he is required to return it in the presence of witnesses. Think of it, if he said, I returned it in the presence of witnesses. Bar Kappara stated: “when X and Y are standing there.” Think of it, if he said, “when X were Y are standing there.” Rebbi Abba, Rav Hamnuna, Rebbi Ada bar Ahava<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is not legally necessary, it is recommended that requests for repayment be made in front of witnesses to obtain admission by the debtor of the existence of the debt as presupposed in the Mishnah.</i> in the name of Rav: A case came before Rebbi, and he said, let X and Y come<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The questions are besides the point. If there were witnesses, it is not a question of whether a statement of the defendant can be believed, nor whether he should swear; it is up to the court to listen to the witnesses. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 41b" href="/Shevuot.41b">Babli, 41b</a>, the question is raised if the return was made in the presence of witnesses but not those specified by the lender. It seems that the formulation by Bar Kappara requires return only in the presence of the specified witnesses.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The following does not concern oaths but judicial rules concerning real estate. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 169b" href="/Bava_Batra.169b">Babli <i>Bava batra</i>169b</a>.</i> If somebody produced both a document and <i>ḥazaqah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">“Grasping”, a legal term with different meanings in different domains of law (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i>5:5 Note 100</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.9"><i>Qiddušin</i>1:1 Note 30</a>.) It can mean taking possession by active use, but here it means a presumption of permanence of an established <i>status quo ante</i>. The particular application meant here is validation of squatter’s rights after three years of uncontested occupation (which must be combined with a claim, not necessarily proven, of rightful acquisition, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.3.2"><i>Bava qamma</i>7:4 Note 49</a>.)</i>. Rebbi says, it should be judged by the document. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, it should be judged by <i>ḥazaqah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously all means of proof should be admitted; the question is which should be given the greater weight in the judges’ deliberations.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Jeremiah: A case came before Rebbi Jehudah following Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The text is questionable since “R. Jehudah” clearly is Rebbi, (R. Jehudah ben Simeon) as follows from the sequel, and the sentence is missing a verb. Probably one should read: Rebbi judged following Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze`ira: How? Did Rebbi switch his opinion? There he said, it should be judged by the document. But here he said it should be judged by <i>ḥazaqah</i>. Would we say, before he changed his opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rebbi was a student of his father Rabban Simeon, he certainly started out following the latter’s opinion and only later developed his own, differing, approach.</i>? Even if you say, after he changed his opinion. Rebbi only wanted to determine the truth of the matter<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Theoretical preferences should never impede the search for the truth. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 170" href="/Bava_Batra.170">Babli <i>Bava batra</i>170a/b</a>.</i>. “If somebody occupied real estate on the basis of a document of sale<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὠνή “document of sale”.</i> and it was found invalid, this is not <i>ḥazaqah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.2.4">Note 38</a>, proof of legal possession by proof of undisturbed occupation is admissible only if based on a claim of legal acquisition. This claim does not necessarily have to be proven; the person in possession might claim, e. g., either that he inherited under intestate rules or that the sale document was eaten by the rats. But if he tries to prove his claim by presenting a forged document, any claim of <i>ḥazaqah</i> based on the document must be thrown out by the court. The only problem is whether he should be allowed to plead a substitute claim.</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta <i>Bava batra</i>2:2</a>.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah said, in dispute<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jeremiah (the fourth generation Amora, not the first generation Rav Jeremiah) thinks that Rebbi would prohibit any substitute claim while Rabban Simeon would allow an examination of proof of undisturbed occupation even after rejection of the document.</i>. Rebbi Samuel said, Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abinna: it is everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If the document proving the claim is found invalid, the claim of squatter’s rights is also invalidated. In this Rebbi and Rabban Simeon agree.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, there if one produced both a document and <i>ḥazaqah.</i> Here if he came with a document<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between Rebbi and his father is about a case where both a document and a claim of <i>ḥazaqah</i> are presented to the court, where either one alone would be sufficient. The Tosephta deals with a case where a forged document was produced and only after it was rejected a claim of <i>ḥazaqah</i> was entered.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, there if he presented two groups of witnesses; here one who contradicts his own proofs<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi and Rabban Simeon disagree on how to handle a case where the claimant produces two sets of witnesses testifying to different aspects of the case. The Tosephta states that after a claim of documentary proof has been rejected, a subsequent claim which is logically inconsistent with the first one will not be considered by the court.</i>.
Rebbi Marinus was guarantor for his daughter-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">He warranted all claims she might have against her husband from the marriage contract; Babli <i>Bava meṣia`</i>17a.</i>. They went to court before Rebbi Ḥama the father of Bat Qappara and Rebbi Hoshaia<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This must read: R. Ḥama the father of R. Hoshaia and Bar Qappara. The latter’s father was R. Eleazar haQappar.</i>. After he had admitted he said to him, I gave. They asked the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya; Rebbi Ḥiyya asked Rebbi: One who became liable in court cannot be trusted. What is the meaning of “he cannot be trusted”? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he pays on his own initiative, he is trusted when he says, I gave. But from the orders of others, he cannot be trusted<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The question here and in the next case is, on whom is the burden of proof. Even though the rule is that “the burden of proof is on the claimant,” since his daughter-in-law had to go to court against him, he has to prove (by witnesses or a receipt) that he paid. Had he not initially denied his responsibility, the burden of proof would have been on the daughter- in-law.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said, also for oaths it is the same. What is the meaning of “also for oaths it is the same”? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he swears on his own initiative, he is trusted when he says, I swore. But from the orders of others, he cannot be trusted.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the condition that “the acknowledgment must be of the kind of the claim.”</i>“You are holding for me a pound<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λίτρα, a Roman <i>libra</i> of twelve (Troy) ounces.</i> of gold,” “I am holding for you only a pound of silver;” he is not liable. “You are holding for me a gold <i>denar</i>,” “I am holding for you only a silver <i>denar</i>, or a <i>tressis</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">With the good Mishnah mss. (Kaufmann ms. and Maimonides’s autograph) read: וטריסית. <i>Tressis</i>“three <i>as;</i> something of little value.”.</i>, or a <i>dupondius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">“Two <i>as</i>; two bits”.</i>, or a <i>peruṭah</i>,” he is liable since all kinds of coin<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Even if for a claim of a gold <i>denar</i> the defendant admits only a debt of one <i>peruṭah</i>, 1/4800, it is a valid admission which makes the defendant liable for an oath.</i> are one.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “You are holding for me a pound of gold,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ma`aser šeni</i>5:5. In the opinion of S. Lieberman, the Tosephta is quoted here to indicate that the rules for Second Tithe and judicial oaths are different.<br>Second Tithe is the part of the harvest which the farmer should eat with his family at the place of the Sanctuary. If this is not practical, the crop can be redeemed and the sanctity transferred to the coins which then have to be spent in purity at the place of the Sanctuary. Problems arise when in the wallet chosen for the sanctified coins other coins are found which do not directly correspond to the farmer’s memory.</i>“Eight of gold<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Gold coins, but seemingly different from the standard gold <i>denar</i>(or, after Diocletian’s currency reform, the <i>solidus</i>.)</i> I have in the wallet and one finds there eight gold <i>denarii</i>; or fifty t<i>etradrachmas</i> and one finds 200 <i>denar</i>; or 200 <i>denar</i> and one finds 50 <i>tetradrachmas</i>; these are profane. Eight gold <i>denarii</i> I have in the wallet and one finds fifty <i>tetradrachmas</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">A gold <i>denar</i> usually is counted as 25 silver <i>ii</i>. 8 gold <i>denarii</i> are 200 silver <i>denarii</i> or 50 <i>tetradrachmas</i>.</i>; or 200 <i>denar</i> and one finds 50 <i>tetradrachmas</i>, these are Second [Tithe].” “ ‘You are holding for me a pound of gold,’ ‘I am holding for you only a pound of silver;’ he is not liable. ‘You are holding for me a gold <i>denar</i>,’ ‘I am holding for you only a silver <i>denar</i>.’<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be not a copy of the Mishnah but a text similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.5.4">Tosephta <i>Ševuot</i>5:8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.5.4">9</a>. The background text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.5.4">Tosephta 5:9</a>: “‘You are holding for me a gold <i>denar</i> as gold coin,’ ‘I am holding for you only a silver <i>denar</i>, or a <i>tressis</i>, or a <i>dupondius</i>, or a <i>peruṭah</i>,’ he is liable.” The incomplete sentence in the Halakhah seems to be a text, not found in the Tosephta, “ ‘You are holding for me a gold <i>denar</i>,’ ‘I am holding for you only a silver <i>denar,</i>’ he is not liable.” The formulation of the text points to the first two Amoraic generations during the military anarchy in the Roman Empire, when the gold denar was a unit of accounting but not an actual coin. Then the status of a claim for a gold <i>denar</i> is similar to the claim for a pound of gold; if it is answered by an admission of silver units it is not an admission of the kind of the claim. But a claim for an actual gold coin which is answered by an admission of silver or bronze coins is an admission of the kind of the claim. In the rabbinic literature of the Middle Ages, זָהוּב is the name of the standard gold coin of the author’s country.</i>” Rebbi Jacob bar Abinna said, earlier, we were saying, “a gold <i>denar</i>” is a detail; “gold <i>denarii</i>” are not detail. If he had said, “a gold <i>denar</i>, a gold coin” it would have been correct<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">To hold the person liable who admits owing a silver <i>denar</i> when a gold coin was claimed.</i>. It was found stated, “a gold coin of a <i>denar</i>,” he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: He is liable; the same in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 40a" href="/Shevuot.40a">Babli, 40a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “You are holding for me a <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical and Accadic measure of volume, 30 <i>seah</i>.</i> of grain;” “I am holding for you only a <i>letek</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">A biblical measure of volume, half a <i>kor</i>.</i> of legumes;” he is not liable. “You are holding for me a <i>kor</i> of produce;” “I am holding for you only a <i>letek</i> of legumes;” he is liable since legumes are subsumed under produce. If one sued for wheat and the other acknowledged barley, he is not liable, but Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In all other sources, including the following Halakhah: Rabban Gamliel. He considers all grain to be of related kind.</i> declares him liable. Somebody who sued for amphoras of oil and the other admitted to pitchers, Admon says since he admitted of the kind of the claim he has to swear. But the Sages say, the admission is not of the kind of the claim<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Admon (an authority of pre-rabbinic times) holds that the content of the vessels is determining; the Sages give preference to the determination of the vessels of storage.</i>. Rabban Gamliel said, I see<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">To see = to accept as obvious.</i> the words of Admon.<br>If somebody sued for both vessels and real estate; if [the other] admitted the vessels but denied the real estate, or the real estate and denied the vessels, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">One does not swear on claims of real estate (Mishnah 1). Therefore if the defendant acknowledged his debt of vessels, there is nothing left to take the oath. If he disputes all claims to vessels, there is no acknowledgment since admission of the duty to hand over real estate does not count.</i>. If he admitted part of the real estate he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">One does not swear on claims of real estate (Mishnah 1). Therefore if the defendant acknowledged his debt of vessels, there is nothing left to take the oath. If he disputes all claims to vessels, there is no acknowledgment since admission of the duty to hand over real estate does not count.</i>. Part of the vessels he is liable since non-guaranteed property obligates guaranteed property to be sworn about<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kiddushin 1:5" href="/Mishnah_Kiddushin.1.5">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i>1:5, Note 474</a>. If the defendant is liable to take an oath, the claimant can add to the oath all of his outstanding claims even if they in themselves would not force an oath. This is known as “rolling over of oaths.”<br>A property is guaranteed if the seller as a matter of routine must guarantee the title to the property; this is the case with real estate.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “You are holding for me a <i>kor</i> of grain;” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A copy of this Halakhah is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.4.2"><i>Ketubot</i>13:4</a>, fully explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:5:1-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.5.1-3">Notes 79–89</a>. A parallel is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 40a" href="/Shevuot.40a">Babli 40a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 108b" href="/Ketubot.108b"><i>Ketubot</i>108b</a>.</i> Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Our Mishnah if he sued him for wheat and he admitted barley; but if he sued him for two kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Unrelated matters.</i> and he admitted one of them, everybody agrees that he is not liable. Rebbi Abbahu said about that of Rebbi Immi: Why did we state, “Rabban Gamliel declares him liable”? It comes to inform you of the power of Rabban Gamliel, how far he declares liable. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Not only if he claimed wheat and he admitted barley to him, but if he claimed two kinds and he admitted one of them, the words of the Sages are that he is not liable. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is only if he claimed two kinds and he admitted one of them, but if he sued for wheat and he admitted barley according to everybody he is liable. Following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: A person went to court before Rav: When he claimed from another wheat, barley, and spelt. Rav told him, wait until he charged you with anything he has to charge, and in the end you will swear one oath about everything. Rebbi Abbahu said, so argued Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish against Rebbi Joḥanan: In your opinion, since you say that if he claimed two kinds and he admitted one of them, according to the Sages he is not liable, did we not state “if he claimed vessels and real estate; if he admitted the vessels and denied the real estate, the real estate and denied the vessels”? For vessels about vessels he is liable, not so much more for vessels and real estate? He answered him, to transfer an oath to him by the following Mishnah, “since guaranteed property obligates non-guaranteed property to be sworn about.” This does not even disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. But did we not state: “Somebody who sued for amphoras of oil and he admitted to vessels”? How do we hold? If he sued him for vessels and oil, and he admitted one of them, everybody agrees that this refers to the claim. But if he sued him for vessels and he did (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This has to be deleted.</i> admit oil, everybody agrees that what he admitted did not refer to the claim. Rebbi Ze`ira and Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Samuel: If he sued for vessels full of oil, one said vessels but not oil, the other said oil but not vessels. (Thrown)<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">For the inappropriate נידה read ניחא “it is understandable”.</i> vessels but not oil. Oil but not vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Oil not in vessels is lost.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Abin formulates it as oil buckets. Can one not ask him? If he was paralyzed<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between Admon and the Sages is one of semantics; any judge could immediately resolve the question by asking the parties what they meant. The only occasion for the disagreement is if one of the parties cannot be interrogated since he became paralyzed.</i>.
So is the Mishnah: If he denied part of the real estate he is liable, part of the vessels he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that the mentions of “real estate” and “vessels” have to be switched since one does not swear about claims to real estate. It is pointed out that the entire suit is not about what the defendant admitted but what he denied. There is an oath if the denial is partial, not if it is complete.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not swear on the claim of a deaf-and-dumb person, an insane person, or a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">These persons have no standing in court; they cannot bring any action.</i>. One does not put an oath on a minor but one swears for a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This oath is not the judicial oath based on a biblical decree but the rabbinic oath (Mishnah 7:8) instituted for creditors of orphans who want to collect debts incurred by their deceased father. They have to swear that the debt was not paid.</i> and the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple is not a person, cannot bring a suit, or be sued. But if a person dedicates all his property to the Temple, then the creditor can claim to be paid before the Temple may take possession. He has to swear an oath similar to that instituted for creditors of orphans.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not swear on the claim of a deaf-and-dumb person, an insane person, or a minor,” etc. It is written: <i>If a man give to his neighbor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>.</i>, to exclude the minor<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since a minor is not able to act in law, anything the minor may give does not leave his guardian’s power. Since a minor cannot legally give a deposit, he cannot reclaim it nor ask for an oath in connection with such a deposit. The Yerushalmi <i>Ma`aser šeni</i>4:4 (Note 67, <i>Eruvin</i>7:6) finds this in the first words of v. 6, <i>if a man give</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 42a" href="/Shevuot.42a">Babli 42a</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i>22:6.</i>. So far if a minor gave to him and the minor requested from him. If he gave it as a minor and requested it as an adult? The verse says, <i>his neighbor;</i> only if giving and requesting are equal<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">If the giving is legal, the request for an oath is legal; this excludes the giving of a deposit by a minor, which is not legal. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 106b" href="/Bava_Kamma.106b">Babli (<i>Bava qamma</i>106b</a>) and the <i>Mekhilta</i> derive this from v. 8, <i>before the judges shall come their mutual affair</i>; the oath is possible only if both parties have the same standing.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, is that not obvious by what Rebbi Joḥanan said, one who claims a claim of theft in respect of his neighbor’s lost object is liable? “Where is my lost object?” He told him, it was stolen<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah 8:7; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 106b" href="/Bava_Kamma.106b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>106b</a>. A lost an object with enough unique features that the finder would have been required to publicly ask for its owner to come and reclaim it. B found the object. A has witnesses who saw B taking the object. When A comes to ask B, the latter claims that it was stolen. Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a> lists lost objects as subjects of judicial oaths, it is clear that B has to swear upon A’s request even though A never handed the object to B. This excludes an interpretation like that given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.5.2">Note 83</a>. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 79b" href="/Yoma.79b">Babli <i>Yoma</i>79b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 48b" href="/Yevamot.48b"><i>Yebamot</i>48b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 8a" href="/Sotah.8a"><i>Soṭah</i>8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 17a" href="/Zevachim.17a"><i>Zevaḥim</i>17a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 90a" href="/Zevachim.90a">90a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 94b" href="/Zevachim.94b">94b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 69b" href="/Menachot.69b"><i>Menaḥot</i>69b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 3b" href="/Keritot.3b"><i>Keritut</i>3b</a>).</i>. Rebbi Abba said, explain it if he told him, you already asked me when you were underage and I was freed from swearing for you<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">An adult can ask for an oath regarding a deposit which he made underage only if the respondent does not claim that he already asked for the deposit back when he was still underage and unable to force an oath.</i>. <i>An oath of the Eternal shall be between both of them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:10" href="/Exodus.22.10"><i>Ex</i>. 22:10</a>.</i>, to exclude the heir<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Only the original parties have enough knowledge of the transaction to be able to swear. Heirs can only swear rabbinic oaths, to state that their father did not inform them that the claims were moot or similar formulations. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Mišpaṭim</i>16.</i>. Rebbi Illa in the name of Rebbi Yasa: The <i>baraita</i> is about the heir<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> explaining the verse <i>between them</i> is directed also to the heirs. Since normally only the defendant has to swear, the expression <i>between them</i> is interpreted as biblical endorsement of the rule that if the defendant is disqualified as a witness he also is disqualified from swearing; in that case the claimant has to swear that he is entitled to the money. Heirs, who cannot swear in cases of claims against the father’s estate, can as claimants force oaths of debtors to the estate. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 47b" href="/Shevuot.47b">Babli 47b</a>.</i>.
<i>Between both of them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:10" href="/Exodus.22.10"><i>Ex</i>. 22:10</a>.</i> it will never move from between them. If the one forcing the oath forces the oath lying, at the end it<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The oath will destroy him.</i> will come over him. If the one swearing swears lying, at the end it will come over him. What is the reason? <i>I shall take it out, oracle of the Eternal Sabaot, and it will come into the house of the thief and the house of him who swears falsely in My Name</i><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 5:5" href="/Zechariah.5.5"><i>Sach</i>. 5:5</a>. “It” is the curse mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 5:4" href="/Zechariah.5.4">v. 4</a>.</i>, etc. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said, destructive angels have no joints. What is the reason? <i>From roving about the Earth and walking there</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 2:2" href="/Job.2.2"><i>Job</i>2:2</a>, Satan’s answer. Having no joints at knees or ankles, he could never sit down. The order of topics is better in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 6:1" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.6.1"><i>Lev. rabba</i>6(1</a>) where it is explained that the Satan has to move perpetually because he has no joints but the curse of a false oath comes and dwells at length in the house of the swearer.</i>. But here, <i>it will destroy its wood and its stones</i><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 5:5" href="/Zechariah.5.5"><i>Sach</i>. 5:5</a>. “It” is the curse mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 5:4" href="/Zechariah.5.4">v. 4</a>.</i>. Come and see, things which the fire will not burn, a vain oath will destroy. Rebbi Jonah said, about falsehood. Rebbi Yose said, even on truth. <sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The Aramaic of the following paragraph shows that this is a sermon, rather than a halakhic discussion. In slightly different form <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 6:1" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.6.1"><i>Lev. rabba</i>6(1</a>).</i>Ḥaggai was preaching following Rebbi Yose. There was a case about a woman who went to knead dough with another and had two <i>denarii</i> bound into the seam of her head cover. These fell out and were rolled into a loaf. She went back to he house, wanted them and did not find them. She returned to her and said to her, give me the two denarii which fell from me in your house. She told her, I do not know. If she<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">As always in rabbinic literature, in relating bad things about oneself one always used the third person (“this man, this woman”) instead of the first. The woman must have used an oath formula to be punished for vain oaths.</i> would know of it, she should bury her son. She buried him. When they returned from his grave, she heard a voice saying, if she had not known about them she would not have buried him. She said, if this woman<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">As always in rabbinic literature, in relating bad things about oneself one always used the third person (“this man, this woman”) instead of the first. The woman must have used an oath formula to be punished for vain oaths.</i> knows about them, she should bury her other son. She buried him. They came to console her and cut one loaf and found two denarii rolled into the loaf. This says, whether innocent or guilty, never swear an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">It is better to pay, or not to sue and not collect money, than force or swear any oath.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> About the following one does not swear: slaves, and documents<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">It is better to pay, or not to sue and not collect money, than force or swear any oath.</i>, and real estate, and gifts to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Most of these will be shown in the Halakhah to be outside the biblical criteria. The Temple cannot ask for an oath since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a> restricts the judicial oath to transactions between human persons.</i>. Not about double restitution, nor quadruple or quintuple; the unpaid trustee does not swear, the paid trustee does not pay<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The statements (also in Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>4:8) refer to the subjects about which one does not swear. Cf. Mishnah 8:1.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, one swears about things<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">In all other sources, including <i>Šeqalim</i>2:1, the reading is: sacrifices. If a person makes a vow “to sacrifice <i>an</i> animal”, and if then the designated animal is lost or develops a defect, he has to provide a replacement; this is a vow implying a guarantee. But if he vowed “to sacrifice <i>this</i> animal,” and anything happens to the designated animal, he does not have to provide a substitute; there is no guarantee.</i> for which he is responsible if they be alienated, but does not swear if he is not responsible if they be alienated.<br>Rebbi Meїr says, there are things which are like real estate and they are not like real estate but the Sages do not agree with him. How is this? “Ten bearing vines<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">To be harvested. R. Meїr considers ripe fruit as already harvested and therefore movable, the Sages as part of the tree as long as they are hanging on the tree.</i> did I hand over to you,” but the other says, “they are only five.” Rebbi Meїr declares liable for an oath but the Sages say, anything connected to the ground is like ground.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “About the following one does not swear,” etc. If he transgressed<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">He swore about a claim to one of the items excluded by Mishnah 7 from being settled by oaths.</i> and swore, Rebbi Joḥanan said he brings a sacrifice for the oath<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since the oath was illegitimate, it is treated as “blurted oath”.</i>; Rebbi Eleazar says, he does not bring. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, so does Rebbi Johanan answer Rebbi Eleazar. No. If you pronounced about an oath about testimony which may apply to slaves, documents, and real estate, what can you say about oaths concerning deposits which do not apply to slaves, documents, and real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This argument is very elliptic. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a> notes that oaths about testimony have no restrictions on the topics covered, whereas oaths about deposits have almost no restrictions on the people subject to the oath. Where they are equal is that people suspected of perjury are not allowed to swear (Mishnah 7:4).</i>? But an oath for testimony is not in court<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:3. On the other hand, an oath about deposits is possible only in court.</i>. Would the court admit an oath of a suspect person<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since oaths for testimony are not administered by the court, it may easily happen that the oath is wrongly applied to a person either disqualified as a relative or as a suspect (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.5.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i>3:6</a>). But how could an oath about deposits be administered by the court to a disqualified person or about a disqualified subject? It can only happen if the person swears without waiting for instructions from the court, which characterizes his action as a blurted oath subject to all its rules.</i>? Only when he transgressed and swore, you say that he brings a sacrifice for his oath<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since the oath was illegitimate, it is treated as “blurted oath”.</i>; here also if he transgressed and swore, you say that he brings a sacrifice for his oath<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since the oath was illegitimate, it is treated as “blurted oath”.</i>.
Some want to understand it from here<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The exclusions from oaths about deposits enumerated in the Mishnah.</i>: <i>An ox, or a donkey, or a sheep</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a>, about the oath of the paid trustee. Breaking, kidnapping, and dying are all mentioned in the verse.</i>. Since these are particular in that they are subject to breaking, kidnapping, and dying, this excludes real estate which is not subject to breaking, kidnapping, and dying. I shall exclude real estate but shall not exclude slaves. Since these are particular in that they are subject to a fine, this excludes slaves which are not subject to a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The person who kidnaps a slave does not have to pay the double restitution exacted from a thief.</i>. I shall exclude slaves but shall not exclude documents since they are subject to fines. Since these are particular in that they are subject to overcharging, this excludes documents which are not subject to overcharging<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The thief of documents has to pay double restitution for the value of the paper on which the documents are written. On the other hand, documents are not traded as commodities but for the financial values of the contracts written on them. Commodities are subject to the rules of overcharging or underpaying; a transaction which differs more than +/- 16 ⅔% from the going market rate can be voided on the request of the injured party. Financial speculation, such as over the counter stock or bond trades, are not protected by this law (which is based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14"><i>Lev</i>. 25:14</a>.)</i>. From here they say, if somebody sells documents to the spice trader<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">If an IOU has been paid, it can be sold as packaging material; then it is a commodity. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 56b" href="/Bava_Metzia.56b">Babli <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>56b</a>, where the <i>baraita</i> is quoted as proof that the laws of overcharging apply to penny matters.</i>, it is subject to claims of overcharging. Rebbi Ismael explained: <i>Or a person, if he would swear</i>, a general statement. <i>To cause evil or cause good</i>, a detail. <i>Anything which a person will blurt out</i>, a repeat general statement. A general statement followed by a detail followed by a general statement, you only argue in the pattern of the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 8:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.8.2.3">Halakhah 8:3, Note 83</a>. However, this reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a> is out of place since the argument is about <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a>. The text should be similar to that of <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Mišpaṭim</i>15 (pp. 300–301): <i>About anything criminal</i>, a general statement. <i>About an ox, or a donkey, or a sheep,</i> detail<i>.</i> <i>About anything lost</i>, a repeat general statement.</i>. Since the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a>.</i> is about monetary claims, [originating] from himself, which can be collected by court order, having monetary value, which is a fixed value, and are movables, and one is not liable for punishment and fines. Monetary claims, excluding one who said to another, give me the 200 <i>denar</i> which you promised me but never gave<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">A promise does not generate an enforceable claim.</i>. [Originating] from himself, excluding one who said to another, you cursed me and shamed me, who is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since the paradigm is a deposit made by the claimant, the defendant’s oath is only about actions initiated by the claimant. Suits about matters initiated by the defendant’s actions cannot be settled by oaths.</i>. Which can be collected by court order, excluding one who said to another, you raped or seduced X’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The only persons who can go to court are the woman or her father.</i>. It has monetary value, excluding documents<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Since the value of the document is not determined by the cost of the paper on which it is written.</i>. Which is a fixed value, excluding slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The value of the slave largely depends on his education, which has no standard value attached to it.</i>. Movables, excluding real estate. One is not liable for punishment, excluding one who said to another, you set fire to my grain stack on the Sabbath; who is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">No money can be recovered for actions which carry the death penalty, even if there are no eye witnesses and therefore no court procedures are possible.</i>. One is not liable for fines, excluding double, quadruple, or quintuple restitution which are fines<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Only simple restitution of stolen property is within the purview of a civil court. Fines can be imposed only by a duly authorized criminal court where trials by oath are impossible.</i>.
Somebody tears up another’s documents without the latter’s agreement. Rebbi Ḥanania and Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">These are either R. Ḥanina of Sepphoris and R. Mana I or R. Ḥinena and R. Mana II. In the next paragraph appears R. Mana II, two generations after R. Immi. The rule is attributed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 98" href="/Bava_Kamma.98">Babli, <i>Bava qamma</i>98a/b</a>, to Rava who is a known adaptor of Galilean rules and an older contemporary of R. Mana II.</i>, one said, he is liable; the other said, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that he has to pay for the value of the paper or parchment which he destroyed. The problem is whether he has to pay for the mortgages for which the documents were written (assuming that there are no witnesses available who could write a replacement document.)</i>. The one who said he is liable, as a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Since destruction of the document involves both the destruction of the writing material (for which payment can be forced in court if the value was more than a <i>peruṭah</i>) but also the lien on the debtor’s property which is not something recoverable in biblical law. It has to be dealt with in local police law.</i>. The one who said he is not liable: he is like one who obstructs the depositions of witnesses. A widow who grabs documents is like a person who ties up the mouths of witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Since even an existing mortgage document can be used for foreclosure only if in court it can be proven to be genuine by the witnesses to the document or witnesses to the identities of the signatures affixed to it. While suborning perjury is criminal, we find no statute against physically preventing witnesses from appearing in court.</i>.
Rebbi Immi said, those who write, “on condition that I have the right to seize movables,” cannot collect<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">While there is freedom in stipulating money terms in contracts (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.8.5"><i>Ketubot</i>5:10, Note 227</a>), this is valid only in matters that do not involve court actions. In both Talmudim, court-ordered foreclosures are restricted to real estate. Therefore, a contract which provides foreclosure of movables, while valid, cannot be enforced. Only in the Middle Ages, when Jews in many cases could not hold real estate, was the restriction to real estate lifted by Gaonic decree.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, if he writes and declares, “even though the Court will not enforce,” he collects<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">If the involvement of the court is denied, there remains a private contract in money matters with unlimited powers of stipulation. While no foreclosure can be obtained from any court, the delinquent party can be judicially censured for not keeping his word (<i>Bava meṣi`a</i>4:2).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">This has a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.2.4"><i>Bava qamma</i>1:2, Notes 118–122</a> (<b>ק</b>). The Mishnah has to be understood that R. Simeon only frees from liability for an oath which is applicable only to disagreements between humans, but not for payment which was fixed at 125% for concealed debts against humans in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-25" href="/Leviticus.5.20-25"><i>Lev.</i>5:20–25</a>, and for amounts due to God in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-15" href="/Leviticus.5.14-15"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–15</a>. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 25" href="/Bava_Metzia.25">Babli <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>25a/b</a>).</i>“Rebbi Simeon says, both most holy and simple <i>sancta;</i> about any <i>sancta</i> for which he is responsible if alienated, I am reading <i>against his neighbor and he lied</i>; but about any <i>sancta</i> for which he is not responsible if alienated, I am reading <i>against the Eternal and he lied</i>.” Rav Huna said, both qualified and disqualified <i>sancta,</i> if he is responsible if alienated, even if they are for the Eternal, I am reading <i>against his neighbor and he lied</i>; but if he is not responsible if alienated, I am reading <i>against the Eternal and he lied</i> but not <i>against his neighbor and he lied</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One swears only on matters of measure, or weight, or count. How is that? “I handed over to you a full house; or I handed over to you a full wallet,” But the other says, “I do not know; take what you left with me;” he is not liable. One says, up to the gutter; the other says, up to the window; he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is a measurable difference.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of Mishnah 9.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, One says, a large candelabra, but the other says, a small candelabra; he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Since “large” and “small” are properties defined by measurement, the claim was a claim by measure. On the other hand, since a candelabra cannot be partially small or large, the admission was not of the kind of the claim.</i> since we have stated: “One swears only on matters of measure, or weight, or count.” Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, explain it if it was a candelabra composed of sections<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The candelabra can be disassembled and reassembled in different sizes; the admission was of the kind of the claim; R. Ḥanina’s statement is unquestionably correct. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 43a" href="/Shevuot.43a">Babli 43a</a>.</i>. One says, a large belt; the other says, a small belt. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated something but we do not know what he stated. If you say “liable”, a Mishnah disagrees, as we have stated: “One swears only on matters of measure, or weight, or count.<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, “small” and “large” do refer to two substantially different entities; the admission is partial admission of a claim referring to things measurable.</i>” If you say “not liable”, a Mishnah disagrees, as we have stated: “If one says the adult one and the other says the underage one, let the seller swear that he sold the underage one. If both say that they do not know, they shall split.<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>8:5. The buyer bought one of two slaves, one adult and one underage, with their garments or other property. There is a dispute about which slave was sold. Even though there is no oath about sales of slaves, there is an oath about their garments or other possessions, and an oath about the sale of the slave can be attached to that oath by rollover (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.4.1">Note 70</a>.) We found a case where Mishnaiot <i>Ševuot</i>6:9 and <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>8:5 present an irreconcilable contradiction.</i>”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One gave a loan to another on a pledge and the pledge was lost. He told him, “I loaned you a <i>tetradrachma</i> and it was worth a šeqel,” but the other one says “no, a <i>tetradrachma</i> you loaned me and it was worth a <i>tetradrachma</i>”; he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor asks for a <i>šeqel</i>(half a <i>tetradrachma</i>), the debtor refuses. He denies the entire claim; the burden of proof is on the claimant.</i>.<br>“I loaned you a <i>tetradrachma</i> and it was worth a šeqel,” but the other one says “no, a <i>tetradrachma</i> you loaned me and it was worth three <i>šeqel</i>;” he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor agrees that he owes a <i>denar</i>; he admits part of the claim and has to take the oath.</i>.<br>“You loaned me a <i>tetradrachma</i> on it but it was worth two,” and the other says “no, but I loaned you a <i>tetradrachma</i> and it was worth a <i>tetradrachma</i>;” he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor asks for a <i>tetradrachma</i>, the creditor denies it completely; the creditor does not have to swear.</i>.<br>“You loaned me a <i>tetradrachma</i> on it but it was worth two,” and the other says “no, but I loaned you a <i>tetradrachma</i> and it was worth five <i>denar</i>;” he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor admits that he owes 3 <i>denarii</i> for the pledge, the debtor asks for four. The creditor has to swear.</i>. Who has to swear? The one who holds the pledge, lest one swear and the other produce the pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The court has to be careful to avoid pushing people into perjury. The oaths always have to be formulated to minimize the possibility of public perjury. Cf. <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>1:1, Note 3.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, the lender can be believed if he said, I lent to you up to the value of the pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of Mishnaiot 10–13 (which in most Mishnah texts form a single Mishnah.) The statement of R. Joḥanan explains Mishnah 12, where the creditor claims that the amount of the loan does not exceed the value of the pledge.</i>. The Mishnah says, also the borrower can be believed, as we have stated: “He told him, ‘I loaned you a <i>tetradrachma</i> and it was worth a <i>šeqel</i>,’ but the other one says ‘no, a <i>tetradrachma</i> you loaned me and it was worth a <i>tetradrachma</i>‘; he is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">If the borrower disputes the claim, the burden of proof is on him.</i>” A person encountered another in the market and said, you owe me two <i>denarii</i> and your pledge is worth two <i>denarii</i>. He answered, I owe you one <i>denar</i> but my pledge is worth two <i>denarii</i>. The case came before the judges of Nahardea. They said, since everybody agrees that it is worth two <i>denarii</i>, he has to bring witnesses on the other [<i>denar</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">They put the burden of proof on the creditor for the amount disputed by the debtor.</i>. They had not heard that Rebbi Joḥanan said, the lender can be believed if he said, I lent to you up to the value of the pledge.
A person encountered another in the market and took away his linen cloth<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σάβανον, Latin <i>sabanum</i>.</i> saying, “this sheet will not leave my hand until you give me what you are holding for me.” It came before Samuel. He told him, return his linen cloth to him and sue him. Does Samuel follow the judges of Nahardea? There, the pledge was held by him, here the pledge was not held by him<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">If the pledge was handed over by the debtor to the creditor, practice has to follow R. Joḥanan against the judges of Nahardea. But if a pledge was taken by force, it has to be returned before the case can be heard in court. (<i>Sefer HaˋIttur</i> II כבb reads <i>R</i>. <i>Zeˋira</i> instead of <i>Samuel</i>. This reading is not likely to be correct.)</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah 12, where the creditor swears to free himself from paying.’</i> Samuel says, the oath does not move away from between them. If the lender swears, he swears and takes. But if the borrower swears, the other will present the pledge. “Who has to swear? The one who holds the pledge.<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 43b" href="/Shevuot.43b">Babli explains, 43b</a>, if both have to swear, the holder of the pledge always has to swear first.</i>” Rav and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish say, about the first part: “ ‘You loaned me a <i>tetradrachma</i> on it but it was worth two,’ and the other says ‘no, but I loaned you a <i>tetradrachma</i> and it was worth a <i>tetradrachma</i>;’ he is not liable. ‘You loaned me a <i>tetradrachma</i> on it,’ ” etc. “And the other says, ‘I do not know’.<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">What is the rule if in any of the situations of Mishnaiot 10–13 the defendant claims ignorance?</i>” Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna in the name of Rav: You do not know, the other knows<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">If the answer is taken as partial admission, the person would have to swear. Since by his own testimony he is unable to swear, he has to pay. If the answer is not taken as partial admission, then by the rule that in a case of a defense of “perhaps” against a claim of “certain” judgment has to be given for the certain claim (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 5:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.5.4.4">Chapter 5, Note 72</a>), he has to pay.</i>. Rebbi Joshua [the Southerner]<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Read דְּרוֹמִיָא as quoted in Rosh (Chapter 6, No. 28) in a reference from Nahmanides’s commentary on this paragraph from the Yerushalmi.</i> asked before Rebbi Jonah: There you say, you do not know, the other knows; but here you say so? There is desecration of an oath, here there is no desecration of an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of Mishnaiot 11 and 13 there is an oath to be imposed and it is up to the court to devise procedures which make sure that the court does not become a party to perjury. But in the case of “perhaps” against “certain”, no oath can be imposed and there is no need for rules of precedence.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All who must swear by Torah standards do swear not to pay. The following do swear<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">By rabbinic (popular) standards.</i> and collect: The journeyman, a person robbed or injured, and one whose opponent is suspected of perjury, and the grocer on his account book<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">These will be explained in the following Mishnaiot.<br>Probably one should vocalize פִּינַקְסוֹ, Greek πίναξ “wooden (wax covered) writing tablet.”</i>. How does the journeyman do it? He tells him, “give me my wages which you are holding;” he says, “I gave.” He swears and collects<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since one assumes that the employer is occupied with many things while the journeyman has only his wages on his mind.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, only if there is partial admission. How is this? He tells him, “give me my wages of fifty <i>denarii</i> which you are holding;” but he says, “you received from these a gold <i>denar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">25 silver <i>denarii</i>. By biblical law, the employer would have to swear since he partially denied the journeyman’s claim.</i>.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “All who must swear by Torah standards,” etc. One understands from what is said, <i>an oath before the Eternal shall be between them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:10" href="/Exodus.22.10"><i>Ex</i>. 22:10</a>.</i>, would we not know that if he does not swear, he has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the entire paragraph is about the obligations of the paid trustee in case the deposit was lost. Only the case in which he does not have to pay is described; from this it follows that in all other circumstances he has to pay.</i>? Why does the verse say, <i>its owner shall take it, and he does not have to pay</i>? But from the moment the owner accepted his oath, he does not have to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 45a" href="/Shevuot.45a">Babli 45a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 107a" href="/Bava_Kamma.107a"><i>Bava qamma</i>107a</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i>16, <i>dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai ad loc</i>., (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 6:7-9" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.6.7-9">Tosephta 6:7</a>.)<br>Probably this statement has to be interpreted following the parallels in the Babli and the <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i>: The oath grants the trustee absolute immunity. Even if afterwards witnesses are found who prove that the trustee stole the deposit, he cannot be made to pay. This cannot be inferred from the first part of the verse quoted earlier.<br><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> treats the statement as paradigm for all oaths prescribed in the Torah and sees in it proof for the statement that “All who must swear by Torah standards do swear not to pay.”</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose: Is this only following Rebbi Meїr or even following the rabbis? Did not Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian name of R. Yasa who is named correctly in the parallel, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:10:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.10.5">Halakhah 4:14 (Note 118</a>).</i> say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Meїr is the one who says that out of a negative one understands a positive? <i>Its owner shall take it, and he does not have to pay</i>, therefore if he does not swear, he has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Why should this simple inference be particular to R. Meїr and require R. Joḥanan’s statement?</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: The unpaid and the paid trustees may stipulate to be like a borrower<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">It really should read: The depositor may stipulate with a paid or unpaid trustee that they should be strictly responsible for the deposit like a borrower (Mishnah 8:1). Since this is a money matter, the biblical conditions are only valid if there are no explicit dispositions for deviations from these standards (<i>Bava meṣi`a</i>5:5 Note 81). The verse may be read: If the owner accepts the oath, the trustee does not pay; this leaves open the option that the owner stipulated that he would not accept the oath.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina said, everybody agrees that in the language of the Torah out of a negative one understands a positive; where do they disagree? In everyday speech<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">There is no disagreement with R. Meїr in the interpretation of scriptural verses but there is much disagreement in the interpretation of vows and informal texts.</i>.
Rebbi Abin said, since the householder has many occupations they instituted that the journeyman should swear and collect<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 45a" href="/Shevuot.45a">Babli, 45a</a>, this is an anonymous statement. Since it comes at the end of a lengthy discussion, it is Amoraic.</i>. [Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The insert is from a Genizah text; it is superfluous and questionable in that it fixes the origin of the institution in Babylonia (“there”).</i> they instituted that the journeyman should swear and collect;] similarly they instituted for the householder that if its deadline had elapsed, he cannot swear and collect<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>9:13, Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>10:5. The journeyman is hired for the day. It is sinful not to pay the journeyman immediately after his work is done (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut</i>. 24:15</a>). Therefore one may assume that the laborer asked for his wages immediately and that he was paid since it is difficult to assume that the householder would sin in withholding the wages. But if there are witnesses that the laborer asked and was not paid, the presumption of permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i> requires one to assume that he was not paid until the opposite is proven.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, if there are witnesses that he claimed it in time, he swears and collects<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>9:13, Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>10:5. The journeyman is hired for the day. It is sinful not to pay the journeyman immediately after his work is done (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut</i>. 24:15</a>). Therefore one may assume that the laborer asked for his wages immediately and that he was paid since it is difficult to assume that the householder would sin in withholding the wages. But if there are witnesses that the laborer asked and was not paid, the presumption of permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i> requires one to assume that he was not paid until the opposite is proven.</i> even after a year. Rebbi Yose said, he has only that single day<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the Tosephta and holds that the inversion on the rules of oaths is valid only for 12 hours after the end of the working day. Later the householder would have the option to swear and to free himself from paying since it must be assumed that the laborer would have gone to court immediately had he not been paid. This opinion has no parallel elsewhere.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagreed. One said, if he claimed from him during daytime, he has only that day; if in the night he has only that night<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The hours worked by a laborer hired for the day are set by communal standards (Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>7:1) but at most are from daybreak to nightfall.
