Therefore, a day laborer must be paid between the end of his working day and the end of the following night; a worker hired for the night must be paid before the end of the next day.</i>. But the other said, if he claimed from him and he said, I gave. But if he said, I shall give, the presumption is that he gave<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If the laborer claims that he was not paid and his employer says that he was paid, this is a situation analogous to that of an oath about a deposit and the laborer swears and collects. But if the householder agrees that he has to pay, the court has to assume that he will keep his word and fulfill his religious duty in paying promptly.</i>. We do not know who said what. Since Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: if he claimed from him during daytime, he has only that day; if in the night he has only that night, therefore Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina is the one who said, if he claimed from him and he said, I gave. But if he said, I shall give, the presumption is that he gave. Rebbi Mana said, sometimes he claims after its time and it is considered as in time. How is this? He claimed from him and he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The laborer claimed and the employer said.</i> said to him, did I not say to you that I gave at that date? That date becomes as if it were yesterday; one gives him one measure<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The 12 hours of the next day to claim, swear, and collect under the rules of the day-laborer.</i>.
If he paid him in advance; in that case he swears and collects<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The employer claims that he paid the day laborer in advance but he has no witnesses and no receipt. The law is not changed; the laborer swears and collects.</i>. If a pledge was in his hand; in that case he collects without swearing<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">If the laborer had been paid, he would have returned the pledge.</i>. If he was a slave. Would the court admit a slave to an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since the slave has no standing in court, he could not be punished for perjury. An oath sworn by someone exempt from the sanctions for perjury is worthless and an insult to the court.</i>? If he was suspect? Would the court admit a suspect person to an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Either he is suspected of a false or vain oath or he gains his livelihood by fraud, as explained in Mishnah 4.</i>? If both were suspect, it is the dispute between Rebbi Meїr and Rebbi Yose: <sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4.</i>“If both were suspect, the oath returns to its origin, the words of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of his words is in dispute between Babylonians and Galileans; cf. Halakhah 4, end.</i>; Rebbi Meїr says, they shall split the difference<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither party can swear or has proof; the claim is classified as “money in doubt” which is split evenly between the parties (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i>2:1 Note 9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.1.1"><i>Bava qamma</i>5:1 Note 4</a>).</i>.”
It is obvious: If the householder dies, the journeyman swears to his heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">And collects his wages. Since the Babli does not mention the topic, this is accepted practice. If the day laborer dies, the employer swears to free himself from payment.</i>. Even if the journeyman dies, do his heirs swear to the householder’s heirs? They made this ordinance only for the journeyman. Maybe for his heirs? Rebbi Eleazar said, if he claimed<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the text is confirmed by a Genizah fragment, it cannot be amended to read “if he hired him before witnesses” as seems to be implied later in the text. R. Eleazar restricts the possibility of the laborer to swear and collect to the situation when he requested payment before witnesses and afterwards comes to court. But if there are no witnesses to the fact that he was not paid on the spot when he requested payment, i. e., there is <i>prima facie</i> evidence that the employer did not fulfill the commandment “to pay on his day”, the employer must be given the benefit of doubt and the option of swearing to deny his debt.</i> before witnesses. [But if he claimed without witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets is added from a Genizah fragment. It is necessary; its existence was conjectured by some commentaries (<i>Ridbaz</i> and <i>Noam Yerushalmi</i>).</i>, he can tell him,] I paid your wages. Rebbi Joḥanan said, does one not in money matters argue “because<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.1.7">Halakhah 6:1, Note 25</a>. The later R. Yudan denies the legal validity of “because” arguments in court but R. Joḥanan is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 45b" href="/Shevuot.45b">Babli 45b</a> to agree with Samuel that this kind of argument is valid.</i>?” Because he can say to him, I did not hire you, he can tell him, I hired you and paid your wages<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If the laborer was hired without witnesses or contract, he has no way to establish the fact that he was a day laborer in the service of the person from whom he claims wages and cannot request the court to admit his oath.</i>. A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.6.1">Tosephta 6:1</a>.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in this text, “Eleazar” should everywhere be replaced by “Joḥanan” since only R. Joḥanan requires that the hiring be documented.</i>: “When was this said? If he said, I gave, but the other said, I did not take; I gave you your wages, but he says, I did not take. You hired me, but he says, I did not hire you. The burden of proof is on the claimant.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">There is a word missing in the text of the Tosephta, which makes the text incomprehensible. The Tosephta notes that the special rule which allows the laborer to swear and collect is restricted to the case of the Mishnah, where there is no dispute about the relation of employer and employee, only whether the latter had been paid or not; <i>but</i> a dispute whether such a relationship existed at all must be adjudicated by the general principle that the burden of proof is on the claimant. It seems clear that this situation cannot occur if the hiring was documented.</i>” It is difficult for Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in this text, “Eleazar” should everywhere be replaced by “Joḥanan” since only R. Joḥanan requires that the hiring be documented.</i>, if he hired him before witnesses, how can this one say, I did not hire you?
Rebbi Abba from Carthage asked: “You worked for me one day and I paid you.” But the other says, “I worked for you for two days and you did not give anything to me.” It is obvious that he takes for the first day without<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This must read “with”. Since the employer asserts the existence of the hire, the rule of the Mishnah applies.</i> oath. The second is the disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Joḥanan, if the hire was not documented the employer must be believed and any additional claim by the laborer has to be proven (e. g., by witnesses). There is no occasion for an oath about the additional sum. According to R. Eleazar, if the claim was made before witnesses, there is no limit to the right of the laborer to swear and collect.</i>. “I hired you for a <i>tetradrachma</i> and gave it to you.” But the other says, “you hired me for two <i>tetradrachmas</i> and did not give me anything.” It is obvious that he takes the first <i>tetradrachma</i> without<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This must read “with”. Since the employer asserts the existence of the hire, the rule of the Mishnah applies.</i> an oath. The second is the disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Joḥanan, if the hire was not documented the employer must be believed and any additional claim by the laborer has to be proven (e. g., by witnesses). There is no occasion for an oath about the additional sum. According to R. Eleazar, if the claim was made before witnesses, there is no limit to the right of the laborer to swear and collect.</i>.
Rav Huna said, if after he swore he brought witnesses that it was standing at the feeding trough, the oath already had robbed it<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is not a continuation of the preceding but refers to the first paragraph of the Halakhah where it was stated that if the claimant accepted the defendant’s oath, the latter does not have to pay (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 7:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.7.1.2">Note 7</a>). Rav Huna [in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 106a" href="/Bava_Kamma.106a">Babli, <i>Bava qamma</i>106a</a>, a statement of Ilfa (Hilfai) endorsed by Rav Huna in the name of Rav] holds that the oath effects a transfer of property rights; if the claimant accepts the oath, the defendant later ate his own animal, not the claimant’s. However, since here the acquisition is qualified as robbery the claimant can bring a new action for robbery whereas in the Babli Rav Huna in the name of Rav qualifies this as acquisition and denies any obligation on the part of the defendant.</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Rav Huna: <sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 8:4.</i>“ ‘Where is my ox?’ He told him, it was lost. ‘I am putting an oath on you,’ and he said Amen. But witnesses testify that he ate it. He has to pay the value. If he confessed on his own, he pays the value, an additional fifth, and a reparation offering”. The Mishnah if he ate it and only later swore to him. What Rav Huna said, it he swore to him and only later ate it.
Rebbi Jehudah’s argument seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.7.1">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i>6:7</a>. In R. Jehudah’s opinion an arsonist has to pay for everything he destroyed even if he could not have known of its value.</i> he says, even if he does not know. But here he says, not unless he knows<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since in this and the following Mishnaiot R. Jehudah always requires partial admission by the debtor, information of the debtor is assumed. (Since the reading “knows” is confirmed by the Genizah fragment, the word cannot be emended to “admits”.)</i>. There since it is not in the form of a biblical oath even (about a biblical oath)<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">These words are not in the Genizah text, do not make any sense here, and have to be deleted.</i> he does not know. But here it is in the form of a biblical oath<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Bava qamma</i> there is no oath. Here R. Jehudah requires partial admission to base the administration of an oath on biblical grounds even though the oath itself is a rabbinic institution.</i>.
May one roll over a biblical oath on a biblical oath<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.3"><i>Soṭah</i>2:6 Note 167</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.13"><i>Qiddušin</i>1:5 Note 520</a>. If a person is required to swear in court, the opposing party can add to the text of the oath any other oath he might have cause to let the other party swear without having to prove every case separately. For biblical oaths this is proven from biblical texts in the places quoted. For an obligation to swear by biblical standards to add an oath which is a rabbinic institution seems to be a natural consequence. The only problem is whether the obligation to swear a rabbinic oath is cause for the opposing party to add biblical oaths without presenting sufficient proof for that obligation.</i>? Let us hear from the following: One rolls over a biblical oath on a biblical oath, an instituted oath on an instituted oath, a biblical oath on an instituted oath. An instituted oath on a biblical oath? There, one swears and pays, but here in one case he swears and collects, in the other he swears and does not pay<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The question is answered in the negative. The rabbinic oaths mentioned in this Chapter (and probably also the one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.3.2"><i>Bava qamma</i>10:3</a>) are not obligations but privileges; there is no reason to pile obligations on top of these.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 49a" href="/Shevuot.49a">Babli (49a</a>, top) agrees for the oaths described in the Mishnah. The answer of the Babli has to be more nuanced since it introduces an Amoraic institution of שְׁבוּעַת הֵיסֶת “an oath of instigation”, unknown to the Yerushalmi, if judgment is given by a preponderance of evidence but not beyond a reasonable doubt. These rabbinically instituted oaths are obligations and can be used as pegs to pile on other obligations.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How about a person robbed? There were witnesses that he entered another’s house to take a pledge without authorization<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Either by the debtor or by the court. This in itself is a sin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:10-11" href="/Deuteronomy.24.10-11"><i>Deut</i>. 24:10–11</a>). The witnesses did not see what he took. The debtor swears and takes based on this <i>prima facie</i> evidence of wrongdoing by the creditor.</i>. He said to him, give me my vessels which you took but he answers I did not take; this one swears and takes. Rebbi Jehudah says, only if there is partial admission. How is this? He tells him, you took two of my vessels but he says, I took only one.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How about a person robbed,” etc. “In another’s house,” not in his courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:10" href="/Deuteronomy.24.10"><i>Deut</i>. 24:10</a> only forbids to enter the debtor’s house to take a pledge. This condition does not appear in the Babli; in <i>Ṭur Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i>90 (<i>Bet Yosef</i> Note 9) there is an extended discussion about the validity of this statement.</i>. “To take a pledge,” not for another purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rules of the Mishnah imply a suspension of ordinary rules of evidence, they have to be followed to the letter.</i>. Rebbi Issac said, and only by witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">While the crime does not have to be proven, the <i>prima facie</i> evidence of illegal entry has to be established beyond a reasonable doubt.</i>. If two entered to take pledges from him? It should come like the following: “If he was injured between the two …<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A sentence is missing here as indicated in the not well legible Genizah text. It was omitted by the scribe from the word שׁניהן to the next occurrence of the same word. But the meaning of the missing sentence is easily reconstructed. If a person was injured when attacked before witnesses by two people, but the witnesses cannot identify the person who actually injured him, he has no civil claim against any of them. Similarly, if witnesses saw two people entered but they did not see which of them actually took something, the rule of the Mishnah cannot be applied.</i>” But if both were entitled to take pledges? What should be seen there? If they saw two [men] hitting him with sticks<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If two creditors were seen entering together it can be compared to the case that witnesses actually saw both perpetrators hitting their victim with sticks. Then the victim can successfully sue both and the debtor can invoke the Mishnah against both creditors.</i>.
If they<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Witnesses saw a man throwing pebbles onto another’s property but they did not see the damage done.</i> saw him throwing pebbles and broken vessels were found there, he<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the property can collect damages even though he has no proof that the pebbles actually did the damage. The proof of criminal behavior by the stone thrower is enough.</i> collects without oath.
<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly reduced form of this text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.7.3"><i>Bava qamma</i>6:7 (Notes 111–115</a>) and Alfasi <i>Bava qamma</i> No. 125. It is clear that R. Joḥanan and the editors of the Yerushalmi endorse R. Ḥiyya’s judgment and reject R. Ismael ben R. Yose’s.</i> May one claim extraordinary things? One may, following what Rebbi Joḥanan said, a person may be rich on the market place and poor in his house; rich in his house and poor in the market place. <i>There is one who poses as rich</i><i>and has nothing, poses as poor and has great wealth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:7" href="/Proverbs.13.7"><i>Prov</i>. 13:7</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba told<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This introduction, missing in both <i>Bava qamma</i> texts, makes it questionable whether a real case is recounted here.</i>: Bar Ziza’s sharecropper deposited a pound of gold<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">An enormous fortune, under the Severan emperors the equivalent of 900 <i>ketubot</i>.</i> with somebody. Bar Ziza and Bar Ziza’s sharecropper died; the case came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose. Would one not say that all that Bar Ziza’s sharecropper had was Bar Ziza’s property? It should be given to Bar Ziza’s sons. Bar Ziza’s sons came. He said, the adult ones should take half and when the underage grow up they should take half. Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose died; the case came before the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya who said, that argument means nothing since there are people who do not advertise themselves. It should be given to the sharecropper’s sons. The trustee told him, I already disbursed half of it. He told him, what you gave, you gave by court order and what you will give, you will give by court order. May the sharecropper’s sons say to Bar Ziza’s sons, give us what you took? They can tell them, what was done was done by court order. May the underage ones say to the adult, let us share with you? May they tell them, we found a find? Rebbi Isaac said, the case between the adult and underage ones is only comparable to a gift given to them.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The injured one, how? They were testifying against him that one came to his hand whole and left injured<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">And it is a reasonable inference that the second person was injured by the first.</i>. He says, you injured me, but the other says, I did not injure; this one swears and collects. Rebbi Jehudah says, only if there was a partial admission. How is this? He said to him, you injured me twice but the other says, I injured you only once.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The injured one, how,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.6.2">Tosephta 6:2</a>, <i>Bava qamma</i>9:28 (S. Lieberman <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Bava qamma</i> p. 109.)</i>“Rebbi Jehudah was calling them beaters<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel sources, חביבאי, חבבאי, כאי, הכאב which might support a reading חכאכי (or something similar) “wrestlers”.</i>. Any time they are rubbing<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Or “wrestling”.</i> one another, he swears and collects<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If there are witnesses for a physical altercation but no witnesses for any detailed happening.</i>. If he was bitten at a place where he could not bite himself, he collects without oath<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 46b" href="/Shevuot.46b">Babli 46b</a>, an Amoraic statement in the name of Samuel.</i>. At a later time if one says, you injured me, but the other says, I did not injure, this is like any other claim<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah. If the witnesses saw him whole and immediately after he was seen in an altercation he was seen injured, he swears and collects. But if the injury was only certified later, the general rules of evidence will apply to any claim.</i>.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> And if the opposing party was suspect of perjury, how? Equally oaths about testimony, or oaths about deposits, or even vain oaths, if one of them was a dice-player, a lender on interest, a participant in pigeon contests, or a dealer in sabbatical produce<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This list of people not admitted as witnesses nor credible in their oaths is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.5.1"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:6 (Notes 44–47</a>.)</i>, the opposing party swears and collects. If both of them were suspect, the oath returns to its place, the words of Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Meїr says, they shall split<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of his words is in dispute between Babylonians and Galileans; cf. Halakhah 4, end.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “And if the opposing party was suspect of perjury,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs are found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 1:7:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.1.7.2-6"><i>Roš Haššanah</i>1:9</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:5:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.5.2-16"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:6</a>, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:5:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.5.2-6">Sanhedrin Chapter 3, Notes 51–69</a></i> The dice player is the one who plays with small stones<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ψῆφος “pebble, cube; the stones used for mosaics and tokens used in elections.” Rashi defines as “marbles”.</i>. “Not only the player with stones, even one who plays with shells of nuts or pomegranates<sup class="footnote-marker">66a</sup><i class="footnote">Pomegranates (<i>rimmonim</i>) do not have shells but skins. Perhaps the reference is to Greek ῥύμβος, ῥόμβος, “magic wheel, instrument used in pagan mysteries; rhomb”, from ῥόμβέω “to spin, cause to whirl” (E. G.)</i> one does not accept unless he break his stones, and be checked out, and repent in complete repentance.” One who lends on interest one does not accept unless he tear up his IOU’s, and be checked out, and repent in complete repentance.
Participants in pigeon contests, that is one who bets on pigeons. Whether one bets on pigeons or bets on any domesticated animal, wild animal, or bird, he is not accepted unless he break his tools of the catch and repent in complete repentance.
Dealers in sabbatical produce, that is the sabbatical trader. “Who is a sabbatical trader? One who sits idle all the years of a sabbatical cycle. In the sabbatical year he becomes active and trades in sabbatical produce. One does not accept him before another sabbatical year starts and he can be checked out that he repented in complete repentance.” Rebbi Yose stated, two sabbatical periods. <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the entire paragraph is about the obligations of the paid trustee in case the deposit was lost. Only the case in which he does not have to pay is described; from this it follows that in all other circumstances he has to pay.</i>“Rebbi Nehemiah says, repentance in money, not repentance in words; that he say to them, here are 200 denars, distribute them to the poor, for I earned them from forbidden produce.” They added shepherds, extortionists, and any who are suspect in money matters, that their testimony be invalid. Rebbi Abbahu said, shepherds of small animals.
Rav Huna said: Who is the Tanna of “participants in pigeon contests”? Rebbi Eliezer, as we have stated there: “Participants in pigeon contests are disqualified from testimony.” Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: Is that statement in <i>Sanhedrin</i> Rebbi Eliezer’s? He told him, it is everybody’s opinion. So said Rebbi Yose: We knew that he was disqualified for testimony in money matters. What does he come to testify about? For as he is disqualified in money matters, so he is disqualified to testify in criminal trials. The witnesses for the New Moon are held to the standards of criminal trials, as we have stated: “Any testimony for which a woman is not qualified, they are not qualified for.” Who stated this? The rabbis! Do the rabbis follow Rebbi Eliezer? They agree with him and disagree with him. Rebbi Huna in the name of Rebbi Jonah said: It follows Rebbi Eliezer in everything. It turns out that this disagreement parallels another disagreement, as it was stated: A perjured witness is disqualified for any and all testimony required by the Torah, the words of Rebbi Meїr. Rebbi Yose said, when has this been said? When he was found perjured in criminal matters. But if he was found perjured in money matters, he is disqualified only from that particular testimony. It turns out that Rebbi Yose parallels the rabbis and Rebbi Meїr Rebbi Eliezer.
When does one accept one who is suspect in matters of oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the Tosephta quoted in the preceding paragraphs has very stringent conditions, in practical cases the court may apply a more lenient standard.</i>? From when he comes to the court and says, I am suspect. Where do we hold? If one who stands here and is obligated to swear for another, does the court deliver an oath to a suspect person<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">If he is a known suspect, what difference does it make if he declares what the court already knows?</i>? But we must hold about one who has a suit at a court where he is unknown and tells them, this man<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This is himself. Since the statement is derogatory, one uses the third person instead of the first.</i> is suspect. Also about one who stands and another owes him an oath and he forgives it<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If he is ready to pay rather than have another person (who is not suspect) swear an oath on his demand.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of the statement of R. Yose in the Mishnah (whom practice has to follow in preference to R. Meїr.) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 47a" href="/Shevuot.47a">Babli 47a</a>.</i> Rav Hoshaia said before Rebbi Immi in the name of the rabbis there: the oath returned to Sinai. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: the oath returned to the parties. He who said, the oath returned to Sinai, it is as if there was no testimony; the burden of proof is on the claimant<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If the parties are not able to swear, the special rules of the Mishnah cannot be applied and the suit has to be settled by the regular standards for civil suits.</i>. He who said, the oath returned to the parties, since that man is suspect and cannot swear for me, get up and pay me<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This does not apply to the oaths which are the topics of the present Chapter, where the claimant swears and collects, but to the biblical oaths of trustees who swear in order to be freed from payment (Mishnah 1). If such a trustee is barred from swearing, he has to pay. In fact, the opposing party has to swear to collect; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 6:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.6.5.2">Halakhah 6:6, Note 88</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The grocer on his account book, how? Not that he says to him, it is written in my account book that you owe me 200 denars<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">If the trader claims that the customer received the merchandise but did not pay, he has to prove his claims in court according to local standards; there is no proviso for an oath in the proceedings.</i>, but he said to him, give my son two <i>se’ah</i> of wheat, or my worker small change for a tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Because most people are illiterate, there is no standard requirement for a receipt.</i>. He says, I gave, but they say, we did not take. He swears and collects, and they swear and collect. Ben Nannas said, how? Both are induced to swear falsely<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation of the oaths is in the hands of the judges; they have to be careful not to create a situation in which they are seen to cause perjury (cf. <i>Neziqin Bava Meṣi`a</i>1, Note 3.) Since the person who gave the instructions incurred a liability both with the grocer and the workers, both parties can collect since both offer to swear but under the circumstances cannot be allowed to.</i>! But he takes without an oath and they take without an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading in all sources except for R. Nissim in his commentary to Rif; his reading has to be considered an unjustified emendation. Practice has to follow the anonymous Tanna.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The grocer on his account book,” etc. “Was ‘the grocer on his account book’ not said about one extending credit? For he can tell him, you wrote with this [hand], erase with the other [hand]”<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 6:4-5" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.6.4-5">Tosephta 6:4</a>. In the absence of proofs, the customer can always claim that he paid his debt but the seller failed to update his books. The customer is not required to preserve the receipt for his payment; the seller has to organize his accounts so that open accounts can be used as proof in court.</i>.
Rebbi says, I am saying that these have to swear in the presence of those because of the embarrassment.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 47b" href="/Shevuot.47b">Babli 47b</a>. He takes care of Ben Nannas’s objection by requiring both parties to swear in the presence of the other (and, in the Babli’s version, in the presence also of the person who has to pay). It can be expected that in this case the party not entitled to payment will not dare to swear falsely.</i>
That is to say, only if he did not introduce them<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">If the employer directs his employees to the grocer and separately instructs the grocer to give them merchandise on his behalf, he becomes liable to both parties. But if he instructs the grocer in the presence of his employees, he has discharged his duty towards his employees; his debt to the grocer is treated by the general rules of obligations.</i>. But if he introduced them, the householder has no liability, following what Rebbi Jehudah bar Shalom said: One did introduce the porters to the food merchants<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κάπηλος “(raw or cooked) food merchant; tavern keeper”.</i>. They came, but they sent them away without giving them anything. The case came before Rebbi Shammai. Since if the food merchants had said that they took, would at the end not these and those collect? Therefore, they shall collect immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the Mishnah, the merchants incur no risk since they have regress on the employer. But in the case here, the merchants admit that they did not deliver; the employees have the right to demand immediate payment. Since they were directed to the merchants and these agreed to the order, the merchants have immediately to deliver to the employees, who have no regress on their employer, but the merchants can later bill the employer and collect.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He said to the grocer, give me produce for a denar; that one delivered and said, give me the denar. He said to him, I gave it to you and you put it into <i>anpalē</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi “a wallet for coins.” No etymology for this word has been given. At other occurrences in the Talmudim, the word seems to stand for ἐμπίλιον, <i>impilia</i>“felt socks.” This meaning is inappropriate here. A better explanation is ἀμφελόνη “garment, throw-around” (E. G.) or one might read with the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 6:4-5" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.6.4-5">Tosephta (6:4</a>) אונכלי explained by Kohout as ἀνάκωλος [χιτωνίσκος] “a short sleeved outer garment.”</i>: the householder has to swear<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The simple statement, “I gave it to you,” is insufficient. The manner of delivery must be described.</i>. If he gave him the denar and said, give me the produce; he said to him I delivered them to you and you brought then to your house: the grocer has to swear. Rebbi Jehudah says, the one in possession of the produce is advantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since both parties agree that the transaction was not on credit, in his opinion the buyer does not have to swear since in his place cash transactions always implied delivery after payment. If the grocer claimed that the transaction did not follow the norm, the full burden of proof is on him as claimant.</i>.<br>He said to the money changer, give me <i>oboloi</i> for a <i>denar</i>; that one delivered and said, give me the <i>denar</i>. He said to him, I gave it to you and you put it into <i>anpalē</i>: the householder has to swear. If he gave him the <i>denar</i> and said, give me the <i>oboloi</i>; he said to him I delivered them to you and you threw them into you wallet: the money changer has to swear. Rebbi Jehudah says, it is not the practice of money changers to give an <i>as</i> before he took the <i>denarii</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This is the same argument as in the preceding Mishnah. The money changer will not give out even an <i>as</i>, a quarter (or fifth) of an <i>obolos</i>, without first being paid.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He said to the grocer, give me produce for a <i>denar</i>;” etc. Rebbi Ḥanina said, it is to be differentiated: “Rebbi Jehudah said, when? If the container was lying between them. But if it was in the possession of one of them …<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Jehudah in Mishnah 6 is not a statement flatly denying the opinion of the anonymous Tanna in the first case, but it has to be read in the light of a text close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 6:4-5" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.6.4-5">Tosephta 6:4</a>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, when has this been said? At a time when the container was lying (in houses) [between them], but if it was in the possession of either of them, the burden of proof is on the claimant.” The incomprehensible text in parentheses is from the Erfurt ms. (Zuckermandel), the text in brackets from the <i>editio princeps</i>. A similar text is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 48a" href="/Shevuot.48a">Babli, 48a</a>. Five different explanations of the text are offered by the commentary <i>Sifte Cohen</i>(<i>Šulḥan Arukh Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> 91:1, Note 33) in more than 4 folio pages. Rashi does not comment on the text; the author of <i>Šulḥan Arukh</i> follows Maimonides who explains the expression “at a time when the container was lying between them” to mean that the transaction occurred in the public domain. If the container full of produce was in a private domain, the owner of that domain is in possession and there can be no oath; any claimant will have to produce proof according to the regular standards of civil trials. <i>Sifte Cohen</i> disagrees with this explanation since he reads בְּמַחֲלוֹקֶת as “in dispute”; meaning that R. Jehudah disagrees with the anonymous Tanna. But this is the simple meaning of the Mishnah; if R. Ḥanina notes that the statement of R. Jehudah has to be interpreted following the Tosephta, he clearly states that the text has to be read as an explanation of the opinion of the anonymous Tanna as given in the translation.</i>”
Also this<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Also in Mishnah 7 does R. Jehudah point out a case where the anonymous Tanna will not follow the rules of his own Mishnah.</i> needs to be differentiated. It is said about a stranger<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ξένος.</i>. But to a fellow town dweller he will give an <i>as</i> before he takes his <i>denar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">With people known to him the money-changer will not refrain from deals involving credit.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i>9:7</a>, explained there in Notes 159–163. The <i>ketubah</i>(the marriage contract intended to provide financial support for the woman if the marriage is dissolved by divorce or the husband’s death) is compromised if not the full amount is due since the wife has signed a receipt for partial payment. The testimony of a single witness can be contradicted by an oath of the opposing party by biblical rule. If the <i>ketubah</i> has to be paid by the heirs from the estate, the estate is diminished; one requires the widow to swear that the entire amount is due her. The same holds if the rights of a mortgage holder whose claim is posterior to the lien created by the <i>ketubah</i> are diminished by the payment, or if a divorcee claims payment from the property of her absentee former husband.</i> If she had compromised her <i>ketubah</i>, she shall not be able to collect without an oath. If one witness testifies that [the <i>ketubah</i>] was paid, she shall not be able to collect without an oath. From encumbered property, or from orphans’ property, she shall not be able to collect without an oath. If she collects in his absence, she shall not be able to collect without an oath.<br>Similarly, the orphans can collect only by an oath; “an oath that our father did not instruct us<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">On his death bed.</i>, our father did not tell us<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Before this time.</i>, and we found nothing in our father’s documents<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">No receipt was found.</i> that this note of indebtedness was paid.” Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa testified<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">All statements of testimony in the Mishnah are practice. In the known Babli mss., the formulation is an noncommittal “said” instead of “testified.” But since the Yerushalmi version is also that of Rif and Rosh, it must have been the Babli version of their texts.</i> that even if the son was born after the father’s death he swears and collects. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, if there are witnesses that the father said on his death bed that this note of indebtedness was not paid, he collects without oath.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbui Ze`ira said, this came down in the manner of biblical oaths. And a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> is not from the start in her hand for collection, but only for collection from now on and in the future. Since she claims a document about 200 paid and she says, it is only one <i>mina</i>, she collects without oath<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph refers to Mishnah 8 and is a reworking of one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.7.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:7, Note 166</a>, in a number of details opposing the opinions given there.<br>As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.7.7.1">Note 87</a>, the oath of a woman who wants to collect her <i>ketubah</i> when a single witness asserts that it already was paid is biblical. R. Ze`ira notes that the oaths required in the other cases are formulated on the model of that oath.<br>The rules for collecting a <i>ketubah</i> are not identical with the rules for enforcing an IOU since it may be assumed that a woman does not marry in order to be divorced or become widowed; if she is widowed she may prefer not to claim the <i>ketubah</i> but to be permanently supported by the heirs. This means that the woman acquires the <i>ketubah</i> not with the intent of presenting it for collection. The <i>ketubah</i> becomes an enforceable claim only by the owner presenting it for collection.<br>In <i>Ketubot</i>, it is stated that if the <i>ketubah</i> is presented with an attached receipt, and the woman claims that the receipt is only for half of the amount and the other half is still due her, this is a compromised <i>ketubah</i> and she may collect only by swearing to the validity of her claim. But here she presents a <i>ketubah</i> for the standard 200 <i>denars</i> but declares that she only claims 100 <i>denars</i> (a <i>mina</i>); therefore she collects without an oath. This is not a compromised but a diminished <i>ketubah</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 7:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.7.7.3">Note 94</a>).</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.8.1"><i>Mishnah Ketubot</i> 9:9</a>.</i>: “If she had compromised her <i>ketubah</i>, she shall not be able to collect without an oath. How is this? If her <i>ketubah</i> was a thousand <i>denars</i> and he says to her, you received your <i>ketubah</i>, but she says, I received only one <i>mina</i>, she cannot collect without an oath.” It was stated, one who had compromised, not one who had diminished<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.7.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:7 Note 167</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:6:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.6.8"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 8:6 Note 69</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 87b" href="/Ketubot.87b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>87b</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This is a fragment from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.7.6"><i>Ketubot</i>9:7 (Note 185</a>) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:6:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.6.12"><i>Sanhedrin</i>8:6 (Note 72</a>): “Rav Ḥisda asked: Because he walked two steps, does he lose? If he compromised it outside the court, he collects. Because he compromised it in court, he cannot collect?” It refers to a statement by R. Abin (or Ba) not quoted here and not related to the topics of the Mishnah.</i> Rav Ḥisda said, because he went two steps … Because he compromised it in court you say, he cannot collect?
“Similarly, the orphans can collect only by an oath;” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is from <i>Ketubot</i> Chapter 9. The first paragraph is from 9:8, Notes 185–197 (and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.2.2"><i>Giṭtin</i>5:3 Note 52</a>.).</i> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: One pays from an orphan’s property only if interest is due; Rebbi Nathan says, also a woman’s <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 22a" href="/Arakhin.22a">Babli <i>Arakhin</i>22a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, because of her sustenance. Rebbi Mattania said, who is worried about sustenance? Rebbi Simeon! Since Rebbi Simeon said, all depends on the female<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <i>Ketubot</i> and <i>Giṭtin</i> reads: “on the collection,” i. e., on the woman’s decision whether to ask for payment of the <i>ketubah</i> or for upkeep (lodging, food, and clothing) from the estate.</i>. What about it? For attraction<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 84a" href="/Ketubot.84a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>84a</a>.</i>, that everybody should jump to marry her. Rebbi Nathan said, also for robbery and torts. Rebbi Yose said, we stated both of these. Robbery from the following: “If it was mortgageable, he has to pay.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.1.1">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i>10:1</a>.</i>” For torts from the following: “One pays from minor orphans’ property only from the least valuable.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.3.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭtin</i>5:3</a>.</i>” So is the Mishnah: One pays for torts from minor orphans’ property only from the least valuable. But was it not stated, the son shall stand in the father’s place; one estimates torts from the most valuable land, creditors from average quality, and a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> from the least valuable? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it as everybody’s opinion about testamentary dispositions<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">In the other two sources correctly: “R. Yose ben R. Abun said, here about an adult orphan, there about an underage orphan.” The sentence here intruded from Halakhah 10 where it belongs. The word used here is Greek, διαθήκη “written will”. Cf Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i> 1:7.</i>.
Here you say, “one does not swear on the claim of a deaf-and-dumb person, an insane person, or a minor.” But there you say, “one swears for a minor and the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Both quotes are from Mishnah 6:6.</i>” If one pays from the minor’s property. <sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">From here on the text is in <i>Ketubot</i>9:9–10, Notes 201–204.</i> But can one collect from a person in his absence? Rebbi Jeremiah said, explain it about a contract for which interest is due. Would the court collect interest? Explain it that it was guaranteed for a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 50b:1:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.50b.1.1">Tosaphot <i>Giṭtin</i>50b <i>s.v</i>. יתומין</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i>9:9–10, Notes 205–212.</i> Alexis said before Rebbi Mana: We act much better than you do. We send edicts<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διατάγματα.</i>. If he comes, it is good. If he does not come, we irrevocably give away his properties. He answered him, we also act in this way; we have the heralds announce for thirty days. If he comes, it is good. If he does not come, we irrevocably give away his properties. He said to him, think of it, if he was in a distant place. He said to him, we send three letters<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Ketubot 88a:10:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Ketubot.88a.10.1">Tosaphot <i>Ketubot</i>88a, <i>s. v</i>. ורבא</a>.</i>, one after thirty days, one after the next thirty days, and one after the next thirty days. If he comes, it is good. If he does not come, we irrevocably give away his properties. Rebbi Ḥanina said, that is, if he was present at the trial and then fled. But if he was not present at the trial, we cannot irrevocably give away (nor)<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read with <i>Ketubot</i>“but only”. This reading is confirmed by a Geonic responsum (S. Assaf, <i>Responsa Geonica</i>, Jerusalem 1942, p. 103) which declares the statement to represent (Babylonian) general practice.</i> announce.
<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">A shortened version of <i>Ketubot</i>9: 9–10, Notes 213–216.</i> You find that one may say that Ḥanan and Rebbi Simeon said the same thing<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 88b" href="/Ketubot.88b"><i>Ketubot</i>88b</a>.</i>. Just as you say that practice follows Ḥanan, so practice follows Rebbi Simeon.
Rav said, if the debtor died, the creditor swears to his heirs. Even if the creditor died, the creditor’s heirs swear to the debtor. But if the debtor died first and after him the creditor, the creditor already was obligated for an oath to the debtor’s sons and nobody makes his sons inherit his oath<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 48a" href="/Shevuot.48a">Babli 48a</a>. Since the sons cannot inherit the father’s oath they cannot swear and the borrower’s estate does not have to pay.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan heard and said, should that one eat and enjoy it<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is little doubt that there was a loan, the creditor’s sons can swear the orphans’ oath (Mishnah 9), which is not the father’s oath, and collect on basis of that oath. In the Babli, this opinion is ascribed to R. Eleazar.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This is a shortened version of a paragraph in <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>1:8 (Notes 113–115); two different name traditions are reported in <i>Sefer Ha`iṭtur</i>1, 41b (Notes 49–51).</i> Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: A document<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">A term derived from Accadic <i>šaṭāru</i>“to write”, usually used for documents of indebtedness. The statement is in the Babli, <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>20b.</i> produced by the creditor in the creditor’s handwriting is invalid, for I am saying that he has practiced writing documents. This implies that in the hand of another it would be valid. Rebbi Abinna in the name of Samuel: It is always invalid unless it be in the creditors’s possession but in the debtor’s handwriting<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">A document not signed by outside witnesses.</i>. What about this? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, explain it according to everybody about a testamentary gift<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">A codicil in the testator’s handwriting attached to a will always is valid.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following have to swear without a claim: Partners<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If a partnership is dissolved, each partner has to swear that he did not retain any common property.</i>, and sharecroppers<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The owner may ask the sharecropper to swear that he delivered the entire crop to the landlord who then will return his share to him.</i>, and trustees<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐπίτροπος. He administers the estate of another person and at the end of his tenure has to swear that he did not illegally take anything for himself.</i>, and a wife trading in the house<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband gives her capital which she uses for trade, he is entitled to the entire gain and can demand that his wife swear that she did not retain anything of the net gain.</i>, and a family member<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">One of the brothers who acts as managing trustee for an undistributed inheritance. If the inheritance is distributed, he has to swear that he did not illegally take anything for himself.</i>. If he says, what do you claim from me? “I want you to swear to me,” he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The person obligated to swear cannot ask the person demanding the oath whether he suspects him of any dishonesty.</i>. After partners or sharecroppers distributed, he cannot force them to swear. If an oath was rolled over to him for another cause, one may roll everything over to him<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 7:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.7.1.9">Note 43</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.3"><i>Soṭah</i>2:6, Notes 166–168</a>. If a person is required to swear, the one demanding the oath can add to the text of the oath any claim he might have against the person swearing even if totally unrelated to the matter before the court and for which he could not force a separate oath.<br>A precondition for rollover, which is a biblical rule, is that the original oath must be by biblical standards; a purely rabbinic oath cannot be extended. This implies that the oath required by the Mishnah is considered biblical since the person acting for others in money matters automatically agrees that he is responsible and thereby fulfills the requirement of partial admission (Mishnah 6:1).</i>. The Sabbatical year remits an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Sabbatical remits debts (Mishnah <i>Ševi`it</i>10:1), the creditor cannot after the Sabbatical year ask for any oath regarding the remitted debt (Tosephta <i>Ševi`it</i>8:6).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sefer Ha`ittur</i>1, 43a (Note 81): R. Yose bar Bun.</i> said, this means if he trades without account; it does not apply if there are accounts. Also the family member trades without accounts<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If there is a regular account book which lists all transactions, the person trading can open his book for inspection instead of swearing, and therefore avoid the impression that he admits part of the claim. This rule is not in the Babli, nor in Rif or Maimonides.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira agrees with Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">This does not refer to the preceding paragraph; the third generation R. Ze`ira cannot be said to be dependent on the fifth generation R. Yose or R. Yose ben R. Bun. The reference is to a statement in <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>3:1 Note 20, that it is the free choice of a person to swear or to pay up and that a person willing to swear is free to change his opinion if too many other items are added to the oath.</i>. A man came for judgment before Rebbi Ze`ira who required him to swear for two <i>denars</i><sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">A very minor sum.</i>. He told him<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The opposing party in the suit.</i>, do I not owe you two <i>denars</i>? Here they are thrown to you. He<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The opposing party in the suit.</i> answered him, and cloth of fine wool X and Y. Rebbi Ze`ira said, either you give him everything he claims from you or you swear to him everything which he rolls over to you<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 7:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.7.1.9">Note 43</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.3"><i>Soṭah</i>2:6, Notes 166–168</a>. If a person is required to swear, the one demanding the oath can add to the text of the oath any claim he might have against the person swearing even if totally unrelated to the matter before the court and for which he could not force a separate oath.<br>A precondition for rollover, which is a biblical rule, is that the original oath must be by biblical standards; a purely rabbinic oath cannot be extended. This implies that the oath required by the Mishnah is considered biblical since the person acting for others in money matters automatically agrees that he is responsible and thereby fulfills the requirement of partial admission (Mishnah 6:1).</i>. How far does one roll over? Rebbi Joḥanan said, until he say to him, you are my slave<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 28a" href="/Kiddushin.28a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>28a</a>.</i>. Think of it, could he be Cohen and Hebrew slave<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Hebrew slave may have his earlobe pierced if he refuses to leave his state of servitude (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:6" href="/Exodus.21.6"><i>Ex</i>. 21:6</a>) but a Cohen with a pieced earlobe is barred from service in the Temple; no court could tolerate that a Cohen would be sold as a Hebrew slave.</i>? Is there such a slave today<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since the institution of Hebrew slavery was intrinsically intertwined with that of the Jubilee, it was abolished with the Babylonian captivity never to be reinstituted (cf. Introduction to Tractate <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 3" href="/Kiddushin.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> p. 3</a>).</i>?
Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, watch it for it is otherwise<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">From the arrangement of the Mishnah one gets the impression that partner, sharecropper, trustee, etc., are freed from their oath in the Sabbatical year. But the Sabbatical eliminates only oaths referring to loans which are actually annulled in it.</i>. What do we say? Watch it, for it is not negligible. “Owner,” “owner.<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 24:14" href="/Exodus.24.14"><i>Ex</i>. 24:14</a> anybody having a law suit is called בַּעַל; in the laws of annulment of debts (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:2" href="/Deuteronomy.15.2"><i>Deut</i>. 15:2</a>) the creditor is called בַּעַל. This sets up the argument of “equal cut” indicating that the person going to court is one subject to the laws of the Sabbatical.</i>” Since the owner mentioned there is subject to annulment, so also here he is subject to annulment<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Since after annulment the creditor cannot go to court to collect the debt, neither can he go to court to have an oath imposed on the debtor. (Explanation of the commentary <i>To`afot Re’em</i>, R. Abraham Abba Schiff, to <i>Sefer Yere’im</i>§164 Note 17). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 49a" href="/Shevuot.49a">Babli 49a</a> has a different interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:2" href="/Deuteronomy.15.2"><i>Deut</i>. 15:2</a>.</i>. The Sabbatical annuls things which are his hand’s loans<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The expression used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:2" href="/Deuteronomy.15.2"><i>Deut</i>. 15:2</a>.</i> and annuls the corresponding oaths; the Sabbatical does not annul things which are not his hand’s loans, nor does it annul the corresponding oaths. “For anything which the Sabbatical annuls it annuls the corresponding oath; anything which the Sabbatical does not annul it does not annul the corresponding oath<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ševi`it</i>8:6. There is no redundancy here. The preceding sentence was the argument, the last sentence is the tannaitic formulation.</i>.”

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The same Mishnah already was part of <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>7:9.</i> There are four kinds of keepers: The unpaid trustee and the borrower, the paid keeper, and the renter. The unpaid trustee swears about everything<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">He has to swear that he did not take anything and that in case anything was lost it was not his fault.</i>; the borrower pays for everything; the paid keeper and the renter swear about breakage, abduction, and death, but pay for the lost and the stolen<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">They do not pay for what happened beyond their control.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There are four kinds of keepers,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there are four kinds of keepers and they become liable only through acquisition<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 79a" href="/Bava_Kamma.79a">Babli, <i>Bava qamma</i>79a</a>, this is treated as an old (tannaitic or pre-tannaitic) rabbinic institution. Since keepers incur financial responsibilities, the moment in time when responsibility is transferred from owner to keeper must be well defined. The rules of this transfer are borrowed from the rules of buying and selling. Any act which would transfer property from the seller also transfers responsibility from owner to keeper; absent such an act no responsibility is transferred.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Also in matters of robbery they become liable only through acquisition. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, a <i>baraita</i> said so: “one who contracts with another for a field,”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>9:12. If somebody contracts to care for a field and then neglects his duty so that there is no yield, he can be sued for damages only if there was an act of acquisition.</i> etc. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, Rebbi Abun, also in theft cases they become liable only through acquisition<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The act of stealing is completed andthe thief responsible for double restitution only after an act of acquisition, such as putting the stolen object in his pocket. If the thief is discovered while he is dragging away some object in the owner’s house, he is not liable for double restitution since as long as the object was not lifted it was not acquired by the thief as long as he did not reach the public domain.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah in the name of Rebbi Phineas, our Mishnah said so: “If he was pulling it to lead it out but it died on the owner’s property, he is not liable. If he lifted it or had it led outside the owner’s property when it died, he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i>7:8, Notes 83–84, describing rustling of animals.</i>” If he lifted it inside the owner’s property it is as if he had taken it out of the entire property of the owner. If he was pulling it and it left, only after it left the entire property of the owner. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it was standing at his feeding trough<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 31b" href="/Ketubot.31b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>31b</a>, an opinion ascribed to Rabinna II. An animal pulled or pushed into the public domain is not thereby acquired by the thief; it becomes acquired only when it is brought into the thief’s private property.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: If it was standing at his feeding trough then even if he did not pull it<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since we hold that the property of a person acquires abandoned property found on it (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.4"><i>Giṭtin</i>6:2, Note 71</a>). Since the owners do not know where their lost object is, they cannot make a mental reservation which would annul the acquisition.</i>! But we must deal with the case that it was standing in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>loc. cit</i>., opinion ascribed to Rav Aḥa (bar Rava), of the last generation of Babylonian Amoraim.</i>. Does Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya follow the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say, “he shall be hit by less or more”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>3:13. The dishonest keeper in the opinion of the House of Shammai has to pay the larger of the values of the object either at the time it was given to him or when it should have been returned. The House of Hillel makes him pay the value at the time he took possession, R. Aqiba the value at the time of the claim. This has nothing to do with the problem here. The Mishnah there continues: “If somebody intends to take the deposit, the House of Shammai hold him liable, but the House of Hillel say, he is liable only from the moment he takes it.” Since in the public domain acquisition is effected only by lifting the object, not by dragging it, liability for cattle rustling in the public domain exists only for the House of Shammai.</i>? How do we hold? If it was standing in the public domain and he intended to rustle it when it died, according to everybody he became liable by that intention. If he did not start to pull it, according to everybody he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The statement is elliptic. If he intended to rustle an animal but did not do it, even the House of Shammai agree that thought without action is without legal consequences. If he pulled the animal along, and declared before witnesses that he intended to rustle this animal, even the House of Hillel will agree that he accepted liability for his act.</i>. But we most hold that it was standing at a feeding trough on his property<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in <i>Bava meṣi`a</i> notes that for the House of Shammai full liability starts when the animal enters the robber’s property while the House of Hillel require an act or at least a declared intent of acquisition.</i>. As Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, following the House of Shammai. Rebbi Abun said, there he acquired without the knowledge of its owner; but here when he acquires with the knowledge of its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">While an act of acquisition may be required for liability both of the keeper and of the thief, the details of what is considered as such an act are different in the two cases since acquisition by the will of the owner is less restrictive than that against his will.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 93a" href="/Bava_Kamma.93a">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>93a</a>.</i> It is written: <i>If a man give to another</i>, etc’, <i>to keep</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>.</i>. <i>To keep</i>, not to tear it up. <i>To keep</i>, not to throw it away. <i>To keep</i>, not to give it away as a gift. Rebbi Yose said, only if he said to give it to anyone he likes. But if he said, to X, since he is obligated to keep it is as if he kept it for him<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph gives the rules of the unpaid keeper who in case of loss has to swear that he did not appropriate the article for himself and that it was not lost by his negligence. But if he received the article in order to dispose of it, or to distribute it to the poor, even if it was lost there cannot be any oath. R. Yose points out that this applies only if the charge was to distribute to the poor, not if it was to be delivered to a designated person. Rashi explains in <i>Bava qamma</i> that the depositor cannot sue because he renounced ownership and the poor cannot sue since the keeper was free to give to any poor person of his choice.</i>.
It was stated: <i>If it was stolen from the man’s house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>.</i>. Not from his roof. Rebbi Eleazar said, that means, from an unprotected roof. But a fortified roof is like a house<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Taking anything from a flat roof visible from the outside is not theft but robbery since it was in the open. There can be no double restitution in this case. But if the roof was surrounded by a wall so that nothing deposited there could be seen from the street it is theft and subject to its laws. Keeping a deposit on an open roof is gross negligence on the part of the keeper.</i>.
It was stated, <i>if it was stolen from the man’s house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>.</i>; not from the borrower’s house. <i>If it was stolen from the man’s house</i>; not from the house of the paid keeper or the renter<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The verse speaks of the unpaid keeper, who swears that he did not take it and that he was not negligent but does not pay. The corresponding cases for the paid keeper and the borrower are not mentioned in the verses. The definite article is interpreted to mean that the verse insists that <i>it was stolen from</i> this <i>man’s house</i>; the rule does not apply to others. By the reason explained later it is clear that the borrower cannot swear; he must pay. There is no intrinsic reason in the verse to exempt the paid keeper and the renter. Why are they exempt? In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 11a" href="/Bekhorot.11a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>11a</a>, the verse is read to exclude institutions; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.1.2-4"><i>Bava qamma</i>7:1</a> (5d 46), Notes 10 ff.</i>? Since he is obligated to watch it, it is as if it referred to him<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">There is a reason to extend the rule of the unpaid keeper to the paid one.</i>; for you may say that there are three paragraphs<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6-8" href="/Exodus.22.6-8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6–8</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 9-11" href="/Exodus.9-11">9–11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13-14" href="/Exodus.13-14">13–14</a>. A similar statement in the Babli, <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>94b.</i>. The last one about the borrower, the middle one about the paid keeper and the renter, the first one about the unpaid keeper. The borrower who profits from all pays everything. The paid keeper or the renter, because he profits partially and gives partial profit, swears about part and pays part. The unpaid keeper who does not profit at all swears and leaves. What does he swear? I did not commit anything<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He did not take anything for his personal use and was not criminally negligent.</i>. What is the situation if others know that he did not commit anything? Let us hear from the following: If the thief was found but has nothing with which to pay, may he say to him<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of an object stolen from an unpaid keeper. If he cannot recoup his loss he might be tempted to let the keeper swear in the hope that he might prefer to pay rather than swear.</i>, swear to me that you were not thinking to take it? Let us hear from the following: <i>If the thief was not found</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:7" href="/Exodus.22.7"><i>Ex</i>. 22:7</a>. If the thief was found, the unpaid keeper is absolved from any oath. <i>Mekhilta dR</i>. <i>Ismael Neziqin</i>15.</i>. Therefore, it he was found he is not liable.
It is written, <i>if a man give to his neighbor a donkey, or an ox, or a sheep</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a>.</i> etc. <i>An oath before the Eternal shall be between the two of them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:10" href="/Exodus.22.10"><i>Ex</i>. 22:10</a>.</i>. Stolen, <i>if it was stolen stealing from him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:11" href="/Exodus.22.11"><i>Ex</i>. 22:11</a>. The verse is intentionally misquoted; it is written וְאִם “and if”, not אִם “if”, as noted in the sequel.</i>. Lost, <i>and if</i>, to include the lost one<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This is Rebbi Aqiba’s signature argument that all conjunctions which are not absolutely necessary imply an addition which can only be determined by tradition.</i>. So far following Rebbi Aqiba. Following Rebbi Ismael? Rebbi Ismael stated: Since you say that he pays for theft which is close to duress, for loss which is not close to duress not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>94b, this is characterized not as a statement of R. Ismael but as a Galilean Amoraic statement in the spirit of R. Ismael. A different argument is in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i>16, (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 305).</i>?
For the borrower only the broken is written. From where loss and theft? It is logical. Since the paid keeper and the renter who do not pay for the broken or the dead have to pay for theft and loss, the borrower who pays for the broken or the dead certainly has to pay for theft and loss<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This an argument of R. Ismael; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i>16, (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 306).</i>. It was stated about this: This is an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> which cannot be challenged. From where the abducted? It is said here, <i>it was broken or died</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:13" href="/Exodus.22.13"><i>Ex</i>. 22:13</a>.</i>; and it is said there, <i>it dies or was broken</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a>.</i>. Since there the abducted was included, here also the abducted was included. So far following Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This is <i>not</i> an argument of R. Aqiba but of R. Ismael [<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i>16, (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 306)]. R. Aqiba’s argument is attributed here to R. Ismael.</i>. Following Rebbi Ismael? Rebbi Ismael follows Rebbi Nathan. Rebbi Nathan says, <i>or</i>, to include the abducted. Or following Rebbi Meïr who said, an equal cut at the place it comes from<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 4:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.4.2.2">Chapter 4, Note 33</a>. If <i>broken or died</i> and <i>died or was broken</i> defines an equal cut, since the abducted is mentioned in v. 9 it also is implied in v. 13.</i>. Since there one swears for duress, also here one swears for duress. Still following Rebbi Nathan, for Rebbi Nathan said, <i>or it died</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:13" href="/Exodus.22.13"><i>Ex</i>. 22:13</a>.</i>, to include the abducted one<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">A follower of R. Aqiba has the choice of arguing either following R. Meïr or R. Nathan.</i>.
Rebbi Yudan asked: <sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The proviso that the keeper may have to pay double restitution is mentioned only for the unpaid keeper, where <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a> is interpreted to mean that the keeper claimed the deposit was stolen but it was proven in court that he himself took it. The paid keeper or the renter must pay for stolen items (22:11); in general, a claim that the item was stolen by such a keeper is an agreement that he has to pay; even if the claim is false there is no reason for double restitution. But if the keeper claims that the item was abducted by force, he may swear to this fact to free himself from paying (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9">v. 22:9</a>). An argument is missing here, which is supplied in the Babli, <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>57b, <i>viz.</i>, that in an orderly commonwealth an armed robber will hide his weapons in public and only show them to his intended victim. This makes armed robbery an instance of theft (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.3.4"><i>Sanhedrin</i>8:3, Notes 44ff</a>.). Therefore the question arises whether a false claim of armed robbery makes the paid keeper or the renter liable for double restitution.</i> If he claimed the claim of a thief and duress? He told him, an armed robber came and took it; does he have to pay double restitution of duress? Rebbi Yose said, if it were so one should state “the paid keeper and the renter;” but we have stated: We do not find that the paid keeper and the renter pay double restitution of duress. On this we have stated, “this is an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> which is questionable”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The text is incorrect. The scribe first wrote correctly שאין עליו תשובה “which cannot be challenged” and then corrected it wrongly to the text given. The reference is to the argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> presented in the preceding paragraph where it was stated that the argument is <i>un</i> questionable.
