But if there are instances of theft where the paid keeper and the renter follow the rules of the unpaid keeper, the argument becomes invalid; it is more than questionable, it is shown to be false.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina said, if you say so, should you say, this is an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> which is questionable<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The text is incorrect. The scribe first wrote correctly שאין עליו תשובה “which cannot be challenged” and then corrected it wrongly to the text given. The reference is to the argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> presented in the preceding paragraph where it was stated that the argument is <i>un</i> questionable. But if there are instances of theft where the paid keeper and the renter follow the rules of the unpaid keeper, the argument becomes invalid; it is more than questionable, it is shown to be false.</i>, for he can tell him, let us say that the borrower pays double restitution. On this it was stated, this is an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> which cannot be challenged<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore the claim of armed robbery is not a claim of theft; there never can be double restitution by the paid keeper or the renter. The Babli (<i>Bava meṣi`a</i>57a/b) disagrees and holds that in this case the paid keeper has to pay double restitution (Maimonides <i>Hilkhot Genevah</i>4:4).</i>.
It was said, <i>if its owners were with him, he does not have to pay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:14" href="/Exodus.22.14"><i>Ex</i>. 22:14</a>. If the borrower had asked not only for the use of an animal and/or agricultural or mechanical implements but also had asked their owner to help him in his work, then the disposition over animals or tools never was transferred to the borrower; in case the animal died or it and the tools broke or were taken by force the borrower does not have to pay. But if the authority over animal and/or tools was transferred, the borrower has to pay if anything happens to them.</i>. Does he have to swear? Rebbi Ze`ira said, he swears. Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi La both are saying, he does not swear<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli does not treat the question, which seems to be that even though the owner retains the power of disposition over his property, the borrower might have to swear that he was not in any way the cause of the accident.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi La: “Breakage, abduction, and death for which he is not liable in the cases of the paid keeper and the renter<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a> excuses the paid keeper in the case of an unobserved accident but requires an oath that the keeper did never ever use the animal or object for himself (or the renter that he never overstepped the conditions of his lease).</i>, and the borrower with the owner is not liable, without the owner is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:13" href="/Exodus.22.13"><i>Ex</i>. 22:13</a>.</i>; loss and theft where the paid keeper and the renter are liable, is it not that <i>a fortiori</i> the borrower be not liable with the owner but liable without the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">There is no verse referring to the responsibility of the borrower for cases of loss and theft but it cannot be less than that of breakage, etc. It cannot be more since the conclusion of a logical argument cannot be stronger than the premise.</i>?” For him who says it is obvious that he swears, should he not have to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since the oath would absolve from payment, it is clear that R. Ze`ira holds that the borrower has to pay if he cannot swear. But this contradicts the argument of the <i>baraita</i> since it also applies to the case where the owner works with the borrower.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This is the late Amora R. Ḥinnena, not the early R. Ḥanina mentioned earlier.</i> in the name of Rebbi Yudan: A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ze`ira. “The borrower, for whom the Torah was restrictive, with the owner is not liable, without the owner is liable; the paid keeper, for whom the Torah was lenient, <i>a fortiori</i> with the owner should not be liable, without the owner should be liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In fact his responsibility depends on whether there was a formal act of transfer of responsibility, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 8:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.8.1.2">Note 4</a>.</i>” If you are saying, his problem was swearing, he should have stated “the paid keeper and the renter<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since in general the paid keeper and the renter follow the same rules. But since the renter pays the owner for the use, in matters of payment there is reason to differentiate between the two.</i>.” That means he only needs it for payment. It is difficult for Rebbi Ze`ira: the borrower swears if the owner was with him; if the owner was not with him he must pay. The unpaid keeper swears, whether the owner was with him or was not with him<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i>. You are saying that where the borrower pays the paid keeper swears<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If the loss was because of the unlawful actions of third persons.</i>. Where the paid keeper swears, the unpaid keeper should not be liable. Where the paid keeper pays, the unpaid keeper swears<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If the loss was because of the negligence of the keeper, when the object was lost, or probable negligence, when it was stolen.</i>. What do you state about an unpaid keeper when the owner be with him<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As stated before, if there was no formal transfer of responsibility, the unpaid keeper does not even swear.</i>? But some are asking, what do you state about an unpaid and a paid keeper, whether or not the owner be with him<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since the distinction is made only for the borrower, we do not even know whether such a distinction is of any relevance for the other kinds of keepers.</i>? Rebbi Abin said, <i>any word of criminality</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This explicitly excludes the distinction about the participation of the owner for paid and unpaid keeper; the previously quoted <i>baraitot</i> are contradicted.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, do we not find that the Torah treated loss and theft equally for the borrower? Therefore, we shall treat breakage, abduction, and death equally both for the unpaid and the paid keepers<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since the oath would absolve from payment, it is clear that R. Ze`ira holds that the borrower has to pay if he cannot swear. But this contradicts the argument of the <i>baraita</i> since it also applies to the case where the owner works with the borrower.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of the Halakhah does not refer to Mishnah 8:1 but to <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>3:13. There exists a full parallel in the Escurial ms. of <i>Neziqin</i>(E), reproduced above, and a not quite complete version published by R. H.Y.D. Azulai almost 200 years before the discovery of E; cf. <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>3:13, Note 89. The Leiden ms. there quotes only the first line followed by “etc.” This justifies considering the text of E as second source for the text here.<br>A short parallel to the discussion here is in the Babli, <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>44a.</i> What is the reason of the House of Shammai? <i>Any word of criminality</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>3:13 states that according to the House of Shammai, a keeper becomes unfaithful and liable for any and all damages to the deposit if he thinks of appropriating it for his own use; according to the House of Hillel only the actual taking triggers liability. The House of Shammai include in “word of criminality” also unspoken words which would prevent the keeper from swearing to his innocence.</i>. How do the House of Hillel explain the reason of the House of Shammai? “Criminality,” <i>any word of criminality</i>. Explain it if he appropriated it through an agent<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Giving orders certainly involves words. If the order was criminal, the person giving the order can no longer swear truthfully that he did not take the deposit even if he himself never touched it.</i>.
Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina asked, what was said, to take? Took<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second part of Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>3:13: “If he tilted the [wine] amphora and took a <i>quartarius</i>, and then it broke, [the unpaid keeper] pays only for a <i>quartarius</i>. If he lifted it and took a <i>quartarius</i>, and then it broke, he pays for all.” It is assumed that the amphora did not break because of any action of the keeper’s; then the unpaid keeper does not have to pay for the loss. But if he lifted the amphora with the intent of stealing from it, it becomes his property and he is liable for the entire value of amphora and contents. The question now is whether “took” means that he actually removed anything from the amphora’s contents or whether just moving the amphora with the intent of taking triggers full liability. Since in a sale of movables it is not the payment which transfers property rights and liabilities from seller to buyer but the buyer’s moving the item, it is not unreasonable to read “took” as “intent to take” since action of moving is decisive.</i>? Did not Rebbi Hoshaia state, “it broke”; if it turned into vinegar he pays for everything<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">R. Hoshaia qualifies the first statement. If he only tilted the amphora but did not move it from its place and it broke he only pays for what he took, to apply only to the case of breakage. If he tilted the amphora, took some wine, and then the wine turned into vinegar, he is liable to pay for all the wine since we say that tilting the wine and pouring from it caused the rest to turn into vinegar.</i>? It turned into vinegar only because of its spoilage<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Read with E: “the motion”.</i>. Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar. “Grabbing<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical expression שָׁלַח יָדוֹ “sent his hand”, mentioned both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:7" href="/Exodus.22.7"><i>Ex</i>. 22:7</a> and 10.</i>” which was mentioned for the unpaid keeper makes him liable only if he diminished<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Only if he actually took from it, not if the volume diminished by his handling the amphora for his own purposes.</i>. Rebbi Samuel asked before Rebbi Abbahu: What has been said about taking? That he diminished or even if he did not diminish? He said to him, is that not Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina’s question, to take, took? If you say “to take”, even if he did not diminish<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">But it lost volume after he handled it not for its own sake.</i>. If you say “took,” only when he diminished. If you say “to take”, if he returned it either to its former place or to another place he is not liable. If you say “took,” if he returned it to its former place he is not liable, to another place he is liable.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “Guarding” is mentioned for the unpaid keeper and “guarding” is mentioned for the paid keeper, but “guarding” mentioned for the unpaid keeper is not similar to “guarding” mentioned for the recipient of a fee. “Guarding” mentioned for the unpaid keeper; if he guarded it sufficiently he is not liable. For a paid keeper one estimates it only relative to his body. Therefore one judges him; if the guarding was appropriate he is not liable, otherwise he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Text of E: “Guarding mentioned for the paid keeper, even if he enclosed it in an iron wall one is not satisfied that he guarded. If it was in his power to save, he is liable. If it was not in his power to save, he is not liable.” This refers to the rule that the paid keeper is liable for theft and loss.</i>. One does not say, if another had been there, he could have saved<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since the keeper is paid, it would have been the responsibility of the owner to hire a sufficient number of guards to watch over his property.</i>. If he saved, he is not liable; if he did not save, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This belongs before the preceding sentence; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 8:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.8.1.12">Note 62</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: “Grabbing” is mentioned for the unpaid keeper and “grabbing” is mentioned for the paid keeper, but “grabbing” mentioned for the unpaid keeper is not comparable to “grabbing” mentioned for the paid keeper. “Grabbing” mentioned for the unpaid keeper does not make him liable unless he move it<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">As made clear in Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>3:13.</i>. “Grabbing” mentioned for the paid keeper, he is liable from the moment he puts his staff and his bag on it<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since the paragraph about the paid keeper (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9-11" href="/Exodus.22.9-11"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9–11</a>) refers to animals, the example given refers to animals. If the keeper has to shepherd an animal, he cannot use it as his beast of burden even for the most minute load to his own benefit.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Nehorai heard this and said, I am not accepting this, but in any case he is not liable unless he move it<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">He requires an act of acquisition; he is liable only if he makes the animal move from the place where he put his bag on it.</i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: But did not Rebbi Hoshaia state it in this way? If “grabbing” had not been mentioned for the unpaid keeper as unnecessary<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since earlier we had established a strict order of liabilities, the unpaid keeper being liable less than the paid keeper or renter who in turn are liable less than the borrower, a mention of liability after “grabbing”, appropriating the deposit, for the unpaid keeper would automatically imply the same for the paid keeper and the renter. In the first version, the argument is upside down as R. Yose points out.</i>, I would have inferred from the recipient of a fee. Since the recipient of a fee, with whom the Torah was strict, does not become liable unless he move it, it is only logical that an unpaid keeper, with whom the Torah was lenient, not be liable unless he move it. For which purpose was “grabbing” mentioned for the unpaid keeper since it was unnecessary? To be strict with the recipient of a fee; from the moment he puts his staff or his bag on it he is liable. Rebbi Yose said, if you say so, you infer about the premise to restrict it. For one could say to him, since the recipient of a fee, with whom the Torah was strict, does not become liable unless he move it, the unpaid keeper, with whom the Torah was lenient, should not be liable even if he move it. But it must be the following: “Grabbing” should not have been mentioned for the paid keeper as unnecessary, for I would have inferred from the unpaid keeper<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since earlier we had established a strict order of liabilities, the unpaid keeper being liable less than the paid keeper or renter who in turn are liable less than the borrower, a mention of liability after “grabbing”, appropriating the deposit, for the unpaid keeper would automatically imply the same for the paid keeper and the renter. In the first version, the argument is upside down as R. Yose points out.</i>. Since the unpaid keeper, with whom the Torah was lenient, is not liable unless he move it, the recipient of a fee, with whom the Torah was strict, becomes liable when he moves it. For which purpose was “grabbing” mentioned for the recipient of a fee since it was unnecessary? To be strict; from the moment he puts his staff or his bag on it he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mention of “grabbing” in v. 10 is unnecessary if it meant the same as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:7" href="/Exodus.22.7">v. 7</a>, it follows that the rules of liability for the paid keeper are more strict than those for the unpaid keeper. The detail, in what these rules are more strict, is not mentioned in the biblical text; it must be left to the traditional understanding of these rules.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He said to the unpaid keeper, “where is my ox?” He told him, “it died,” but it broke, or was abducted, or stolen, or lost<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">These are the cases enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9">9</a>, where the unpaid keeper swears but does not have to pay. Breakage refers to vessels, not to animals; it is included since it appears in the verse.</i>. “It broke,” but it died, or was abducted, or stolen, or lost. “”It was abducted,” but it died, or broke, or was stolen, or lost. “It was stolen,” but it died, or broke, or was abducted, or lost. “It was lost,” but it died, or broke, or was abducted, or stolen. “I want you to swear;” he answered “Amen”. He is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he swore under a false category, since he swore correctly to the fact that he does not have to pay he is not liable for the sacrifice required for a false oath.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He said to the unpaid keeper,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.19"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:10, Notes 190–192</a>.</i> Rav said, he is not liable for a keeper’s oath but is liable because of a blurted oath. Rebbi Joḥanan said, since it is a religious duty to appease him, he is not liable because of a blurted oath. In Rav’s opinion, is there no religious duty to appease him? One appeases with truthful statements, not with lies.
Rebbi Joḥanan asked: May the oath of a deviant woman<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The suspected adulteress who is put under oath by the Cohen, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num</i>. 5:22</a>.</i> be liable for a blurted oath? Where do we hold? If she is in error in sin and oath she is not deviant<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">If she slept with a man thinking it was her husband and swears while in this belief, she is not forbidden to her husband and not deviant.</i>. If she is intentional in sin and error there is no sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">There is no sacrifice for an intentional false oath.</i>. Rav follows Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Halakhah 3:1. R. Aqiba admits the possibility of sacrifices for inadvertent blurted oaths (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>) referring to past events as well as future ones but R. Ismael allows only future- directed blurted oaths. Rav (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 3:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.3.1.2">Chapter 3 Note 7</a>) admits only past-directed blurted oaths; as just shown this is not possible in the case of the deviant woman.</i>. If Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr explains the repetition “Amen, Amen” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:22" href="/Numbers.5.22"><i>Num</i>. 5:22</a> that the first one is past directed, the second future directed. R. Ismael might agree that this is a special case where a biblical verse also requires the inclusion of references to past events. Then the question of R. Joḥanan might make sense since the possibility of an oath by a deviant woman depends on past events but the designation as blurted oath must depend on future happenings, not covered by the previous argument.</i> would argue like Rebbi Meïr it would follow, since Rebbi Meïr said<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Soṭah</i>3:1</a>.</i>: “Amen that I was not defiled, Amen that I shall not be defiled.” But you may question this<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since practice would follow neither R. Ismael nor R. Meïr there seems to be no point for R. Joḥanan’s question.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “Where is my ox?” He told him, “I do not know.” “What are you telling?” But if had died, or broke, or was abducted, or stolen, or lost. “I want you to swear;” he answered “Amen”. He is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he swore under a false category, since he swore correctly to the fact that he does not have to pay he is not liable for the sacrifice required for a false oath.</i>.<br>“Where is my ox?” He told him, “it was lost.” “I want you to swear;” he answered “Amen”. Witnesses testify that he ate it: he has to pay its value<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay restitution. But since he did not claim falsely that it was stolen, there is no fine. In general there is no fine imposed if the culprit confesses before witnesses testify against him.</i>. If he confessed himself he pays the value, and a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-25" href="/Leviticus.5.20-25"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20–25</a>.</i>.<br>“Where is my ox?” He told him, “it was stolen.” “I want you to swear;” he answered “Amen”. Witnesses testify that he stole it: he has to pay double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This is not the double restitution of the common thief (since the owner himself handed the animal or vessel over to him) but the double restitution required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i>. If he confessed himself he pays the value, and a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-25" href="/Leviticus.5.20-25"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20–25</a>.</i>.<br>He said to a person on the market, “where is my ox which you stole?” This one says, “I did not steal,” but witnesses testify that he stole it: he has to pay double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This is not the double restitution of the common thief (since the owner himself handed the animal or vessel over to him) but the double restitution required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i>. If he slaughtered or sold it, he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex</i>. 21:37</a>.</i>. If he saw that witnesses appeared and said, “I stole but did neither slaughter nor sell,” he only pays its value<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">He did not swear; there is no additional fifth and sacrifice. There is no fine for the confessed thief; therefore he pays only the value even though he falsely claimed not to have slaughtered or sold the animal.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He said to the borrower, “where is my ox,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava qamma</i>65a/b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 8:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).8.8">Tosephta <i>Bava qamma</i>8:8</a>.</i>: “He<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A paid keeper or renter who pays for stolen and lost deposits but pays no double restitution if he in fact stole it but claims that it was lost.</i> claimed a claim of loss, swore, and confessed. Whether before witnesses came or after witnesses came he pays the value, a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-25" href="/Leviticus.5.20-25"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20–25</a>.</i>. If he claimed a claim of thief, swore, and confessed before witnesses came he pays the value, a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice. When after witnesses came he pays double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This is not the double restitution of the common thief (since the owner himself handed the animal or vessel over to him) but the double restitution required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i> and a reparation sacrifice; the fifth is counted for him in the double payment, the words of Rebbi Jacob. They said to Rebbi Jacob, where do we find a reparation sacrifice without fifth? He said to them, if he swore, and swore, and swore, we find a reparation sacrifice without fifth. But the rabbis say, there is a fifth for the capital; there is no fifth for oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Jacob holds that if he swore falsely he has to pay the fifth of the value of the deposit but if he then repeats the false oath there is no underlying money involved and therefore no money due. The rabbis hold that double restitution is only due from the thief after conviction by the court. For payment after confession neither fifth nor a sacrifice are due.</i>.”
Another explanation: <i>He shall pay the whole worth and add its fifths to it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:24" href="/Leviticus.5.24"><i>Lev</i>. 5:24</a>. The plural “fifths” implies that for repeated false oaths about the same subject additional fifths are due (<i>Sifra Ḥovah</i>, <i>Wayyiqra II, Parašah</i>13:12; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 103b" href="/Bava_Kamma.103b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>103b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 54b" href="/Bava_Metzia.54b"><i>Bava meṣi`a</i>54b</a>). This disproves R. Jacob’s statement.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, it was stated thus: “He claimed a claim of loss, swore, and dedicated a sacrifice. Since if he confessed after witnesses came it would be sanctified, it is sanctified here. He claimed a claim of thief, swore, and dedicated a sacrifice. Since if he confessed after witnesses came it would not be sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">A reparation offering is obligatory; it cannot be voluntary. Since the rabbis hold that double restitution excludes oath and confession, no sacrifice is possible even in the case where the verse demands double restitution in any case, as for the paid keeper who falsely claimed that a thief had stolen the item (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 8:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.8.3.5">Note 74</a>).</i>, it is not sanctified here, but following Rebbi Jacob it would be sanctified.”
Rebbi Joḥanan said, if somebody claims against another a claim of thief he pays double restitution. If he slaughtered or sold, he pays<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The following implies that one has to read “does not pay.”</i> quadruple or quintuple restitution. There, they say, he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia they transmit R. Joḥanan’s saying as requiring multiple restitution; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 106b" href="/Bava_Kamma.106b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>106b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Pedat in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: There<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4.</i> we state and it supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “‘Where is my ox?’ He told him, ‘it was lost.’ ‘I want you to swear;’ he answered ‘Amen’. Witnesses testify that he ate it; he has to pay its value<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay restitution. But since he did not claim falsely that it was stolen, there is no fine. In general there is no fine imposed if the culprit confesses before witnesses testify against him.</i>. If he confessed himself he pays the value, and a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-25" href="/Leviticus.5.20-25"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20–25</a>.</i>.” Is there eating without slaughtering<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah does not require multiple restitution for eating the animal entrusted to him. This proves that there is no quadruple or quintuple restitution and double restitution only if the keeper claims that it was stolen from him.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, explain it if another slaughtered<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 71a" href="/Bava_Kamma.71a">Babli, <i>Bava qamma</i>71a</a>, emphatically disagrees, in the name of R. Joḥanan. Since the verse brackets slaughter and sale together and a sale is impossible without a third party, so slaughter by a third party acting on his orders also triggers the liability of the thief.</i>. And the following said so: He said to one in the market place, “where is my ox which you stole?” He says, “I did not steal.” “I want you to swear,” and he says “Amen.” He is (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read “is liable.”</i> liable. Because he is one in the market place. But if he said it to one of the keepers, that one would be liable<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read “would not be liable (for quadruple or quintuple restitution).”</i>. The <i>baraita</i> when he ate it and then swore. What Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he swore and after that ate it<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If the unpaid keeper had sworn or the paid keeper had paid, the animal became his and if he slaughtered afterwards there can be no additional restitution.</i>.
“He said to the recipient of a fee,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one who claims before his neighbor a claim of thief in a case of loss is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">A person who finds a lost object with distinguishing marks by which the owner can convincingly describe it is obligated to return it to its owner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:1-3" href="/Deuteronomy.22.1-3"><i>Deut</i>. 22:1–3</a>); by picking it up he automatically becomes an unpaid keeper. If the owner hears that the object was found by that person and comes to reclaim it, if the finder had honestly told him that he lost it again he would not be liable since he was an unpaid keeper. But if he falsely claimed that it was stolen, he is liable for double restitution required for any false claim of theft (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>).</i>. “Where is my lost object?” He told him, “it was stolen.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, one who claims before his neighbor a claim of thief is liable only after an oath. What is the reason? It is said here “grabbing” and it says there “grabbing.” Since “grabbing” mentioned there only applies after an oath, so also “grabbing” mentioned here only applies after an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">“Grabbing” is mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:7" href="/Exodus.22.7"><i>Ex</i>. 22:7</a> regarding court procedures involving an oath for an unpaid keeper who falsely claims that the object was stolen and has to pay double restitution. It also is mentioned in v. 10 regarding the paid keeper who for an actually stolen object has to pay its value (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:11" href="/Exodus.22.11">v. 11</a>) but double restitution for a false claim of theft for which he swore falsely.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one who claims a claim of loss, swore to him, and afterwards claimed a claim of thief is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 107b" href="/Bava_Kamma.107b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>107b</a>. Since he discharged his obligations towards the owner by his oath, the second claim is irrelevant.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan asked: May one be liable for a blurted oath in case of an oath regarding a claim of thief<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">If he swears for the second claim (truly or falsely), is this a blurted oath in the meaning of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>?</i>? The argument of Rebbi Joḥanan seems to be inverted. There he said, if he claimed a claim of loss, swore to him, and afterwards claimed a claim of thief is not liable. And here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">How can he say he is not liable and then make him liable for a blurted oath?</i>? There it is obvious to him, here it is problematic for him<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">He is not liable for an oath about a deposit; this implies nothing for the rules of blurted oaths.</i>. What is problematic for him? He saw and found it simple<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The previous answer is incorrect. He saw that the answer is simple: since the second oath is not required it would be a blurted oath if true and a false oath if false, sinful in any case.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph said<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 106b-107a" href="/Bava_Kamma.106b-107a">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>106b/107a</a>. He notes that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a> in general is read as applying to court proceedings (6:1 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.8.1.1">Note 1</a>, <i>Bava meṣi`a</i>1:1 Note 9) expressing the general conditions when an oath can be imposed. Therefore the double restitution imposed at the end of the verse has to be in such a proceeding, and double restitution imposed on the paid keeper or renter is imposed on the same basis by the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 8:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.8.3.5">Note 101</a>.</i>, one who claims before his neighbor a claim of thief is liable only after he denied in court. Where do we hold? If about him who already owes an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">By court order.</i> to his neighbor, even if he was swearing out of court he is liable. But we must hold about him who saw them coming. They wanted to make him swear but he jumped in and swore<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Before there was any court proceeding. This oath does not protect him from having to swear another oath imposed by the court; therefore it does not trigger double restitution.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: When it was standing at his feeding trough<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 107b" href="/Bava_Kamma.107b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>107b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked: how was it said? “If it was standing” or “even if it was standing”<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">What kind of testimony will make him pay double restitution? Since he is a keeper and the animal was delivered to him, it needs an act of acquisition to be stolen and then this has to be classified as robbery, rather than as theft. If the animal was standing at a feeding trough on his property,there it belongs. If he claims that it was stolen, he is liable for double restitution. If he took it as his property, he is a robber, the animal is his property, he has to pay for it, but any oath will be irrelevant since it would not be about the other’s property. But if the formulation was “even if it was standing at his feeding trough” then it would apply even if he took it by robbery. (Rashi in <i>Bava qamma</i>.)</i>? If you say “even if it was standing,” it makes no difference<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Whether he took the animal before he swore or after.</i>. If you say “if it was standing,” then the argument of Rebbi Joḥanan is inverted. There, he said, if he claimed before him a claim of loss, had been swearing to him, selected a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">For his false oath.</i>, and then claimed a claim of duress, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">For double restitution, since by the oath and payment he acquired the animal and the second claim is baseless; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 8:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.8.3.5">Note 102</a>.</i>. But here you are saying so? Rebbi La said, there is a difference since he absolved himself of confession by the oath<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 107b" href="/Bava_Kamma.107b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>107b</a>. Since he discharged his obligations towards the owner by his oath, the second claim is irrelevant.</i>. They objected to the opinion of Rebbi Ze`ira: “‘Where is my ox?’ He told him, ‘it was stolen.’ ‘I want you to swear;’ he answered ‘Amen’. Witnesses testify that he stole it: he has to pay double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This is not the double restitution of the common thief (since the owner himself handed the animal or vessel over to him) but the double restitution required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i>. If he confessed himself he pays the value, and a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-25" href="/Leviticus.5.20-25"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20–25</a>.</i>.” But here when he moved it<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">He did not move it but acquired it by the oath as if he had moved it after buying.</i> by claiming that it was lost. Afterwards he claimed a claim of thief and is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 107b" href="/Bava_Kamma.107b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>107b</a>. Since he discharged his obligations towards the owner by his oath, the second claim is irrelevant.</i>. Explain it that he swore to him but slaughtered it afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">When he already had acquired the animal by his oath.</i>. The students of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Julianus say, explain it that he slaughtered it when it was lying down<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">He was an honest keeper beforehand; the animal was still its owner’s property. He did not move the animal with the intent of appropriating it; the animal was acquired by slaughter. This argument presupposes that quadruple or quintuple restitution applies only for slaughter or sale after theft.</i>. Is there slaughter without sale<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">It should read: “Is there restitution for slaughter without prior theft”?</i>? Following Symmachos who said, there can be slaughter and sale without theft<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that quadruple or quintuple restitution is independent of restitution for theft; theft has not to be proven, only illegal slaughter. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 75b" href="/Bava_Kamma.75b">Babli (<i>Bava qamma</i>75b</a>) he states that if there were witnesses for the theft whose testimony stood up and witnesses for slaughter or sale which were found perjured, the accused has to pay double restitution for theft and the perjurers double or triple restitution for the false accusation, showing that slaughter or sale can be separated from theft. (The thief of cattle who sells his booty has to pay five times, not seven.)</i>. Samuel said, if there came no witnesses of the theft, but there came witnesses of the slaughter; he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that while quadruple or quintuple restitution applies only to stolen animals, the theft has not to be proven in court if there are witnesses for illegal slaughter.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he saw that witnesses to the theft were approaching and he said “I stole,” since his confession is inoperative he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In this entire paragraph, “liable” has to be replaced by “not liable” and vice versa. The principle that a confession frees a person from a fine is valid only if the confession preceded the appearance of witnesses since witnesses make his confession unnecessary. If the thief waits until the injured party has found witnesses he has to pay double restitution: the value of the object which he took as a debt and the double as a fine.</i>. If he saw that witnesses to the slaughter were approaching and he said “I slaughtered,” since his confession is (another opinion: not)<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Copyist’s notes; the copyist’s correction of the incorrect text from which he copied.</i> operative<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that the witnesses have no knowledge about how he acquired the animal which he slaughtered; he could have bought it from the owner or from a thief. The person who bought from the thief cannot be sued for multiple restitution. Therefore his confession is as irrelevant as is the witnesses’ testimony; there is no place for any liability. If he also admits to having stolen the animal he liquidates his debt by paying its value.</i> he is not liable (another opinion: is liable). Rebbi Ze`ira asked: that witnesses to rape were approaching and he said “I raped”? Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This is the late Amora R. Ḥinnena, not the early R. Ḥanina mentioned earlier.</i> said, the Mishnah follows Rebbi Simeon who said the main claim is for the fine<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5:6, Note 79.</i>. Therefore his confession is inoperative and he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In this entire paragraph, “liable” has to be replaced by “not liable” and vice versa. The principle that a confession frees a person from a fine is valid only if the confession preceded the appearance of witnesses since witnesses make his confession unnecessary. If the thief waits until the injured party has found witnesses he has to pay double restitution: the value of the object which he took as a debt and the double as a fine.</i>. But the rabbis say, the main claim is not for the fine. Therefore his confession is operative<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">For the rabbis the main claim of the father of an underage rape victim is for the diminution of his daughter’s chances on the marriage market; this is a claim for damages, not a fine, and in this respect “the admission of the debtor is worth 100 witnesses.”</i> and he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In this entire paragraph, “liable” has to be replaced by “not liable” and vice versa. The principle that a confession frees a person from a fine is valid only if the confession preceded the appearance of witnesses since witnesses make his confession unnecessary. If the thief waits until the injured party has found witnesses he has to pay double restitution: the value of the object which he took as a debt and the double as a fine.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He said to the borrower, “where is my ox?” He told him, “it died,” but it broke, or was abducted, or stolen, or lost<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">These are the cases enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9">9</a>, where the unpaid keeper swears but does not have to pay. Breakage refers to vessels, not to animals; it is included since it appears in the verse.</i>. “It broke,” but it died, or was abducted, or stolen, or lost. “ ”It was abducted,” but it died, or broke, or was stolen, or lost. “It was stolen,” but it died, or broke, or was abducted, or lost. “It was lost,” but it died, or broke, or was abducted, or stolen. “I want you to swear;” he answered “Amen”. He is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since the borrower pays for any of the reasons mentioned, by his oath he does not deprive the owner of any money. Therefore no sacrifice is due.</i>.<br>“Where is my ox?” He told him, “I do not know.” “What are you telling?” But if had died, or broke, or was abducted, or stolen, or lost. “I want you to swear;” he answered “Amen”. He is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">He denies ever having borrowed an ox. His oath is to the detriment of the owner; a sacrifice is due in addition to the restitution of 125% of the value.</i>.<br>He said to the recipient of a fee or to a renter, “where is my ox?” He told him, “it died,” but it broke or was abducted; “it broke,” but it died or was abducted; “it was abducted,” but it died or broke; “it was stolen,” but it was lost; “it was lost,” but it was stolen. “I want you to swear;” if he said “Amen”, he is not liable.<br>“It died, or it broke, or it was abducted,” when it was stolen or lost. “I want you to swear;” if he said “Amen”, he is liable. “It was lost, or stolen,” when it died, or broke, or was abducted; he is not liable. This is the principle: Anybody who swears to his benefit is liable, to his detriment is not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: If it was neither to his benefit nor to his detriment, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">There is a <i>baraita</i> which disagrees with the Mishnah and holds that a false oath is a false oath even if the false formulation does no harm to the other party.</i>. Rebbi said, a <i>baraita</i> says so: “If he said to the borrower, the unpaid trustee, the recipient of a fee, or the renter, ‘where is my ox?’ This one said, ‘it died.’<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 6:7 starts in this way; but the <i>baraita</i> must have the person who swore falsely not to the detriment of the depositor as liable to the penalties of a false oath.</i>” May he tell him, “come and swear to me that you never thought of rustling it”<sup class="footnote-marker">128,</sup><i class="footnote">Could the depositor ask that the paid trustee or renter, or even the borrower, who paid for the lost or stolen object, nevertheless swear that they do not hold the object in their possession? The first argument is against, for the oath will not change the monetary situation. The second argument is in favor; there is a value to one’s own possession which cannot be expressed in monetary terms. The answer is not given, nor is the question raised in the Babli. In the absence of guidance, a court cannot act.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of an object stolen from an unpaid keeper. If he cannot recoup his loss he might be tempted to let the keeper swear in the hope that he might prefer to pay rather than swear.</i>? In any case he would not pay; what could he say? “Even if you would pay me a lot, I want from you what is mine.”
